HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE DEMOCRAT;
For All of His Admonitions, Clinton Shows Ebullient Side

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM 

DATELINE: PHILADELPHIA, Sept. 25

 BODY:
President Clinton has issued standing orders to his staff that overconfidence is the cardinal sin of front-running campaigns, and he tries hard to honor that dictum as he swoops around the country, striving to squeeze an extra hour of sunlight from the shortening days of fall.
"This is no time for complacency in the political campaign," the President told a Democratic Party fund-raising reception here this afternoon. "Believe me, it is not over. In some ways, it may be just about to begin." 
 But occasionally Mr. Clinton cannot quite help himself and gives in to his own upbeat mood, as he did this morning at Robert Morris College in Coraopolis, Pa., outside Pittsburgh.
His stated agenda was the official announcement introducing an inflation-proof Government bond, the latest in a series of modest proposals for the middle class.
But the President's broader business was a reverie on the boundless future he sees ahead with himself in continued charge, and the work he wants yet to do.
"Four years ago, just before the Presidential election, I came to Pittsburgh," Mr. Clinton told the students, "and said that I had entered that campaign because -- and I'd like to quote -- I didn't want my daughter to grow up to be part of the first generation of Americans to do worse than her parents, because I knew we could do better with more opportunity and more responsibility and a stronger American community.
"Well, today I'm here to tell you that I'm not worried about that anymore, because America is on the right track and our children are going to do better."
The President went on to sketch a glimmering vision of expanding global markets, rising exports, increased access to education, a robotic mission to Mars, expanded health insurance coverage, a higher minimum wage, better job training and research advances that "will one day in the not-too-distant future enable every set of parents that has a little baby to get a map of the genetic structure of their child."
Mr. Clinton's latest prescription was the issuance of new Treasury bonds that will guard against inflation by tying their principal, and so their total return, to the Consumer Price Index.
The Treasury, which had considered the idea for years, announced in May that it planned to offer such bonds. Today Mr. Clinton simply re-announced the plan, fleshing out some details, in this electoral swing state where polls show him well ahead.
The new bonds are to be issued starting in January, in denominations as low as $1,000. By the following January, the Treasury is to offer as well a new inflation-protection savings bond, in amounts as small as $50.
The Dole campaign promptly dismissed the idea.
"You'd need a microscope and a pair of tweezers to find Bill Clinton's vision for a second term," said a Dole spokeswoman, Christina Martin. "Rather than acknowledge his true liberal plans to increase taxes and raise spending, Clinton is trying to hide behind an ever-increasing heap of mini-proposals that would expand the size and scope of government one inch at a time."
Sparring erupted, too, over terms for the second scheduled Presidential debate, a town-hall-style encounter in San Diego on Oct. 16.
The Clinton campaign spokesman, Joe Lockhart, accused the Dole campaign of a last-minute balking at a final agreement embodying terms decided by both camps last weekend. For its part, Mr. Dole's organization said it simply wanted a lectern that would help the nominee, his right arm disabled by war wounds, to take notes, and that it was proposing to move the start of the debate to 7 P.M., Eastern daylight time, from 9, so as not to conflict with any baseball playoff game that might be played that night, starting shortly after 8.
But Mr. Lockhart said the Clinton camp had warned of a possible baseball conflict in the original negotiations and had also agreed to allow candidates to use a "three-sided structure" to hold notes, a structure to be designed by the sponsoring Commission on Presidential Debates.
Asked as he walked down Locust Street here this evening with Mayor Edward G. Rendell whether he thought Mr. Dole did not want to debate, Mr. Clinton smiled and said: "There'll be debates. I'm sure of it."

GRAPHIC: Photo: President Clinton consulted with a special assistant, Stephen Goodin, at a campaign stop yesterday at Robert Morris College near Pittsburgh. (Paul Hosefros/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Liberties;
Slouching Toward Hartford

BYLINE: By MAUREEN DOWD 

DATELINE: ST. LOUIS

 BODY:
There is a fatalistic splendor to Bob Dole's campaign.
It isn't even really a campaign anymore. It's something more interesting, more absurd, more dark, an inarticulate slouching around cornfields in Ohio and half-empty gyms in Missouri, boldly courting bad luck, bad timing and bad metaphors, inexorably sound-biting itself toward doom. 
 The candidate who swallows, mutters and subverts his words is now totally dependent on a good performance in a debate in Hartford. He is so opaque that the reporters were arguing the other day about whether Mr. Dole was ranting about taxes on "bird seed" or "bird feed."
The campaign is still struggling to master the basics -- who they could get to write some good speeches, how they should handle the local press.
As he has since Iowa last winter, the more Mr. Dole campaigns, the more voters he drives away. He's hurting Republican chances of holding the House and Senate. He's losing Reagan Democrats. He's threatening his party with a historic rout. But that's not the weird part. The weird part is that he's not panicking. Clinically speaking, the Republican candidate is a perfect illustration of what a famous predecessor of Freud's once described as "the lovely indifference of hysterics" -- except that Bob Dole's indifference is sometimes unlovely.
He will give up leisurely wooing the G.O.P. base this week to spend several days tanning in Florida. (He's tanned, he's rested, he's losing.)
Consider yesterday's odd little rally at St. Louis University here. Mr. Dole's introductory music was "Play That Funky Music, White Boy" and the Beatles' "Tax Man." A Dole advance man in the back sent up a few wistful puffs from a little smoke-blowing machine. And a student in front of Mr. Dole waved a sign -- "Kemp's the Dude."
This is the sort of campaign where Mr. Dole, asked about Democratic "lies," detours to give Phil Gramm a little whack. "Phil Gramm says we've got to start pulling this wagon," he said. "Well, he didn't quite make it up the hill. So I got the wagon now, and I'm working on it."
This is the sort of campaign where Mr. Dole, trying to tar Bill Clinton as a liberal who wanted tax increases and bigger government, somehow ends up defending him as a steadfast man of principle. "These are not the actions of a finger-in-the-wind politician that's waiting for the polls," he said of the finger-in-the-wind President who's always waiting for the polls. (A headline in The Washington Post on Tuesday announced, "Clinton Says He Is No Liberal.")
Nothing is resonating -- the attacks on the President's character, on his liberalism, on his wife and her "power grab on health care" and on the inane inhaling-not inhaling question. It's not entirely the Dole campaign's fault. Bill Clinton presents a target unprecedented in American politics: a perpetual-motion machine, moving left, right and center, signing bills to please one constituency and promising to fix the bills he just signed to please another constituency.
Republicans just shrug, waiting for the end. Robert Teeter, the Michigan pollster who helped manage the last disastrous Republican race, Bush '92, came to see Mr. Dole's speech to the Economic Club of Detroit. He said afterward that perhaps the public saw Mr. Dole for too many years from the vantage point of looking down at his forehead on C-Span, as he presided over Senate floor fights. "The public doesn't like all that fighting," Mr. Teeter said.
It is a little poignant to watch, this lion-in-autumn performance by a man who dedicated his life to public service. There are affecting moments.
In Shelby, Ohio, Mr. Dole worked a rope line and a mother offered him her baby to hold.
"I'm afraid I might drop it," he said, but then gingerly cradled the baby in his left arm, with some help from a Secret Service agent.
Republicans wonder why Mr. Dole is so serene in the tumbrel. Why on earth did he want the nomination? Was it just for the sake of getting it, or keeping other people from having it?
He seems oddly resigned to his fate, losing to one more golden boy for whom he has little respect, losing to someone who didn't do it the hard way. That's how his story always ends, isn't it?

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE THIRD PARTY;
Perot Shifts Focus From the Presidency To Dole and Politics

BYLINE: By ERNEST TOLLERSON 

DATELINE: ST. LOUIS, Sept. 25

 BODY:
Barred from the Presidential debates and denied the prime-time television slots he wants for his lectures on public policy, Ross Perot has found a sharper focus for his 1996 campaign by once again running not so much for President as running against Washington political institutions, particularly Bob Dole.
In recent days, as he has fought for more television exposure and for a spot in the debates, Mr. Perot has taken on the the bipartisan Commission on Presidential Debates and the television networks. But he has singled out Mr. Dole, the Republican Presidential candidate, for criticism. 
 Even though, initially, President Clinton's representatives said they wanted Mr. Perot in at least one debate, negotiators for both the Clinton and Dole camps decided last Saturday night to exclude him. Nonetheless, on Sunday, it was Mr. Dole whom the Texan criticized several times on the NBC News program "Meet the Press."
In Mr. Perot's speech last night at St. Louis University, he ridiculed Mr. Dole's 15 percent tax cut plan without mentioning his name. "Neither party is going to face the problems and if you just watch what they've been saying, you heard his speech here today," Mr. Perot said. Then Mr. Perot lowered his voice an octave or two, as if to mimick Mr. Dole, saying, " 'I'm going to cut your taxes, and I'm going to give you this, and I'm going to give you that, and you give me your vote in return.' And they must think you're really stupid."
And earlier today, before Mr. Perot arrived here for the speech this evening, his supporters sought to portray Mr. Dole as a coward for opposing Mr. Perot's inclusion in the debates. On "Meet the Press," Mr. Perot described Mr. Dole "as the point man in wanting to make sure that I had no voice at all" and later asserted that Mr. Dole's opposition would not play well with independent voters or Reform Party members meeting in caucuses to endorse Congressional candidates.
"See, Senator Dole did something really dumb politically," Mr. Perot said. "He poisoned the attitudes of millions of independent voters who put the Republicans in power in the House and Senate. I don't think it would have forever poisoned the environment in this world to have me involved in the debates."
Mr. Perot even pulled Mr. Dole's war record into the discussion.
"But Senator Dole for his own self-interest was willing to sacrifice the Senate and House races so he wouldn't have to confront me," he said. "Now here is a guy who is supposed to be a war hero. You'd think he'd be willing to stand up and talk to another person, wouldn't you?"
Still, even though Mr. Dole may have opposed Mr. Perot's inclusion in the debates, it is the television networks and the debate commission that are the Reform candidate's main obstacles in getting his message across.
On Tuesday Mr. Perot asked the Federal Communications Commission to find that by refusing to sell him weekly 30-minute blocks in prime time, the networks had violated Federal rules guaranteeing candidates "reasonable access" to the broadcast system. He also argues that the networks violated the equal-time rule by not letting him buy 45-minute slots immediately after the Presidential and Vice-Presidential debates set for next month.
On Monday Mr. Perot sued the debate commission in Federal court, seeking to be included in the forums, or have them canceled.
"The Democratic and Republican parties should not be permitted to consolidate their monopoly on the political process by closing off the Presidential debates," Mr. Perot's lawsuit says, warning that excluding him "will only deepen the nation's cynicism about government."
This sharpened attack on politics in general, echoing themes from Mr. Perot's 1992 campaign, has led some analysts to question whether Mr. Perot's candidacy is taking a back seat to his broader goals of conducting a national television teach-in on his favorite issues, and building the Reform Party that he founded and financed into a truly competitive alternative to the major parties.
"I have never thought that Perot had a serious chance with the economy as strong as it is of actually winning the Presidency," said Gordon Black, a pollster and Perot adviser in 1992 who talks periodically with Mr. Perot's aides. "I have thought from the beginning that Perot could in fact educate the American people in such a way that it would fundamentally alter our direction in politics."
Indeed, on "Meet the Press" on Sunday, Mr. Perot discussed party building. He predicted that his candidacy could capture 25 percent or more of the vote on Nov. 5. This, he said, would give the Reform Party major-party status and more Federal money in 2000. It would, he added, also end the Democrats' and Republicans' ability "to punch us around" on access to the debates.
But Mr. Perot and his aides adamantly say that he is committed to winning the Presidency. "This is a very serious bid for the White House, $29 million in a two-month campaign," Russell J. Verney, the Reform Party's national coordinator, said recently. "We have ample money to run a competitive campaign and win. We're trying to demonstrate that you do not have to be independently wealthy to run in a new political party."
Because Mr. Perot has agreed to accept Federal money to run his campaign, he is limited in spending from his own fortune. Choosing to work within a finite budget, he is particularly interested in the free television exposure offered by the debates, as well as the legitimacy that being in the forums brings.
Indeed, for Mr. Perot, who sparingly goes out on the stump to mingle with voters, television exposure from debates and "infomercials" are his candidacy's primary oxygen source.
The debate commission's decision may be causing Mr. Perot to have second thoughts about how he decided to finance his campaign. In August he said he would accept $29 million in Federal money, and limit his spending to that amount plus whatever else he can raise in individual contributions of up to $1,000 each. He also said he would not accept donations from political action committees. Sometime this week, Mr. Verney said, Mr. Perot will ask the Federal Election Commission to waive its $50,000 limit on what a candidate may contribute from his own resources because, he said, the debate commission has put Mr. Perot at a disadvantage.
How Mr. Perot's efforts play with voters this year remains to be seen. But with the level of anxiety over the economy lower than in 1992, he is facing a fundamentally different electorate.
Most of the 1996 electorate is not shopping for an alternative candidate, said Curtis Gans, director of the Committee for the Study of the American Electorate, a nonprofit, nonpartisan research and public policy group in Washington.
"They might have looked for a centrist last November," he said. "But I don't think they are looking for an outsider right now. They are looking for somebody who can capably handle the affairs of state right now."
Mr. Gans describes the 1996 election as one in which voters believe they cannot "waste" their vote. Those who "dislike and mistrust" Mr. Clinton, he said, will vote for Mr. Dole. And those who are afraid of the "the Tom DeLay-Dick Armey freshman type of Republicanism," he said, referring to two members of the Newt Gingrich team in the House, will vote for the President.

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 3; Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1123 words

HEADLINE: EMPIRE BLUE CROSS SEEKING TO BECOME FOR-PROFIT GROUP

BYLINE: By MILT FREUDENHEIM 

 BODY:
Empire Blue Cross and Blue Shield, once one of the country's biggest insurers of last resort, plans to announce today that it will seek approval from New York State to become a for-profit company, bringing to a close its historic role as a safety net for the sickest people of the state.
To make the transformation, Empire would establish a charitable foundation to provide money to help the more than 2.5 million New York residents who do not have health insurance. 
 The planned conversion reflects the fierce competition in the health care market. With the deregulation of hospital rates in the state, Empire is losing the special treatment that was supposed to subsidize its unprofitable coverage of many people with no place else to go. To stay alive, the company is trying to convert its traditional fee-for-service money-losing business to managed care organizations, which can charge customers less but have little left over for social obligations.
For the tens of thousands of people who buy coverage from Empire as individuals, rather than through their employers, a conversion to a for-profit company would push them into health maintenance organizations, in which their health care options would be limited. Empire said that its 850 subscribers who are permanently disabled would continue to be covered under the plan.
The company said it planned to hold public hearings on the conversion to for-profit status before seeking approval from the state Insurance Department, which had already paved the way for changing Empire's rapidly growing managed health care business to a for-profit division. Approval is also required from the Attorney General, the State Supreme Court and the Federal Securities and Exchange Commission.
Under the conversion plan, the foundation would initially own 100 percent of the stock in the new for-profit Empire and over the next five years would sell almost all its holdings. The sales would establish a market value for the company, which could be far higher than Empire's current book value of $300 million. Empire executives said they would issue more stock later and sell it to the public to raise money to expand the company's business.
"We would get capital to invest in technology and partnerships with the best doctors and hospitals," said Dr. Michael A. Stocker, the president and chief executive of Empire Blue Cross and Blue Shield. "For the consumer, that would bring lower costs, improved service and state of the art products."
Consumer advocates were generally positive when told of Empire's plans yesterday.
"The challenge for consumer groups now is to see if Empire's conversion plan fits the public good and to help to flesh it out in defining the mission of the foundation," said Mark Scherzer, president of New Yorkers for Accessible Health Coverage, a coalition of voluntary health organizations with support from nurses and social workers.
Blue Cross companies in half a dozen states have changed partly or totally to for-profit status, and a few -- notably in California, Indiana, Wisconsin and Missouri -- have issued stock to public shareholders.
Moving to avert controversies that have delayed similar conversion plans by Blue Cross companies in Ohio and California, Empire said its executives would not receive windfall payments in stock or cash in the change-over.
Dr. Stocker said Empire's proposal "avoids many of the pitfalls experienced by other Blue Cross and Blue Shield plans and recognizes the public's investment in Empire over the years."
Blue Cross and Blue Shield plans were started in the Depression of the 1930's to help working people to pay hospitals and doctors. For decades Blues plans had little competition but in the 1980's and 1990's they lost millions of customers as many big companies eliminated jobs and turned to lower-priced managed care insurers to try to hold down medical-cost inflation.
In New York, Empire Blue Cross was a power in the state government for years but was slow to keep up with changes in health care economics as government regulations were lifted and marketplace forces took over. Amid ugly charges of corruption and mismanagement, Empire's top executives were displaced.
But the nonprofit company has recently emerged from a long decline. In the late 1980's it insured 11 million people, or about 60 percent of the state's population. It now has 4.7 million enrolled, of which 523,000 are in managed care plans -- health maintenance organizations, discount networks and combinations called point of service plans, which permit customers to go to any doctor or hospital if they pay for the privilege.
And in a promising sign for future profits, the number in managed care has grown by 34 percent, from 389,000 last Jan. 1, Dr. Stocker, the Empire chief, said.
A for-profit company would "add $7 million to New York State tax rolls," he added.
James A. Barba, president of Albany Medical Center and the chairman of a watchdog committee set up by the state to monitor Empire following reports of its mismanagement and heavy losses, said Dr. Stocker had told him about the planned announcement but that he had not had a chance to read it.
In March, the oversight committee said in a report to Gov. George E. Pataki and the Legislature that Empire Blue Cross was "a charitable asset that must be preserved" because it was created under the state's nonprofit corporation law.
"Our panel was very concerned about the value of the public asset," Mr. Barba said. "It sounds like this would effectively transfer that value to a not-for-profit that would have a defined social mission in health care. I like that."
Mr. Barba added, though, that he would suggest the charitable foundation be run by a board with a majority of directors who would not come from Empire.
Charles Bell, a spokesman for Consumer's Union, the advocacy group, said he was pleased that "Empire recognizes that its assets belong to the public." But he said it would be "a terrible conflict for Empire to retain control over how those assets would be managed and disbursed."
The company said that the foundation board would be independent but did not spell out the proposed membership. The national Blue Cross and Blue Shield Association has threatened to revoke the rights to its famous trademarks and name from Blue Cross and Blue Shield plans if they change to control by outsiders.
After struggling over this issue with consumer groups and state officials, California Blue Cross finally agreed to establish two foundations with combined assets of $3.3 billion in cash and stock. Blue Cross executives kept control of the stock, while an independent board was named for a cash-rich sister foundation.

HEADLINE: POLITICS;
In His Own Words

 BODY:

PRESIDENT CLINTON
 
Speaking yesterday at Robert Morris College in Coraopolis, Pa.:
"We have to continue to stay on the cutting edge of change. You should support an investment of an appropriate amount of your tax dollars in research and technology, because that is the key to our future. Let me give you some examples that I think you can all identify with. 
 "The Human Gene Project at the National Institutes of Health, also being supported in universities all across America, will one day in the not-too-distant future enable every set of parents that has a little baby to get a map of the genetic structure of their child. So if their child has a predisposition to a certain kind of illness or a certain kind of problem, or even to heart disease or stroke in the early 40's, they will be able to plan that child's life, that child's upbringing, to minimize the possibility of the child developing that illness or that predisposition, to organize the diet plan, the exercise plan, the medical treatment that would enable untold numbers of people to have far more full lives than would have been the case before. It will also create huge numbers of new, fascinating high-wage jobs that have not even been invented yet for people like the young people who go to Robert Morris."

HEADLINE: POLITICS: NEWS ANALYSIS;
A Muted Dole Persona

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr. 

DATELINE: ST. LOUIS, Sept. 25

 BODY:
Bob Dole is a rare bird among modern politicians. Most, with the help of hired troupes of packagers, strategists and pollsters, make themselves more attractive to the public at large than they are to insiders who know them well and understand their weaknesses as well as strengths.
With Mr. Dole, it is the other way around. 
 Ask legislators of either party, journalists who have covered him for many years, or other old Washington hands. Almost without exception, they will tell you that he is strong, decent, honest, a world-class legislative craftsman. When he retired from Capitol Hill, Senator Dianne Feinstein, a California Democrat, described him as a man whose word could always be trusted, by friend or foe.
But as Mr. Dole campaigned again this week in states where he ought to be doing better than he is -- Michigan, Ohio, Missouri and Iowa -- it was clear that precious little of that respect has filtered through to voters, especially the swing voters so influential in Presidential elections. In polls and interviews beyond the Beltway, a startling number of them describe him as mean or vindictive, shifty, too old, inept.
"He reminds me of Barry Goldwater in 1964," said Robert Teeter, the Michigan consultant who helped guide George Bush's unsuccessful re-election bid in 1992. "People on the Hill liked Goldwater much more than Lyndon Johnson, including a lot of Democratic senators. They liked his humor and his charm. But the country as a whole saw him as an extremist, and he never had a chance."
Bob Dole would have faced a difficult race this year no matter what people thought about his personality. The nation is at peace, the economy reasonably strong, and he faces an incumbent -- no formula for easy electoral success.
Still, charm always counts for something in politics, and it may count for more in an election like this one, with few issues catching the public interest and a Democratic nominee, Bill Clinton, who throughout his career has used his own considerable charm as a life raft in the whirlpools of politics. Personality has certainly not worked to Mr. Dole's benefit in the contest to date.
Some senior Republicans think the public sees Mr. Dole as a man out of step with the times, a man known to most Americans not for patient backroom negotiations with Presidents and Democratic leaders in the Senate but for slashing, sometimes sarcastic speeches on the Senate floor, televised on C-Span, and for his often highly combative appearances over the years on the Sunday political talk shows.
He is known, in short, as a partisan, in a day when partisanship is unpopular, and as a Washington fixture, in a day when Washington is unpopular. People mention him in the same breath as Speaker Newt Gingrich, whom he resembles in neither style nor substance.
Mr. Dole's resignation from the Senate was supposed to change those perceptions, but it does not seem to have done so. Impressions built up over decades do not fade away in a matter of months. Another reason, perhaps, is that he has not really changed his approach. Understandably, given his many years in Congress, he retains the viewpoint of the lawmaker, even when he remembers to remind his audience that he is no longer a member of the Senate. He still talks about the tax problems of Subchapter-S corporations, which may produce knowing nods in the cloakroom but evoked only uncomprehending stares Tuesday in rural Shelby, Ohio.
He is no more the hail fellow, well met, than ever. He draws no strength from plunging into crowds, in the style of Bill Clinton or Robert F. Kennedy or Lyndon B. Johnson. To make things worse, he gives the impression of "a man in a box," as a staff member put it Tuesday, unable to be fully himself.
"Someone, I don't know who, has stripped Bob Dole of the things that made him appealing," a Midwestern Republican official said. "This was a man who got things done in Washington, who broke the logjams people hate. This was a man with a fine, ironic sense of humor, which he used to puncture stuffed shirts. But none of that shows up now."
When the Dole wit occasionally peeks through, audiences find it appealing. In Detroit, for example, a preliminary speaker made a remark about having recently made a dive in a Navy submarine. When his turn came, Mr. Dole could not resist commenting, "the last dive I took was that one out in Chico, Calif.," where he fell off a stage when a railing gave way. The audience laughed and clapped.
But there are few spontaneous wisecracks these days. The old iconoclasm about politics and politicians is muted, at least in public. And it is noticeable that Mr. Dole, a master of give-and-take with television questioners in his Senate days, gives few interviews to anchormen or radio talk-show hosts as he moves around the country. One theory among aides is that he was made gun-shy by the storm kicked up when he off-handedly questioned whether tobacco was addictive.
Whether as a result of his own decision, that of his advisers, or both, the Republican nominee has become almost bland -- the last thing anyone would have expected a year ago.
Mr. Dole seems uncomfortable talking about his wartime experiences, something that most men of his generation have avoided, especially those from his part of the country, the Great Plains, where real men are men of few words. For years he, too, avoided the subject. Yet now he mentions his wounds almost compulsively, in almost every speech, as if they are his only hope of establishing his bona fides in the brave new world where people touch and feel and share.
It is not much more successful than Adlai E. Stevenson's episodic efforts to shed his aristocratic skin and pose as a man of the people, or Hubert H. Humphrey's half-hearted attempts to play the Cold Warrior instead of the Happy Warrior, or Michael S. Dukakis's brief whirl in the turret of a tank.
The result is a campaign that for now at least lacks thrust and urgency. The schedule is slack. The speeches are full of inconsistencies. In Detroit, Mr. Dole spoke at the outset about an economy in tatters but at the end about the boom in Michigan (which is echoed throughout this region). At St. Louis University this morning, he lambasted President Clinton as "liberal, liberal, liberal," after spending weeks portraying him as a waffler without real convictions.
None of this is lost on his more perceptive aides, most of whom now regard the two nationally televised Presidential debates next month as his last best chance to "show people who he really is." That was to have been accomplished in the primaries; it was not. It was to have been accomplished at the convention; it was not. Now, in the words of one discouraged old friend, there are only six weeks left for "his simplicity and plainness of character to shine through."

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Empire Blue Cross Plans To Seek For-Profit Status
Empire Blue Cross and Blue Shield of New York, one of the country's biggest nonprofit health care companies, said it would seek state approval to become a for-profit company that would no longer be the health insurer of last resort. To stay competitive, Empire is trying to convert its fee-for-service business to managed-care organizations, which can charge customers less but have little left over for social obligations.
Empire said it would try to continue its traditional role by setting up a charitable foundation that would provide money to help more than 2.5 million New York residents who do not have health insurance. [Page A1.]
 
Home Resales Slip Again
Home resales fell modestly in August, the third straight monthly decline as the housing market showed signs of leveling off. The National Association of Realtors said sales of existing houses fell one-half of 1 percent to an annual rate of 4.13 million. [D2.]
 
Plan for Japan's Finance Ministry
Japan's governing coalition announced a program for reorganizing the powerful Finance Ministry. But the plan -- which would leave the ministry mostly intact while creating an independent financial inspection and supervisory commission -- will probably disappoint proponents of greater change. [D4.]
 
Lehman Not So Likely a Target
Investors and analysts cite a number of potential acquirers for Lehman Brothers, and a quarterly earnings increase will hardly dispel the talk. Still, the rumors may be overdone. Market Place. [D10.]
 
Genetics Institute in 2 Deals
The Genetics Institute said it had developed a library of several thousand genes and related proteins that could be scanned for potential treatments, and announced deals with Genentech and Chiron for low-cost access to the library in return for a share of profits from any resulting drugs. [D4.]
 
Protein Design Labs' shares soared 61 percent. A drug it developed with F. Hoffman-LaRoche proved effective in preventing the rejection of transplanted kidneys. [D4.]
 
Bid for Loewen Becoming Hostile
Service Corporation International said it would pursue its acquisition of the Loewen Group, despite the Loewen board's rejection of a $3.1 billion buyout offer. [D2.]
 
I.M.F. Sees Steady Global Growth
The International Monetary Fund said it expected annual growth among industrial nations of around 2.5 percent until the year 2000. But it suggested that the United States raise interest rates to head off inflation. [D5.]
 
What's That You Say? Have a Beer.
Good advertising, the saying goes, sparks conversation. But can conversation spark good advertising? Heineken, the Dutch brewer, thinks so and its current campaign promotes its beer with a series of TV commercials that focus on snippets of conversations. Advertising. [D9.]
 
S.E.C. Call for Metric Trades
A member of the Securities and Exchange Commission said that American stock markets should convert to a decimal-based system of pricing within three years. [D8.]

HEADLINE: THEATER REVIEW;
Daughter Feasting On Grief

BYLINE: By BEN BRANTLEY 

 BODY:
GRIEF walks in circles in the National Theater of Greece's hypnotic interpretation of Sophocles' "Elektra." The emotion, like the tragedy's anger-shrouded heroine, traces an orbit of despair that seems as fixed, in its way, as the figures on Keats's urn.
In the title role, Lydia Koniordou moves in a repetitive, fluid path that corresponds to the oval of sand that dominates the stage at City Center, where the production (the company's first in New York in two decades) runs through Sunday. The play may end with the redressing of the past wrongs that have enslaved and embittered Elektra. But one leaves the theater with the sense that the chain of dark memories and lamentations can never really be broken. 
 More than any of the great plays of the classical Greek theater, "Elektra" is an anatomy of a single emotion. While it addresses the cycle of murder and retribution in the bloody House of Atreus covered by Aeschylus' and Euripides' versions of the same tale, its focus is far less on the horrific events of a legend than the way they have shaped one mind.
Much of the tragedy, accordingly, is a sustained, if diversely inflected, keen, like that of a woman at a wailing wall. The story of the unholy murder of Elektra's father, Agamemnon, by his wife, Clytemnestra, and her lover, Aegisthus, is told again and again. Elektra insists on it. "Can it be honorable to forget the dead?" she asks, in response to the admonitions of the chorus, which observes, quite justly, that this woman knows nothing of moderation.
"Elektra" has been a favorite vehicle for actresses of strapping bravery and ambition, most notably Katina Paxinou, the Greek actress best known here for her Oscar-winning performance in "For Whom the Bell Tolls." It is risky territory.
In uncertain hands, Elektra can seem like the kvetch of all times, tempting audiences to sympathize with the character's mother and sister and the chorus, who keep telling her what amounts to "Enough of the complaining already, honey." The challenge is in universalizing her monomania while holding on to her unique psychological strength.
Fortunately, mourning does indeed become this Elektra. Ms. Koniordou, who is also the show's director (in association with Dimitris Economou), has found a transfixing vitality in the part that speaks to everyone who believes that only remembrance can keep the dead alive.
Her life may have "gone by without hope," as she says, since the murder of her father in her childhood, but this is not a woman who has been stunted by grief. Indeed, she has feasted on it.
As Elektra alternates between contemplation of revenge and her own desired death, the commanding, large-boned Ms. Koniordou, all feverish eyes and knifelike gestures, seems only to grow in a perverse, superhuman willfulness. (Portraying her mother, Aspasia Papathanasiou seems to have shriveled inversely from fear and bitterness.)
Ms. Koniordou's performance and the production itself, although spoken in modern Greek, have a purely physical eloquence in which a harrowing ferocity of spirit is contained in ritualized movements. (On the other hand, the translated supertitles are bizarrely, even comically, clunky; you would definitely do well to read the play before seeing it.)
The sense of a circle, again, is crucial. As Elektra quarrels with her more practical, gentle-spirited sister, Chrysothemis (Tania Papadopoulou), and her tyrannical, conflicted mother, the women orbit each other in a slow, stately dance, maintaining an isolating distance.
The hems of their shawls and dresses (the striking, simple set and costumes are by Dionysis Fotopoulos) trace corresponding arcs in the sand. And when the pattern is broken, with the characters lunging into a fierce embrace or stranglehold, it is viscerally shocking.
The 17-woman chorus, meticulously choreographed by Apostolia Papadamaki, reinforces the same spiritual geometry, breaking and regrouping into curved or angled lines that suggest both awed timidity and the cohesiveness of a social order. There is mellifluous harmony and, at moments, confused dissonance in their collective voice. Before Elektra's imposing sorrow, they can reprove, recoil or console. They cannot, finally, assimilate it.
Takis Farazis's music, a primal, haunted blend of percussion and woodwinds, and the sung lamentations all feed into the sense of a world in which mourning and the hope of revenge have become in themselves a religion. Deliverance finally arrives, in the form of Elektra's long-exiled brother, Orestes (Miltos Dimoulis), and the rhythms speed up accordingly as the play gallops toward a blood-soaked denouement.
Actually, once the real action takes over in "Elektra," the production loses its grip. This is partly because the men -- who also include Stefanos Kyriakidis as Aegisthus and Alexandros Mylonas as Orestes' mentor -- don't really have characters to play. But one also suspects that Sophocles' greater interest was in the psychology of a woman who made a cult of her suffering.
This Elektra achieves her apotheosis not with the play's end, but when she is earlier told (falsely) that her brother has been killed. "Now all I hold is nothingness," says Elektra, her sculptural face gone hollow, and the circle of anguish in which she has been trapped seems frozen into eternity.

ELEKTRA
 
By Sophocles; directed by Lydia Koniordou, in association with Dimitris Economou; sets and costumes by Dionysis Fotopoulos; choreography by Apostolia Papadamaki; lighting by Alekos Yiannaros; music by Takis Farazis; modern Greek translation by Yorgos Heimonas. The National Theater of Greece, presented by ICM Artists, in association with Kritas Productions. At City Center, 131 West 55th Street, Manhattan.

With: Lydia Koniordou (Elektra), Miltos Dimoulis (Orestes), Aspasia Papathanasiou (Clytemnestra), Tania Papadopoulou (Chrysothemis), Stefanos Kyriakidis (Aegisthus) and Alexandros Mylonas (Tutor).

HEADLINE: In Defense Spending, How Much Is Too Much?

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In "Tooth Fairy Defense Budgets" (Op-Ed, Sept. 25), Jonathan Rauch argues strongly against President Clinton's and Bob Dole's budget proposals because, after adjustment for inflation, they both imply cuts in future United States military spending. Mr. Rauch's arguments also have a "tooth fairy" quality, because they ignore important factors that must be considered as part of any rational military spending policy. 
 First, it must be noted that the United States, with a military budget in the neighborhood of $260 billion, is spending almost as much as it did at the peak of the cold war, when the Soviet Union was spending as much as we were, if not more. The cold war is over, the Soviet-East European bloc has been dismantled, and the Soviet Union itself has broken up. The military performance of Russia in Chechnya is a good indication that we have nothing to fear from this quarter.
Is $260 billion enough to meet our current military needs? One way of approaching this issue is to ask how much other nations are spending. According to The Defense Monitor, a newsletter published by the Center for Defense Information, the United States is spending almost as much as the combined military budgets of Japan, France, Britain, Germany, Russia, China, Italy, Saudi Arabia and South Korea. Certainly, this country has the greatest military superiority of any nation in modern times.
Another factor that is largely ignored by Mr. Rauch is that the United States has many important needs, currently unmet, that could be financed by reductions in military expenditures; to put this another way, increases in military spending lead to cuts in nonmilitary expenditures. In fact, Mr. Clinton's budget proposal for 1995 included more than $10 billion in such cuts. Limiting military spending makes sense in a nation that is near full employment and has a large budget deficit and an irrational bias against increasing taxes.
 
FRANKLYN D. HOLZMAN
Lexington, Mass. Sept. 25, 1996
 
The writer is a fellow at the Davis Center for Russian Studies at Harvard University.

HEADLINE: Without Wall Street, There Is No Main Street

 BODY:
To the Editor:
In Russell Baker's Sept. 24 column on the end of the era of big government, he writes that "shrewd bottom-line guys will find a way to convert human misfortune into good numbers for Wall Street." This statement may be colorful for a columnist but is a serious disservice to the nation, its history and its attitudes. 
 Capitalism, or Wall Street as Mr. Baker calls it, has created a nation that has done more to solve the problems of human misfortune than any other nation in history. Problem-solving companies (financed by capitalists) have been created that have expanded the human potential, increased our longevity and enriched the daily experience of millions by virtue of the freedom provided by the free-market economic system we often carelessly take for granted.
The world is littered with well-meaning socialist or Communist countries that in their quest for utopian societies have left more misery in their wake. It is time for people like Mr. Baker to understand that Wall Street is really America's Main Street.
 
HARVEY P. MALLEMENT
New York, Sept. 25, 1996
 
The writer is a managing partner of a private equity investment firm.

HEADLINE: CURRENCY MARKETS;
Swiss Rate Cut Weakens Mark And Drives Up Dollar's Value

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
A surprisingly large cut in Swiss interest rates weakened the German mark yesterday, driving the dollar sharply higher.
Continuing improvement in the American bond market also contributed to the second consecutive strong showing for the dollar. It rebounded on Wednesday from a selloff the day before prompted by the Federal Reserve's decision not to raise interest rates. 
 Yesterday, the Swiss rate cut in particular created a big demand for dollars, as traders moved out of Swiss francs. Lower rates tend to diminish a currency's value. "People bought dollars in Europe and we followed through," said Peter Ullrich, vice president at Giro Credit Bank in New York. He said the Swiss move "really gave a turbo-boost to the dollar."
In late New York trading, the dollar rose to 1.5233 marks, from 1.5156 marks on Wednesday. It also rose to 110.66 yen from 110.44 yen, and to 1.2528 Swiss francs from 1.2398 francs. They were the highest levels for the dollar since mid-July. The British pound ended unchanged at $1.5610, partly a result of the pound's own strength against the mark and other European currencies.
The Swiss central bank's half-percentage-point reduction in the discount rate put the key rate at 1 percent, the lowest in nearly two decades. The move was intended to help stimulate a lethargic economy.
But the move was also seen as a sign of broader economic trends suggesting that Switzerland's neighbors would have to eventually reduce interest rates, as well, to help improve growth. "I think this reaffirmed to traders that rates are going to be cut in Europe and that Germany will cut rates at some point," said Ralph Delzenero, vice president for foreign-exchange marketing at the First National Bank of Chicago. "Traders tend to forget they still have a recession over there."
The dollar received additional help yesterday from weakness in the mark over further evidence that plans for a single European currency beginning in 1999 would not be disrupted. The latest sign came from Trade Minister Augusto Fantozzi of Italy, who said his country's 1997 budget plans would help bring its deficit down to 3 percent of the gross domestic product, an important criterion for European countries to join in monetary union.

HEADLINE: CREDIT MARKETS;
Bond Prices Post Increase For 4th Day

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Treasury prices advanced for a fourth consecutive session yesterday, bolstered by a durable goods report that pointed to a slowing economy.
For a second full day after the Federal Reserve's decision to leave interest rates unchanged, worries about higher rates were greatly eased in the market. 
 The price of the 30-year Treasury bond rose 17/32 , to 9811/32 . The bond's yield, which moves in the opposite direction from the price, fell to 6.88 percent, from 6.92 percent on Wednesday.
Bond prices were also bolstered by a Treasury five-year-note auction.
But prices were chiefly supported by a report showing orders for durable goods fell 3.1 percent in August, after rising a revised 1.4 percent in July. Without transportation, the durables orders were down 1.6 percent.
Traders said the decline was larger than the average economists had forecast, which was for a decline of three-tenths of a percent.
It left the impression that the large increase the market saw in July was a distortion and not a signal that the economy was strengthening. While the July report had raised worries that the economy was strengthening, yesterday's Commerce Department report dispelled fears.
"Although durable goods orders tend to be erratic, the signs of a manufacturing slowdown are emerging," said Kenneth J. McCarthy, head of the Economic Intelligence Company.
Donald Fine, chief market analyst at Chase Asset Management, said "new orders for durable goods are still exhibiting healthy, moderate growth, but at the same time the trend is slowing, albeit at a very gradual pace."
The data appeared to confirm to many traders and analysts that the Federal Reserve took the right step in not raising rates.
"In this environment," Mr. McCarthy said, "it was certainly appropriate for the Fed to hold off increasing rates, and we expect that as more evidence of slow growth emerges in the next few months, the Fed will opt to leave rates unchanged in 1997."
But Mr. Fine said that he expected that Fed policy makers "most assuredly will maintain a bias toward tightening until conditions dictate otherwise."
"Indeed, the Fed still has its finger on the trigger as it hunts inflationary prey," he said.
The Treasury's $12.5 billion sale of new five-year notes went about as expected, traders said. The notes were awarded at a high yield of 6.409 percent, down from 6.568 percent in August and the lowest since April 24, when it was 6.279 percent.
The bid-to-cover ratio, or number of investors bidding for the issue compared with the total size of the sale, was above average at 2.57 to 1.
Damon Whelchel, an analyst with MMS International, San Francisco, said that before yesterday's auction the average bid-to-cover over the last 12 auctions was 2.41 to 1.
Doug Burtnick, senior fixed-income analyst with Technical Data, Boston, said, "With two auctions down, the markets are floating near the highs of the recent trading range."
Even though market sentiment has shifted, he said, the market will probably need some fresh news to push it higher. "Investors are generally reluctant to buy into strong rallies, so they have their fingers crossed for a bit of a backup into next week."

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
Reappointed, I.M.F. Chief Asks New Funds

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
The International Monetary Fund's newly reappointed managing director, Michel Camdessus, presented a substantial shopping list for his organization today, asking for more than $100 billion in new money to enable the fund to safeguard an international financial system increasingly dominated by volatile capital flows.
But senior American monetary officials said his demands were far too ambitious and constituted an opening bid in the coming debate over increasing I.M.F. resources. Still, Secretary of the Treasury Robert E. Rubin said at a subsequent news conference that the Administration favored strengthening the fund so it would be better equipped to deal with future Mexico-type crises. 
 Mr. Camdessus, 63, a former French central bank governor who has just been given his third five-year term at the I.M.F.'s helm, said at his own news conference before the annual ministerial meetings of the fund and the World Bank here that he wanted the fund's 180 nations to double its $200 billion in capital -- or at a minimum, to increase it by 50 percent.
He also called for the creation of 26 billion new reserve units, or special drawing rights, with an approximate value of $36 billion. They would be distributed to members according to a formula favoring formerly Communist countries, which never received these assets. Both the increase in the I.M.F.'s capital and the drawing rights distribution are subject to approval by approval by Parliaments, which could take two years or more.
Mr. Camdessus stressed that I.M.F. lending to emerging and formerly Communist economies introducing market-oriented reforms had soared to $17.2 billion last year from $3.6 billion in 1989.
He said he was now confident that the I.M.F. would vote to sell as much as $2 billion worth of its gold to help provide debt relief for the poorest countries, if national contributions proved insufficient as he feared.
He recalled that the world's richest nations were currently doubling to $55 billion a special credit line they offer the I.M.F. in times of financial upheaval, known as the General Arrangement to Borrow.
Secretary Rubin, speaking in advance of Saturday's meeting of finance ministers from the Group of Seven leading industrial nations, said that his main concerns were to continue strengthening the international financial system and to encourage steady economic growth worldwide.
He said ministers would concentrate on the financial initiatives that Group of Seven heads of government had endorsed at their Naples and Halifax meetings in the last two years, intended to ward off new financial crises in the emerging economies and to encourage private investment.
This involves increasing the I.M.F.'s resources, improving disclosure so that markets are better informed about the finances of emerging economies, and strengthening regulatory authorities.
The debt-relief initiative has split the Group of Seven, with the United States, France, Britain and Canada favoring I.M.F. gold sales and Germany and Italy opposing them, contending that the sales would undermine the institution's credibility. Anticipating passage, Mr. Rubin said that once gold sales were agreed to, he would ask the United States Congress for a cash contribution, as well.
This is in line with Mr. Camdessus' original proposal that gold sales provide half the I.M.F. share of the plan's cost -- also being borne by the World Bank, with national contributions making up the rest.
Discussing growth, Mr. Rubin said Japan must maintain its economic recovery by increasing demand. And he said he wanted to review Europe's single-currency plan, which some economists consider deflationary.
The senior American monetary officials said Mr. Camdessus was asking more than Washington and other Group of Seven governments were likely to agree to. They said that a review of quotas and a special S.D.R. distribution had been accepted in principle but not in numerical detail.
And they recalled that at the I.M.F.'s 50th anniversary meeting in Madrid two years ago, Mr. Camdessus had split the members with a proposal to increase the fund's financial importance by allowing it to issue special drawing rights worth $53 billion. That plan -- while welcomed by poorer countries -- was vetoed as inflationary by the Group of Seven, which offered rights worth $23 billion instead.

HEADLINE: POLITICS: POLITICAL MEMO;
A Coattails Reversal From '94 Races

BYLINE: By RICHARD L. BERKE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 25

 BODY:
It may be hard to imagine given Bob Dole's standing in the polls, but a Democratic candidate for the United States Senate closed his television commercial with this tag line: "A majority of the time, I vote with the Republican leader, Bob Dole."
But that was 1994, when Senator Kent Conrad of North Dakota and many other Democrats, were "running from Bill Clinton like scalded dogs," in the words of Haley Barbour, the Republican Party chairman. That year, at least, it was fashionable for Republicans (and at least one Democrat) to hitch their electoral fortunes to the then Senate minority leader, Mr. Dole, or the man who would be Speaker, Newt Gingrich. 
 Today, most Republican candidates never mention Mr. Gingrich and rarely boast about (let alone mention) the signature tax cut proposal of Mr. Dole, now their Presidential nominee. Democrats, meanwhile, are clamoring to be photographed beside President Clinton when he pays a visit to their area.
Politics, by nature, is a volatile business, but never in recent history has there been as stark a role reversal in the dynamic between Presidential and Congressional candidates as since the 1994 mid-term elections. The turnabout underscores how quickly voters' perceptions about their political leaders can change. But it also reflects a shift in how the parties are presenting themselves -- and the degree to which both sides may have overreacted in gauging the public's yearning for a Republican Congress that would pull the Government rightward.
Even in North Carolina, where Mr. Dole is ahead in the polls, many Republicans are not particularly eager to tie themselves to the man at the top of the ticket.
"I mean, this ain't Reagan running this year," Marc Rotterman, a Republican consultant who is handling two Congressional campaigns in the state, said the other day.
Just two years ago, Republican Congressional candidates were all too eager to promote their party's leaders, putting a national cast to their races and depicting Mr. Clinton as the villain. They also presented an issue agenda, the Contract With America. Democrats, meanwhile, ran their own races; even Mr. Clinton's pollster at the time, Stanley Greenberg, advised some Democrats against linking their own campaigns to the President.
This year, it is as if Republicans are heeding Mr. Greenberg's advice that "voters want to know that you are fighting to get things done for them, not that you are advancing some national agenda."
And, Democrats seem to be following Mr. Barbour's playbook from 1994, when he counseled Republican candidates that "the only way to change Washington is to change Congress." This year, when Mr. Clinton visits a town, there is hardly any room in the V.I.P. section, as Democrats who did not want to be seen near their President in 1994 squeeze next to him. Some Republicans -- all but given up on Mr. Dole's prospects -- are even assuring their constituents that they work well with the Democrat in the White House.
Another unexpected dynamic this year is the like-minded interests between Mr. Clinton and Republican Congressional candidates, with each offering themselves as a guard against the other. Even as Vice President Al Gore has been dispatched to attack the "Dole-Gingrich Congress," Mr. Clinton has founded much of his campaign on the notion that he provides a check on the conservative Congress. Just the same, Mr. Dole almost never utters the word "Gingrich" these days, and many Republican Congressional candidates, worried that Mr. Dole will lose, offer themselves as a check on the Democrat in the White House.
Brett Bader, a Republican consultant, said his party's Congressional candidates in Washington state will be most successful if they send the message, "If you are going to vote for Clinton, fine, but you are going to need someone to watch over him."
The altered dynamics make for an odd spectacle on the campaign trail; this year it is the Democrats who invoke Mr. Gingrich, while many Republicans insist that their races are being waged on local matters.
Perhaps the most obvious contrast is in Virginia, where just last year, Republican candidates for the state legislature sought to repeat their party's national success in 1994. Emulating the House Republicans' contract, candidates in the Old Dominion campaigned on a "Pledge for Honest Change," which called for welfare reform and cuts in taxes and spending. Mr. Clinton was so anathema in the state that Democratic candidates pointedly called themselves "Virginia" Democrats -- or advertised no party at all.
But the Republicans failed in their bid to take over the legislature. And this year, with polls showing that Mr. Clinton has a respectable shot at becoming the first Democratic nominee to win Virginia since 1964, he is now the surprising new running mate for several of the state's Democratic Congressional contenders.
Tom Horton, a Democratic candidate for Congress in the Washington suburb of Springfield, sent out a flyer last week featuring a photograph of himself with Mr. Clinton at the White House. His opponent, Representative Tom Davis, a freshman, is keeping his distance from Mr. Dole. Asked whether he mentioned his party's nominee when he campaigns, he replied, "Depends who I'm talking to."
In rural Sperryville, Bob Weinberg, a Democrat running for the House, proudly said he was campaigning on the slogan, "Re-elect Bill and take back the Hill."
Mr. Weinberg, who criticizes the Gingrich Congress at every opportunity, marveled at Mr. Clinton's popularity in Virginia. "You can feel it at all the county fairs," he said. "The Clinton bumper stickers go like hotcakes. People at the county fairs tell me they've never seen Democrats get such a friendly reception, and I'm sure it wasn't my smile. It was the economy."
Republicans acknowledge the problem. Representative Cass Ballenger, who is considered a safe bet for re-election in his western North Carolina district, said his goal -- and that of his 234 Republican colleagues in the House -- will be to help drag Mr. Dole to victory. "The coattails issue is going to be reversed," he said. "We've got 235 Congressmen. If we can go home and campaign hard, we might put Bob Dole in with us."
Still, some Republicans worry that Mr. Dole's weakness in the polls might discourage voters from turning out, and thus deprive down-ballot candidates of support. "If they get so turned off they don't come out at all," said Virginia Blankenbaker, a Republican who is seeking a House seat in Indianapolis, "it could hurt." She said it "would be counterproductive" to have Mr. Dole campaign on her behalf -- even though Indiana has long been viewed as a Republican state -- adding that "I need to stay with my race and keep it isolated" from the national ticket.
As much as many Republicans are keeping their distance from Messrs. Dole and Gingrich while Mr. Clinton is enjoying a burst of popularity, remnants of 1994 attitudes remain.
In North Carolina, for instance, few Democrats go out of their way to mention the President. "Maybe they're softening a little bit," Gov. James B. Hunt Jr., a Democrat, said of critical attitudes toward Mr. Clinton in his state, "but the basic feelings are what they were."
And in Alexandria, Va., Representative James P. Moran Jr., a Democrat, avoids talking about Mr. Clinton on the trail. "He's running his own campaign, and that's fine; we're running ours," he said. "I got more votes than he did in '92."
While Mr. Moran's Republican rival, John Otey, is not to the point of boasting about Mr. Dole in his advertisements, he still runs proudly with his party's nominee. "We're both from Kansas, and we're both veterans," Mr. Otey said. "I'm just honored to be on that ticket."
Donald H. Rumsfeld, national chairman of the Dole campaign, contends that it is perfectly natural for Mr. Dole and Mr. Clinton to play down Congress: "They're both running for President of the United States. That is an executive job, not a legislative job."

HEADLINE: Durable Orders Drop 3.1%, but What Does It All Mean?

BYLINE: By ROBERT D. HERSHEY Jr. 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
Two days after the Federal Reserve decided that the economy was cooling down enough that no interest-rate increase was necessary, fresh figures today showed that the Fed was right, some economists said.
Then again, other analysts said the latest numbers may be saying almost nothing about the economy's pace or the prospects for inflation. 
 The Commerce Department's report that orders for durable goods fell a surprisingly high 3.1 percent in August "indicates clearly that economic growth is slowing significantly this quarter and will remain slow over the next few quarters," Elliott Platt and John Bailey, economists at Donaldson, Lufkin & Jenrette, told clients.
Although this optimistic view seemed to win at least mild endorsement from the bond market, where interest rates eased somewhat, many analysts disagreed or remained neutral.
"Not at all," asserted John R. Williams, a senior analyst at the Bankers Trust Company, when asked whether the drop in orders, the biggest since April, showed that the Fed was wise to sit tight. "The durable goods number is extremely volatile," he added, suggesting that last month's decline be regarded as a routine statistical reverse from July's 1.4 percent advance.
At the same time, analysts largely agreed on disregarding a rise in first-time claims for unemployment benefits, to 340,000 from 329,000, for the week ended Sept. 21. While this increase also seemed to support the Fed's stance, the figures were undoubtedly skewed by Hurricane Fran. They have also shown a pronounced tendency in recent years to rise at this time of year in spite of the Labor Department's efforts to adjust them for recurring seasonal factors.
Another tidbit published today, the Conference Board's monthly tally of help-wanted advertising, also showed no trend. The index edged down one point in August, to 82, remaining in the 80-90 range where it has hovered for two years.
The report on durable goods, which is closely monitored despite wide swings and the fact that it covers less than 20 percent of the economy, this time inspired markedly different reactions even within the same institution.
At the First Union Corporation, Joel L. Naroff, chief bank economist, said from his Philadelphia office that the durables report "provides some support" for the view that the economy will slow enough to avoid monetary tightening by the Federal Reserve. But at the company headquarters in Charlotte, N.C., David Orr, the chief capital markets economist, called durables "such a flaky monthly number" that "probably not much" should be made of it.
Despite the broad nature of the decline, Robert G. Dederick, economic consultant to the Northern Trust Company, was similarly reluctant to use today's figures to judge the Fed. "I guess they show that the committee didn't necessarily do the wrong thing," he said with a note of skepticism. He said his own adjustments to the figures to filter out the most erratic items showed August's orders to be little changed from June levels.
But another Chicago economist, John E. Silvia of Zurich Kemper Investments, enthusiastically supported Tuesday's decision. "The Fed did a fine job, a wise thing," he declared, pointing to what he said was unassailable evidence that since spring orders for durables "are either flat or have clearly slowed down."
Declines last month included 7.9 percent for transportation equipment, the third consecutive drop; 5.6 percent for electrical machinery, the first decline since May; 2.7 percent for primary metals, and seven-tenths of 1 percent for industrial machinery other than electrical machinery.
Excluding military items, which rebounded 9 percent after a July drop of 40.9 percent, durables orders fell 3.5 percent, the report showed.
Orders for capital goods, excluding both military hardware and civilian aircraft, were down 5 percent in August after advancing 3.9 percent in July and 2.6 percent in June.
Shipments of durable goods, the Commerce Department report also said, rose three-tenths of 1 percent last month while unfilled orders declined by that amount.
"Let's see what the September report brings," suggested Ed Yardeni and Debbie Johnson, economists at Deutsche Morgan Grenfell/ C. J. Lawrence.

HEADLINE: Egg Man Hangs On Amid Glitter;
TriBeCa Dairy Wholesaler Is Link With Pushcart Past

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS MARTIN 

 BODY:
Well before a city planner in the 1970's christened the area TriBeCa -- Triangle Below Canal -- the blocks just north of Chambers Street and east of Greenwich Street were the eggs, butter and cheese section of New York. Serious businessmen, usually chomping on immense cigars, stalked about in fedoras making deals involving thousands of dozens of eggs. The hundreds of belching delivery trucks and squirming pushcarts meant it often took more than an hour to drive one block.
The New York Mercantile Exchange, now in the World Trade Center, may be best known for helping set world oil prices, but it was the Butter, Cheese and Egg Exchange of New York until 1882, and was on Harrison Street, in the wholesale produce area known until recently as Washington Market. 
 The continuing swagger of the egg district was apparent in the 1930's, when Mae West purred that she was dating "a big butter-and-egg man from the city." The old-timers still remember the grand figure cut by J. D. Salinger's father, Sol, as he prowled the pandemonium with his cane and spats, and they laugh about the hard-boiled butter-and-egg tycoon who habitually pocketed dime tips left on neighborhood lunch counters.
"The whole world was here, and now it's all gone except for Steve," said Oliver Allen, a writer who moved to a TriBeCa loft in 1982. He meant Steven Wils, the last butter-and-egg man in TriBeCa. Mr. Wils owns the family wholesaling business, Harry Wils & Company, at 182 Duane Street.
And though Mr. Wils, who is 47, has 16 trucks chugging in and out of Duane Street, it is a vast mistake to say he does things in the way of his grandfather and father. They went door-to-door peddling the eggs they had personally gathered from upstate farmers in a Model T, sometimes sleeping in barns overnight. Then they began getting eggs in barrels, packed between layers of oats from the Midwest, and ultimately in boxcar loads barged across the Hudson from Jersey City.
Even that is quaint by today's standards. Today's egg farms are immense, with five million birds laying onto conveyor belts in one Ohio henhouse. First the grocery chains monopolized the business, and now the giant farms are eliminating even those middlemen. But Mr. Wils hangs on in the old family digs, admittedly augmented by expanding real estate holdings, now totaling five industrial buildings in a property market so fashionable it is called Hollywood East.
And he sells more eggs than his family predecessors would have imagined, more than 8,000 dozen a week, not to mention three boxcars full of butter. He survives as a middleman in a dying business by reversing the strategy of his father, whose trick was to corner supplies. Instead, he doggedly pursues the city's most expensive restaurants with products like truffles and extra-virgin olive oil to send along with the dairy staples. "Once we were supply driven, and now we're customer driven," he explained.
The old egg men began to recede after their produce counterparts on the other side of Greenwich Street were moved to Hunts Point in the Bronx in 1967. Their extinction was sealed by the rise of huge distribution networks and other major changes in how their business was conducted. Finally, the soaring price of lofts for residential use eased the wholesalers' way to New Jersey or Florida.
But on a deeper level, it is possible to suggest that they simply and inexorably grew irrelevant in a post-industrial city suddenly desperate for clean fingernails.
"There was a kind of congestion and liveliness that's very European," remembered Gerd Stern, who moved his family's butter, cheese and egg company, the Otto Stern Company, to Cresskill, N.J., a decade ago. "As countries grow more civilized, they tend to hide that kind of activity. And you lose something. You lose a lovely little piece of the world."
Mr. Wils clings to the memories, as evidenced by the old photographs and century-old price sheets he loves to collect. He tells how his grandfather Samuel founded the company in 1921 after escaping Eastern European pogroms. Eggs were an immigrant business with small start-up costs: you could pick up 30 dozen for $10 and sometimes almost double your money.
Steven Wils began helping in the business at age 15, but was not taken with the long hours that forced his father to leave the family's home in East New York, Brooklyn, from 5 A.M. to 7 P.M. But while Steven was a sophomore at the State University at New Paltz, his father fell ill and he returned home to work for five months.
He liked it, but not well enough to abandon his other dreams. He returned to college, where he switched his major from psychology to art and finally to philosophy. Upon graduation, he headed for San Francisco and became a water bed salesman.
But he began to long for New York, where people appreciated his cutting sense of humor. "You'd be sarcastic and no one would get it," he said of Californians.
After a six-month stay in Israel, where he toyed with the idea of dropping out and moving to India, he decided to return to work with his father, Harry, an irrepressible raconteur. The step was regarded suspiciously by the district's old-timers.
"People didn't want their sons to go into the business," he said. "It was dirty. It was long hours. You were dealing with a food commodity."
The young Mr. Wils made one demand. He wanted his father to buy a building on Duane Street for the business and for his own home -- this in 1974, when almost nobody lived south of Canal. Though he kept it to himself, part of his motivation was to be near "a great bar" down the street, the Barnabus Rex, which is defunct.
"My father thought I was absolutely crazy," Steven Wils said. "Dad said, 'Why should I buy a building when I can rent for almost nothing?' "
But he bought the building when buildings there were going for $20,000. Now, single floors in such edifices can command $1 million. He went on to buy two more buildings and sign long-term leases for two others.
Mr. Wils's adventure in downtown living began with the installation of a toilet in his 2,500-square-foot loft. He enjoyed the artists' moving in around him, one of whom designed his olive oil can. In 1983, he married Madelyn Goldberg, a television producer, and they now have three sons living in the original loft, which has since been joined to another.
"When I met him, he was just a butter, egg and cheese man," Ms. Wils said with a laugh. "Now, he's become special ingredients."
The special ingredients approach often means searching out novel products for restaurateurs, like dried strawberries or exotic mushrooms. It means holding an annual event in his warehouse -- miraculously transformed with glittering chandeliers to bring together chefs and suppliers from as far afield as France. Two local pastry chefs won $1,000 prizes for their sugary creations.
"Steve helps to start food trends," said Eberhard Muller, executive chef of Lutece.
David Bouley, the chef, said, "He has helped us develop different qualities of milk and butter." Mr. Bouley, who made the TriBeCa restaurant named after him one of the city's best and is now moving around the corner to 36 Hudson Street, added, "He is bringing his business to a higher level."
But Mr. Wils's most fundamental strategy is ample doses of the sort of old-fashioned hustle his father taught him as a boy -- scurrying up to the Tavern on the Green at a moment's notice with a case of butter is de rigueur. Such mobility is clearly the biggest advantage of his Manhattan location: almost all his prime customers are 15 minutes or less away. He will sometimes personally make deliveries by subway.
But despite such epicurean eagerness, the last man's days in the old neighborhood may be numbered. He says he needs more and better-arranged space than even five old buildings can provide and has been scouting sites in the Bronx and New Jersey.
He said he had made four calls to the city's Economic Development Corporation asking for help finding bigger digs, and perhaps subsidies, without a reply.
Russell A. Harding, a spokesman for the development agency, said yesterday that it had no record of Mr. Wils's calls but that it had a number of programs that might help him. "We look forward to discussing his options with him," Mr. Harding said.
City Councilwoman Kathryn E. Freed, who is fighting to help keep her constituent and his 60 jobs in the city, if not in the neighborhood, said, "He's kind of like the dinosaur that's still hanging in there."
And, hey, he cooks a terrific egg -- sunny side up, of course.
