HEADLINE: Discontented Workfare Laborers Murmur 'Union'

BYLINE: By JOE SEXTON 

 BODY:
Angry about their working conditions, some welfare recipients required to work for their benefits say that with the work should come the many protections available to other workers. While not objecting to work itself, these laborers complain that they are occupying a new basement tier in the labor force, one that lacks regulations on hours, wages and safety that other workers take for granted.
There are 30,000 workfare laborers in New York City, and officials say the new Federal welfare law could push the total to more than 100,000. With numbers like that, even murmurs of complaint ring loud to the city's municipal union leaders. 
 Those leaders, worried about the future of jobs for their own members, have questioned the program and begun to speak out about the recipients and the basic rights of workers. Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, eager not to jeopardize the program he has called a great achievement of his administration, has agreed to meet today with Stanley Hill, the head of the largest union of city employees, to broker a resolution.
"At the start, the unions willingly stereotyped us as a bunch of transients, and only now have they opened their eyes to what is taking place," said John Gantt, who will report for his latest workfare assignment next month. "Some of us can't believe they have come around. Others believe it's about damn time."
He is among a small band of workers who are not waiting for help from the unions and are trying to organize on their own. They are distributing leaflets and badges, preaching solidarity and planning ways to make the city take notice.
This nascent effort at unionizing is merely one more reflection of the considerable discontent that prevails among the thousands of men and women who have swept streets, transported corpses at city hospitals or performed other chores in the city's Work Experience Program, or WEP. Participants work at least 26 hours each week in return for benefits that can be as spare as $68.50 in cash and $60 in food stamps every two weeks.
Any organizing efforts face steep legal barriers. Under Federal law, welfare beneficiaries in workfare programs are not entitled to bargain as a group with an employer. They do not come under the jurisdiction of the National Labor Relations Board, which administers union elections.
But their complaints echo grievances that have served for decades as rallying cries for unions: low wages, conditions that include no gloves for street cleaners, no safety training and scant chance for advancement into permanent jobs. They say that as the city grows increasingly reliant on their labor, their sheer numbers will give them some leverage.
"We have to come together or else we'll sound like a bunch of complainers," said Barbara Stewart, a welfare recipient who said she worked for her benefits as a clerk at a welfare office in downtown Brooklyn. "There are more than 30,000 of us. There has to be some power in that."
Exactly how much power remains a big question. Leaders of the group organizing the workfare recipients, WEP Workers Together, say they have a couple hundred members. Willie James, the head of the Transport Workers Union in the city, agreed last week to allow thousands of workfare participants to clean subways and buses alongside union workers, but also raised the possibility that his union could later capture their numbers if they got real jobs.
Mr. Hill said that at his meeting with the Mayor, he was willing to "go to bat" for the welfare recipients. He said that the workfare program in the city was not being run properly, that the guidelines about union jobs not being filled by welfare workers had been violated in some cases, and that the program was not leading to meaningful numbers of people entering the real workplace.
"I'd love to organize as many of them as we could, but we don't have the legal leverage," Mr. Hill said of the recipients. "The program needs to be fixed. It needs to be about jobs, real jobs. The fear about worker and job displacement is real, and it's a national fear."
Other union leaders, perceiving the recipients as either a threat or potential allies, have begun to speak out about the conditions. Victor Gotbaum, Mr. Hill's predecessor as executive director of District Council 37 of the American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees, the umbrella union of nonuniformed city workers, said the unions had reason to be nervous.
"We tried to unionize the welfare recipients a decade ago," Mr. Gotbaum said. "They were ruled not to be workers. We wanted to give them protection, and I don't think we even spoke of dues. They are an exploited, underpaid group. But our problem wasn't a large one then. Candidly, it now appears overwhelming."
Workfare participants have no unified or singular voice. The city says that a large portion of the 100,000 people who have moved through the program have jobs, but officials have not conducted long-term studies. There are many who are grateful for the chance to be doing anything other than nothing.
But interviews with dozens of workfare participants in recent weeks and days make clear that the dominant sentiments on the city's strange labor landscape are anger, frustration, and, particularly with the recent awakening of the city's unions, a kind of cynical amusement.
Many believe workfare is a thinly veiled effort to create a vast pool of cheap labor; others describe the program as vindictive, inflexible and aimed chiefly at removing people from the welfare rolls; others offer accounts of more modest slights and disillusionments, from the thoughtless placement of people in jobs for which they are not best suited to the sign above a restroom in a Sanitation Department building that reads: No W.E.P. Workers Allowed.
Some recipients working at Bellevue Hospital, transporting patients or changing beds, say there are so many workfare workers now at the hospital that they are "falling over each other," with virtually none obtaining full-time jobs. "Why should they actually hire anyone?" asked Pat Simmons, a 49-year-old woman who said she had worked 26 hours a week at Bellevue for nearly two years without gaining a real job. "We all but work for free."
Luis Fernandez, a former automobile mechanic who has spent recent months cleaning streets in Brooklyn, said there were no uniforms for him to wear, often no boots or gloves. Mostly, he said, he is given orders. "We are the garbage," he said.
While city officials have insisted that welfare workers have not taken up jobs currently or formerly performed by union workers, participants in the program said that distinction has become severely blurred. There are recipients working in clerical jobs in welfare offices, and in any number of other agencies. One even worked as an aide to a senior official at the Human Resources Administration, the city agency that carries out the workfare program.
"Those union workers should be worried about their jobs," said Kevin Anderson, a 22-year-old welfare recipient picking up trash in Marcus Garvey Park in Harlem one morning this week. "Shoot, we have been doing their jobs for months. I don't even see the city workers around here much. Except when they come to watch us do their jobs."
The recent display of both interest and alarm by the city's labor unions has provoked ambivalence among the workfare participants. "They have a right to be mad," Miguel Hernandez, a welfare worker sweeping up in Van Cortlandt Park in the Bronx, said of the city's unionized workers. "Taking a broom, then taking your job -- we're not comfortable with that." But Daisy Padua, a 50-year-old welfare worker in the Bronx, said of the city program, "I don't mind if the union loses members as long as they come through with a job."
The effort to organize welfare workers was the brainchild of public assistance recipients who had brought their experiences and gripes to three separate nonprofit welfare advocacy organizations -- Community Voices Heard, the Fifth Avenue Committee in Brooklyn and the Urban Justice Center in Manhattan.
The organizations, along with representatives from several unions in the city, have provided staff and technical support, and have pledged to provide legal representation in the event of a job action. They said they were not aware of any similar attempt around the country.
The organizers have met with leaders of District Council 37, Mr. Hill's union. Roughly 40 welfare workers marched in the Labor Day parade earlier this month alongside members of Communication Workers of America Local 1180, which represents city computer technicians and clerical supervisors. A work slowdown -- recipients will show up for work, but do the bare minimum -- is under consideration for later this fall.
Wendell Ortiz, a former Parks Department employee who is one of the organizing effort's founders, said he quit his city job in protest over the program he had to help administer. "In the Parks Department adults were taking orders from teen-agers who were there working as seasonal employees," he said. "No one got jobs. People were working without any concerns about safety. There was no overtime, no vacation."
Colleen M. Roche, a spokeswoman for the Mayor, said City Hall had no comment on the organizing effort or on the specific complaints made by the recipients. Several organizers have charged that city workers and security guards at workfare sites have threatened welfare workers who were handing out flyers or recruiting members.
If nothing else, the recent maneuverings by the Mayor and the unions have brought the thousands of workfare participants front and center in the debate about welfare reform and city economics.
"The WEP workers don't want the city unions to fight the workfare program so that it is killed, because these people want to work," said Ed Ott, a political director of C.W.A. Local 1180. "But they want it to be fair. They want it to be a real job creation program. They believe their fundamental relationship with the government has changed. They are workers now. They want the full protections any worker is entitled to."

HEADLINE: All-Stars in a Garden Encore

 BODY:
Four years ago, Dave Checketts had a brainstorm. With the popularity of the National Basketball Association growing rapidly, Checketts, then the Knicks' president, thought it was time for the All-Star Game to return to Madison Square Garden.
Yesterday, New York was officially awarded the league's 48th annual showcase event, to be held Feb. 6-8, 1998. The weekend is projected to generate $25 million for the city's economy and will mark yet another coup for the Garden proprietorship, which will bring the Grammy Awards to the building next year. 
 "I think it secures your place as the No. 1 address for entertainment and sports in the world," Checketts, now the Garden's president, said yesterday on the steps of City Hall.
The old Garden, situated at 49th Street and Eighth Avenue, was host to the 1954, 1955 and 1968 games. The 1968 contest was played on Jan. 23, less than a month before the current arena opened.
Checketts took part in yesterday's announcement along with N.B.A. Commissioner David Stern, Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani, the Knicks team president Ernie Grunfeld and the broadcaster Marv Albert. The Garden beat out bids from Portland and Philadelphia.

HEADLINE: A New For-Profit School Sets Off a Turf Battle in Detroit

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

DATELINE: DETROIT, Sept. 26

 BODY:
Like a company invading another market, a cash-strapped Michigan public school district opened a for-profit school in midtown Detroit today with the explicit purpose of making money, infuriating Detroit school officials who called it "educational piracy."
The economics are simple: The school district of Romulus, Mich., plans to collect $5,300 a year in state education aid for each student taught in Detroit. Romulus has hired a small, private company to run the school for $4,240 a student and will pocket the difference. 
 The private operator will pay students who dropped out of Detroit high schools as much as $900 a year in transportation and lunch money to attend. But this morning, some of the more than 100 youths who showed up for the first day of school, said that money was not their motive for attending.
The company running the school, Baron Schools Inc., is partly owned by a local entrepreneur, Peter Bakama, who has been operating an adult education vocational school in a northern suburb of Detroit for at least two years. Dr. William M. Bedell, the Romulus superintendent, said he thought that Mr. Bakama had been working from a home office and could be reached only by beeper.
Dr. Bedell said he viewed the new school as a good way to raise money for his 4,100-student district. "I'm not a social worker," he said on Wednesday. "We were looking for ways to help our kids and it looked like it was within the law and would not hurt Detroit." He said the school would be geared to dropouts, for whom the city does not receive state education aid anyway.
Detroit school officials are nonetheless furious.
"We see this as an act of educational piracy," said Dr. Arthur M. Carter, a deputy superintendent. "We see this as a school district treating students as cash cows without any interest in their educational development."
The Detroit school system's lawyers have asked Romulus schools for information about student attendance, teacher training and other issues at the new school, and may seek a court ruling to close it or block state aid, Dr. Carter added.
This morning, youths mostly in their late teens gathered outside a small, two-story building with peeling white paint this morning, waiting for classes to begin. Several students said that they had heard they would be paid $50 every two weeks to attend. The students were recruited partly through word of mouth and partly through door-to-door visits by Baron Schools staff over the last two months. Dr. Bedell said that while the details were still being worked out, fortnightly payments of $50 were likely. But not one of the students cited the money as a reason for attending.
"I want to make something of myself," said Chaka Respess, a 19-year-old from southeastern Detroit who dropped out of 10th grade four years ago because she found school too boring. Miss Respess said that she planned now to study cosmetology.
"I already know how to do hair, so pretty soon I'll have a salon," she said.
Reese Smith, a 17-year-old from southeastern Detroit, said that she had completed 10th grade last spring at a Detroit public high school. But her mother decided over the summer to enroll her in the Romulus school here because it was smaller.
"She thought there'd be less peer pressure" to engage in "everything -- sex, drugs, everything," said Miss Smith, who also planned to study cosmetology.
Robert Howard, a 19-year-old from west Detroit, said he left school five years ago, in ninth grade, when he was jailed for what he described as "hurting people." Mr. Howard said that he would attend the car mechanics course here because he now believed that, "Education is the key to everything."
Romulus officials say the arrangement in Detroit was made possible by a new state law designed to give parents more choice in where they send their children to school. Signed in June by Gov. John Engler, a Republican, the law sets state aid to each municipal school district based on the number of enrolled students from anywhere in its county.
Detroit and Romulus, 11 miles to the southwest, are both in Wayne County, Mich. Previously, state aid was given according to the number of enrolled public-school students who are residents of the municipality.
The Romulus outpost opened on the eve of a crucial date in determining state aid. The attendance number on on Friday will determine how much Romulus will receive, subject to revision after a second head count in February.
Dr. Bedell opposed the new state education aid law before its passage, worrying that some students in Romulus, a fairly low-income township, would seek to attend better-financed schools in adjacent communities in Wayne County.
The aid is typically paid in 10 installments beginning in December. Dr. Bedell said that the Romulus schools will cover all of the Detroit school's expenses until then and then reimburse itself from the money that Baron Schools will receive.
Romulus is also lending some textbooks and curriculums to the new school, and the school's teachers are on the Romulus payroll, Dr. Bedell said. But Mr. Bakama is hiring the teachers, who are not unionized, and setting their pay rates.
Dr. Bedell's assistant tried to reach Mr. Bakama through his beeper so that he could be interviewed, but Mr. Bakama did not call back.
Teressa Staten, the chief deputy superintendent of the Michigan Department of Education, said that the state would check the quality of the education provided in the new school, known as the Romulus-Baron School of Choice, but would not do so immediately.
"The onus is on the local board of education" in Romulus, she said. "We hope that their purpose is to give these kids an education which leads to a high school diploma."
Romulus and Mr. Bakama had planned to open the school in a downtown Detroit building just a dozen blocks west of the City Hall, but ended up using another building in midtown Detroit today when fire marshals declared on Wednesday that the downtown building was unfit for immediate use as a school.
Dr. Bedell said the curriculum at the new school would include English, math and science, as required by state law, but taught as part of vocational education, an effort to give students a practical reason to stay in school, he said.
Education experts agreed the experiment appears to be the first of its kind, but they had mixed views about it. Chester E. Finn Jr., a senior fellow in the Washington offices of the conservative Hudson Institute, said the experiment could prove a very good idea if it delivered a high-quality education. "I'm all in favor of entrepreneurial school districts colonizing other school districts if they can deliver the goods educationally," he said.
But Heidi Steffens, a policy analyst on for-profit schools at the National Education Association, a 2.2-million-member union and professional organization for public school employees, said she was was appalled. "I have never before heard of a public school fronting for a private entrepreneur in order to raise money for its own schools," she said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Waiting for the classes to begin yesterday at a new for-profit school that was set up in Detroit by a neighboring school district were, from left, Reese Smith, Robert Howard and Kieran Perdue. (Peter Yates for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: POLITICS: ON THE AIR;
PBS Breaks Further Ground With Debate of Congressional Leaders

BYLINE: By JAMES BENNET 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
The Public Broadcasting Service believes it has glimpsed the future of political journalism, and it is, of course, earnest.
In a year of extraordinary experimentation in political coverage, the TV-and-crumpets crowd at PBS has led the broadcast television field. While commercial networks have gnashed their teeth over how much free time they should dribble out to Presidential candidates, PBS is merrily ladling it out to politicians -- and to the electorate. 
 On Sunday night, public broadcasting will present its latest experiment, a nationally televised hourlong debate among the Congressional leadership. Before and after that debate, 73 PBS stations around the country will broadcast debates or other forums involving local House and Senate candidates.
The debate comes as polls suggest that Republican control of Congress is shaky. "This year you could argue the election of the Congress is as important as the election of the White House, in setting the future direction of the country," said Ellen Hume, executive director of PBS's Democracy Project, which helped organize the debates.
Ms. Hume said that ever since the nasty 1988 Presidential election campaign, academics, journalists and politicians had been wondering "why this is such a negative experience for everybody -- the candidates, the journalists and the citizens."
In search of a remedy, she said, the Democracy Project "is supposed to be a lab for trying new kinds of news and public affairs programming that works hard to engage citizens." It is experimenting with programming like debates, free time for candidates, and specials about issues and citizens' lives and concerns.
It may sound as American as apple pie, but, for precisely that reason, this approach, like other forms of so-called "public journalism," has its skeptics.
"The myth of America is being dusted off again," said Bill Kovach, the curator of the Nieman Foundation for Journalism at Harvard. "And a lot of it's good; I don't want to denigrate it. But an awful lot of it's pseudo-journalism. If there's kind of a celebratory tinge to your journalism, it's easy to overlook things that don't fit into your mold."
For example, he said, following the public's lead in determining what is important may cause journalists to play down issues that have not yet had an obvious effect, like recent changes in welfare.
The national Congressional debate on Sunday night is to take place in the House of Burgesses in Colonial Williamsburg. Speaker Newt Gingrich and the Senate majority leader, Trent Lott, will square off against their Democratic counterparts -- Representative Richard A. Gephardt and Senator Tom Daschle, the minority leaders.
Jim Lehrer, of "The Newshour With Jim Lehrer," will moderate -- but only moderately. After two opening questions from him, the four politicians will debate resolutions on the economy, foreign policy and other matters, said Dan Werner, the president of MacNeil/Lehrer Productions and executive producer of the debate.
Mr. Werner said Congressional races now had a "national tinge," because the Republicans waged a national campaign in 1994 to gain control of both houses. But, through its member stations, PBS is also giving viewers a chance to see their local candidates debate. At present, candidates in 71 House races and 18 Senate races are scheduled to participate, although the roster keeps shifting as candidates get cold feet.
PBS officials and Congressional historians said they believed that a similar national debate among Congressional leaders took place in 1972, also on public television, but they could not find a record of it today.
Several commercial cable and broadcast networks have also been experimenting this year to try to allay viewers' cynicism and apathy about politics. On the TCI cable network, the program "Race for the Presidency" has been providing free time since last fall for candidates to state their views. Last week the Fox television network inaugurated a similar feature, giving each Presidential candidate at least one minute a week. And in a series called "Democracy in America '96," CNN has been stepping back from covering the political horse race to examine, in hourlong prime-time specials, what it calls "the deep-rooted fears of ordinary Americans."
Some students of television believe that the networks are starting to respond to criticism of political coverage.
"Television coverage of news generally, and probably politics particularly, has taken quite a beating for being pretty thin stuff, and pretty sensationalized," said Roderick P. Hart, a professor of communications and government at the University of Texas.
But even now, the organizations that are acting most decisively to address that charge, he noted, are "PBS and CNN, not the high-dollar operations."
The jury remains out on whether viewers are responding to this experimentation. For example, PBS has been broadcasting a series called "Citizens '96," examining issues like "family values" through the lives of actual American families. But the ratings have not been great, Ms. Hume said. "We've had zero promotion budget," she said, "so we cannot say we have had a huge audience for any of these."
But, she added, viewers who have discovered such PBS programs are happy with them: "We get E-mail from people saying, 'Thank goodness someone is finally listening to us.' "

HEADLINE: U.S. CENSUS FINDS FIRST INCOME RISE IN PAST SIX YEARS

BYLINE: By STEVEN A. HOLMES 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 26

 BODY:
Household income rose in 1995 for the first time in six years, the Census Bureau reported today, as the number of poor people in the United States dropped by more than 1.6 million, reaching historic lows for blacks and the elderly.
The bureau said half the households in the country had incomes of at least $34,074, an increase of 2.7 percent over the previous year. The inflation-adjusted increase of $898 was the first rise in median household income since 1989, the year before the nation's last recession, and it was the largest increase in household earnings since 1986. 
 At the same time, the percentage of the nation's population that is poor dropped to 13.8, from 14.5 in 1994. It was the second consecutive year of a decline in the poverty rate.
The report said that 29.3 percent of blacks in the United States lived below the poverty line in 1995. For the first time since 1959, when the Census Bureau started keeping such statistics -- and probably for the first time in the nation's history -- the poverty rate for African-Americans dipped below 30 percent.
Also for the first time, the poverty rate for the nation's elderly last year dropped below that of working-age people, making those 65 years of age and older the group with the lowest percentage of poor people.
The Clinton Administration seized on the relatively rosy news contained in the report to praise its economic record. "Today it is clear that more and more of our people are sharing in that prosperity," President Clinton said. "We are growing -- and growing together."
Much of the rise in household incomes was in the Midwest, a crucial region in the Presidential campaign.
But Bob Dole, Mr. Clinton's Republican challenger, seized on some less encouraging news in the report: its finding that while household income has increased, earnings for both male and female full-time workers declined. This indicated that more people are working in a household or that wage earners are working at more than one job.
Mr. Dole said the bureau figures did not reflect the economic anxiety of Americans. He maintained that the rise in household incomes stemmed primarily from more people working per household, rather than an increase in individual wages for full-time workers.
John D. Mueller, an economist and former aide to Jack Kemp, the Republican Vice-Presidential nominee, said: "President Clinton obviously is going to make the most he can with these figures in comparison with other administrations. But if you compare the number of people who have gotten employment and the number of jobs created, there are four new jobs for every three additional workers employed."
And Nelson Warfield, the Dole campaign spokesman, said, "These cold economic statistics may please Bill Clinton, but they do nothing to comfort a homemaker trying to buy groceries or a wage earner worried about the next paycheck."
The improvements in household income and poverty rates were concentrated in the Midwest, a region that underwent a wrenching economic downturn in the early 1980's as such industries as steel and automobile manufacturing were downsized.
Median household income increased by 7.2 percent in the Midwest, but it rose only slightly in the Northeast, the South and the West.
In Connecticut, median household income fell to $40,243 last year, from $42,262 in 1994. But household income rose in New York, to $33,028 in 1995 from $32,803 in 1994, and in New Jersey, to $43,924 last year from $43,478 in 1994.
"The industrial Midwest has been particularly helpful in bringing up household incomes," said Labor Secretary Robert B. Reich. "Unemployment there has been so low that even in the low-wage service sector many people are getting jobs they otherwise would not."
In a reversal of what has been a long-term trend toward greater income inequality, the Census Bureau report said the poor, the working class and the middle class increased their share of the nation's aggregate income, while the percent of total household income earned by the top 20 percent declined slightly, to 48.7 percent from 49.1 percent.
Overall, the three separate reports issued today by the Census Bureau -- one on incomes, another on poverty and a third on health insurance coverage -- painted a rosy picture of the nation's economic well-being.
Incomes increased across the board, and rose sharply for some groups, including households headed by blacks and women, where they have historically lagged.
But the report also noted a steep decline of 5.1 percent in income for Hispanic households. The drop in Hispanic earnings contrasted sharply with the 2.2 percent rise in incomes for non-Hispanic whites and the 3.6 percent increase in earnings for African-American households.
Indeed, the Census Bureau noted that 1995 marked the first time the Hispanic poverty rate was greater than that for blacks.
And with continuing immigration and the loss of benefits to legal immigrants who are not yet citizens, some economists expect the Hispanic poverty rate to worsen.
"We just enacted a welfare bill that has very deep cuts in all basic forms of assistance to legal immigrants, not an insignificant number of whom are Hispanics," said Robert Greenstein, executive director of the Center on Budget and Policy Priorities, a liberal research group. "I would think that in the years ahead we will reach all-time highs in Hispanic poverty."
Perhaps the most eye-catching aspects of the reports, however, are the economic journeys they trace for blacks and the elderly.
In 1959, according to the Census Bureau, 35.2 percent of the elderly were poor, the highest percentage of any age group in the country, and about double the rate of working-age people, 18 to 64.
Primarily as the result of Social Security and increases in private pensions, the poverty rate among the elderly was 10.5 percent last year, for the first time dropping below that of working-age people.
While the gains were not as dramatic as those for the elderly, blacks have also shown a steady decrease in their poverty rate, to 29.3 percent last year from 55.1 percent in 1959.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Dallas Publisher to Buy Providence Journal Co.
The A. H. Belo Corporation, publisher of The Dallas Morning News, agreed to buy the Providence Journal Company of Providence, R.I., for $1.5 billion in cash and stock.
The acquisition will add the Providence company's nine owned and four operated television stations to Belo's seven television outlets, creating the 10th-largest television group in the country, measured by household reach. [Page D2.]
 
Cash Surges Into Mutual Funds
Cash flows into mutual funds that invest in stocks, having bounced back from a rocky July with net in-flows of $17.9 billion in August, continued to surge in September. More impressively, flows into a more speculative arena -- junk bond funds -- were a record $1.68 billion in August. Market Place. [D8.]
 
Durable Goods Orders Off 3.1%
Orders for durable goods fell 3.1 percent in August, and first-time claims for jobless benefits rose to 340,000. The fresh economic data indicate that the economy is cooling, so the Fed was right not to raise interest rates, some economists said. [D4.]
 
Economic Data Fail to Lift Stocks
After stocks flirted with records on the economic news, the Dow industrials fell 8.51 points, to 5,868.85. The Nasdaq composite index rose 3.32 points, to 1,227.98. [D8.]

Bond prices advanced for a fourth consecutive session, and the long bond's yield fell 4 basis points to 6.88 percent. [D7.]
 
Household Income Rose Last Year
Household income rose in 1995 for the first time in six years, the Census Bureau reported, gaining 2.7 percent to a median $34,074, as the number of poor people in the United States dropped by more than 1.6 million. [A1.]
 
Valujet Gets Approval to Fly
The Transportation Department gave Valujet permission to resume flying. It was grounded three months ago for numerous safety violations that came to light after its fatal May 11 crash in the Everglades. [A24.]
 
Fund Managers in Merger Talks
Invesco of Britain and the AIM Management Group of Houston said they were in merger talks that could create an international fund manager with $144 billion in assets. [D8.]
 
I.M.F. Head Seeks $100 Billion
The I.M.F.'s reappointed managing director, Michel Camdessus, asked for more than $100 billion to safeguard a volatile international financial system. Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin said that the Administration favored strengthening the fund to deal with crises, but American monetary officials called Mr. Camdessus's demands too ambitious. [D5.]
 
Silicon Graphics Shares Slide
Silicon Graphics' shares tumbled following the company's report late Wednesday that its fiscal first-quarter results would fall short of analysts' expectations. But the shares moved up $1 from the day's low to close at $22.125, down $1.375. The company faces two problems: it must recall 3,900 machines because of defective chips, and customers are waiting for new models. [D7.]
 
Fashionable Drivers Wanted
To promote its 1997 model Z3, BMW of North America is linking with media and retail partners for a $2 million effort that focuses on fashion. Stuart Elliott: Advertising. [D2.]
 
Computer Hacker Indicted
Kevin Mitnick, a 33-year-old computer hacker, was charged in Los Angeles with 25 felony counts for attacking systems and Internet service providers. Federal officials called it the most comprehensive computer crime indictment ever. [A24.]

LENGTH: 1000 words

HEADLINE: POLITICS: Campaign Trail;
THE PRESIDENTIAL RACE

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINES 

 BODY:

A Bridge Too Far
Granted, Bob Dole started it. At the Republican convention, he asked voters to let him be "a bridge to a time of tranquillity, faith and confidence in action." But then the White House joined in. And now this bridge business is becoming the "Where's the beef?" of the 1996 Presidential season: catchy, easy to understand and overworked. 
 President Clinton has seized on the bridge metaphor like a snapping turtle on a broomstick. His bridges always run "to the 21st century." They are "big enough, strong enough, wide enough for everybody to walk across," and "everyone has a right to walk on the bridge." They focus a lot, on "making sure that each and every American has the right to make the best of his or her life." They have a foundation, "the world's finest education system."
They go just about everywhere: to classrooms hooked up to the Internet, to college for everyone, to stronger families, to "strong economy, good schools, safe streets, a clean environment, healthy children, successful families and communities." And so on.
By the count of the Nexis database, which collects most Presidential remarks, Mr. Clinton has wielded the word "bridge" 243 times in speeches this month. That is an average of 9.346 bridges per appearance.
The President's opponents have responded in characteristic but muted fashion. "These are all toll bridges," Mr. Dole likes to say.
Ross Perot is, well, classic Perot. "We start with a noble cause, or an artist's sketch of a beautiful idea, like the bridge, and then we enact massive legislation without a blueprint or checking to see if there is rock beneath the soil for a solid foundation," he said in one of his television commercials. "There's no detailed planning, no cost estimates -- just laws and giant cost overruns."
Maybe politics will declare a moratorium on bridge metaphors. If anyone seriously expects that to occur, there's a bridge in Brooklyn that he ought to take a look at.
 
Cabinet-in-Waiting?
Everyone knows who Bill Clinton chose to run the Government as President. So why shouldn't they get a sneak peek at Bob Dole's Presidential organizational chart?
Maybe they will. Officials in Mr. Dole's campaign are considering having their candidate disclose before the election whom he would like to name to the Cabinet should he become President. That would not be unprecedented in national politics; as a candidate for the Republican nomination in 1976, Ronald Reagan announced his choice for Vice President, Senator Richard Schweiker of Pennsylvania.
But never has a candidate formally revealed his Cabinet in advance. A Washington lawyer with two decades of Democratic campaign experience, Robert Barnett, said he had run across the concept before and doubted its merits. "It has a surface attraction -- 'Oh, boy; if you vote for X candidate, you also get Y Cabinet member,' " he said. "But most people aren't going to change their vote because of Y Cabinet member. It can look awfully presumptuous."
But the upside, at least in the eyes of Dole supporters, is fairly obvious: a Dole Cabinet-in-waiting would presumably demonstrate a moderate brand of conservatism, reassuring voters who fear the far right. It would raise the profile of a campaign that, so far, has not grabbed the electorate by its lapels.
It also would offer a comparison to Mr. Clinton's Cabinet, whose occupants have occasioned more than the usual allotment of ethical embarrassments and criminal inquiries in the last four years.
Scott Reed, Mr. Dole's campaign manager, declined to discuss the prospects for an early announcement of Cabinet picks. But he did suggest that the people Mr. Dole would attract would easily outshine what he called "the Clinton Cabinet Hall of Shame."
There is one other powerful incentive. Naming a Cabinet now would no doubt permit Mr. Dole to whisper, over and over, those three little words that America supposedly longs to hear: General Colin Powell, General Colin Powell, General Colin Powell. . . .
 
All Dressed Up, but . . .
Hundreds of journalists who cover Presidential politics soon will get a package in the mail stuffed with freebies: a tote bag, T-shirt, buttons, a lapel pin and a sheaf of handouts, all promoting St. Louis and its Washington University as the site of the campaign's first Presidential debate.
One small problem: St. Louis is not a debate site any more. The Clinton and Dole camps agreed to drop Washington University, the first of three proposed debate locales, during negotiations earlier this month. The last-minute cancellation leaves the city and the institution in the position of a suitor who has rented a tuxedo, waxed the Chevy and bought a spray of roses, only to find that his date is going to a rival school's prom.
St. Louis had put up a $500,000 donation from Anheuser-Busch Companies and $250,000 from a local business group to stage the debate and attendant affairs, including free food and entertainment for the traveling press. And Washington University was in mid-primp when the debate fell through, soundproofing its 4,000-seat arena, laying power cables and pumping up the air-conditioning to keep the debate atmosphere a constant 68 degrees.
Busch officials are discussing the fate of their $500,000 gift with officials of the Commission on Presidential Debates, but are assured of getting their money back if they want it.
And the university, which staged one of the 1992 debates on short notice, is taking matters in stride. "One thing we want everyone to understand is that this is politics," Fred Volkmann, the vice chancellor for public affairs, said this week. "What we're really out is a lot of time and a lot of volunteer work."
 
Sound Bite
"What we've had is sort of a marriage here between some on the far -- not on the far, but some of the supply side area and some of the deficit hawks." -- Bob Dole, describing the genesis of his economic package to supporters last week. MICHAEL WINES

HEADLINE: NEW JERSEY DAILY BRIEFING;
Lag in Health Coverage

BYLINE: By TERRY PRISTIN 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
New Jerseyans were better off financially than most other Americans last year, but a comparatively high percentage were not protecting themselves from the potential costs of catastrophic illness, the Census Bureau reported yesterday.
The surveys showed that New Jersey's median income last year was $43,924, up slightly from 1994 and second only to Alaska's. New Jersey had a poverty rate of 7.8 percent last year, the nation's third-lowest after Alaska and New Hampshire. The state's poverty rate has fallen from 9.2 percent in 1994 and 10.9 percent in 1993. 
 The Census Bureau also reported that 14.2 percent of the state's residents were not covered by health insurance, up from 13 percent in 1994. Twenty-six states ranked higher than New Jersey in percentage of residents with health coverage. Related article, Page A1.

HEADLINE: FUTURES MARKETS;
Copper Prices Continue Rally As Industry Uses Up Inventory

BYLINE: By Bloomberg Business News 

 BODY:
Copper prices surged yesterday as the drop in London Metal Exchange copper stockpiles accelerated, showing consumption by manufacturers was climbing.
On other markets, corn and soybeans fell, while gasoline and oil prices rose. 
 Copper inventories fell by 10,252 tons, to 272,630 tons, between Tuesday and Thursday, the London Metal Exchange said in the second of its two weekly reports. Warehouses in Europe, Asia and the United States posted declines. It was the seventh consecutive drop in stockpiles and the biggest decline since June.
For the week, stockpiles fell 19,979 tons, or 7.3 percent, accelerating a decline that began earlier this month as a seasonal lull in manufacturing drew to a close.
"The summer doldrums, when we saw stockpiles rising, are over," said Nick Fabiano, a trader with Asarco Inc. in New York. "Business is good. Copper scrap and wire mills are very busy."
Copper for December delivery rose 0.8 cent, to 91.25 cents a pound, on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, the highest since Sept. 4 and the fourth straight gain.
The stockpile drop comes amid signs of robust economic growth in the United States, which could increase demand for copper.
"We've seen good demand for copper in the U.S.," said Jim Wurtsmith, a vice president of Omnisource, a copper scrap recycling firm in Fort Wayne, Ind. "That's partly why we are seeing stockpiles decline."
Corn fell to a seven-month low, and soybeans also dropped, as forecasts for mild weather in the Midwest eased fear that frost would reduce the autumn harvest.
December corn fell 2.75 cents, to $2.0325 a bushel, on the Chicago Board of Trade, the lowest since Feb. 8. November soybeans slid 7.5 cents, to $7.8325 a bushel.
Gasoline rose, lifting oil prices amid expectations that American refiners might trim production because of low profit margins.
October gasoline rose 1.61 cents, to 64.60 cents a gallon, on the New York Mercantile Exchange. The rally pulled other oil prices higher. November crude rose 44 cents, to $24.60 a barrel, and October heating oil rose 0.59 cent, to 71.49 cents a gallon.

HEADLINE: Fed Officials Were Divided At Aug. 20 Rates Meeting

BYLINE: AP

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Divided Federal Reserve officials, while keeping interest rate policy steady this summer, agreed that they needed to be vigilant against any resurgence of inflation, according to minutes released today.
Members of the Fed's policy-making group, the Federal Open Market Committee, focused on data indicating the economy was slowing without the help of an interest rate increase and was not generating significant inflation pressures, minutes of the Aug. 20 meeting disclosed. 
 A majority concluded the "prudent course" was to await further developments that would permit them to assess the need for tighter credit with a "higher degree of confidence."
But, the minutes said, the members agreed "the committee should remain particularly vigilant to incoming information bearing on the outlook for inflation."
But some members expressed greater fears of imminent inflation threats, arguing "the evidence suggested that a prompt policy action was needed to contain inflation" and avoid difficult problems for policy makers later.
But in the end, only Gary Stern, president of the Federal Reserve Bank of Minneapolis, voted to raise rates. It was his second consecutive lone dissent. The other 11 members voted to leave rates unchanged.
The panel met again this week and again voted against any rate change, despite continuing division over policy.
Although eight of the 12 Fed regional bank presidents, according to a report by the Reuters news service last week, requested an increase in the discount rate, Fed officials voted Tuesday to keep it unchanged at 5 percent. The discount rate is the rate the Fed charges commercial banks for loans.
Many analysts interpreted the lack of action on interest rates as a sign committee members believed the economy was continuing to slow on its own and not threatening renewed inflation.
It was the panel's last meeting until Nov. 13, eight days after the Presidential election.
Voting members include the 7 Fed governors in Washington and 5 of the 12 regional presidents. All of the presidents, who rotate in voting, participate in deliberations at the meetings, which are held eight times a year.

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE RUNNING MATE;
Kemp Leads G.O.P. Charge in California

BYLINE: By RICHARD W. STEVENSON 

DATELINE: FILLMORE, Calif., Sept. 27

 BODY:
Jack Kemp began a busy four-day campaign swing through California today, part of what aides sought to portray as an intensive effort to put President Clinton on the defensive in a critical electoral battleground.
Vigorously disputing reports that Bob Dole's advisers see no real chance of carrying California, Republican strategists said that the campaign had spent about $1.25 million on television advertising in the state in the last two weeks and that it would continue to invest considerable money and time here for the duration of the campaign. 
 "Rumor aside, we are going to spend a lot of time in California, a lot of money in California," said Mr. Kemp, the Republican Vice-Presidential nominee. "We think this is a critical state."
Later, speaking from the back of a train that he rode into town from a few miles away, Mr. Kemp stressed his longstanding support for rewriting the tax code, but he never specifically mentioned Mr. Dole's plan to cut tax rates by 15 percent. Mr. Kemp's aides had said the tax-cut message was to be the main theme of his appearances in California.
Democratic officials said the Republicans were exaggerating both their advertising spending in the state and their chances of making inroads against President Clinton, who has maintained a lead in the double digits in most polls in California.
Bill Carrick, a senior adviser to the Clinton campaign in California, said that the Dole-Kemp ticket had spent only about $300,000 a week on television time, about half what the Republicans report and that such spending would not be enough to make much of an impact in a state as large as California. Moreover, they said, Mr. Dole has not been able to narrow the consistent 15-point gap in the polls.
"We're glad they're spending money and candidate time here rather than somewhere that they have a chance to win," Mr. Carrick said. "There's an effort by local Republicans to say it's a competitive campaign so they can continue to get national resources to support their Congressional candidates."
But Republican strategists insisted that their commitment to winning the state was real. They said Mr. Kemp would not spend four straight days campaigning here if it was not.
Republican advisers said their own surveys showed that the gap had narrowed to around 10 points. Yet even as Dole campaign aides argued that the state was still winnable, they acknowledged that their strategy was aimed in part at forcing the Democrats to continue spending their time and money defending electoral turf that Mr. Clinton has worked hard to make impregnable.
"We're not going to abandon a state that's so significant to them, where we've been able to cut the lead so dramatically," said Wayne Berman, Mr. Kemp's political director and a senior Dole strategist. "If we can win California -- and we can -- Clinton will lose. There is no scenario he can construct to win the Presidency and get re-elected without winning this state."
Mr. Dole's campaign has sent conflicting signals for the last month about its intentions toward California. The state, with 54 votes, is the single biggest electoral prize and one that Mr. Clinton has unabashedly cultivated through regular visits and special attention to its problems.
Some political analysts said the Republican commitment to the state, to the extent that it is not an effort to keep the Democrats off balance, could fade if heavy campaigning and advertising did not show Mr. Dole narrowing the gap more significantly by mid-October.
Aides said that Mr. Kemp, who will travel throughout the state and do dozens of interviews with local newspapers and television and radio stations, will be followed here next week by Elizabeth Dole. Mr. Kemp, they said, will return next month, as will Mr. Dole, who made a quick swing through here last week.
Mr. Kemp's foray will include trips to poor areas of Los Angeles and Palo Alto, to the bedrock conservative area of Orange County and to the inland cities of Fresno and Bakersfield.
In a direct strike at one of Mr. Clinton's most important bases of support, Mr. Kemp will travel on Monday to Silicon Valley, where he will make an appearance at the software company Netscape and highlight the endorsement of the Republican ticket by a group of 100 chief executives of high-tech companies. Mr. Kemp is expected to use his Silicon Valley speech to exhort corporate America to play a more active role in addressing issues like poverty and education.
Mr. Kemp, 61, who was born in California and grew up there, campaigned today in Fillmore, the hometown of his wife, Joanne, who was celebrating her 60th birthday. Before a crowd of several thousand people, Mr. Kemp was repeatedly interrupted by a group of largely Hispanic hecklers protesting the Republican ticket's support for a California ballot initiative that would ban some types of affirmative action and for the immigration bill pending in Congress.
Mr. Kemp's spokeswoman, Alixe Glenn, said the protest was an "indecent, disrespectful, despicable display" engineered by the Clinton campaign to "disrupt Joanne Kemp's birthday party."
Several protesters said they were local high school students who had been organized by a Hispanic student organization. A few protesters carried Clinton-Gore signs.
Mr. Berman said California voters should be particularly receptive to the Republican pledges of lower taxes and less regulation because they were volatile and subject to considerable economic anxiety. He said they had suffered through a deeper recession than the rest of the country and had adapted to a shrinking military industry, a decline in the real estate industry and constant upheaval in the technology business.
Mr. Clinton's campaign here is focused on taking credit for a rebound in the state's economy in the past few years. But even though the economy in California has been bouncing back, unemployment here, at 7.1 percent, remains a full two percentage points higher than for the nation as a whole.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Jack Kemp campaigned yesterday in Fillmore, Calif., the hometown of his wife, Joanne. It was her birthday, and she was greeted by the crowd. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE DEMOCRAT;
Clinton Sets His Sights as Wide as Texas

BYLINE: By TODD S. PURDUM 

DATELINE: HOUSTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Armed with shiny black cowboy boots, the obligatory Stetson and his best down-home drawl, President Clinton stormed through Texas today, campaigning for Democratic Congressional candidates and hoping to beat longhorn-size odds by carrying the state for his party for the first time since 1976.
"If the people are willing to give me a fair shot and look to the future, yes, I think we can carry Texas because it's right for the American people," Mr. Clinton said at a rally in Fort Worth, voicing a sentiment that might have got him laughed out of town two years ago. 
 Even now, virtually none of the President's senior aides share that assessment, despite recent polls showing Mr. Clinton running neck and neck here with Bob Dole, or slightly ahead. But the President, born in Arkansas, retains a sentimental attachment to his old neighboring state, dating to his salad days as the George McGovern campaign coordinator here in 1972. He insisted on scheduling a 18-hour day that took him from Longview in the east to Fort Worth in the north, then to Houston for an evening rally and fund raiser tonight.
"He just won't give it up," one senior aide said with weary affection as his boss prepared to address thousands of supporters in downtown Fort Worth under chilly clouds at midday. In fact, Mr. Clinton's 18-month tack to the center has left him doing better here than he could have expected in 1994, when many voters in the state were incensed at his unsuccessful effort to allow homosexuals to serve openly in the military and his successful one to ban 19 types of assault weapons.
Even the more pessimistic aides in the White House now hope that Mr. Clinton might help swing the balance in eight Congressional races in which Democrats are contending for open seats or running for re-election, which could help the party retake the House of Representatives.
Accordingly, the day was filled with Texas-sized talk and optimistic iconography, from Mr. Clinton's dropped g's and genial hyperbole to his visit on the tarmac at Ellington Field here with the astronaut Shannon W. Lucid, who returned to Earth on Thursday after setting a record -- 188 days -- for an American in space.
"It's a monument to the human spirit," Mr. Clinton said of Dr. Lucid's stay aboard the Russian space station Mir. "It's an amazing, amazing achievement."
For his part, Mr. Clinton basked in monumental images of his own. In Longview, the President spoke in front of a pair of miniature oil derricks and a red, white and blue banner proclaiming in Old West-style type that he was "Expanding the Frontier of the American Economy." The town's world-famous Kilgore Ranger Band from Kilgore College saluted him with the theme from "How the West Was Won," while the theme from "The Magnificent Seven" blared repeatedly from loudspeakers in Fort Worth.
When some Dole supporters appeared at the back of the crowd in Fort Worth and Mr. Clinton's partisans began jeering, the President broke in magnanimously: "No, no. Don't boo them. They're welcome here. This is America. Everybody's welcome here."
For nearly 150 years, since Texas was admitted to the union in 1845, no Democrat won the Presidency without carrying the state until Mr. Clinton did so in 1992. That year, without making any real effort in Texas, Mr. Clinton won 37 percent of the vote, compared with 41 percent for George Bush and 22 percent for Mr. Perot. No Democrat has carried the Dallas-Fort Worth area or greater Houston since Lyndon B. Johnson in 1964, and none has carried rural areas since Jimmy Carter in 1976.
The state's 32 electoral votes -- second-highest after California -- remain a valued prize, and Mr. Clinton insisted to the crowd in Fort Worth that if Texas Democrats talked to their neighbors in the 39 days remaining until the election, they would agree that "we might oughta vote for this President."
The White House political director, Douglas Sosnik, said Mr. Clinton's effort in Texas this year "is the first time in almost a generation that we're coming in here" and attempting to help local candidates and raise money to be used in campaigning in Texas. Since Mr. Carter's defeat in 1980, Mr. Sosnik said, Texas has "basically been an export state, been a cash cow for national Democrats."
Instead, Mr. Clinton expected to raise at least $1 million tonight for the Democratic National Committee at the home of Tillman Furtita, a restaurateur, with the bulk of it to be earmarked for use in the state.

HEADLINE: Reaping What the Boomers Sow;
Getting Down and Dirty in the Garden Is Big Business

BYLINE: By SALLY JOHNSON 

 BODY:
At age 45, Lea Rasabi acquired a passion for peegee hydrangeas. Like a teen-ager with a crush, she drove along the streets of her Queens neighborhood this summer, coveting the treelike shrubs that bloom luxuriantly in August. She wanted one for her yard.
A year ago, Mrs. Rasabi hardly knew a petunia from a peony. But she and her husband had just bought their first house, and for the first time, she was developing a sense of, well, roots. 
 "I'm obsessed," she admitted, glancing at the growing pile of gardening books and magazines on her family-room floor. "I want to know everything there is to know. And I want to know it now."
The baby-boom generation embraced gardening in the 80's as millions of first-time home buyers set about decorating their yards as well as houses. Now the huge generation is fueling a new, potentially bigger, expansion for the industry. Older boomers find themselves with more time and money for hobbies, as their children leave home, even as others like Mrs. Rasabi are still buying first houses and filling them with flowers, herbs and imported Italian pots.
Baby boomers' discovery of the virtues of family and home as they settled down was behind much of a near doubling of lawn-and-garden spending, from $12.0 billion in 1985 to $22.2 billion in 1995. But their penchant for following fashion in all aspects of life helped retailers by adding teak furniture and goat skin gloves to the basic inventory of plants and tools in catalogues and garden centers.
In a marketplace where discounters, catalogues and even grocery superstores are crowding independent nurseries, the clearest gains are among home-center chains like Home Depot, Lowe's and Builders Supply and Lumber, which offer less selection but low prices. Builders Supply is a unit of the Pulte Corporation.
Yet in a business where convenience and expert advice can count as much as price, local garden centers appear to be holding their own. And upscale catalogues like Smith & Hawken plan to grow alongside their customers' appreciation of the joys of fine gardening.
"Our research shows that the fastest-growing group is the group we call decorators," said Kathy Tierney, president of Smith & Hawken, which mails 10 million to 13 million catalogues a year. "That's why we've added things like lighting, furniture and practical clothing. It's the values of gardeners that we want to appeal to: quality, patience, long-term commitment."
From the 1980's on, the new generation of homeowners and parents eschewed the vegetable patches and marigolds of their parents for the more sophisticated perennial borders of England and the subtle, pleasing textures of Oriental gardens.
"They choose gardens with an esthetic appeal: perennial borders, herb gardens, edible landscaping," said Will Raap, president of Gardener's Supply Inc., the largest mail-order supplier of gardening equipment and tools. "The core movement was about gardens as a form of personal expression, gardens that were smaller than before but far more complex."
Complexity equated to market opportunity. In only five years lawn and garden retail sales grew 75 percent, to $20 billion in 1990, before recession temporarily slowed growth, according to the National Gardening Association, based in Burlington, Vt., which conducts monthly and annual household surveys.
The association's research director, Bruce Butterfield, predicts similar growth in the next few years as the baby-boom generation ages.
"Our research indicates that people show an increased interest in gardening in their 50's, and right now in this country, one baby boomer is turning 50 every 7 seconds," he said.
Another factor is people in their early 30's, bringing up the rear of the generation, who have just bought homes and started families, said Chris Little, president of the Meredith Publishing Group in Des Moines, which publishes Better Homes and Gardens and other home and gardening magazines.
"We see that younger group as being particularly interested in enhancing the value of their homes," Mr. Little said. "Then, too, you're seeing a societywide trend toward more concern about home and family."
He sees gardening themes turning up in advertisements for everything from windows and home furnishings to cars and over-the-counter medications. "Obviously, it means that lots of market researchers are picking up the trend," he said.
In 1979 David Smith and Paul Hawken began importing English gardening tools and selling them by catalogue from Mill Valley, Calif. Benefiting from the time-starved 80's, catalogues like Smith & Hawken flourished, growing at twice the rate of the retail industry as a whole.
Now, with at least two dozen catalogues dedicated to gardening, mail-order companies are testing a variety of strategies to stand out from the pack. Smith & Hawken, which was sold in 1993 to the CML Corporation, which also owns Nordic Track, was one of the first to add clothing, furniture and decorating accessories. Its other strategy has been to open 21 retail stores in the last three years, in affluent suburbs and city neighborhoods from lush Bellevue, Wash., to arty SoHo and genteel New Canaan, Conn.
"Our business is now half and half, catalogue and retail," Ms. Tierney said. "We're finding that our customers in urban areas are younger than the average. We're hiring knowledgeable staff and holding in-store events. We're looking for solutions for people who are time-impoverished."
The 13-year-old Gardener's Supply, based in Burlington, Vt., on the other hand, has decided against the retail strategy. Instead, Mr. Raap said, his solution, still in the formative stages, is to create gardening networks.
"We're looking at expanding our wholesale operation so that we can help out nurseries and garden centers at the same time we're helping ourselves," he said. "We're also looking at strategic collaborations with catalogues that don't duplicate our products -- maybe a seed or bulb catalogue or a company like White Flower Farm that sells high-quality plant material."
Another idea, he said, is to develop a network of gardening experts, who would double as distributors of Gardener's Supply products. They would be trained through a master gardening course the company has started with the University of Guelph in Ontario for use at home. It will be offered beginning in January.
Such plans pose little competitive threat to small independent nurseries and garden centers; indeed they might help them if the partnerships work. By contrast, the newest and most feared players in the industry are the megastores, from mass merchandisers like Wal-Mart Stores to national and regional home centers like Home Depot, based in Atlanta, and the Orchard Supply Hardware Corp., based in San Jose, Calif., which Sears, Roebuck recently bought to expand its hardware business into California.
"Garden retailers are being eaten alive by mass merchants," Mr. Raap said.
Surveys by the National Gardening Association show, for example, that 18 million households bought gardening supplies in home centers in 1995, compared with just 10 million in 1985. During that time, the number of households shopping at local garden centers rose only slightly to 32 million, from 30 million, while catalogue shoppers increased to 8 million in 1990, from 5 million in 1985, and then fell to 7 million in 1995.
Many people, of course, spread their purchases among catalogues, their local nursery and chain stores.
Katrina Blauvelt, a spokeswomen for Home Depot, said the company has been in the lawn-and-garden business since its founding in 1978. Now, all 465 stores in the United States and Canada have 28,000-square-foot lawn-and-garden centers that sell everything from fruit trees to fence posts; in addition, tools like rakes and shovels are sold inside the store.
"People are doing everything from planting window boxes and house plants to putting in fish ponds and garden paths," Ms. Blauvelt said. "Our customers include everybody from beginners to very experienced gardeners."
Megastores have unquestionably made life harder in the 1990's for merchants such as Ronald Keil, owner of Keil Brothers in Bayside, Queens, where Mrs. Rasabi shopped for hydrangeas on a sunny morning. She enlisted the help of Alex Lavy, a patient and knowledgeable young employee, who cheerfully answered a long list of questions.
Keil Brothers has been in Queens for 66 years, Mr. Keil said. He believes it will continue to thrive, in part because of employees like Alex.
"Sure, the big chains are directly impacting our business," Mr. Keil said. "You can buy plants and cut flowers in every supermarket now. So we have to have better selection, better quality and better service. We have to offer our customers the newest introductions in plant material, the most disease-resistant, hardiest varieties."
Beyond that, he said, the key to survival is service. "We try to answer their questions as best we can," he said, "and we always try to have a big smile on our faces."

HEADLINE: Family Income Finally Rises

 BODY:
The upbeat report by the Census Bureau this week on family incomes and poverty showed that the recovery is finally raising living standards for American families, rich and poor. Income of the typical household rose by almost 3 percent last year, the largest increase in a decade. For the typical black family, income last year was $3,000 higher than in 1992. Poverty rates fell, with the percentage of blacks in poverty, 29.3 percent, falling to its lowest level since data were first collected in 1959. Child poverty fell faster than in any year since the mid-1970's. Even the nation's stubbornly high level of inequality fell more last year than in any year since the late 1960's.
Ordinarily, there would be nothing surprising about rising income and falling poverty in the fifth year of a recovery. But for too long this recovery was "jobless," producing meager gains in living standards. Even now wages are nearly stagnant. But at least in the past two years, employment has taken off. Family income is rising because more people are working and those who work are putting in longer hours. 
 The recent gains, though welcome, ought to be kept in perspective. Poverty would have to fall four more years at last year's rate just to reach the level that was achieved 23 years ago. Black poverty last year, the lowest in recorded history, remains far worse than white poverty. Family incomes are rising, but remain below 1989 levels for all but the richest. Inequality has fallen for two years, but the average income of families in the bottom 20 percent is only one-twentieth as high as the average income of families in the top 5 percent. Child poverty remains at an obscenely high 20 percent.
Peering into the future, Congress made matters needlessly worse. The welfare bill that it passed, and the President signed, will throw millions of children and adults into poverty. Just as the economy opens the door to prosperity for families, rich and poor, Congress has slammed the door back shut for the most disadvantaged.

LANGUAGE: ENGLISH

TYPE: Editorial

HEADLINE: Blue Chips End 4.07 Higher In an Uninspired Session

BYLINE: By The Associated Press 

 BODY:
Stocks drifted higher near the close yesterday, but the lethargic tone that took hold earlier in the week continued as investors awaited new incentives to extend its late summer rally into early autumn.
The Dow Jones industrial average rose 4.07 points, to 5,872.92, pulling higher in the final minutes after meandering in negative territory for most of the session. The small gain cut the Dow's loss on the week to 15.54 points. 
 Broader measures also finished higher, with technology and speculative shares outperforming blue-chip issues throughout the day. But without any big news again, trading remained lifeless in the aftermath to Tuesday's decision by Federal Reserve not to raise interest rates.
"It has been a very unenthusiastic session," said Eugene Peroni, director of technical research at Janney Montgomery Scott in Philadelphia. "Investors are focusing on the individual stories rather than the overall market or the overall economy."
Mr. Peroni said the slow grind since Tuesday has been constructive. "It seems to be arresting the excesses that cropped up before Tuesday and reduces the need and likelihood for a deep and sustained drop," he said.
The Fed's decision had validated optimism that economic growth was slowing enough to avoid an increase in interest rates that would be sharper and sooner than expected. The news cheered bond traders. But the stock market had already rallied to record levels in anticipation of Tuesday's Fed meeting.
The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index rose 0.33 point, to 686.19. The Nasdaq composite index rose 2.07, to 1,230.05.
Pepsico, down 2 1/8, to 28 1/8, was the most active issue on the Big Baord after announcing on Thursday that it was overhauling its international soft drink operations and considering selling some restaurants.
The Dow's biggest gainer was General Electric, which rose 1 1/8, to 91 1/2, on reports of a Goldman, Sachs upgrade. Walt Disney rose 1, to 63.

HEADLINE: Japan's Premier Calls Elections for October

BYLINE: By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF 

DATELINE: TOKYO, Sept. 27

 BODY:
Prime Minister Ryutaro Hashimoto dissolved Parliament today, setting the stage for new national elections that may reconfigure Japanese politics.
Mr. Hashimoto's party is expected to do well in the elections, which will be held on Oct. 20, and most bets are that he will remain Prime Minister. But his party may not win a majority, meaning that he would have to form a new coalition that could help set off a political realignment. 
 These elections are regarded as particularly important because they will be the first under a new voting system that is expected to mean the extinction of some small parties and a reduction in the importance of conservative rural voters. Under the new system, for the first time since 1924 Japanese will elect a single legislator from each district, as in the United States.
"The time has come to ask for the people's support in determining how to guide Japan into the future," Mr. Hashimoto declared.
"The battle is at hand," he added, "and I want to give our every effort to win."
Mr. Hashimoto could have waited until next summer to call an election, but the opposition is now in disarray and his Liberal Democratic Party is doing relatively well in the polls. It is benefiting from a slowly reviving economy and a sense that Mr. Hashimoto has moved to resolve problems with American bases in Okinawa.
To be sure, Mr. Hashimoto has not lived up to the enthusiasm that greeted him when he took office in January, seeming young and dynamic by the standards of Japanese politics. But if voter attitudes toward the 59-year-old Mr. Hashimoto are lukewarm, they are positively cool to the main opposition leader, Ichiro Ozawa, head of the New Frontier Party.
"This is an election on which we are staking the very existence of the New Frontier Party," Mr. Ozawa told party members today. "More important, this is also an election on which we are staking the possibility of securing Japan's future and public happiness for the new generation."
A recent poll by the Nihon Keizai newspaper found 22 percent support for the Liberal Democrats and only 7 percent for New Frontier. A full 49 percent were undecided, and a surprising 8 percent said they would support a new party, the Democrats.
The Democratic Party, which is to be formally introduced on Saturday, is trying to emerge as the leading force for change in Japan, appealing to voters who are weary of corrupt and tired politicians who seem to be manipulated by the bureaucracy. It is led by Yukio Hatoyama, the scion of a wealthy political family who is sometimes compared to President Kennedy, and Naoto Kan, the energetic Health Minister and perhaps the most popular politician in Japan.
Because it is so new, the Democratic Party is a wild card in the election, and it could emerge with the balance of power in a coalition. It is conceivable, though unlikely, that it could win enough seats to insist on the prime minister's job in a coalition with the Liberal Democrats.
Politicians say that the new electoral system makes the outcome difficult to forecast, but that the most likely outcome is that the Liberal Democrats will dominate a coalition. This would probably mean policies similar to the present ones.
"I think it's going to be a coalition very much along the lines of what we have now," said John F. Neuffer, a specialist on Japanese politics at Mitsui Marine Research Institute in Tokyo. In any case, he noted, there are few major differences among the political parties.
"Basically, everybody agrees on all of the issues," he said. "This is a largely issue-less campaign."
The one topic that has aroused some interest is whether the national sales tax should be raised as scheduled in April from 3 percent to 5 percent. Voters are opposed to the tax increase, and so the opposition is trying to make an issue of it.
The opposition is also calling for major administrative reform, including a dramatic reduction in the number of government ministries. But since all the major parties are calling for this, even the governing Liberal Democrats, voters seem skeptical of all the proposals.
In the old electoral system, Japanese elected several legislators from each district. But now 300 legislators will be chosen in single-seat constituencies, with just one member of Parliament for each district. An additional 200 legislators will be chosen by proportional representation based on votes for each party.
The combination of the new system and the surly voter mood is expected to be nearly fatal for the Social Democratic Party, which recently changed its name from the Socialist Party in a desperate attempt to broaden its appeal. The Socialists were the main opposition party for most of postwar Japanese history, but they seem to be fragmenting and may not survive much longer.
"Our identity as an independent party has not become clear, and we have provoked criticism and distrust," the Social Democrats' leader, Tomiichi Murayama, said today. "But we've had some calls of support just now, and we've got to win in this election at all cost."

HEADLINE: 2d-Quarter Growth Was Most in 2 Years

BYLINE: AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 27

 BODY:
The economy accelerated at a 4.7 percent annual rate in the second quarter, the fastest in two years, but analysts say the growth has now downshifted to about half that pace.
"Third-quarter G.D.P. growth appears to have slowed sharply from the second-quarter pace," said Bruce Steinberg, an economist for Merrill Lynch & Company. "We believe that G.D.P. rose at just a 1.5 percent to 2 percent rate in the third quarter." 
 The G.D.P., or gross domestic product, is the total output of goods and services within the United States. It advanced at a 2 percent rate during the January-March quarter after an anemic rise of three-tenths of 1 percent in the final three months of 1995.
But growth took off in the April-June quarter, racing ahead at the fastest pace since a 4.9 percent increase during the second quarter of 1994, the Commerce Department said today.
The second-quarter figure was in line with analysts' expectations. The first official figures for the third quarter, which ends Monday, will not be available for another month.
The bond market rose sharply after the report was released. Analysts said the market reflected growing agreement that the economy had moderated since spring and was not in need of an increase in interest rates to curb inflation.
And even though recent data suggest a slowdown in growth, analysts agree that the economy remains sound.
"There's enough fuel in the economy in terms of income growth, employment gain, stock market strength and overall consumer confidence to boost consumer spending in the important Christmas quarter," said Lynn Reaser, an economist for Barnett Banks in Jacksonville, Fla. She foresees a growth rate of 2 percent to 3 percent during the October-December period.
Federal Reserve policy makers apparently agree that the economy has moderated since the second quarter. At their Tuesday meeting, they left interest rates unchanged. There had been speculation that the Fed panel might raise rates in an attempt to slow the economy and head off any surge of inflation.
An index of inflation reported in today's G.D.P. figures supports the belief that little inflation exists. The index rose 2.1 percent, even slower than the 2.3 percent increase in the previous three months.
"The second quarter was the best of all worlds: low inflation and strong growth," Ms. Reaser said.
But some investors in stocks may not agree. The report also showed that corporate after-tax profits fell two-tenths of 1 percent, or $700 million, the first drop since a fall of five-tenths of 1 percent a year earlier.
However, most major components shared in the second-quarter growth.
Consumer spending, two-thirds of economic activity, spurted ahead at a 3.4 percent rate, virtually the same as the first quarter's 3.5 percent.
Housing was up at a 16.3 percent rate, more than twice the 7.4 percent pace from January through March.
Renewed inventory building added $10.1 billion to the G.D.P. in the second quarter, after businesses liquidated stockpiles in the first, subtracting $17.6 billion from the January-March G.D.P.
Government spending grew at a 7.7 percent rate, up from 1.6 percent in the previous quarter.
The trade deficit, however, remained a drag in the second quarter. It subtracted $10.7 billion from the G.D.P., although that was down from $19.1 billion in the first quarter.
Business investments, a major source of strength earlier, slowed to a 3.8 percent growth rate, from 11.6 percent in the first quarter.
The various changes left total G.D.P. at an annualized $6.89 trillion, after adjusting for inflation, compared with $6.81 trillion in the January-March quarter.
