HEADLINE: For Canadians, Is U.S. Gaze Friendly?

BYLINE: By ANTHONY DePALMA 

DATELINE: TORONTO, Sept. 27

 BODY:
A hearing before a United States Congressional committee this week that bluntly assessed what a breakup of Canada would mean for the United States attracted little attention in Washington. But it reverberated loudly here, leaving many Canadians either flattered by the attention or resentful of outside interference.
The hearing on Wednesday before the House Subcommittee on the Western Hemisphere focused on the security and economic implications of a secession by Quebec. 
 "Here it hadn't really sunk in yet that the country almost broke apart and may still do so," said Mel Watkins, a professor of Canadian Studies at the University of Toronto. Having the unraveling of Canada laid out in a Washington hearing room, Professor Watkins said, made Canadians pay attention in a way they never would have if the same arguments had been made in Ottawa.
The hearing, broadcast in Canada and covered extensively by local newspapers, made many Canadians sqirm, especially in Quebec, as witnesses described the chaos that could follow if Quebec voted to pull out of Canada, which almost happened last October, to Washington's surprise.
And some thought the United States should mind its own business. "An exercise that is off the charts on the paranoia index," is how a CBC commentator described the hearing.
"When big brother takes an interest, it can go both ways," said Alan W. Sullivan, president of the Canadian Institute of International Affairs, an independent organization in Toronto. "The United States is such an important player in the Canadian psyche that it grates on Canadian nerves when the United States does pay attention to us, as well as when it doesn't pay attention to us."
The United States has considered Quebec a strictly domestic issue for Canada, its most important trading partner.
But the Congressman who organized the hearing, Tom Campbell, a Republican from California, argued that the United States was not paying enough attention to what was going on north of the border. He said the North American Free Trade Agreement raised direct issues for the United States, like whether an independent Quebec would automatically be allowed to join Nafta.
A view presented several times at the hearing was that admission for sovereign Quebec would not be automatic, and that by stating so clearly, Washington would help the people of the province understand the consequences of secession.
One witness, Charles F. Doran, had already raised tempers in Canada with an article published this month in Foreign Affairs magazine speculating on the future of Canada. Mr. Doran, a Distinguished Professor of International Relations at Johns Hopkins University, argued that if Quebec seceded, Canada would unravel.
The poor eastern provinces of Canada would be physically cut off from the rest of the country, he wrote, and the wealthier western provinces would have little incentive to remain in a union that subsidizes the poor provinces, or gives too much power and control to Ontario, the most populous of the 10 provinces.
Professor Doran speculated that Alberta, a sparsely populated prairie province, might even try to apply for statehood with the United States. That prompted this headline in The Financial Post, a Toronto daily: "Give us your poor, your tired, your huddled masses -- and Alberta."
Many Canadian newspapers welcomed the hearings because of the perception that Washington could analyze the Quebec issue without the histrionics it raises in Canada. But there was also some embarrassment over the airing of Canada's internal problems in Washington.
Neither the United States State Department nor the Canadian Government sent any representatives to testify. In fact, no Canadians testified at all; the four witnesses were all Americans.
"In a way it was a classic Canadian-American story, filled with a lot of absurdities," Professor Watkins said. "There it was, a hearing about Canada, with no Canadians testifying. And though it was supposed to be for the benefit of the United States, the hearing room was filled with only Canadian journalists."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Representative Tom Campbell, Republican of California, organized a hearing on Quebec. (Associated Press)

HEADLINE: Rally Runs Out of Fresh Data, And Bond Prices Take a Dip

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
The recent rise in prices of Treasury securities stalled yesterday, in the absence of strong fresh data.
The 30-year bond fell 8/32 , to a price of 983/32 to yield 6.90 percent, up from 6.88 percent on Thursday. 
 The reports issued yesterday included the University of Michigan's index of consumer sentiment, which edged lower this month, to 94.7, from 95 in the midmonth report and 95.3 in August. Also, there was a slight downward revision in the second-quarter growth of gross domestic product, a development that provided some support for a market looking for a deceleration of growth in the months ahead.
Stuart D. Taylor, president of Taylor Consulting Inc. in Little Rock, Ark., is not overly bullish about the current bond market. "One thing I've learned over 14 years of trading bonds is to pay attention to my hunches," he said. "The idea that the market is ready to move significantly higher just doesn't feel right."
He noted that the market had passed a variety of technical tests and that prices of industrial commodities were turning supportive for bonds, "but at this stage I would have to classify myself as uncomfortable with an overly bullish opinion," he said.
Anthony Chan, chief economist at Banc One Investment Advisers, said the market action reflected more of a pause than an abandonment of the view that the economy is slowing enough to justify the Federal Reserve's decision on Tuesday to leave interest rates unchanged.
"While moments before the Fed meeting ended, a broad spectrum of market participants were braced for a token tightening, today we are seeing a virtual 180-degree turn," Mr. Chan said. "More and more investors are now even beginning to openly discuss that the next Fed move could actually be an easing."
At the very least, he suggested, the Fed should be able to keep monetary policy on hold until inflation shows up in the Consumer Price Index.
Mr. Chan said that next week would be a blockbuster in terms of economic news. He said the market would first focus on the report from purchasing managers in the Chicago area to see how much that index gives back after it surged to 60 last month from 51.2 in July.
A drop in the index of less than a couple of points could stir some concern that the weak durable goods number released on Thursday might have overstated the general trend in manufacturing. That in turn would suggest that the report from the National Association of Purchasing Management, to be released on Tuesday, will be stronger, as could the manufacturing component of the September employment report, due on Friday.
Mr. Chan said that aside from the nonfarm payroll reading in the jobs report, investors would be watching to see if the apparent slowdown in the economy was affecting the figures on average hourly earnings.
"Any monthly average hourly earnings readings that come even close to last month's five-tenths of a percent sizable increase could threaten the most bullish sentiment that is currently permeating the market," he said.

HEADLINE: AMERICAN PROFILES -- Alabama;
Alabama Voters Waver Between Old Principles And a New Prosperity

BYLINE: By KEVIN SACK 

DATELINE: GADSDEN, Ala., Sept. 23

 BODY:
The people of Etowah County, explains the Rev. Aaron Johnson, the preacher at the First Baptist Church of Gadsden, are a little bit different. In their distinctly Southern way, they are a tad mistrustful, a bit suspicious. Even in the best of times, it seems, they fear that distant forces may be conspiring to take their jobs and undermine their values.
Maybe, Mr. Johnson suggests, it is all the talk out of Washington about gun control and gay marriages. Maybe it is because even in the best of times, the economy of this blue-collar city of 45,000 people in northeastern Alabama never quite feels secure. 
 The steel mill has been sold and sold and sold again, three times in a dozen years. With contract talks approaching at the Goodyear factory, the plant manager has distributed an ominous letter warning employees of a "crisis" caused by global competition. Memories linger of the old Dwight Cotton Mill, which was closed by a strike in 1959 and then stood abandoned for 20 years as a reminder of the capriciousness of life.
"They're skeptical, untrusting, almost as if there's a feeling of paranoia," Mr. Johnson said of the members of his congregation. "I made the statement from the pulpit one day that 'just because you're not paranoid doesn't mean they're not out to get you.' And nobody laughed. I may have gotten a few 'amens' on that, but nobody laughed."
Inevitably in a campaign year, that skepticism and uncertainty seeps into the region's politics.
It is not simply that people here are unenthusiastic about their choices, which many describe as being between the lesser of two evils. It is that they are struggling to sort out conflicting impulses. Unlike the old days, when there was only one party in Alabama, voters here, as throughout the state and much of the South, have found that the party that is best for their pocketbooks is not always best for their principles.
They are weighing their often disdainful feelings for President Clinton against the relative prosperity of their lives under a Democratic Administration. They are drawn to the conservative values embraced by the Republican Party, but are uninspired by its standard-bearer, Bob Dole.
When they read the headlines, they see their principles under assault. Down at the county courthouse, lawyers with the American Civil Liberties Union are trying to force Judge Roy Moore of the Circuit Court to remove the Ten Commandments from the wall of his courtroom. When he is not on trial himself, Judge Moore is hearing the divorce case of a lesbian who is seeking custody of her child.
And yet, times are about as good here as they have been in recent memory. The county's unemployment rate is now at 5.1 percent, down from 14 percent in 1984, and is the lowest in 20 years. Sales tax revenue has increased by an average of 15 percent a year for the last five years.
At Gulf States Steel, the second-largest employer in Gadsden, it has been three years since the last round of downsizing. Workers are being paid an average of $17 an hour, and last year they received nearly $5,000 each in profit sharing.
Steven G. Blackwell, a burly young steelworker from nearby Collinsville, used his profit-sharing money to buy lumber for the house he is building. He gives the President much of the credit for the health of the economy.
But in a state where cultural conservatism reigns and character counts, Mr. Blackwell has deep reservations about Mr. Clinton -- about his integrity, about his alleged marital infidelity, about his smoking marijuana, about his support for abortion rights, about "those shady Whitewater dealings."
"He has no morals whatsoever," Mr. Blackwell said of the President.
And so, after voting Republican in the last two Presidential elections, Mr. Blackwell finds himself wavering between Mr. Clinton and Mr. Dole.
"I never thought I would see the day," he said, shaking his head as he lumbered toward the mill gate to start a 3 P.M. to 11 P.M. shift.
Another steelworker, C. T. Abney, who has worked at the mill for 32 of his 54 years, said the economy had persuaded him to vote Democratic.
"If the economy went bad, I probably wouldn't vote for Clinton," Mr. Abney said. "Some of these moral issues I don't like, like this gay business. I don't give a flip for that. But right now the good outweighs the bad."
That Mr. Blackwell, Mr. Abney and other white, blue-collar workers would even consider voting for Mr. Clinton is a somewhat remarkable development. They are exactly the kind of voters who have transformed Alabama into a reliably Republican state in recent years.
But like the steelworkers, Alabamians interviewed across the state last week -- whether at a coffee shop in Birmingham, a feed barn in Snead or an outlet mall in Boaz -- often had difficulty reconciling their optimism about a stable economy with their contempt for declining standards of morality.
They rarely expressed anxiety about the economy and almost never volunteered any interest in tax cuts, the centerpiece of Mr. Dole's economic plan. They voiced amorphous fears that cutting the size of the Federal Government would endanger the elderly and threaten jobs in agriculture, the military and NASA, all major employers in Alabama.
But they also were quick to criticize the Clinton Administration about its moral tone. Many volunteered particularly strong feelings about homosexuality, a subject they tie directly to Mr. Clinton because of his support for allowing lesbians and gay men to serve openly in the military.
"I don't agree with it, giving gays rights in the workplace," said Wayne Ragsdale, a 60-year-old mechanic from Allgood. "If God had meant for two of the same sex to be all right together, he wouldn't have made the other one."
The politics of culture and values is vital in Alabama. Natalie Davis, a Democratic political scientist at Birmingham Southern College, said her "fundamental axiom about Alabama politics is that we vote for people we like, not necessarily people we agree with."
That helps explain why the State Attorney General, Jefferson B. Sessions 3d, the Republican nominee for the Senate, took along both a former Vice President, Dan Quayle (family values), and a former stock car racer, Bobby Allison (Southern culture), on a bus tour of Alabama last week. At a few stops Mr. Quayle actually won more applause than Mr. Allison.
With the exception of Jimmy Carter in 1976, no Democratic Presidential candidate has carried Alabama since John F. Kennedy in 1960. Mr. Clinton has not stepped foot in the state since April 1992. The Governor, a United States Senator and three of the state's seven members of Congress are Republicans.
In January, 10 Democratic judges from Birmingham defected to the Republican Party en masse. And when a special election for sheriff was held in Birmingham this year, the Democrats did not even field a candidate.
History suggests that the President and the Democratic Senate nominee, State Senator Roger H. Bedford, should be considered underdogs this fall. But the most recent polls indicate that both the Presidential race and the Senate campaign are closely contested.
The numbers do not suggest that Alabamians are growing less conservative. Rather, they reflect the ability of Democratic politicians to outrun accusations of liberalism by embracing all manner of conservative positions and by speaking unceasingly about their "Alabama values."
To distinguish itself from the national Democrats, the Alabama Democratic Party adopted its own platform this year, stating that schools should not be hostile to any child's religious belief, emphasizing its support for the death penalty and acknowledging the right of Democrats to differ on such issues as abortion and gun control.
Mr. Bedford boasts of voting for Ronald Reagan in 1980. He opposes abortion rights and allowing homosexuals to serve in the military, describes himself as a lifetime member of the National Rifle Association, favors repeal of the ban on assault weapons and says he would have voted for strict time limits on welfare benefits. Asked in an interview what makes him a Democrat, he replied, "I believe in helping people."
Cultural conservatism is so deeply entrenched here that candidates of both parties seem as eager to profess their faith in Jesus as their opposition to gun control.
In his stump speech, Mr. Bedford, a 40-year-old lawyer from Russelville, makes a point of describing his prayerful recovery from lymphoma in 1990, and how it nurtured a "strong belief in God." Mr. Sessions, who is 49 and from Mobile, is broadcasting a television commercial in which he testifies: "I believe in fundamental values. I believe there is truth. I believe there are principles which are unchanging, that there is a God in heaven who orders the universe."
Back at the First Baptist Church, Mr. Johnson, a loyal Republican, can appreciate that sentiment. But he also acknowledges that the economic edginess in Gadsden may lead people to vote Democratic.
"The question," he said, "is how far will they go with the Democrats if the Democrats leave their morality? How much is their job security worth?"

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE REPUBLICAN;
Dole 'Listens to America,' And Hears Press Criticism

BYLINE: By ADAM NAGOURNEY 

DATELINE: MIAMI, Sept. 27

 BODY:
When Bob Dole arrived on Thursday here in Dade County, the population center of a state his aides believe he must win but where polls show him trailing, he made only a single campaign stop, for a 42-minute question-and-answer forum at a huge and gritty warehouse in the northwest corner of Miami.
This was the 15th in a series of "Listening to America" sessions that Mr. Dole has led since he was nominated for President six weeks ago. The loosely structured forums, often on a particular topic like his economic plan, have become a dominant component of Mr. Dole's effort to unseat President Clinton, taking up far more time than he devotes to major policy addresses and even rivaling the time he spends at more customary political rallies. 
 As he embarks on the final weeks of what his campaign organization views as an increasingly problematic race, however, Mr. Dole's experience with the "Listening to America" format suggests that its value to his bid for the Presidency remains far from proven.
Mr. Dole's aides maintain that the events afford him the kind of favorable coverage from local news media that the candidate says he has not found in the national press.
"It may well bore elite reporters," John Buckley, the nominee's communications director, said of the format. "But it does get covered, and we create news on topics that we are interested in creating news on."
But the coverage by newspapers and television stations here in the aftermath of Mr. Dole's visit, as in other places where he has taken his "Listening to America" tour, suggests otherwise. Miami news organizations either were critical of Thursday's event in their accounts or, perhaps worse, nearly ignored it.
"The questions were mostly softballs, but even then Dole didn't always connect," Michael Putney, a veteran political reporter for the ABC affiliate here, told his viewers in his evening report on Thursday.
None of the Miami television stations led their early-evening newscasts with Mr. Dole's appearance. One devoted just 30 seconds to it. Another relegated it to the bottom of the news lineup, after reports on the resurgence of Pan Am and Valujet, a high school student's unsuccessful effort to blow up his mathematics teachers and a traffic accident on Interstate 95.
All the television reports on Mr. Dole's visit focused on his political difficulties rather than on his message, and portrayed the forums themselves as staged. This morning The Miami Herald put its account on the front page -- of the second section.
Beyond the campaign's objective of local coverage, Mr. Dole regularly points to these events as an opportunity for him to hear the unfiltered views of Americans ordinarily little involved in the political process. But here, as has been typical elsewhere, the audience had been stocked by local Republican leaders, and those present were for the most part gentle in their handling of him.
The theme of the forum was women in business, and by design the audience was all women, mostly Cuban-Americans. Interviews with some of them -- before a Dole aide ordered a reporter to stop talking to them and leave the floor -- also suggested that they were nearly all Republicans who supported Mr. Dole.
The nominee's aides say these are the kind of easy-to-arrange, photographically alluring events that win attention for candidates. But even Mr. Dole's supporters question the value of having him pose for pictures, as in this case, in a warehouse with stacks of plastic containers behind him and cases of Heinz Worcestershire sauce in front of him. Further, said one former Dole media adviser, Mike Murphy, who left the campaign in a recent shake-up, the candidate's organization needs to make the forums "more newsworthy and less sterile."
Mr. Dole's difficulties with the "Listening to America" forums might be less significant had he not invested so large a share of his relatively modest campaign schedule in them. From the start, the events have been a subject of fairly critical newspaper accounts that have portrayed them as staged and suggested that rather than giving Mr. Dole a taste of the country's concerns, they simply echo the nominee's own views. "Doggone it," Susan Feeney wrote in The Dallas Morning News, "Mr. Dole was 'listening to America,' and it sounded a lot like him."
Mr. Buckley, the Dole campaign's communications director, dismisses such news accounts as an example of "elite reporters' having to show off." In fact, though, similar criticism can be seen and heard in smaller media markets' local coverage as well.
And it appears to have rankled the candidate. When the forum here opened with a question about his tax cut proposal, Mr. Dole, who has not held a formal news conference since March, devoted part of his answer to something else, remarking with evident irritation that he understood that a newspaper reporter had been inquiring as to how this event had come about.
"I know somebody was here earlier," he said, "some reporter saying: 'Well, these are all the same. Who's going to do this? Who's going to do that?'
"He must have left disappointed, because it's not staged, not rehearsed. We're here to talk about America."
The woman whose question led to that response was Alina Garcia, a beauty salon owner, who said she had been invited to the forum after calling friends on the local Republican committee.
Mr. Dole embarked on these sessions after the Republican National Convention, and they are clearly patterned on the performance there by his wife, Elizabeth. But where Mrs. Dole took a hand-held microphone and wandered comfortably through the audience for her chat, her husband customarily stays settled in his chair, occasionally rising to answer questions but evincing none of the same level of ease with this kind of campaign banter.
Such events are nothing new in American politics. Richard M. Nixon was among the first to use them. As early as 1979, George Bush, in his own quest for the Presidency, was conducting "Ask George Bush" forums, in which he sat on a stool in the middle of a community assembly. And Mr. Clinton made them a regular part of his 1992 campaign.
But as Marlin Fitzwater, press secretary in the Bush White House, noted the other day, the approach has varied over the years. In 1992, President Bush, following Mr. Clinton's lead, began holding such forums in local television studios. The advantage was that doing so dramatically expanded the audience; the disadvantage, as Mr. Fitzwater recalled, was that the television stations helped choose those attending.
Mr. Murphy, the former Dole media adviser, said that before leaving the campaign he had urged that the nominee adopt the studio approach. But the campaign stayed with untelevised forums.
Here in Miami, the audience was selected by both the campaign and a longtime Dole supporter, Remedios Diaz Oliver, the president of All American Containers Inc., whose warehouse was the setting for the event.
One in attendance, Pamela Wilde-Cole, told Mr. Dole, "I believe that the difference between Hillary and Bill and you and Liddy is character, is decency, honor and character."
At that, those in Mr. Dole's "Listening to America" audience burst into applause and, as one, rose to their feet to end the session with a prolonged standing ovation.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Bob Dole at a "Listening to America" forum in Miami, the only campaign stop he made after arriving in Dade County on Thursday. (Stephen Crowley/The New York Times)

HEADLINE: MIDDLE EAST CONFLICT: IN WASHINGTON;
Limits on U.S. Leverage: The Needs of Both Sides

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER 

 BODY:
The armed clashes between Israeli soldiers and Palestinian police officers, which have deeply shaken assumptions about lasting peace in the Middle East, have also shaken the Clinton Administration, which was looking for a quiet life in foreign policy until the Presidential election.
In the last few days, State Department officials have worked around the clock, in person and by telephone, to try to bring Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu of Israel together with Yasir Arafat, the Palestinian leader, possibly within a day, in a meeting that might produce calm in the streets and something positive for both sides. 
 But the United States finds itself with unusually little leverage on the Israelis, part of the price Washington is paying for its fruitless efforts this year to secure the election of Mr. Netanyahu's Labor Party rival, Shimon Peres. Given Mr. Netanyahu's skepticism of Washington's real intentions and given the American election campaign, American influence on the Israeli Government is probably as low as it has been since Yitzhak Shamir, the previous Prime Minister from Mr. Netanyahu's right-wing Likud Party, opened a new West Bank settlement on the eve of every chilly visit to Washington.
Mr. Netanyahu, while open to American suggestions and telephone calls, has already rejected American advice to reseal the tunnel that runs parallel to the Western Wall, at least temporarily. And he has rebuffed suggestions that Secretary of State Warren Christopher or his Middle East envoy, Dennis Ross, make a quick visit to the Middle East. Mr. Netanyahu is setting his own pace and looking first to his own conservative domestic constituents.
Despite the enormous potential leverage that the United States has over Israel -- from $3 billion a year in aid to important sharing of military and security intelligence -- no American Administration, Democratic or Republican, wants to pick a fight with Israel during an election year. It is one reason that President Clinton himself has delegated the problem to Mr. Christopher, and has not himself engaged in any telephone diplomacy, let alone rush to Israel, as he did after the suicide bombings this spring, to offer his support.
But there is also a real question of whether any serious confrontation with Israel would produce the result Washington wants, especially in this case, when Mr. Netanyahu feels that any indication of weakness on his part, or the least bow to foreign pressure, could bring down his already fractious Government.
Mr. Arafat, too, is being careful, American officials say. He does not want to be pressured by the Americans into a meeting with Mr. Netanyahu that produces nothing. Having promoted the Palestinian protests about the Israeli decision to open another entrance to an archeological tunnel along the Haram al-Sharif or Temple Mount, Mr. Arafat lost control of the demonstrations and even control of a number of his own Palestinian police.
Mr. Arafat does not want to be pushed into giving orders for calm that could be ignored or that turn out to produce no Israeli concessions, a senior American official suggested, and thus undermine him instead of letting him consolidate some of the political gain the violence has secured him.
"Using leverage isn't about whether you can force one side or another into doing something," the official said. "Force doesn't produce real commitments, and it would cost us in the long run. We have to be viewed as a useful, reliable interlocutor by both sides. That's where the real leverage comes."
American officials have largely given up on the hope that Mr. Netanyahu will shut the new tunnel entrance while an Israeli-Palestinian commission studies the problem. So the Americans are concentrating on getting Mr. Netanyahu and Mr. Arafat together in a way that can produce a more rapid carrying out of what Israel has already agreed to do under their peace accord, including the removal of most Israeli troops from the West Bank town of Hebron, and create a process and agenda for future meetings.
"It will be hard, even impossible, for Netanyahu to change his position on the tunnel," a knowledgeable official said. "But he is prepared to move on other issues that the Palestinians really want, like Hebron."
Still, the official acknowledged, "the last three days complicate what could have been done," increasing pressure on Mr. Netanyahu from his own Likud Party to offer the Palestinians nothing but the stick.
It may even be that Mr. Arafat, by promoting protests intended to increase pressure on Mr. Netanyahu before scheduled negotiations, has made it harder for the Israeli to deliver what he had planned.
"The judgments have been bad on both sides," a senior official said with frustration.
Since Mr. Netanyahu's narrow election in late May, American officials have worked hard to push him toward a serious engagement with Mr. Arafat, on the general principle that as one official said, "there is no neutral gear in the Middle East."
Lack of progress means deterioration, they believed, and they worried that Mr. Arafat could lose out to more militant elements among Palestinians unless he could show his constituents the ability to deliver at least some of their aspirations. Growing political frustration and economic pain, among Palestinians who saw no gains and felt that Mr. Arafat was being treated with contempt, was bound to explode.
But clearly progress was too slow, and now the Americans are trying to salvage what they can from a peace process Mr. Clinton hoped would smooth his way into a second term.
An Administration that has devoted such a large proportion of its diplomatic energies to the Middle East is bound to be tarred by the violence, which is itself an indication of a more general disillusion with the image of a comprehensive peace that Mr. Clinton and Mr. Peres sold so hard, and perhaps oversold, even to themselves.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Technology Shares Top Blue Chips in Slow Market
In a lethargic stock market, technology and speculative shares outperformed blue chips, helping the Nasdaq composite rise 2.07 points, to 1,230.05. For the week, the index was up 10.36 points. 
 The Dow Jones industrial average rose 4.07 points, to 5,872.92. The small gain cut the Dow's loss on the week to 15.54 points. Analysts said the slow period helped mop up earlier speculative excesses. [Page 40.]
 
The bond market's rally stalled after four days, with the 30-year Treasury bond falling 8/32 , to a price of 983/32 to yield 6.90 percent, up from 6.88 percent on Thursday. [38.]
 
Court Delays Telephone Rules
A Federal court ordered a stay on new regulations adopted by the F.C.C. on Aug. 8 so that GTE and other local phone companies could present oral arguments against the rules, which govern opening local phone companies' networks to new competitors like long-distance carriers and cable TV. [39.]
 
Discovery and BBC in Venture
Discovery Communications and the British Broadcasting Corporation announced a joint venture under which Discovery will spend more than $500 million to produce new programs and start cable and satellite television channels in the United States and around the world with the BBC. [38.]
 
Fed Focusing on Inflation
Divided Federal Reserve officials, while keeping interest rate policy steady this summer, agreed that they needed to be vigilant against any resurgence of inflation, minutes of its policy meeting showed. [38.]
 
G.D.P. Grew at a 4.7% Rate
The economy accelerated at a 4.7 percent annual rate in the second quarter, the fastest in two years, the Commerce Department reported, but analysts say growth in the gross domestic product, has now downshifted to about half that pace, suggesting no increase in interest rates is needed to curb inflation. [38.]
 
Microsoft Sees Margins Slimming
Microsoft warned that its 1997 operating margins might narrow from a year earlier because of slower revenue growth and higher operating expenses, notably spending for research and development. In after-hours trading, the shares fell $1.25, to $133.125. [38.]
 
S.E.C. Sues Florida Firm
The Securities and Exchange Commission accused a Florida firm, Atlantic Capital of Orlando, of paying $477,580 in bribes to seven brokers at other firms as part of what it called a stock-promoting scheme that netted $2.6 million in illegal profits. [40.]
 
Suit Against I.R.S. to Proceed
A Federal judge in Binghamton, N.Y., refused to dismiss a lawsuit against the I.R.S. brought by 737 former I.B.M. employees. They contend that part of their severance payments were not taxable wages but nontaxable settlements of potential personal injury claims for emotional distress and other wrongs. [38.]
 
Alcoa to Cut
2,900 Jobs
The Aluminum Company of America said that it would reduce its work force by 4 percent, cutting 2,900 jobs, and take a $65.5 million charge against third- quarter earnings. Wall Street appeared unimpressed, as Alcoa's shares slipped 12.5 cents, to $58.875. [39.]
 
CBS to Give Candidates Air Time
CBS announced it would offer free television and radio time to President Clinton and Bob Dole for "unfiltered" two-minute pronouncements on four campaign issues to be determined by a CBS News poll. [11.]
 
Monetary Union, a Goal for Italy
Determined to make Italy among the first countries entering Europe's monetary union, the center-left Government began debate on a $41 billion budget for 1997 that would halve the deficit and impose new taxes. [3.]

HEADLINE: SUNY Tries Off-Peak Pricing At Some Struggling Campuses

BYLINE: By KAREN W. ARENSON 

 BODY:
Airlines do it. Hotels do it. Phone companies do it. They all offer off-peak pricing -- discounts to customers who use their services at less crowded times.
Now the State University of New York is trying the idea at its two-year colleges, which are faced with declining enrollment at some campuses and pressures to pay more of their own way. 
 SUNY's trustees voted this week to allow 30 community colleges and 5 colleges of technology to set lower tuition for students who take courses at night, on weekends or during the summer, or who attend classes at other sites with empty seats.
It is too early yet to say how the program will work for SUNY. Some of the community colleges are expected to begin offering discounts in the spring semester, though they have yet to figure out how large the discounts will be or whether off-peak pricing will ultimately be useful.
The technology colleges have begun offering a 23 percent reduction in price this semester to part-time students taking classes at underused sites off campus, but are not yet offering lower prices on campus.
Colleges everywhere have gradually been adopting more flexible pricing systems. Many offer lower per-credit prices for programs like summer school, continuing education and weekend colleges. SUNY's decision was seen by some educators yesterday as moving a step further.
"This is an example of how market forces are coming more and more into play in higher education," said Theodore Marchese, a vice president of the American Association for Higher Education in Washington and editor of Change magazine. "There is more differential pricing today than years ago, when low tuition, independent of field or the time of study, was the rule."
The reasons for SUNY's pricing effort are varied. In some cases, the campuses are seeking to halt a slide in enrollment. But others are responding to budget cuts in Albany and to the pressure being applied by the Pataki administration to take more of a free-market approach, in which the campuses compete for resources and students. In all cases, though, the colleges are aiming the discounts at new students, not those who are already enrolled.
Private colleges and universities have generally had more freedom to change their prices and to offer adjustable prices through scholarships and other financial aid. But public colleges like SUNY generally have less flexibility, and their prices are often set by state legislatures or state boards of regents who do not always take into account the competitive needs of specific institutions.
"In private colleges, hardly any two students pay the same tuition," said Joseph L. Kennedy, president of SUNY's College of Technology at Canton, near the Thousand Islands. "In public education, you can't go case by case, you have to price by category."
William F. Massy, an economist at Stanford's Institute for Higher Education Research, said that setting lower prices at certain times "makes a lot of sense."
"When fixed-cost facilities are not being used heavily, why not charge a lower rate?" he asked. "It makes all kinds of sense to do marginal cost pricing if the facilities are lying idle and you can hire faculty."
In California, Terry Colvin, a spokesman for the office of the president at the University of California, said that people at the university would watch the SUNY plan with interest.
"One of the realities of higher education is that facilities have become the most expensive part of the operation, but on nights and weekends are 90 percent empty," he said. "When you dovetail that with projections of climbing enrollments, you reach the conclusion that either we're going to have to build new facilities or find a better use for the ones we have."
He said the university had not tried offering discounts, but instead has imposed extra fees for certain categories of students, like those in professional schools.
In 1991, for example, the university started charging medical students more than other students, and the differential has been raised each year. The fee is more than $4,000 a year above the regular student fee of $3,700 a year, and will rise even more in the future.
Under SUNY's plan, the two-year colleges would be allowed to charge lower tuition to students who attend classes on campus during off-peak hours, like evenings or weekends, or off-peak parts of the year, like summer and between terms in the winter, to encourage better use of college space. They will also be allowed to offer lower tuition to businesses or other institutions that use their sites for part-time instruction.
Each community college campus will decide on its own how to proceed.
Corning Community College will probably start next fall, said its president, Eduardo J. Marti, who said his campus has not yet decided how it will use the new freedom to set different prices for different categories of students.
"This gives us the flexibility to be more competitive," he said. "It makes sense for us to charge less to some students. It's a win-win situation, since students will benefit by being able to attend at a lower cost, and the college will benefit by hopefully having more students."
Corning's student population has fallen slightly in recent years, Dr. Marti said, from an enrollment the equivalent of 3,150 full-time students three years ago to about 3,000 now.
The five technical colleges have agreed to adopt the same pricing structures. They are starting with a program in which part-time students taking classes off campus will be charged $99 per credit, rather than the $128 they have paid.
But Mr. Kennedy at Canton said that since Corning and the other technical colleges were all residential and used their classrooms at night for their existing students, he doubted that lower prices for night classes would work for them.
Another program Canton is trying this year is lowering the rate for out-of-state students to $5,000 from $8,300 in hopes of drawing Canadians. Currently it has only a few, though it is only 55 miles from Ottawa.
"With room and board and other add-ons, $8,300 a year in tuition brought our cost close to $20,000 Canadian dollars," Mr. Kennedy said. "We're hoping that the $5,000 tuition, though it's still pretty steep, will make it more affordable. Having 50 Canadian students could bring a different flavor to our campus."

HEADLINE: Pavel Sudoplatov, 89, Dies; Top Soviet Spy Who Accused Oppenheimer

BYLINE: By DAVID STOUT 

 BODY:
Lieut. Gen. Pavel A. Sudoplatov, a legendary Soviet spymaster who plotted and carried out assassinations with cold-blooded efficiency and claimed to have engineered the theft of atomic secrets from the United States with the aid of four eminent scientists, died Tuesday at his home in Moscow. He was 89.
General Sudoplatov had been suffering from vascular disease and recently had a stroke, Moscow newspapers reported. 
 General Sudoplatov began his rise in the state-security system in the 1930's and attained the rank of lieutenant general in the K.G.B. But in 1953, after Stalin's death, he was arrested along with Lavrenti Beria, the much-feared head of the secret police, and spent 15 years in the gulag. He was not officially "rehabilitated" until 1969.
General Sudoplatov left a remarkable legacy. He was a master of the art of "silent" elimination of enemies of the Soviet state -- silent in the sense of untraceable but not necessarily noiseless. In his memoirs, he recalled killing a luncheon companion with a bomb in 1938.
The general also wrote of organizing a unit to hunt down and kill Leon Trotsky, one of the architects of the Russian Revolution with whom Stalin had grown deeply disenchanted because they disagreed about the future of Communism. Trotsky was killed in a suburb of Mexico City on Aug. 20, 1940.
General Sudoplatov told of those and other deeds in "Special Tasks: The Memoirs of an Unwanted Witness -- a Soviet Spymaster," written with his son, Anatoli, and Jerrold and Leona Schecter. Mr. Schecter is a former Moscow bureau chief for Time magazine.
Published by Little, Brown & Company in 1994, "Special Tasks" caused a sensation because of the general's detailed assertions that the Soviet Union had obtained atomic secrets with the aid of J. Robert Oppenheimer, the director of the Manhattan Project's laboratory at Los Alamos, N.M., and Niels Bohr, Enrico Fermi and Leo Szilard, three other physicists with central roles in the development of the atomic bomb.
General Sudoplatov said that Oppenheimer and the other scientists passed the secrets to people they knew to be Soviet agents and that they did so both because they wanted to help the Soviet Union defeat Hitler and because they believed that spreading knowledge of the secrets of making nuclear bombs would promote world peace.
The general's assertions outraged many American scientists, some of whom cited examples of what they said were unambiguous and provable errors of fact. The Federal Bureau of Investigation said after a 1995 investigation that it had found no "credible evidence" to back up the general's claims. The Russian Foreign Intelligence Service agreed, stating that General Sudoplatov's allegations "do not correspond to reality." The Schecters stood by their work, however.
But the events in General Sudoplatov's career about which there is little or no dispute make a fascinating, if sometimes chilling, story.
Born in the town of Melitopol, Ukraine, in 1907, Pavel Anatolievich Sudoplatov joined the Red Army at the age of 12, when it was battling not only the pro-czarist, anti-revolutionary White Army but also Ukrainian nationalist forces.
When he was 14, his Red Army unit was depleted by losses and needed a telephone operator and decoding clerk in its intelligence division, "and that is how my career in the security service began," the general wrote in his memoirs.
As a young agent in 1938, he was summoned by Stalin, who wanted to get rid of Yevhen Konovalets, a Ukrainian nationalist leader with whom Pavel Sudoplatov was well acquainted.
Stalin asked the young agent, "What are the personal tastes of Konovalets?" who was then in exile in Rotterdam, the Netherlands.
"Konovalets is overly fond of chocolate candies," Pavel Sudoplatov replied.
Stalin suggested he think about that. The agent did, and supervised the design of a box of chocolates rigged with a bomb set to go off half an hour after the box was changed from a vertical to a horizontal position. He met the Ukrainian nationalist for lunch, set the chocolates on the table as a gift and said good-bye, stopping long enough to buy a light raincoat and hat and to hear the bomb go off.
General Sudoplatov told of arranging the killing of Trotsky by cultivating Communist sympathizers in Mexico, including some who had fought against Francisco Franco's fascist forces in the Spanish Civil War. "For us, enemies of the state were personal enemies," he wrote.
The general said he personally selected Ramon Mercader, a civil war veteran, to do the killing. Mercader drove a pick-ax into Trotsky's head. The assassin spent two decades in a Mexican prison and was honored in Moscow upon his release.
General Sudoplatov directed Communist underground activities against Nazi Germany in World War II. In his memoir, he speculated that Raoul Wallenberg, the Swedish diplomat who has been credited with rescuing many Hungarian Jews from the Holocaust, may have been killed in a Soviet prison in 1947.
The general's wife, Emma, died in 1988. He is survived by his son, an economist in Moscow.
"Despite my rehabilitation, my medals have not been returned to me," the general complained at the end of his book. "Let no one forget that I, too, have been a victim of political repression."

CORRECTION-DATE: September 30, 1996, Monday

 CORRECTION:
An obituary on Saturday about Pavel A. Sudoplatov, a former Soviet intelligence official, misstated the role of J. Robert Oppenheimer in the Manhattan Project to make an atomic bomb. He was the scientific director of the Los Alamos laboratory, where the first atomic bomb was made, not the head of the Manhattan Project.

HEADLINE: High Is Low

BYLINE: By Ann Douglas. 
Ann Douglas teaches United States ethnic studies at Columbia University. She is the author of ''Terrible Honesty: Mongrel Manhattan in the 1920's.

 BODY:
At one moment in ''The Shawshank Redemption,'' the hero, played by Tim Robbins, takes over the sound system of the jail in which he's being held. He puts on a record of Mozart, flooding the prison with a duet from ''The Marriage of Figaro.'' The other inmates freeze as the music hits them, their dulled faces transfigured. We're meant to understand that Robbins's good taste is a sign of his innocence, that by bringing high art to his fellow prisoners, he has allowed them to glimpse the long-forgotten country of their own possible redemption.
 In the opening scene of Martin Scorsese's 1973 masterpiece, ''Mean Streets,'' the camera cuts between a home movie of a baptism and Harvey Keitel lying in bed, a cross around his neck, clearly in some sort of spiritual crisis. On the soundtrack we hear Ronnie Bennett singing 
 ''Be My Baby,'' a hit of 1963. It's a seductive, haunted song, wonderfully performed -- a wish already clothed in the sound of loss. It's also a palpable expression of how this character's mind and heart work, an instrument of his being. 
 Both movies aim to be wrenching, but only ''Mean Streets'' succeeds. Though the music in ''The Shawshank Redemption'' is gorgeous, it's part of an uneasy, bogus moment, one dependent on the assumption that so-called ordinary people are starved for high culture, that only ''great'' art can awaken longing for a better life.
 Perhaps inevitably, the United States -- the closest thing to a parent that mass art possesses -- has harbored some of popular culture's fiercest detractors, many of whom in the post-World War II decades persistently linked it with fascism. Today, however, the conflict between elite self-expression and pop fare looks increasingly like a put-up job by critics short of attention. One hundred years from now, such high-low distinctions will be virtually forgotten: what is great art, whether Michelangelo's frescoes for the Sistine Chapel or Cole Porter's song ''Night and Day,'' but art whose excitement time can sometimes sanitize but never destroy, art that has kept its sex appeal? In 2096, Wole Soyinka's memoir of his early life in Nigeria, ''Ake: The Years of Childhood,'' for example, and Michael Herr's dazzlingly dark account of Vietnam, ''Dispatches,'' will find readers among the fans of Stephen King's ''Shining'' and ''Misery,'' while Martin Scorsese's films of urban life and Lee Tamahori's shattering portrait of Maori life in Auckland, New Zealand, ''Once Were Warriors,'' will share Web space with the works of Carl Dreyer and Cezanne.
 Although a new distance has indeed opened up between the realms of high and low art in the last century, it has been less the result of growing mass indifference to the finer things of life than of the creation, for the first time in history, of small but affluent elites capable of sustaining art that never found, or no longer attracts, a mass audience. More ''high'' art, not less, stays in circulation today, and it does so precisely because of the technology responsible for mass culture.
 In any case, the vast majority of artists we revere today were wildly successful in their own time. True, Melville and Dickinson had to wait half a century for their audiences, but Sophocles, Shakespeare, Handel, Dickens, Balzac, Verdi, Stowe and Twain were among the most popular artists of their day. Some American fare once deemed pop, like the music of Louis Armstrong and Irving Berlin, has already achieved canonical, even classic, status, proving itself capable of quickening our deepest appetites for pleasure and rigor.
 For almost a century now, mass art has been among the United States' most reliable exports, and while high art has usually been Europeanbased and white, American pop art's makers have been ethnically and racially varied. People always ask of art a glimpse of their own faces in the mirror, but who has the power to command pro-tracted mirror time has been shifting for most of this century. W. E. B. Du Bois's famous declaration in 1903 that ''the problem of the 20th century is the problem of the color line'' has already been borne out by the recent history of civil rights and immigration. By 2015 much of the world's population will be gathered in megalopolises like Bombay, Lagos, Shanghai, Jakarta and Mexico City. By 2050, 50 percent of the population of the United States will be ''minority groups'' -- a phrase that increasingly smacks of wishful thinking.
 The high-low division in the English language -- between the version spoken by the privileged Anglo-American elite and that spoken by the lower classes and by immigrant or colonial outsiders -- isn't holding up any better than it is anywhere else. The most striking thing about the literature in English of the last half century is the appearance of successful nonwhite writers, including Toni Morrison, Louise Erdrich, the Nuyorican Cafe poets and the ghetto rappers, and, abroad, non-English writers writing in English like George Lamming, Chinua Achebe and Salman Rushdie, all from former British colonies. The version of English that inspires many of them is not classic British English but its far more recent branch, American English, less for its actual linguistic habits than for the example it sets. The process of reinvention by which Americans, particularly black Americans, have created their own form of English has direct relevance for new English speakers elsewhere.
 As a participant in the Bandung Conference of 1955, a historic meeting of representatives from 29 Asian and African nations, Richard Wright was exhilarated to discover that English was the language of choice and foresaw a fresh chapter in its ''long and glorious history.'' Wright believed that older English speakers suffered from ''a genius for calling things by the wrong names,'' an inability to recognize ''actions initiated by someone else.'' So what Westerners called ''adventure'' and the spread, or defense, of ''civilization'' was also, from the perspective of those they conquered or controlled, slavery and exploitation. In Wright's view, only the once-colonized newcomers to English in the United States and elsewhere possessed the antidote to this malady; they alone could close the gap between the language's promise and its performance by opening its bold declarations of human rights to those the original speakers always tacitly excluded, those they subjugated and colonized.
 Wright's notion of a language disorder finds fresh evidence in contemporary modes of self-definition. While officially abandoning much of the self-glorifying language Wright found so suspect, we have not yet found alternative terms to describe who or where we are.
 What happened to that bold Logos of an era, the ''generation''? After the glamour of the ''Lost Generation'' of the 1920's and the hip cachet of the ''Beats'' of the 1950's, we descended to the ''Baby Boomers'' of the 1960's and 1970's. So far no one has come up with a better term for today's youth than ''Generation X.'' When we do define ourselves and our era, it is a bewildering matter of identity by prefix, an affair of ''posts'' and ''neos,'' as in ''post-industrial,'' ''post-modern,'' ''post-colonial,'' ''post-Marxist'' and ''post-Freudian,'' or ''neo-Marxist,'' ''neo-Freudian,'' ''neocolonial,'' ''neoconservative'' and ''neoliberal.'' The prefix craze has affinities with Hollywood's penchant for sequels and spinoffs. The past is clearer, and more bankable, than the present. We're the folks who come after the moderns, the colonialists, the Marxists and the Freudians. Something happened between the time of the ''modern'' T. S. Eliot and that of the ''post-modern'' Thomas Pynchon, but we can't seem to bring ourselves to say what precisely has changed, who exactly we now are. Why this hesitation?
 It's hard for people to understand, much less title, a show in which they have only a bit part, especially if they are accustomed to playing the lead. They don't see the new leads as stars, but as usurpers, and quite likely inadequate to the task. For the traditionally elite group of white Euro-Americans, the ''post'' syndrome is a way of keeping the cultural spotlight beamed on those they come after -- those stunningly iconic, apparently invincible, earlier members of their own group -- while keeping in obscurity those who now threaten to succeed, redefine and in the process displace them. 
 Non-Hispanic whites still control the crucial powers of naming and defining in our society, and the definitions they put forth, while implicitly claiming universal applicability, in fact cover whites only. The black generation of the 1920's was not ''lost,'' nor did African-American artists of the 1950's see themselves as ''Beat.'' What relevance, if any, does the term ''Baby Boomer'' have for African-Americans, Latinos, Asian-Americans or Native Americans? Predictably, intellectuals have titled today's show after their own absence. ''Post'' sounds self-deprecating, but sometimes it means nothing more than ''post me,'' ''post us,'' a kind of apres moi le deluge mentality, a civilized shirking before the coming menace of unnameable formlessness.
 In fact, what comes ''post us'' is not formless or unnameable, but new and charged, like the face of a stranger startling us from daydreams in a crowded space. Nor does the ''post us'' really exclude us. Shakespeare, Mozart and Melville are safe, safer perhaps than they might wish to be, even as George Wolfe's peerless black tap extravaganza, ''Bring In Da Noise, Bring In Da Funk,'' already preserved on video at Lincoln Center, and Audre Lorde's brilliant memoir, ''Zami: A New Spelling of My Name,'' join them in our esteem. Although white Euro-Americans can no longer monopolize the naming process, they, too, are ethnics; whatever groups hold global and national power in the future, their world will still in part depend on the astonishing achievements of their white Western predecessors.
 On medieval maps, the Americas were labeled ''Terra Incognita.'' In the half millennium since, they have been the place where people of all races and nationalities have collided, thrown upon the kindness or cruelty of strangers. Isn't it time that we got to know ourselves? It's not about ''posts'' and ''neos'' or low versus high. It's about the Race Era, about global economic policies that bring feast to a few and famine to many, about new forms of language and electronic communication. On line accommodates work by geniuses and hacks alike. Anyone can be an author now, and it looks increasingly as if everyone will be. ''People are always seeking self-expression,'' wrote C. L. R. James, the Trinidadian-born historian and critic, ''and where they find it, they stay.''

 Its only message -- cats are lovable -- is similarly generic and universal, and pace T. S. Eliot, comes through even if you can't make out the words. Whatever else progress brings in the millennium, it is unlikely to rescue unborn generations from the musical flypaper that is ''Memory.''

Vincent Canby
Theater columnist,
The New York Times

Glengarry Glen Ross, David Mamet, 1984
It seems possible that by 2096 people will think in the sound bytes of computerese, and the formerly live theater will be devoted to electronic, visceral, virtually real spectacles staged in gigantic amphitheaters. Standing apart from this scene and hectoring heroically, a small but influential group of fanatics will be keeping alive America's late-20th-century Theater of Language as represented by the works of David Mamet. The faithful will crowd tiny halls to watch and be confounded by such stark, mysteriously plain plays as ''American Buffalo,'' ''Glengarry Glen Ross'' and ''The Cryptogram.'' Audiences in 2096 won't easily understand Mr. Mamet, who will seem as demanding and rich as Shakespeare is for us today, and far more remote.

MOVING IMAGE

Stanley Cavell
Professor of philosophy,
Harvard University

Groundhog Day, directed by Harold Ramis, 1993
A small film that lives off its wits and tells a deeply wonderful story of love. It creates a version of the question I ask here -- of what will endure. Its version is to ask how, surrounded by conventions we do not exactly believe in, we sometimes find it in ourselves to enter into what Emerson thought of as a new day.

Janet Maslin
Chief film critic,
The New York Times

The Godfather, directed by Francis Coppola, 1972
This parable will say as much about the future as it does about the past. Narrative methods may shift to accommodate the shortened attention spans of tomorrow's movie audiences -- but great story telling, realistic acting and painterly visual compositions won't go out of style.

Stephen Holden
Culture critic and
reporter, The New York Times

Star Wars, directed by George Lucas, 1977
George Lucas's fantasy epic Star Wars'' ushered in the era of the space-age blockbuster, in which movies began seeing themselves as intergalactic pop myths featuring heroes who embodied a computer-perfected man-machine ideal. Its story provided an all-purpose term to label a fearsome enemy (''the evil empire'') and its screenplay came up with a mystical pop prayer (''May the force be with you'') that was at once supernatural and secular. Not simply multicultural, its futuristic vision of life in the universe might better be described as multiglobal. Movies would never again be so earthbound.

David O. Russell
Writer and director (''Spanking the
Monkey,'' ''Flirting
With Disaster'')

Network, directed by Sidney Lumet, 1976
Apart from the unrealistic and sentimental sequence of everyone shouting ''I'm mad as hell,'' etc., the film is a stunningly accurate portrait of a media conglomerate that was considered broadly satirical in 1976 but hardly seems so today. Who, 20 years ago, would ever have imagined half-hour infomercials or round-the-clock murder trials and tabloid talk shows? By 2096, ''Network'' will be viewed as almost quaint in scale, but a historic cinematic marker of the early days of the media-benumbed culture, when the possibility of an alternative still seemed remotely imaginable.

Eric Bogosian
Writer, performer
and actor

Taxi Driver, directed by Martin Scorsese, 1976
As we approach the millennium and New York City plummets into order and cleanliness, crime-free and cool, we also say goodbye to a sordid and very photogenic era in our city's history. That's why in centuries to come, ''Taxi Driver'' will be the work of art most often examined, not only for its garish, expressionistic version of the Big Apple but for the teeming madness inhabiting its residents. Scorsese, Schrader and De Niro had the guts to reveal the urban male hunger for blondes, hatred of blacks and love of guns. And not incidentally, because that's how these things happen, this film brought together a collection of artists at their peak to create an awkward film that is one of the great works of art of the late 20th century.

WRITTEN WORD

Cynthia Ozick
Novelist and essayist

Humboldt's Gift, Saul Bellow, 1975
A hundred years from now, E-mail and the Internet will no doubt be obsolete; the cellular phone and the fax will be as dead as a doornail; but Saul Bellow's mind and language -- never. Even when the dust of our most advanced laptops has begun to interest the archeologists of the future, Augie March and Herzog and Tommy Wilhelm and Henderson will still be swarming with turbulent life, familiarly bound in portable paper.

Michiko Kakutani
Senior book critic,
The New York Times

White Noise, Don DeLillo, 1985
When ''White Noise'' was published, it read like an apocalyptic satire. Sure, it was a dazzling, virtuosic meditation on death and the terrors of ordinary life, but it was also a darkly comic sendup of a futuristic America, tottering on the brink of extinction. Today the novel's characters -- scholars who specialize in Elvis and cereal boxes, ashram dwellers in Montana, doctors who dispense drugs that promise to alleviate the fear of death -- have grown decidedly more recognizable. Who knows, by 2096 the novel may be read as a grimly naturalistic portrait of millennial America, an America in which people turn to cults and obsessions and conspiracy theories in a desperate effort to lend a sense of order to their lives, an America in which everyone
is mesmerized by the ''sealed-off, timeless, self-contained, self-referring narcotic of television, and ''everything we need that is not food or love is here in the tabloid racks.''

Luc Sante
Author

Gravity's Rainbow, Thomas Pynchon, 1973
Just as Homer was vital through the Dark Ages not just for his poetry but because his epics served as encyclopedias of how life had once been lived, instructing benighted readers on how to conduct wedding feasts or equip sailing ships, so ''Gravity's Rainbow'' will be the totem of a sort of cargo cult. Readers of the future, starved and all but cretinized by merchandising and technology and religion and entertainment, figuratively if not literally living in caves, will dissect it for clues, refer to it like an almanac, plan their lives on its basis, imagine they could reconstruct the 20th century from its pages.

FASHION

Anne-Marie Schiro
Fashion reporter,
The New York Times

The miniskirt
The miniskirt. You can't kill it. Designers tried in the 70's, they tried in the 80's and they're trying yet again in the 90's. They'll probably try again in 2006. But it will prevail because women like it.

Amy M. Spindler
Fashion critic,
The New York Times

The logo T-shirt
Whether it was a ''Star Wars'' T-shirt in the 70's, a preppy alligator, the Polo/Ralph Lauren shirt of the 80's or the Adidas and Nike hip-hop symbology of the 90's, the logo T-shirt represents what fashion historians fear most: the liberalization of fashion. No effort need be made to decode the messages here, because the wearers are willing to (literally, in some cases) wear their identities on their sleeves. Before the heat transfer T-shirt that exploded in the 70's, there was nothing remotely similar in fashion to this desire to be read like a bumper sticker by strangers. After all, what decade but the 70's could bring us such sentiments as ''I'm With Stupid''? What decade but the acquisitive 80's could lead to ''My Grandparents Went to Hawaii but All I Got Was This Lousy T-shirt''? The messages of the heat transfer T-shirt, and the logo T-shirt that followed, made one of the most important movements of the 90's possible. That was the growth of the Gap, which with its plain white pocket T-shirt sent a message just by lacking a distinctive message. Deliberately not telegraphing a message was briefly believed to be the most individualistic dress of all. Until, of course, everyone was wearing white pocket T-shirts.

Holly Brubach
Style editor, The New
York Times Magazine

Destroyed dresses,
Rei Kawakubo, 1983
The punk propensity for safety pins, the torn clothing that Rei Kawakubo presented at Comme des Garcons, the conscientiously torn-apart tattered garments of Martin Margiela and Xuly-Bet all represent a break with the longstanding tradition of dressing a woman to make her look attractive. Beauty as an ideal to which women universally aspire was being deposed, and in its place there arose an alternative notion: of fashion as a form of pure expression -- capable of conveying anger, protest, the mood of a particular moment -- and freed from its former obligations. Such was the climate in which these statements were made that they were seen as weirdly attractive, and at times more powerful than the assiduous everyday efforts of women intent on ingratiating themselves to the world.

GRAPHIC: Photos: The CanonWhat works created in the late 20th century will still be discussed, viewed, read and cherished 100 years from now? The New York Times Magazine asked experts in various disciplines to speculate. Here are their answers.VISUAL ARTSMichael KimmelmanChief art critic,The New York TimesRunning Fence,Christo, 1976The late 20th century is dominated by Conceptualism and its offshoots, and Christo is, let's face it, its most public exponent. He has found a way literally to package art for the masses, and though his wrapped monuments and fabric fences are already gone, their afterimage will exist in the postcards, films, documents, drawings and other memorabilia that were integral to his Rube Goldberg schemes. Posterity will remember him for mixing marketing smarts with dollops of politics and populism: a quintessential late-20th-century cocktail.Robert RosenblumProfessor of modernEuropean art,Institute of Fine Arts, New York UniversityThe Singing Sculpture, Gilbert & George, 1971Gallery visitors saw a poignant, magical image of two artists forever linked to each other and to their art as they simulated with robotic precision, hour after hour, day after day, a music-hall performance to the heartbreaking sounds of that old British song about the homeless of another era, ''Underneath the Arches.'' Visitors also saw a quantum leap in the tonic shuffle of painting, sculpture and the artists' corporeal presence, a mix that opened endless vistas for younger generations.Robert StorrCurator, Museumof Modern Art,New YorkOne Hundred Live and Die, Bruce Nauman,1984The piece consists of 100 phrases in neon that refer to our ordinary desires, needs, appetites, fears and bodily functions. On the left side, 50 nouns or verbs are paired with the word ''live''
on the right side, the same 50 are coupled with the word ''die.'' Read one way the phrases register as simple statements -- these are just the things we do. Read another way the phrases sound imperative -- these things we can't help but do. Whether habit or confusion, they define human nature at its most basic, suspended between life and death.Arthur DantoArt critic, The Nation Robert Irwin, 1992The California artist Robert Irwin would almost certainly qualify as a great artist. The Pace Gallery in SoHo was given over in its entirety to a work called in 1992. The work consisted of three scrims, floor to ceiling and wall to wall, illuminated by violet, green and orange fluorescent lights at the ceiling, creating uniformly illuminated spaces in just those colors. People who moved through those spaces were perceived as silhouettes and disembodied voices through the scrims. It was uncannily beautiful and transformative.George SteinerFellow of ChurchillCollege, Cambridge University, EnglandA photograph of one of Tinguely's ''self-destructs'' burning to the ground. The haughty, elegant construct tumbles to ash. It was an ephemeral happening. It mocked time and the millennial Western esthetic of survival for art and artist. The photograph, itself a ''negative'' and shadow, is its only witness. Each of these gravely playful motifs will, come 2096, seem wholly prophetic. The smile endures, not the cat.MUSICAllan KozinnMusic critic,The New York TimesEinstein on the Beach, Philip Glass, 1976Glass's music thoroughly reflects the energy, malaise, iconoclasm and quirky humor of our times. Look at ''Einstein on the Beach.'' Ostensibly an opera, it replaces the orchestra with an electronic, keyboard-based quasi-rock band, gives rhythm primacy over melody and abandons plot in favor of either weirdly connected or entirely disconnected imagery. What could be more late 20th century?Peter WatrousMusic critic,The New York TimesCiti Movement Wynton Marsalis, 1993He is the man who oversaw jazz's re-emergence as a mainstream form during the 1980's, helped design the largest institutional jazz program in the country and made his gleaming virtuosity as a trumpeter an adjunct to his grand compositional sensibility. He has made jazz worth arguing about. He has guaranteed its vitality and given generations of young jazz musicians a goal.Jon ParelesChief pop critic,The New York TimesBurnin', Bob Marley and the Wailers, 1973Bob Marley became the voice of third-world pain and resistance, the sufferer in the concrete jungle who would not be denied forever. Outsiders everywhere heard Marley as their own champion
if he could make himself heard, so could they, without compromises. In 2096, when the former third world has overrun and colonized the former superpowers, Marley will be commemorated as a saint.Greil MarcusArt and popular- music criticNever Mind the Bollocks Here's theSex Pistols, 1977This Sex Pistols' album will be around a hundred years from now because the heretical tradition to which it belongs -- a tradition based on the notion of the transcendence of sin, and since the 19th century intertwined with the tradition of the avant-garde -- is so old one can fairly bet it, too, will be around. It's a tradition that guards its talismans like rocs' eggs.PERFORMING ARTSBen BrantleyChief drama critic,The New York TimesJoe Turner's Come and Gone, August Wilson, 1986A good ear for dramatic dialogue isn't a recording device. As much as anything else, it's the ability to turn vernacular speech into poetry that makes a playwright endure. This is the great gift of August Wilson, who has chronicled the black experience in America through a series of plays of both substance and lyricism. They can err on the side of overstatement, but greatness and perfection seldom go hand in hand.Frank RichOp-Ed columnist,The New York TimesCats, Andrew Lloyd Webber, 1981This musical's ad slogan, ''Now and forever,'' is not a gag, after all. If Puccini has survived for a century, why shouldn't Andrew Lloyd Webber, who dumbed down Puccini for the easy-listening audiences of our own time? Once its Broadway and West End runs end, ''Cats'' will be adopted by every amateur theatrical troupe in the world
 here's a show that requires only junk for a set and can be performed by casts of any size, age, shape, sex, ethnicity or ability.

SECTION: Section 5; Page 4; Column 5; Travel Desk 

LENGTH: 1183 words

HEADLINE: PRACTICAL TRAVELER;
Air-Crash Pay: Limits Reviewed

BYLINE: By BETSY WADE 

 BODY:
MUCH of the fine print on the back of an air ticket concerns the Warsaw Convention, a 1929 agreement that governs many aspects of international flights, particularly liability in the event of a fatal accident.
In 1966, the top liability limit was set at $75,000, but with time and inflation, the figure has become unrealistically low. Efforts to raise it by amending the Warsaw Convention have failed in Congress, which could not agree to ratify changes. 
 But award limits may be removed by another means, as early as Nov. 1, if the Department of Transportation approves plans submitted to it by a group of world airlines.
As it exists now, the $75,000 limit does not apply if the airline can be proved grossly negligent, a provision that is the source of tortuous trials. Some relatives of passengers lost in the downing of Korean Air Lines Flight 007 when it strayed into Russian air space and was shot down in 1983 still have cases in court. Others received $75,000. This history means the limit is acutely in mind as the investigation of the crash of Trans World Airlines Flight 800 on July 17 goes forward.
If effective Nov. 1, the new policy would not be retroactive, meaning that the T.W.A. liability limit would remain at $75,000 a passenger. But experts say T.W.A. might find it easier to waive that limit than to explain how three months could make such a difference in the bereaved families' financial well-being. John M. McDonald, a spokesman for T.W.A., said that the line could not comment on this possibility so long as the cause of the crash remained under investigation.
Virtually all relatives of air-crash victims file lawsuits, according to Gerard R. Lear of Speiser, Krause, Madole & Lear in Washington and Lee S. Kreindler of Kreindler & Kreindler in New York, two lawyers expert in the area. Sometimes, Mr. Lear said, the airline's insurance company will offer a settlement of $75,000 or more even before a legal filing, but Mr. Lear said that a plaintiff still needed a lawsuit to guard against expiration of the two-year statute of limitations after which no suit can be filed.
As many as 30 percent of the cases actually go to trial to prove damages, usually based on income lost to the families, if airline offers are considered too low.
 
 
 
A Move Toward Change
 With efforts to amend the Warsaw Convention foundering, about two years ago, most of the world's airlines took steps toward ending the ceiling and letting passengers' families collect any damages they could prove they were entitled to in court, which can be as high as several million dollars. The awards might come through court trials, or through settlements as trials approached, but in either case, this would avert protracted proceedings over gross negligence, technically called "willful misconduct." The Department of Transportation, in deploring such lengthy trials, said that they created "particular hardship for those most in need of prompt compensatory awards."
Mr. Kreindler said that the airlines began to see that such protracted disputes were damaging their reputations and creating ill will. The long suits are also costly to the airlines in dollars. Indeed, Hans Ephraimson-Abt of Ridgewood, N.J., whose daughter died in the downing of K.A.L. 007, said he believed that the Korean airline's legal costs now exceeded the amount it would have paid in settlements of all sorts. Mr. Ephraimson-Abt, who has not yet received any money in the case, which is still pending in New York, frequently acts as a spokesman for victims' families.
Discussions have been held by the airlines, the aerospace industry, the Government, the American Trial Lawyers Association, the airlines' underwriters and the families of victims, with the United States Department of Transportation granting the airlines exemption from antitrust prosecution for the purpose.
Late last year, the airlines agreed upon the goal of removing limits at a meeting of the International Air Transport Association in Kuala Lumpur, Malaysia. At the end of July, the statements prepared by this organization were submitted to the Department of Transportation for approval. At this writing, 56 world airlines have signed the declaration of intention.
Transportation Secretary Federico Pena said the agreements would be reviewed carefully to be sure they met the goal of "reforming a system that has taken far too long to fix."
 
 
 
Removing the Ceiling
 The nub of the agreements removes all ceilings from awards made by courts, or settlements approved by courts, on the basis of damages proved by the plaintiffs: loss of a wage earner's support, for example. A ceiling of $146,000 is established in cases where the airline demonstrates that it is wholly blameless.
Four organizations have commented on the plan. The Aerospace Industries Association, which opposed Congressional amendment of the Warsaw Convention, supports this, as does the International Chamber of Commerce. The Association of Trial Lawyers of America would prefer that the United States withdraw from the Warsaw Convention, but supports approval of the plan as a step forward. The Victims Families' Associations considers the new proposals inadequate. It finds the $146,000 ceiling too low, and most of all, it wants American citizens and residents always to be able to take their cases against foreign airlines to American courts, and this is not embodied in the new proposals.
The Warsaw Convention lists four jurisdictions where airlines may be sued: the domicile or the main place of business of the carrier -- usually the same -- the place where the ticket was sold, or the destination of the trip. Since most international tickets are for round trips, the families association says, the destination of the trip is likely to be the place where the ticket was bought.
American companies can always be sued in American courts, the families association points out, but this is not true of foreign carriers, which resist responding in American courts, and do not need to under the Warsaw Convention if the ticket was bought in another country for a round trip to a foreign city. The trial lawyers' wish to abandon the Warsaw Convention arises from their eagerness for a new "fifth jurisdiction," the residence of the passenger.
Mr. Ephraimson-Abt outlines several situations where an American family could not sue in the United States. In one, a college student studying in Europe is seized with homesickness at Christmas and buys a round-trip ticket on a foreign airline to fly home for the holidays and is killed en route. The Warsaw Convention would not require this case to go to an American court.
A spokesman for the Department of Transportation would not comment on whether it was going to approve the agreement or what it would do about airlines that do not accept it. In the past airlines that have not signed international airline agreements have been told that they may not land in the United States, or that if they do land, they will be treated as if they had signed the agreements anyway.
