HEADLINE: FRUGAL TRAVELER;
Rolling Through Amish Country

BYLINE: By SARAH FERRELL; SARAH FERRELL is the associate editor of The Sophisticated Traveler, a part 2 of The New York Times Magazine.

 BODY:
WHAT better way to end the summer than with a little trip to someplace green and bountiful? Rural Pennsylvania, around and beyond the city of Lancaster, sounded good to my husband, Tom, and me. In high season, though, it was going to be difficult to do on the cheap: we were too late to book an inexpensive farm stay (and I'm not sure I'd be crazy about a place that invited me to help with the chores) and the less expensive motels were mostly on U.S. 30, a garish strip of factory outlets and fast-food restaurants that we had been warned to avoid at all cost. In near desperation I called a discount hotel broker, Accommodations Express, of Atlantic City, which lists the Pennsylvania Dutch country among its specialties.
After lengthy consultation, the helpful young woman at Accommodations Express suggested the Historic Strasburg Inn, a few miles east of Lancaster. The price she quoted, $90 a night for three midweek nights, seemed stiff, but was well in line with the rates for standard motels and inns in the area. Since the place sounded good, we decided to economize in other ways. 
 Our major economy was bypassing name car rental companies in favor of Better Buggy of Brooklyn. For $49.95 a day, with 100 free daily miles, we were issued a dented Mitsubishi with more than 80,000 miles on the odometer. In any parking lot, it would be the last car to be stolen.
The Historic Strasburg Inn turns out to be charming, a series of one- and two-story brick or wood buildings containing 101 rooms, set among cornfields on 58 acres. As we get out of the car, the silence is broken by the cooing of mourning doves and a far-off train whistle. Our room, No. 107, has a king-sized bed, reproduction Colonial furniture and hand-crocheted runners on the dresser and bedside tables; the small bathroom has a combination tub and shower. It is cozy, at the price we are paying; it would be cramped at the $145 rate posted on the door.
We check out the recreational facilities, among them a horseshoe pitch, free bicycles (one a tandem) and a meadow just right for playing with the Frisbee that we have brought with us, then inspect the dining possibilities. The a la carte listings at the inn's Washington House Restaurant are fairly steep, but the adjoining By George Tavern has a reasonably priced menu of "lite fare." At the small bar, we order a couple of Yuengling's Black and Tan beers (a fragrant brew made in Pottsville, Pa., $2.50 on draft) and chat with some enthusiastic Australians who are completing a long tour of Canada and the United States. Above our dinner table, next to the door, hangs a portrait and autograph of King George I, one of 11 such pictures and documents that give the tavern its name. We both order the lump crabcake, which is large and delicious and, at $11.95, one of the most expensive options. We have a glass of wine apiece, and the bill, which comes to about $33, persuades us that we can afford to dine here regularly.
After the generous "Continental-plus" breakfast of cereals, breads, fruit and juices that is included in the price of the room, we set off on a drive on the back roads of the Amish country, passing fields of corn and tobacco. Immaculately white barns and silos rise above a morning mist, and we pass a one-room Amish school letting out for recess. We slow down for smart gray buggies, drawn by shiny, high-stepping horses, and are able to identify Amish farmhouses by their green roller blinds, the only window covering, we have read, permitted by stricter orders. Every so often we pass a farm stand, apparently run on the honor system, since there is no one in attendance.
Our destination is the town of Intercourse and the People's Place, a complex aligned with, but not owned or sponsored by, the Mennonite Church. The $3.50 audio-visual presentation is called "Who Are the Amish?" The short answer is that they are an Anabaptist group, stricter in observance than their Mennonite and Brethren cousins, who emigrated from Switzerland at the end of the 17th century in the face of religious persecution. They are thriving; their population in Lancaster County has doubled in the last two decades, to about 18,000, and there is now such a shortage of farms that young men have had to turn to other ways of making a living, especially woodworking (yielding an abundance of roadside displays of whirligigs and gazebos).
The People's Place Quilt Museum is across the street, upstairs from the Old Country Store. A $4 fee admits us to an exhibit of 24 late-19th- and early 20th-century Mennonite quilts, family pieces, with documentation about their makers and recipients. All of them are stunners, as are the several fashionable high Victorian wedding dresses on display -- young women did not usually join the church and take on plain ways until after they were married. One of my favorite quilts, a Tulip Rooster pattern (so called because the principal motif can be read as either), is also the most poignant, since it was made by a dying young mother as a keepsake for her daughter. Downstairs, in the back of the store, plainly dressed women are shopping for quilt fabrics.
I have a specific mission in Kitchen Kettle Village, a complex of food and craft shops. My grandmother, who grew up in this part of the world, used to make pickled watermelon rind, and I am in search of some. The Jam and Relish Kitchen, which makes more than 60 sweets and condiments, seems like a good bet. A savory scent comes from the actual kitchen, where young Amish women are cooking up a batch of barbecue sauce. There are old-fashioned staples like rhubarb jam, and such newer ones as jalapeno jelly. Watermelon pickle is $4.99 for 19 ounces. The young woman who rings up my purchase wears a prayer cap and a simple, solid-colored dress, closed with straight pins -- buttons and hooks-and-eyes are too fancy for the Amish.
Time for a late lunch. We have heard that Stoltzfus Farm Restaurant, just down the road, is particularly authentic and, at $12.95 a person, well within budget. There is no menu -- you just sit down at the table and, before you've finished reading the grace printed on the paper place mats, they start bringing you food, all of it homemade and in unlimited quantities: whole wheat bread and tender white potato bread, applesauce, apple butter, pepper cabbage (somewhere between cole slaw and sauerkraut), a terrific chow-chow, sausage, crisp fried chicken and smoky ham loaf, with buttered noodles, string beans, corn and candied sweet potatoes. This is the food that Tom and I both grew up on and have avoided for years. Silly us -- it is delicious. We limit ourselves to one dessert apiece, shoo-fly pie. Without ice cream.
WE finish our touring day at the Strasburg Railroad, one of several attractions that make the town a mecca for anyone who has even the slightest interest in trains. There, we board a bright yellow open observation car for the 45-minute, nine-mile round trip to the junction at Paradise. Our tickets are $8.50 each, $1 more than for the standard coach, and it is worth it for the cool breeze and the views of pristine farmland. We are drawn by engine No. 90, a behemoth of a former freight locomotive that chuffs and steams most satisfactorily. Three little Amish boys wave to us, and every so often, a cloud of white fritillaries takes to the air beside the track. Back at the depot, we spend another 50 cents to visit the Paradise, a private car built in 1916 and fitted out with wood marquetry, that was used by Harry S. Truman in his 1948 whistle stop campaign.
The following day brings more trains, at the Railroad Museum of Pennsylvania. The $6 fee admits us to the enormous Hall of Rolling Stock, with some 50 pieces of equipment that range from the sort-of-cute (a replica of the early steam locomotive John Bull) to the downright awe-inspiring (the 220-ton Berkshire from 1943). We are in time for a free tour of the outside yard, where about 35 other engines and cars are in various stages of restoration, at the end of which we are astonished to discover that we have been here for three hours.
Moving from the large and serious to the small and frivolous, we drive on to Choo Choo Barn and Traintown, U.S.A., which is a 1,700-square-foot display of miniature trains and trams and cars and ski lifts and Heaven only knows what all, that run through a miniature Lancaster County. There are 17 trains, and many dioramas, some of them animated, with parades, a baseball game, a Boy Scout camp, a circus and an Amish barn-raising, to name just a few. There is also a car crash, a fire and a funeral (non-animated). It costs $4, but you get a lot to look at.
We lunch at Isaac's Deli, part of a Pennsylvania chain with a neon-and-flamingo Miami decor. Since this is Traintown, however, this particular Isaac's also has a room done up as a dining car. Most of the sandwiches are, for some reason, named for birds. Tom orders a hoopoe -- tuna, cheese, bacon and tomato -- while I settle for a sampler of salads. With many refills of iced tea, the bill comes to $14.
Then it's off to Lititz, an old Moravian town about a half-hour's drive away. Lititz is home to the Julius Sturgis Pretzel House, perhaps the oldest pretzel factory in the nation. The tour costs $1.50, and we are each handed a large pretzel as our ticket of admission. At the beginning of our visit, our guide gives us hunks of dough and instructs us in pretzel-twisting. We slowly produce reasonable looking specimens. Then, after a tour of the hellishly hot hard pretzel bakery, we watch a professional pretzel twister forming soft pretzels, with what looks like a single motion, faster than the eye can follow. We're glad we made our faltering efforts first.
We follow the scent of chocolate to the Wilbur Chocolate Company's Candy Americana Museum (free), where we watch grandmotherly women hand-dip chocolates and inspect the old molds and implements and, best of all, a whole wall of antique chocolate pots from all over the world. The attached shop is itself something of a museum with, in addition to chocolates, penny candies of all description, including many (licorice pipes!) that I haven't seen since childhood.
At the General Sutter Inn on Main Street, named for the California pioneer who died here and is buried in the Moravian cemetery, we pick up a free walking tour map published by the Lititz Historical Society. Brochure in hand, we stroll along Main Street, which is lined with 18th-century stone houses. We make a short stop to see Sutter's grave (we are former Californians), then find that we are back where we started, at the pretzel bakery, largely unchanged since 1784.
Back in Strasburg, the bar is teeming with people from Buffalo, who have come for a couple of days in the Amish country and to attend a performance of a biblical epic called "Noah," with 300 animals, 100 live and 200 animated. We had considered the show, but $31 a ticket was too much -- although it works out to little more than 10 cents an animal. On a farewell stroll around the grounds, we notice the faintly shimmering lights of Route 30 in the far distance.
Confusion awaits us on check-out. We are billed for $99 a night, then $89 after I produce my voucher from Accommodations Express and a few phone calls are made. So how much does this room really cost? The answer is multitiered: $109 on a weeknight, $119 on a weekend and $149 for a holiday weekend. We have profited from Accommodations Express's apparent error and have gotten a bargain after all.
We have one final errand as we head home. We are under strict injunction from a friend to stop in Lebanon, to sample the opera fudge at Wertz Candies. Wertz is a third-generation family operation, and by now no one knows why this confection is so named. It bears no resemblance to fudge; rather it is a cream filling, lightly touched with vanilla, coated with pure chocolate liquor (from Wilbur's, as it happens). The impact of black-bitter and silky-sweet is tremendous, and at $8.50 a pound the candy represents the best buy of our trip. At the Farmer's Market we buy a dollar's worth of Lebanon bologna, which, smoky, peppery and vinegary, is to bologna what opera fudge is to fudge.
Lebanon is a good place to end the trip. With its boarded-up downtown movie theater, it is a needed corrective to where we've been, a way station between Vacationland and real life.
 
The bottom line in Lancaster County
 
Logistics
Our chief expenses were lodging, at $89 a night plus tax, and the rental car, which worked out to $327.80 for four days and 112 extra miles, at 25 cents a mile. Gas was $18 more. Admissions and tickets were $28 each. Food ranged from about $22 to $30 a person a day; pretzels, fudge and beer added about $25 to the total.

HEADLINE: Connecticut Q&A: Mario T. Gaboury;
Raising a Voice for the Victims of Crime

BYLINE: By GITTA MORRIS 

 BODY:
THE victims' rights movement in Connecticut is at a critical point in its evolution says Mario T. Gaboury, director of the Center for the Study of Crime Victims' Rights, Remedies and Resources at the University of New Haven where he is also an associate professor criminal justice.
The center was established in 1994 to provide training in victim services administration as well as to work for victims' rights. The center also recently held a conference in North Haven for professionals who help crime victims. 
 Mr. Gaboury explained that the victims' rights movement found its roots in the political and social turmoil of the 1960's. He said the following decade saw an alliance between diverse political and social groups that focused on victims' rights because victims were being left out of the system. Next came a period of rapid enactment of thousands of statutes that had little provision for enforcement.
In his office recently Mr. Gaboury discussed the third phase of the victims' movement, the enactment of a state constitutional amendment that would strengthen the laws and provide enforcement mechanism. Following are excerpts from the conversation.
Q. Who are the proponents for victims' rights?
A. Historically the victims' movement has been nonpartisan or bipartisan and brought together in its earliest days people from the law and order movements and people who had very liberal views from the women's movement and civil rights movement. The first Federal victims' rights statute, the Federal Witness Protection Act of 1982, was co-sponsored by Strom Thurmond and Ted Kennedy. You now have Bill Clinton and Bob Dole both supporting a Federal Constitutional amendment for victims. The same thing occurred here in Connecticut where both Democrats and Republicans, liberals and conservatives said, this is an issue we can agree upon.
Q. Both the House and Senate of the General Assembly have passed a victims' rights amendment to the state constitution, and it will be a referendum question on the November ballot. Why is it needed?
A. Defendants have a very well developed constitutionally-based set of rights. If the victim tries to assert his or her rights to attend court proceedings and to participate in the system, the defendant says, that would unduly influence the jury and create a prejudicial attitude against me, and my rights are superior because they are constitutionally based. So the problem is one of equity. The only way to put victims on equal footing is to put them into state and Federal constitutions. The constitutional amendment first puts victims on par with defendants. Around 21 states have passed such amendments.
Q. What kind of enforcement will there be?
A. This amendment lays the groundwork to provide an enforcement mechanism so if victims don't get their rights they will have some recourse. We're going to have to decide which agencies monitor what goes on. I advocate the establishment of a victims' ombudsperson's office -- several states have them -- to police the judicial, the prosecutor, the law enforcement, the corrections agencies that are responsible for according victims' rights.
Q. Isn't the language of this amendment still too vague to have real clout?
A. It is exactly the kind of language you find in the constitutional-based defendant's rights: A defendant shall not be subject to unreasonable search and seizure. A warrant shall not be issued except upon probable cause. You establish a principle. How these words come to have meaning is through court decisions that take particular cases or controversies that have facts behind them. It's an evolutionary process. You don't just pass a constitutional amendment and boom, everything is better for victims.
Q. Aside from the need to put the victim on par with the accused, what other considerations are there?
A. The second area is to provide more resources to victims. Victims and victim advocate organizations shouldn't have to be scraping and begging to make their budgets each year. We have millions and millions of dollars focused on the criminal justice system that's entirely meant to accommodate the accused in some way.
We should be putting somewhat equitable resources into victims; for example, enough shelters for domestic violence victims. The economic devastation that occurs when victims of domestic violence leave and the fear they have that they and their children are going to continue to be harassed, are the reasons they don't leave.
So why don't we have programs and resources that are even close to the resources that are put into housing, eating, clothing, medical accommodations, educational opportunities for the convicted in prison?
Q. Why do we need to hear from victims concerning the sentencing of the accused? Do many crime victims ask for leniency for the accused?
A. That's an interesting point. The fact is, many times in domestic situations of violence, even when the prosecutor is not happy with it, the victim prefers not to have the abuser receive the stiffest penalty.
Also, you have homicide survivors who have become ardent death penalty opponents. They didn't want the killing of their loved one to occur, and they now don't want the state in turn to kill.
Q. What about the victims' rights advocates?
A. There are only 10 or 12 victim advocates, who work for the Office of Victim Services within the judicial branch of our state government. We are advocating doubling the number. Almost all states have their victim services programs run primarily out of the executive branch. Only Connecticut has a judicial-based victim advocacy program where victim assistance and victim compensation are actually run out of our judicial branch.
Q. What do they do?
A. We try to counsel and educate the victims, but the decision has to be theirs. No victim advocate should tell the victim what he or she should or should not do.
Q. How many victims' rights agencies or grass-roots groups are there?
A. It depends in some degree on how you define it. The more formal ones that have programs, staff and budgets, like the Connecticut Sexual Assault Crisis Centers, the YW program and the Coalition Against Domestic Violence have a dozen offices around the state. Then you have groups like Mothers Against Drunk Drivers and Homicide Survivors that are less formalized, and are more volunteer efforts. They have thousands of members.
Q. Wouldn't it be more effective to concentrate on prevention of crime?
A. You do have many instances where victims' groups are focusing more on prevention and where victims are trying to work with those who are related to the offenders to bring peace to crime-ridden neighborhoods. In a recent case in another state, a juvenile offender, 14 years old or so, killed a peer. The fathers of the offender and the victim have joined forces to develop a program to bring peace in the neighborhood, and they're working together in both crime prevention and trying to get victim and offender mediation. So I don't think the victims' movement is really as monolithic and single-minded as some believe.
Q. How can you explain that the survivors of crime may win a civil suit against the accused, regardless of whether he's been found guilty or not guilty?
A. The basis for this is the two different outcomes. In the criminal trial you're taking someone's liberty away. In America, the foundation of our system is that we guard the individual liberties, and our system is designed to let guilty people go rather than that one innocent person in jail.
On the civil side, we're talking about taking someone's money away in an attempt to have restitution or some kind of damages, paid. We're much easier taking someone's money away.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Mario T. Gaboury directs a center for the study of victims' rights at the University of New Haven. (Gale Zucker for The New York Times)

HEADLINE: Preserving a Shaker Heritage Where a Prison Now Stands

BYLINE: By ALBERTA EISEMAN 

 BODY:
LITTLE is left of the once-thriving Shaker community in Enfield and what remains is difficult to detect. Some of the plain, dignified 19th-century buildings are enclosed within a state prison; a few that are privately owned stand in the shadow of the giant complex of Lego Systems, the toy manufacturers.
Every so often an incident reminds the public of the communal village that lasted from 1790 to 1914. Two years ago, for example, Lego wanted to widen a road that runs by the last remaining Shaker dwelling house, circa 1850, and more recently, preservationists objected to the removal of century-old window frames from buildings on prison grounds. 
 Most of the time, however, the busy residents of the northernmost section of the state drive past the 22 remaining buildings -- the dwelling house, meeting house, mills, barns, shops, all listed on the national register of historic places -- without a thought of the strong-minded, pious men and women who called themselves the United Society of Believers in Christ's Second Appearance.
Known as Shakers because of the ecstatic dances they performed in the meeting house, they were pledged to hard work, celibacy, common property and physical separation from "the world," their term for non-believers.
They built their communities by hand, with men and women assigned to different tasks and living separately as brothers and sisters in communal dwelling houses. They raised their own food, made their own identical clothing, produced their medicines and developed extensive trades like chair manufacturing and seed production -- a specialty for which the Enfield group was renowned.
The principles that governed Shaker life had their roots in England and were brought to the American Colonies in 1774 by a small group led by Mother Ann Lee, the spiritual leader. In the new world the Shakers founded 26 communities and at their zenith numbered 20,000.
The Enfield settlement, 260 strong and one of the most prosperous, was composed of five "families," the name given to the religious and economic units that ordered Shaker lives. The records list 118 buildings -- "remarkably neat and convenient" wrote John Warner Barber in his 1838 "Connecticut Historical Collections," adding that the inhabitants were "simple and plain in their manners; sober and industrious."
Eventually, the population shrank and the land was sold off gradually. In 1914 the bulk of the remaining 1,650 acres was bought by a tobacco farmer, who in turn sold it to the state for a prison in 1931.
Much has been written about the Correction Department's misuse of the buildings and the dispersion of its now-valued contents -- tales of a meeting house stripped of its trademark cabinets and used as a workshop, of prisoners complaining of the hardness of Shaker beds, of artifacts now worth thousands of dollars burned on a pyre. During the 1930's historic preservation was an idea whose time had not yet come, but things do change.
"They are extremely helpful," said an archeologist, William F. Keegan, of the prison authorities. "They do their best. Their intent is to preserve the Shaker buildings."
Few people know more about the state's one enduring Shaker settlement than Mr. Keegan, whose interest was sparked three years ago while studying at the University of Connecticut with Dr. Nicholas F. Bellantoni, the state archeologist.
"Lego was planning an extension," Dr. Bellantoni recalled, "and the Office of the State Archeologist was called in to conduct test excavations to see if anything pertaining to the Shakers would be disturbed. A dozen students and volunteers assisted in the field work throughout the summer of 1993. What Bill did," he continued, "was to extend the project, with historical research, archival work, mapping, statistics. Over the last three years he has put a lot of time and energy into this project -- he's probably become the main expert on Connecticut's Shakers."
"We think of this as a long-term project," Dr. Bellantoni said. "Certainly it's important enough and there is a fascination on the part of the public about the Shakers. Unfortunately we don't have the funding to keep people on it full-time -- we're much too small a department for that."
The uncertainty doesn't seem to faze Mr. Keegan, who received his bachelor's degree last May and held a variety of jobs before starting his college career.
"Shaker societies are infinitely interesting for things other than furniture," he said, despite the craze for Shaker artifacts.
What intrigues him is the society itself: how it was organized, how it became industrialized, how separate was it from the world since it depended on outside markets for its products.
He admires the Shakers' innovative ways, the irrigation and drainage systems they devised, the medicines they created, their financial prowess, the fact that each village had its own doctor, dentist and infirmary.
"When you join a Shaker society, your life improved far more than that of your neighbors," he explained.
During an interview held in the crowded kitchen of his apartment in Storrs, Mr. Keegan showed a visitor some of the artifacts found during various digs: ceramic fragments, painted in shades of blue and red; nails, bricks, bones, a rusty gear from one of the many mills, a forge fan probably used in making horseshoes. Once the objects are identified, analyzed, catalogued and mapped out, he said, they are stored at the University of Connecticut, forming the second-largest collection of Connecticut artifacts.
"The Shakers gave me a gift," he said of the ongoing commitment that he plans to pursue in graduate school, and whenever he can spare time from other archeological work. Meanwhile, he is completing a preservation plan for prison authorities, so they will not unwittingly destroy important landmarks.
Recently, a new discovery came his way: a six-foot by four-foot hand-drawn map of the entire Shaker village in Enfield during the 1890's, found by a resident who was clearing out his parents' home. It shows a quarry, a garbage dump and "several places where I hadn't thought to look for buildings," Mr. Keegan reported. "Things are always surfacing that tell us more and more about the site."

GRAPHIC: Photos: Some of the Shaker buildings in Enfield as they appeared in 1934, above, and in an earlier sketch, left. Excavations have unearthed a gear and furnace fan from the late 19th century, bottom left, and pottery, far left. (Photographs by William F. Keegan)

HEADLINE: The Brave New Face Of Art From The East

BYLINE: By HOLLAND COTTER 

 BODY:
"CONTEMPORARY ART IN INDIA? THERE IS NO contemporary art in India." So an academic friend tartly reminded me a few years ago. How could an avant-garde art exist anywhere in the "timeless" cultures of what we monolithically call Asia? If it did, it couldn't be any good. Too Western. Or too Asian. Or too little of one or the other.
Her assertion was understandable. But it proved wrong. In New Delhi and Baroda, Calcutta and Madras, new art in nontraditional forms, pitched to an international mainstream market, is being produced as you read these words; art schools are flourishing; a network of art galleries is growing. 
 The same is true across much of Asia. The West has long been alert to the excitements of contemporary Japanese art. But now new art is percolating through China (both the People's Republic and Taiwan) and Korea to the vast archipelago of Indonesia, though little finds its way to the art capitals of Europe and the United States.
As it happens, Asian art of all kinds commands the spotlight in the United States this season. Dallas is offering a one-stop-only autumn show of 17th-century Japanese Momoyama period work. Boston and Brooklyn are giving their Chinese holdings star treatment with new installations. "Splendors of Imperial China: Treasures From the National Palace Museum, Taipei," last season's blockbuster at the Metropolitan Museum, is wending its processional way to San Francisco and Washington. And the extravagantly titled "China: 5,000 Years" bows at the Guggenheim in May.
With such historical riches jostling on the horizon, it would be easy to overlook another phenomenon: the wave of contemporary Asian art -- less than a tidal wave but far more than a ripple -- headed this way, whether in the form of sprawling multimedia installations or traditional images given a distinctly 20th-century spin.
The Guggenheim show will include new work from China. But more immediate news breaks on Friday, when the show titled "Contemporary Art in Asia: Traditions/Tensions" opens in New York at three locations: the Asia Society, the Queens Museum of Art and the Grey Art Gallery of New York University. (The shows remain on view at the first two locations through Jan. 5 and at Grey Gallery through Dec. 23.)
Organized by the Asia Society, the show is the largest gathering of its kind yet seen in the city, though with some 70 works by 28 artists it is still a modest affair and makes no pretense of being comprehensive. It taps just five countries: Indonesia, India, the Philippines, South Korea and Thailand. And it focuses on only one of the many varieties of art produced in each today: politically oriented, with a bias toward installation.
Whatever the show shapes up to be, just by taking place it sets important precedents. It not only ushers in little seen Asian art but lets Asians themselves make the introductions. The curator, Apinan Poshyananda, is an American-trained professor of art in Bangkok. The catalogue is almost entirely by Asian critics and scholars. And the show's ambitious itinerary will take it to Canada and to four stops in Asia itself.
One would like, of course, to attribute its arrival to a flash of esthetic enlightenment on all sides. But money is probably the bottom line. Powerful international trade winds have been blowing from an Easterly direction for some time now (Southeast Asia in particular is booming), and all things Asian are hot property to Western cultural institutions, ever alert to private and corporate financing.
And with its integration into a global market economy, Asia has undergone daunting changes. Vast new wealth, accelerated urbanization, a growing middle class, an unprecedented deluge of Western forms and values have together resulted in an increasingly fragmented and destabilized self-image.
WITH THAT SELF-IMAGE AS ITS theme, "Traditions/Tensions" has dramatic material to work with, though the show arrives in New York with a few built-in liabilities.
One is timing. Art based on "identity" has been a growth industry in the United States for almost a decade. "Diversity" is a concept so overworked and emptied of meaning that Bill Clinton felt comfortable using it in his recent speech accepting his renomination for President. And installation is yesterday's cold potatoes to the mainstream art world.
The fact that installation art is pegged as a Western form also raises difficulties. Generally speaking, to Western viewers, West means modern, non-West means traditional. And while the West seldom has qualms about borrowing extensively from cultures outside its sphere, it is known to get impatient if the exchange goes the other way.
Eclecticism and variety, in other words, are the prerogatives of one kind of art; predictable purity the requirement of the other. Thus, superficially considered, the new Asian art risks being dismissed either as wannabe Western or inauthentically Asian. It may, in fact, not be seen as Asian at all.
Which brings up the flip side of the visibility problem: unfamiliarity. Viewers who come to the show without at least some grounding in the social attitudes, religious beliefs and modern histories of the five distinctive countries embraced here are already seriously behind the curve, for much of this art comes straight from specific cultural experience.
Consider the case of Korea. An ancient culture long dominated by China and colonized by Japan, it was suddenly harnessed to a Western-style capitalist economy half a century ago, with explosive results. The population of Seoul, the Korean capital, jumped from 600,000 to more than 10 million between 1968 and 1988. International corporations like Samsung and Hyundai have become household names, and wealth has contributed an avalanche of new art. (At the beginning of the 1990's it was estimated that Seoul had more than 300 galleries and 60,000 artists.) 
There is no question that prosperity opened doors for Korean artists, giving them first-time contact with the cutting-edge art of Europe and the United States, as well as a potential market. But it also altered the fabric of their daily lives.
Old esthetic traditions were threatened by commercialization. (How can brush-and-ink landscape painting compete with Disney?) Western-style individuality went against the grain of a "we" society. And the idea of innovation as an end in itself left the valued notion of cultural continuity in the dust.
Such ambivalence marks the work of Korean artists in the show. Installations by Cho Duck Hyun offer dramatically lighted archival images -- drawings and photographs -- of an older Korea in what the artist considers a gesture of cultural healing. (He invited a Korean shaman to preside over his 1994 installation for the Sao Paulo Bienal.)
And the overtly political paintings of Kim Ho-Suk, like those in the series titled "The History of Korea's Resistance Against Japanese Colonialism," addressed the country's painful interaction with the outside world. Mr. Kim, whose grandfather was a resistance fighter during the Japanese occupation, depicts 20th-century Korean historical figures but in a delicate 18th-century style.
Some of Korea's historical realities -- colonialism, national partition, economic disparities, the sense of a spiritual life on the rocks and a cultural identity growing ever more fragile in a global environment -- are shared by much of the rest of Asia. A highly conflicted national "self," for example, is the subject of a painting by a Thai artist, Chatchai Puipia, titled "Siamese Smile." Its ferociously grinning male head (a self-portrait, in fact), teeth clenched, eyes bulging, is an unhinged version of a classical Thai Buddha, whose placid demeanor has long been the emblem of a country with an unsettling inner life.
Elements of that life are suggested in an installation by Kamol Phaosavasdi, "Mode of Moral Being," in which a replica of a food vendor's cart common in the streets of Bangkok is fitted out with a video screen showing interviews with call girls, pimps and other denizens of the sex trade. The reference is to the city's international reputation as an Asian center of prostitution for a foreign clientele.
A feminist statement is implicit in this multilayered image of consumption, though other artists deal with the theme of gender more directly. A room-size installation titled "Filipina: DH" by Imelda Cajipe Endaya, for example, with its battered suitcases, black-painted brooms and popular religious prints refers to the exploited labor of Philippine maidservants working abroad.
Another Philippine sculptor, Agnes Arellano, takes a different approach to the status of women in an Asian society. She elevates body casts of women, old and young, onto pedestals to suggest a pantheon of goddesses. Ravinder G. Reddy of India works in a similar vein. He gives a fiberglass sculpture of a woman the dark blue skin of the male god Krishna, and he scales up a gold-painted, confidently smiling woman's head to monumental size.
The recurrent references to religion in "Traditions/ Tensions," though in fact rarely devotional in intention, raise problems in a Western context. Comparable images in mainstream contemporary art in New York tend to inspire condescension or discomfort. In Asian art, if the references are understood at all, they may reinforce the cliche of the East as a font of spiritual wisdom and, by implication, little else.
Yet many artists in the show come from cultures in which religion is a serious matter, both as a refuge and as a burden. (The prevalent choice of installation as a format may be explained in part by the presence of altars as central fixtures of everyday Asian life.)
In the polyglot cultures of Indonesia, for example, animism, Hinduism, Buddhism, Islam and Christianity are, for better and for worse, all active forces. Something of their ambivalent energy comes across in an altarlike installation by Dadang Christanto titled "Kekerasan" ("Violence"), a pyramid of terra-cotta heads, their mouths agape in what could be either communal prayer or a collective howl.
WHATEVER THE ATTITUDE TOWARD religion behind such work -- critical in Mr. Christanto's case, but by no means universally so among Indonesian artists in general -- can the subject itself possibly have any relevance to the highly secularized West? It can and it does, for reasons both negative and positive.
Some of the most divisive ethical and political issues in the United States at present -- abortion, gay marriage -- are being fought along lines of religious ideology. At the same time, the growing Asian immigrant presence in America is having an invigorating, transformative effect on the country's spiritual demographics: the Buddhist, Muslim and Hindu presence in cities is flourishing. Chicago alone has 50 mosques; New York has a huge Hindu population; San Francisco is home to Thai, Korean, Vietnamese, Tibetan, Sri Lankan and Cambodian Buddhist populations.
But if Asia is exporting such cultural riches to us, what is it getting in return? In art, at least, many things, including a fresh range of expressive forms and a new theoretical language. The results can be exasperating (much of the show's catalogue is hopelessly mired in a pretentious Esperanto of Western academic theory-speak that makes even the subtlest work sound overdetermined). But they are just as often exhilarating.
And they offer ideas that non-Asian cultures would do well to consider. The paradigm for a vanguard art in Europe and America is based on a perpetual rupture with the past. Contemporary Asian art, by contrast, is as often as protective of history as it is skeptical. Such conservatism is played down in "Traditions/Tensions." But it is evident in other Asian art forms -- from religious painting to modernist abstraction to traditional work -- unrepresented here.
Could any exhibition encompass such complexity? The answer, of course, is no, though at least some sense of the breadth and vibrancy of contemporary Asian art will be on view in "Traditions/Tensions."
Like any trial balloon, it carries the heavy responsibility of bringing unfamiliar work to a new audience. And Asian contemporary art has not yet had the benefit, in the West at least, of the month-by-month, show-by-show weeding process of its New York counterparts, with the accompanying privilege of making mistakes in public, being forgiven and being invited to try again.
One thing is certain, though: the world is changing fast. Asia is a crucial part of that change. And its new art will be a provocative part of our future.

HEADLINE: SUNDAY VIEW;
An Old Romance That Refuses to Rekindle

BYLINE: By Vincent Canby 

 BODY:
Michael Gambon is big not only in size, substance and voice, but also in his singular kind of destabilizing stage presence. There's an element of risk, and therefore of suspense, in everything he does. In this way he well serves David Hare's "Skylight," now at the Royale Theater, where the Irish-born London stage icon is at long last making his Broadway debut. Mr. Gambon and Lia Williams, his co-star, are recreating the roles they originated in London last year in the Royal National Theater production, staged then as now by Richard Eyre, the National's director.
Mr. Gambon has been in movies ("The Cook, the Thief, His Wife and Her Lover," among others) and played Inspector Maigret in the British television series based on the Georges Simenon novels. Earlier this year I had the opportunity to see him at the National, sharing the stage with Simon Russell Beale and rediscovering the dark, dangerous heart that still beats in Ben Jonson's "Volpone." Yet most Americans know Mr. Gambon best for his great howling performance in Dennis Potter's television classic "The Singing Detective," as a man who slips into fantastical daymares to escape his psoriatic arthritis and the hospital ward that imprisons him. 
 Now "Skylight" gives New Yorkers a chance to appreciate the actor in all of his insidious charm and bulky, middle-aged flesh on the stage, which is where he belongs.
Mr. Gambon's performance illuminates, gives focus to and sometimes appears to ride blithely roughshod over "Skylight," a three-character piece in which the playwright's neo-1960's polemics are never disguised as a love story with complete satisfaction. Like so many of Mr. Hare's works, "Skylight" is about a love that cannot be. This time it's about Tom Sergeant (Mr. Gambon), who exemplifies the England created by Margaret Thatcher, and Kyra Hollis (Ms. Williams), his former mistress, who has the sort of activist conscience once associated with the Labor Party when it was far more vital than it has been in recent decades. A bewildered, well-meaning witness to the events is Tom's 18-year-old son, Edward (Christian Camargo), who may represent a better tomorrow.
After a separation of more than three years, Tom, a successful London restaurateur, turns up at Kyra's chilly, threadbare northwest London flat saying, quite nonchalantly, that it's time they saw each other again. It's a snowy winter evening, and if Tom and Kyra have a lot to catch up on, they have even more to thrash out. Since Kyra abandoned him (he says) without explanation, his wife, Alice, has died of cancer and his chain of restaurants, reaching from London to America and the Far East, has gone public. He's exhausted with all of the concomitant bother of dealing with management consultants and shareholders and a son he doesn't understand. He's also lonely, or, as he puts it, "I need a woman to worship."
Though it's clear that the thirtyish Kyra has missed him, she no longer misses the life of limousines and other perks she enjoyed when she worked for the free-spending Tom. She's now finding fulfillment as a teacher in one of the rougher areas of London's depressed East End. As the evening turns into night, it's revealed that Kyra virtually lived with Tom and Alice, who had become her best friend, as a member of a menage a trois Alice was unaware of. Kyra didn't hesitate to make love to Tom as long as Alice didn't know. She left him only when, not quite accidentally, he allowed his wife to learn the truth.
Kyra accuses him of betraying her trust in him, without saying anything about the trust Alice may have had in her. She remembers those days with Tom and Alice as halcyon. She liked being a part of their secure family. Kyra obviously is a suitable case for treatment. She has some profound problems that the play never adequately addresses, but even with her neuroses she's the same prototypical do-good Hare heroine we've seen in "Plenty," "The Secret Rapture," "Racing Demon" and "Strapless," the film he wrote and directed. She's romantic, numbingly articulate and as emotionally needy as she is desperate for independence.
In the person of Ms. Williams, Kyra, like Kate Nelligan's Susan Traherne in "Plenty," is also great looking. With her lithe figure and fine bone structure, she can appear chic in a flannel nightdress and baggy cardigan. That's important in "Skylight," which, for all its earnestness in behalf of social activism, is a very upper-class, though liberal, kind of play.
Because the author has a way of dealing with political issues through the opposing mind-sets of characters who aren't themselves in economic need, his plays have a way of seeming abstract without revealing larger truths. There's a touch of condescension in the way Tom and Kyra attack each other. The glib analyses that they trade are self-consciously peppered with four-letter words, as if these will convince us of the honesty of their talk and the passion of their feelings. A lot of their accusations are accurate: Tom really does see the world revolving around him; he's selfish, and he has bought into the new conservatism and its jargon in which business is referred to as "the creation of wealth."
IT'S ALSO TRUE THAT KYRA can more easily commit herself to people than to a single person. Yet as they go at each other, it always sounds like stage talk. There's no sense of the spontaneous, brainy, serious fun displayed by any pair of battling lovers in a George Bernard Shaw play. Tom and Kyra have wit but not much humor. They're mismatched from the start. Though there are occasional funny lines and moments of genuine pathos, Mr. Hare writes with the kind of ponderous sincerity that allows for few surprises.
This is where Mr. Eyre's production and, in particular, his actors take over. Ms. Williams is a delight in a role that might have been played by the young, not yet take-charge Glenda Jackson. She has bite, beauty and life as she tries to convince Mr. Gambon's Tom that as much as she adores sleeping with him, she has no intention of again becoming a woman worshiped. She may not be quite as convincing when she talks about the secret glory of her mission to teach the underprivileged, but that's because of Mr. Hare's flat-footed text.
As Tom's baffled son, Edward, who both rails against his father and tries to help him, Mr. Camargo is skilled and thoroughly convincing. His friskiness sometimes suggests a fellow even younger than 18, but then all three cast members work nonstop to give this play a pulse you can feel.
No one more so than Mr. Gambon. Though you can assume that his performance has long been fixed, and that it doesn't vary from one night to the next, you can never be sure what, exactly, Tom Sergeant will do next. It could be a dainty, quite weird little pirouette of postcoital satisfaction, a sweeping, decoratorlike appraisal of Kyra's ugly flat, or a sudden, tearful breakdown, embarrassing in its intimacy, in the middle of a speech of self-serving bravado. He stands on chairs before sitting in them. Sometimes he seems to tune out entirely. Like Laurence Olivier when in fine fettle, Mr. Gambon keeps you deliciously off guard.
While Tom seduces Kyra, the actor seduces the audience. This is a risky business, something that can lead to chaos and tear a production apart. Yet Mr. Gambon plays beautifully with Ms. Williams as they alternately move from moments of tenderness to scenes in which cutlery is flung in frustration, or books are thrown as weapons intended to wound.
Mr. Gambon's immense control as an actor seeps into the character to such an extent that Tom comes to control the play as well, which is actually more about Kyra than it is about Tom.
Any objections to Mr. Gambon's work in "Skylight" must be academic. As you respond to the dervish performance, you're also aware of the mysterious talent of the actor within it. The effect is not to destroy the performance but to double the pleasure, a phenomenon as rare and spectacular in the theater as the green flash is on the high seas. Mr. Gambon thus transforms a bluntly satiric text into an evening of grand, unapologetic theatrics.

GRAPHIC: Photos: Michael Gambon and Lia Williams in David Hare's "Skylight" at the Royale Theater--A play, like so many of Mr. Hare's works, about a love made impossible by the opposing mind-sets of its characters. (Sara Krulwich/The New York Times) (pg. 5); Lia Williams and Michael Gambon in "Skylight"--Former lovers rehash the past and thrash out the future. (Sara Krulwich/The New York Times) (pg. 25)

HEADLINE: Explorers Offer the World to the Classroom

BYLINE: By NANCY POLK 

 BODY:
THE Help Wanted advertisement read "Three explorers to travel from Venice to Hong Kong fall 1996. Must speak Russian, Chinese or Turkish, enjoy working with children and computers. Will travel five months with GlobaLearn team, a New Haven educational nonprofit organization."
The trans-Asia trip will be GlobaLearn's third expedition. As teams of explorers venture out into new cultures they share their adventures with thousands of American schoolchildren through the Internet. Schools throughout Connecticut are on line with the GlobaLearn explorers. 
 GlobaLearn was born in the imagination of Marat A. Armbruster, a 27-year-old New Haven resident. After college he spent five months in the Aleutian Islands working at a salmon cannery. As he journeyed from New Haven through Chicago to Seattle and on to Alaska, two young New Haven friends, ages 6 and 7, pushed pins into a large map of the United States to follow his route. He sent them postcards with geographic questions and they were delighted. "Their mother said they had been eager to go to the library to find out more about the places I had been to. It really got me thinking. They never would have been interested in Alaska without that personal connection."
At the University of Michigan he tutored juvenile offenders, ages 8 to 17, in American history. "I quickly realized that my task was not to teach them the text but to engage them in something, anything, because their attention span was so short. What shocked me was how little sense of geography they had. How could history make any sense to them?" His students could not put Detroit, New York, Washington on the map. He decided to try to reproduce his own educational experiences in a modest way.
After high school he wandered through Asia and Europe for two years. His travels motivated him to work hard and explore such subjects as the history of Buddhism at the University of Michigan. Without the earlier experiences he believes he would not have succeeded at the university. He wondered if he could somehow transmit to his students a similar experience of learning through travel. He designed a Dungeons and Dragons style of game. He asked them to choose places to visit. It was an effective strategy. At the end of the class they were able to exactly trace their routes on a map.
These two experiences combined to inspire the creation of GlobaLearn. "I really felt to be able to bring the world to kids in the way I had had the opportunity to do would be valuable," Mr. Armbruster said. "I decided that would be my mission."
He consulted with a friend he calls The Technologist. He had already conceived the notion of expeditions but transmitting the information back was a hurtle. His friend suggested using satellite to beam it back, with computers for distribution. This was before the creation of the World Wide Web. "Now the Web is the perfect medium of delivery for us," he explained.
In 1995 GlobaLearn ran a pilot expedition they called the G-7 Turkey Expedition. Over 270 children in the United States, Canada and Belgium participated. Mr. Armbruster and Raymond Smart, of Wilton, chairman of the GlobaLearn board, went to Brussels to represent the United States at the European Union's ministerial conference on the information society. Four Globalearn explorers in Turkey transmitted their daily journals and conversations with Turkish children via their World Wide Web site.
Visitors to the conference could stop by and question the Turkish children. A Greek student from an international school paused and was invited to participate. "Why would I talk to them? They are my enemy," he said. He was lured into playing with the computer. By the end of the conference he had made friends with Nunu, a Turkish girl. When he was asked why he would talk to her when she was Turkish, he replied, "I don't care, she's my friend."
At St. Thomas Day School in New Haven students closely followed the expedition. When a bombing incident occurred between Kurds and Turks after the expedition had ended worried students called GlobaLearn to make sure their new friends were okay.
The St. Thomas teacher moved the computer into the math class during that period of unrest to reassure the youngsters. The students were working on challenging word problems. To bring greater clarity to the problems they were changed, and GlobaLearn fuel consumption rates replaced the original wording. Mr. Armbruster snapped his fingers and exclaimed, "Like that, the kids grasped it because it was real life to them."
Mr. Armbruster said that parents and teachers saw immediate results because the children were so eager to go on the expedition, to meet other children and to interact with the explorers. Some teachers reported using GlobaLearn as a reward. When students finished more mundane class tasks they were able to go on line.
The second expedition was an eight-week journey that began in March and went around the Black Sea, through coastal communities in Turkey, Georgia, Russia, Ukraine, Romania and Bulgaria. Mr. Armbruster, whose father is Turkish, has many friends and government contacts in Turkey.
This time they had 5,000 fourth, fifth and sixth graders participate during a three-month trek. Six Americans including four explorers, a security/logistics person and a videographer traveled 3,500 miles.
Mr. Armbruster explained that though many tourists have traveled through this region, what set the explorers apart was their goal. "No one had circumnavigated the Black Sea, put together a portrait of this geographical entity and defined it through its children. We used the children to investigate the relationships among industry, the economy, the environment, history and traditions."
One Connecticut site was Beecher Road Elementary School in Woodbridge. Upon their return, Celeste Cordani, a computer teacher at the school, invited the explorers to come to class. The children were excited to meet them and they had a party including food from the Black Sea nations prepared by class mothers. The children asked questions and presented their own reports on the expedition. Questions included, "Did you ever fight with each other? How do you stay occupied when you have nothing to do? How do you get to be an explorer?"
One group of children designed a computer scavenger hunt and asked their classmates, "What is the new name for Constantinople? What is Attaturk?"
Ms. Cordani observed that the project was "a powerful example of how positive Internet involvement can be for kids."
"Some kids really blossomed with this assignment," she said, "kids who had shown no motivation before."
In September GlobaLearn set out for its most ambitious expedition. For six months, 50,000 fourth, fifth and six graders will follow the six-person team on their journey. It will retrace Marco Polo's trade routes through 14 countries and the territory of Hong Kong, 8,000 miles from Italy to Hong Kong.
As with the other expeditions the team will stay with host families and children will be able to communicate with each other. "We are hosted by children 9 to -13. We wake up with them, eat breakfast with them, go to school with them," explained Mr. Armbruster.
Raymond Smart, president of the Smart Family Foundation in Wilton, was impressed with GlobaLearn's grant request. "When I saw their proposal I thought this was a reason to have computers. A computer is merely a delivery system for the software. For the first time here was a software package that was non-biased in its approach, that was comprehensive in its coverage of the curriculum and really easy to use. Most important it looked like something kids could get engaged in."
Getting kids engaged matters a great deal to Mr. Smart. "You can't force kids to learn, which is one of the presumptions that persists." He was taken with the content that GlobaLearn offered. "You have to have material that is seductive. A lot of the stuff I see is not. It covers the curriculum O.K. but the kids are bored out of their minds."
Mr. Smart became so enamored by the GlobaLearn proposal that he agreed to serve on their board and then to become chairman. He accompanied Mr. Armbruster to Brussels to demonstrate GlobaLearn's capacity to link children nation to nation. He now makes it a practice to meet the explorers for an end of expedition party. And he is negotiating to link up the Los Angeles public school system, which would mean the trans-Asia trip could reach more than 300,000 children.
For more information about GlobaLearn their Web site can be visited (http://www.globalearn.org.). GlobaLearn prefers that users register. There is no fee but they are still conducting research and need information from participants.

GRAPHIC: Photo: At the Beecher Road School in Woodbridge, explorers retrace their trip around the Black Sea with fourth graders. (Thomas McDonald for The New York Times)

LENGTH: 1014 words

HEADLINE: County Budgets Feature Tax-Reduction Plans

BYLINE: By JOHN RATHER 

 BODY:
REFLECTING a fair year for the regional economy, the Nassau and Suffolk County Executives are proposing budgets that leave further behind an era of fiscal crisis and express tempered optimism for 1997.
In Suffolk, Robert J. Gaffney's $1.7 billion plan would raise property taxes 3.3 percent, or $21.35 for an average taxpayer. 
 Mr. Gaffney said the county would end the year with a $24 million surplus despite a 0.25 percent reduction in the 8.5 percent sales tax.
In Nassau, Thomas S. Gulotta's $2.2 billion proposal combines a $17 million tax cut with higher spending. Higher sales-tax revenues, an indicator of stronger retail sales, partly explains the contradiction.
Suffolk Democrats complained that Mr. Gaffney, who was elected to a second four-year term in November, sought to raise taxes less out of necessity than to dispense patronage by creating new jobs and hiring consultants.
The minority leader in the County Legislature, Maxine S. Postal, Democrat of Amityville, said the proposed increase would be forgotten in three years, when Mr. Gaffney might seek a third term.
"For the next two years he's home free," said Ms. Postal.
The projected surplus, which would be used to finance the budget, shows a vastly changed picture compared to five years ago, when a record deficit of $99.2 million created a crisis and led to sales-tax increases.
Mr. Gaffney said one troubling sign was an $11 million increase in delinquent tax payments. He asked the County Planning Department to investigate that.
But he said other signs were favorable, adding, "This year we have seen growth in our local economy and an expansion of our economic base and assessed value."
The two counties also escaped major cuts in Federal and state revenues. Most major reductions proposed by Congressional Republicans two years ago were not enacted. In Albany, compromises between Gov. George E. Pataki and Democrats in the Assembly averted other cuts.
Uncertainty remains over costs for social services after Washington made major changes in welfare.
"Everyone thinks it was a great thing," Ms. Postal said. "But it may end up costing Suffolk County more money."
Long Island government officials and economists said they expected stability and slow growth would continue. "The regional economy is clearly improving," said Mr. Gulotta.
He attributed his proposed tax cut to gains in the economy and cuts in county government. County taxes are one-fifth of the total tax bills for Nassau residents.
Mr. Gulotta said county employees declined, from 16,885 in 1977 to 10,180, a 40 percent drop. The proposed budget, he added, would cut an additional 92 positions.
Mr. Gulotta, who is expected to run for re-election, said he had no plans to emulate Mr. Gaffney and proposed to reduce the county sales tax. The 8.5 percent tax is the highest in the state.
"Our emphasis has been to reduce wherever possible the property tax," Mr. Gulotta said. "Our residents pay dollar for dollar on property taxes. The sales tax is subsidized by people who do not live in Nassau County."
According to Mr. Gulotta, nonresidents paid one-third of the sales taxes, or more than $200 million a year.
Thomas Conoscenti, a regional economist who advises governments and businesses, predicted that moderate growth in 1996 would slow slightly. He said he expected retail sales to increase 5.2 percent in final figures for 1996. But the slower economy would mean a 4.1 percent increase in retail sales next year, he predicted.
Mr. Conoscenti predicted that Long Island employment would show an increase of 16,000 jobs this year, a 1.5 percent rise. He said 10,500 jobs would be created in 1997, an increase of 1 percent.
Unemployment of 5.2 percent in Suffolk and 4 percent in Nassau in the first half of this year would decline next year, he added.
Bruce Nyman, the Democratic minority leader in the Nassau Legislature, said the county's economy was in crisis. "We may have bottomed out but I still do not see an upturn," Mr. Nyman said. "Maybe people are shopping more, but I don't see improvements for our small businesses and downtown areas. Anyone who thinks we are not in crisis here on Long Island is a misguided cheerleader or has his head in the sand."
Mr. Nyman said Mr. Gulotta's tax proposal would have little impact, and he questioned how Mr. Gulotta could raise spending and cut taxes simultaneously.
Mr. Gulotta's budget plan will be scrutinized by the budget review office of the new County Legislature. Mr. Gulotta, who had proposed establishing the office, said he welcomed its review. The Legislature also held three budget hearings last week.
The director of the office, Timothy P. Sullivan, said he planned to issue an analysis before Oct. 30, the deadline for adopting a final budget. The earlier date ends years in which the final budget was not voted on until after Election Day.
Mr. Sullivan, former chief fiscal officer of the New York City Council, said he was hired after having responded to an advertisement in The Wall Street Journal. He said he had no ties to Nassau Republicans and Democrats and was not yet even a resident of the county.
Mr. Sullivan's staff of three analysts and a secretary is less than one-fourth of the Suffolk counterpart.
That budget review office has a reputation for independence and for often challenging figures in the Executive's proposed budget. Its analysis has often been the basis for legislators' budget amendments.
The budget deadline in Suffolk is Nov. 7, two days after Election Day.
Last year, in a final vote after Election Day, the Republican majority in the Legislature agreed to extend 0.75 percent in the sales tax that had been scheduled to expire at the end of 1995.
They also transferred a $25 million surplus from a water-protection program to the general fund to avoid what they said would be a deficit caused by lower than budgeted sales-tax receipts.
Mr. Gaffney disagreed but did not veto the move.
The $25 million nearly matched the $24 million surplus that Mr. Gaffney has projected for 1996.

HEADLINE: Berkeley Hopes $120 Million Plant Erases an Image

BYLINE: By JOHN McCLOUD 

DATELINE: BERKELEY, Calif.

 BODY:
SEEKING to overcome what it concedes is a well-earned reputation for being antidevelopment, this city has a new $120 million multipurpose biotechnology plant to show off as a demonstration that such a reputation is outdated.
Built by Bayer Biotechnology Corporation, a division of the German pharmaceutical giant Bayer Group, the 120,000-square-foot plant was completed this spring, the first major offshoot of an innovative 30-year development agreement the company reached with the city in 1992. Bayer has also built or expanded six other buildings with a total of about 70,000 square feet on its campus. 
 "This agreement was groundbreaking in terms of our political history," said the city's planning director, Gil Kelley. "Berkeley's had a reputation for being slow-growth and antibusiness, but we've known for some time that despite the impression of the rest of the world we have some pretty unusual assets. This was a step in Berkeley's history that announced we are not anti-business. It sent shock waves throughout the Bay Area."
Dennis Cohen, a former Berkeley Chamber of Commerce president who developed a high-technology business incubator project in West Berkeley, said that there was no longer a sense that the city was an adversary to business.
"They've been willing to contribute to business programs and make the kind of public investment that would encourage business development," he said.
As a sign of the new approach, the city opened a one-stop permit center that frees people from having to go to multiple departments to process building applications. Regulations have also been streamlined, Mr. Kelley said.
In addition, the city now actively recruits new businesses, especially those that offer blue-collar jobs and entry-level positions for the approximately 20,000 residents out of a population of 103,000 classified both as low- or moderate-income and not students at the University of California.
"Everyone thinks of us in terms of the university, but we also have a large low-income population," Mr. Kelley noted.
The effort may be working. Federal figures indicate that the city had a 14 percent increase in manufacturing jobs between 1987 and 1992, reversing a 15-year decline. Although some industrial companies have left since 1992, more than two dozen others have apart from Bayer have located or expanded in West Berkeley, the city's traditional industrial zone. They have accounted for about 400,000 square feet and a net increase of about 120 new jobs. Mr. Kelley estimated that industrial companies provide about one quarter of the city's total of 68,860 jobs.
Bayer's agreement with the city gives the company the right to develop up to 1.17 million square feet of new construction and retain up to 163,000 square feet of existing space on a 30-acre site it owns in West Berkeley. The company expects to spend $300 million on capital improvements and add 350 jobs during the first 10 years of the agreement.
While the agreement grants Bayer several exemptions from current zoning regulations, including allowing buildings up to 10 stories rather than the four normally permitted by statutes, its most important feature is the assurance that city requirements and regulations for the site will not change with political winds.
According to Bayer Biotechnology's president, Dr. George Scangos, that assurance was crucial to the company's decision to remain and expand in Berkeley. It looked at options in Connecticut, where Bayer's pharmaceutical division has its headquarters, and North Carolina as well as Germany before making a commitment.
"Berkeley's reputation was a significant concern," Dr. Scangos said. "Any company contemplating a several-hundred-million-dollar capital investment would be wary. The concerns were how to be able to plan rationally for a site we knew would be expanding, in Berkeley, which has a very diverse constituency."
Dr. Scangos said Bayer initially considered closing the plant, which has been in operation since 1903 and which Bayer acquired in 1978. Instead, the company decided to make it the center of its worldwide biotechnology division to take advantage of the San Francisco area's growing importance in the world of biotechnology.
Bayer's concern with the city's reputation as antidevelopment proved somewhat justified. Neighborhood and environmental activists forced the company to participate in more than 100 community meetings and spend more than two years addressing local objections before reaching an accord.
To win approval for its plans, Bayer committed itself to spending $12 million on environmental and community projects, including cleanup of past contamination in an adjacent park, creation of a water reclamation program and donation of $1.4 million to help train disadvantaged high school and junior college students for employment in biotechnology.
Although the demands might appear burdensome, Mr. Kelley reported that Bayer has spent and done more than required. "They've been a wonderful corporate citizen," he said.
Some project opponents also appear to have had a change of heart. Laurie Bright, a West Berkeley resident who owns an auto-repair shop a few blocks from the Bayer site, recalled that he expected the international corporation to try to ride roughshod over nearby residents and businesses. Now he supports the project. "I think that Bayer has been very good about keeping their word on all the community mitigations they said they would provide," Mr. Bright remarked.
To the owners of businesses that recently relocated to Berkeley, the city's level of support has come as a pleasant surprise. "We've actually found them to be very accommodating," said Fred DaMert, president of DaMert Company, a specialty-toy maker that relocated from San Leandro, Calif., to West Berkeley earlier this year. "We haven't experienced any of the nightmares we heard about."
But it is also true that most of the new companies are in businesses that produce little or no undesirable environmental impact. Old-style industries that produce noxious fumes, plumes and effluents have been increasingly replaced by companies less likely to arouse the ire of neighbors. DaMert, for example, designs, assembles, packages and distributes its toys from Berkeley but has them manufactured overseas.
ON the other hand, Mr. DaMert pointed out, he employs more people per square foot than the preceding tenant of his space, a maker of airplane paint.
"I have more than 30 employees in a space that used to employ half a dozen," he said.
Other new companies in West Berkeley include Kali Sportsnatural, a nutritional supplement producer; Powis Parker, a maker of document binding machines, and Used Rubber U.S.A., which recycles used inner tubes into handbags and other personal accessories.
Golden Pacific Brewing Company is building a small commercial brewery and, thanks to the city's help in securing state industrial bonds, Pyramid Brewing Company is building an $8 million, 100,000-square-foot brewery that will employ 70 people.
Mr. Kelley said the biggest task now was to get several vacant industrial sites back into operation. The largest is a 10-acre former Colgate-Palmolive plant that has sat unused for more than a decade.
"It's got a lot of hulking concrete buildings that were built to suit a type of industry that doesn't exist anymore. It's difficult to get developers interested, but we've had a couple of bites recently," he said.
If anyone shows interest, he added, the city will be there to help.
"I don't think we have to kick industry out to the suburbs to have a livable city. I think having a broad job base helps make this a livable city. It's taken time for Berkeley to learn this, but I think we finally have," he said.

GRAPHIC: Photo: Gil Kelley, Berkeley planning director, at Bayer Corporation's new $120 million biotechnology plant. (Darcy Padilla for The New York Times)
