SECTION: Section 7; Page 10; Column 1; Book Review Desk 

LENGTH: 1521 words

HEADLINE: The Vatican Sanction

BYLINE: By Stephen Engelberg; Stephen Engelberg, an assistant foreign editor of The New York Times, was The Times's Warsaw bureau chief from 1990 to 1993.

 BODY:

HIS HOLINESS
John Paul II and the Hidden History of Our Time.
By Carl Bernstein and Marco Politi.
Illustrated. 582 pp. New York:
Doubleday. $27.50.
In a century of commanding figures, there have been few more fascinating -- and central -- personalities than Pope John Paul II. He became the spiritual leader of the world's 900 million Roman Catholics at the moment when Communism was beginning to crumble from within. His historic 1979 visit to Poland, when millions of Poles rejoiced in the streets at the unlikely triumph of a Polish pontiff, demonstrated for the first time the possibilities of "people power" behind the Iron Curtain and inspired the birth of the Solidarity movement. Today, his unyielding views on abortion and the ordination of women define a view of the church that puts him at odds with many of his flock. 
 In "His Holiness," two veteran reporters, Carl Bernstein and Marco Politi, one based in Washington and the other in Rome, apply the venerable techniques of Washington reporting to unearth the Pope's secret role in bringing about the end of Communism -- what they portentously term "the hidden history of our time." They interview the Pope's aides, some of whom cooperate only on condition that they not be identified. They speak with President Ronald Reagan; Lech Walesa, the former leader of Solidarity; and senior American intelligence officials. In the United States, they use the Freedom of Information Act to get access to formerly secret American diplomatic cables. In Russia, they obtain copies of Politburo minutes that have been kept secret until now. They expose new details about American covert operations to aid Solidarity.
The results are nonetheless not entirely enlightening. Mr. Bernstein and Mr. Politi, who has covered the papacy for 19 years, amass an impressive array of facts about the inner workings of the Vatican, the Pope's early life and dealings between the Pontiff and President Reagan's emissaries. But their principal assertion -- that the Pope and President Reagan combined in a covert alliance to bring about the end of Communism -- is largely unsupported by the evidence in their book. And their view of the Solidarity movement as an extension of the Catholic Church in Poland misses the more complicated and subtle relationship between the two most potent anti-Communist forces in that nation. Hidden history isn't necessarily significant history.
As recounted in "His Holiness," the secret alliance boils down to a series of meetings between the Pope and Vernon Walters, the Reagan Administration's Ambassador at Large, and William J. Casey, the Director of Central Intelligence. Mr. Walters is said to have shown the Pope satellite photos of Soviet troop movements and to have told him, obliquely, about American efforts to keep Solidarity alive, if necessary as an underground movement, after the Polish Communists imposed martial law in December 1981 and arrested many of its key leaders. Classified cables obtained by the authors show that Mr. Walters shared with the Pope such pearls of American intelligence analysis as the 1982 assessment of the Soviet leader Leonid Brezhnev ("We think he's a little gaga," he is quoted as telling the Pontiff). The authors make vast, implausible claims for the Pope's influence on President Reagan, writing that other Presidents "had waited anxiously at their desks for bombers to return from their missions: Reagan waited for reports from the Pope."
Mr. Bernstein first made the case for a secret alliance between the Pope and President Reagan in a 1992 cover story in Time magazine that was derisively received by senior Solidarity leaders in Poland. The Pope and President Reagan, he wrote then, agreed at a meeting in June 1982 to undertake a "clandestine campaign to hasten the dissolution of the Communist empire." Richard Allen, the Reagan Administration's first national security adviser, was quoted in Time as calling the collaboration "one of the great secret alliances of all time." In the book Mr. Allen is quoted slightly differently, saying it was "one of the greatest secret alliances of all time."
Four years later, in "His Holiness," Mr. Bernstein and Mr. Politi offer a considerably more nuanced explanation of Mr. Allen's words: "Hyperbole aside," they write, "the description contained essential elements of accuracy as both powers pursued their individual goals along parallel tracks: keeping each other informed, always taking each other's sensibilities into account, consulting, searching for common moral and political ground, plugging regularly into each other's enormous intelligence capabilities, but never formally undertaking clandestine activities together." It seems to have been more a confluence of views of two profoundly anti-Communist leaders than an alliance.
There is no doubt that Solidarity flourished as an underground movement, with help from the Central Intelligence Agency, the American labor movement and the Catholic Church, after the imposition of martial law in 1981. But it was by no means a smooth collaboration. Although the Reagan Administration, informed by a well-placed Polish spy, knew in the fall of 1981 of the Polish Government's increasingly serious preparations to impose martial law and arrest the leaders of Solidarity, it did not tip off the anti-Communist movement or warn the Polish Communist leadership against taking such action. (Senior American officials said later they did not want to encourage Solidarity to start a futile uprising; Gen. Wojciech Jaruzelski took the American silence as tacit approval of his intentions, particularly because he knew by November that his plans for martial law had been passed to the Americans by the spy within his ranks.)
The declaration of martial law on Dec. 12 indeed caught Solidarity flat-footed, and nearly all of its key leaders were easily rounded up. Mr. Bernstein and Mr. Politi add a potentially significant chapter to this story. They report that just days before martial law was imposed, Mr. Walters traveled to Rome and gave the Pope detailed intelligence suggesting a crackdown was imminent. According to the book, Mr. Walters showed the Pope photographs of Warsaw Pact troops moving toward the Polish border, and of heavy equipment being assembled -- "military vehicles, personnel carriers and tanks, for use by Polish forces." All of this raises a question not addressed by the book: Did the Pope fail to pass the word to Solidarity or church leaders in Poland? Martial law seemed to take the Polish episcopate totally by surprise, and on Dec. 13 Jozef Cardinal Glemp, the Polish primate, urged Poles not to resist. Later, the Pope would issue a much sterner statement, saying that the church stood "in solidarity with the Polish nation."
The covert assistance from the United States enabled Solidarity to publish and distribute a host of anti-Communist periodicals. They stirred up trouble and raised Poles' spirits through some drab, depressing years. In the end, however, the 1988 decision by the Polish leader, General Jaruzelski, to begin the church-sponsored "Round Table" talks with Solidarity and other anti-Communists did not result from the pressures of a secret operation -- either the church's or the C.I.A.'s. The Rube Goldberg device that was Communist economics had finally reached a point of irrevocable crisis. Inflation was spinning wildly out of control. Production was dropping. Food was hardly available. Painful reforms were needed, and only the anti-Communist opposition had the popular standing and legitimacy needed to ask that the people make the requisite sacrifices. General Jaruzelski eventually agreed to allow Solidarity to run candidates in a Parlimentary election. Months later, the Berlin wall was coming down. The Pope, as quoted in "His Holiness," offers a balanced summation of these events and his role, saying: "The tree was already rotten. I just gave it a good shake and the rotten apples fell."
For a book so focused on Poland and Solidarity, "His Holiness" does little to explain the complex relationship between Poles and their church. Under the Communists, every Mass, every religious gesture, every saint's day, was celebrated as a gesture against "Oni" -- "Them." As became emphatically clear after the fall of Communism, these outward signs of devotion did not mean that Poles universally shared the Pope's views on abortion, divorce or sex education. When the church began pushing its social agenda in the early 1990's, particularly a legislated ban on abortion, its standing quickly plummeted. The politicians with which it was most closely identified fared poorly in subsequent elections. Mr. Bernstein and Mr. Politi explain this startling change by writing that "Poland had changed." That is one possibility. But it seems more likely that the assumptions about Poles' religious allegiance were misguided in the first place, and that senior officials at the C.I.A., the Vatican and the Politburo, and perhaps the Pope himself, did not understand the Poles' view of their church as well as they thought they did.

Copyright 1996 The New York Times Company 
The New York Times

PUB-DATE: September 29, 1996

SECTION: Section 7; Page 14; Column 1; Book Review Desk 

LENGTH: 2425 words

HEADLINE: Doing Our Homework

BYLINE: By Sara Mosle; Sara Mosle is a contributing writer for The New York Times Magazine.

 BODY:

The Schools We Need And Why We Don't Have Them.
By E. D. Hirsch Jr.
317 pp. New York:
 Doubleday. $24.95.
 
Horace's Hope What Works for the American High School.
By Theodore R. Sizer.
198 pp. Boston:
Houghton Mifflin Company. $22.95.
Has anyone noticed that the culture wars have ended? In 1987, when E. D. Hirsch's "Cultural Literacy" appeared, the book was widely regarded as another right-wing grenade lobbed in the direction of multiculturalists, who were attacking the literary canon and, by extension, all of Western civilization. Mr. Hirsch's book may be best remembered for its appendix, a compendium of some "5,000 essential names, phrases, dates and concepts" that "literate Americans know"; it caused him to be lumped with conservatives like Allan Bloom and Dinesh D'Souza, who bemoaned the "closing of the American mind" and the rise of political correctness on college campuses. 
 Some old-fashioned liberals, among them Arthur Schlesinger Jr., were sympathetic to Mr. Hirsch's arguments. Troubled by multiculturalism's ethnocentric offshoots, they fretted over the "disuniting" of American culture. But most progressive educators accused him of dimwittedly promoting the rote memorization of isolated facts; Theodore R. Sizer, an advocate of project-based learning, is their representative. Left-leaning radicals denounced Mr. Hirsch's list as culturally imperialistic: an attempt to impose Eurocentric values on an increasingly diverse population that had little interest in dead white males or their putatively "great" books.
 But no one talks about dead white males anymore; the phrase sounds like the kind of shorthand found in the personal ads: "DWM seeks SWF to share necrophiliac fantasies, classics and fun." Conservatives no longer seem particularly concerned with what children learn; school "choice" has replaced the culture wars on the conservatives' agenda. Exactly what's meant by "choice" isn't always clear. When Republicans talk about it, they're generally referring to "vouchers," or government stipends that all parents, ideally, could use to send their kids to private or even parochial schools. When Democrats talk about it, they're generally referring to "public school choice" or "charter" programs that allow parents to pick which public school their child will attend instead of being assigned to one by geographic zone.
 Just how dramatically this new debate has altered the political landscape is evident in Daniel McGroarty's "Break These Chains: The Battle for School Choice" (Forum/Prima Publishing, $23.95). At one point Mr. McGroarty, a former speechwriter for President George Bush and an occasional contributor to National Review, praises the "Afrocentric focus" of two private, voucher schools in Milwaukee and notes that its "elevating effect . . . on students constitutes an overwhelmingly positive part of the school culture." A conservative touting the ameliorative effects of ethnocentric curriculums on students' self-esteem? Who'd have thunk it? Such a conclusion would have been inconceivable among Republicans just a decade ago. "For some conservative supporters of school choice," Mr. McGroarty concedes, "the Kwanzaa celebrations will come as a shock." But he notes: "Defenders of choice in theory must have the courage of their convictions to accept choice in practice" -- even if it means packing kids off to ethnocentric schools.
 By the terms of this new debate, Mr. Hirsch is a dyed-in-the-wool liberal. In "The Schools We Need: And Why We Don't Have Them" he argues against vouchers and for a national standardized curriculum. The idea won't win him many friends among liberals or conservatives. Most progressive educators believe that schools should be free to develop their own curriculums without having to worry about pressing students to pass exams. Although conservatives often cite the performance of schools in other countries to demonstrate how poorly our own schools prepare American students, Mr. Hirsch suggests that international comparisons actually argue against vouchers. "The Dutch instituted publicly financed school choice many years ago, and today the Netherlands exhibits the least consistent school quality in northern continental Europe," he writes. "Today, some 16 percent of Dutch schools perform below par, compared with 1 to 5 percent of below-par schools in the nonchoice systems of Sweden, Denmark, Japan and South Korea." What these nations do share is a national curriculum. Mr. Hirsch believes that establishing a common curriculum will do far more to make our schools better and more equitable than school choice will alone.
Progressive educators like Mr. Sizer contend that "national standards" will simply lead to more standardized testing. Mr. Hirsch recognizes the limitations of many standarized tests, and offers suggestions for making them both more nuanced and more comprehensive. And he cautions against state governments' using such tests as a "blunt legislative instrument" to impose reform; they must also provide the resources necessary to make meeting such standards possible. But Mr. Hirsch maintains that such tests are far fairer than more subjective means of assessing student performance, such as "portfolios" -- compilations of students' creative work -- which are currently in vogue. And he points to countries like France, where even recent immigrants perform well on national tests that are based on a common curriculum. In short, there's something here for everyone to hate -- partly because Mr. Hirsch is so refreshingly unpolemical. His book presents a sophisticated, scholarly and often compelling argument and it deserves serious consideration, whatever one's political predilections. 
 Mr. Hirsch begins by challenging "the false polarity" between different methods of teaching -- "traditional versus modern," "boring versus interesting," "fragmented versus integrated" -- and by sensibly noting that a good teacher can make almost any subject interesting. "The presumption that the affairs of one's neighborhood are more interesting than those of faraway times and places," he writes, "is contradicted in every classroom that studies dinosaurs and fairy tales." He points out that even such simple issues as desk placement have taken on epic importance. "Placing the desks in semicircles or helter-skelter symbolized the triumph of good, free, modern education over inhumane, artificial, constraining traditional education."
 Many of these ideas derive directly from Rousseau and the Romantics, who thought that children should develop naturally, uncorrupted by civilization and book learning. But as Mr. Hirsch shows, the maxim "teach the child, not the subject" has been prevalent in the better education schools since at least the 1930's. (Mr. Hirsch's own academic field is not education: a professor of English at the University of Virginia, he is also the author of books on Blake and Wordsworth.) As Emerson complained, "We are shut up in schools . . . for 10 or 15 years & come out at last with a bellyfull of words & do not know a thing." It's perhaps poetic justice that Emerson's books are no longer read, partly because his anti-intellectual attitude has flourished. Mr. Hirsch describes Huck Finn as the "archetypal American antibook figure," who decides to "light out for the Territory ahead of the rest, because Aunt Sally she's going to adopt me and sivilize me, and I can't stand it." But this was easy for Twain to say; he already had book learning -- as has anyone who reads about Huck's adventures. It's this hypocritical attitude of the scholarly Twain toward his unsophisticated subject that many well-meaning educators, according to Mr. Hirsch, also display toward their disadvantaged students.
 Mr. Hirsch argues that our schools, in general, do little to improve the economic chances of poor children, and he blames progressive theories of education, which emphasize how children learn rather than what they learn, for this failure. And he suggests that interest in the Internet and other technology has only served to reinforce this inequity by convincing educators that the ability to "access" information, rather than knowledge of particular subjects, will be of primary importance in the 21st century. But research in cognitive psychology completely contradicts such assumptions. You can't learn only how to learn about, say, the Civil War. To learn about any subject, you have to build on previous knowledge -- what cognitive psychologists call "domain specific" information -- which in turn makes more learning possible.
 This is what we mean when we talk about the "learning curve" of a new subject being steep. The more books one reads about the Civil War, the easier they are to read because the main characters in the story become increasingly familiar. But the ability to read about American history doesn't necessarily translate into the ability to read, say, about physics or Shakespeare. To the novice, these subjects have their own daunting and unfamiliar vocabularies.
 When Mr. Hirsch talks about "cultural literacy," what he's talking about is the full set of information, both fragmentary and complete, that a person possesses about not only culture but economics, history, science and math. Without such "intellectual capital," just reading a newspaper, with its references like "supply and demand," "Machiavellian tactics," "black holes" or "The Picture of Dorian Gray," can be overwhelming. The point
 isn't to memorize a list of phrases or words, but to have a working knowledge of enough subject areas to be able to decipher new information in a variety of disciplines as it comes along. Mr. Hirsch adduces Alfred North Whitehead's remark that the point of learning isn't to "cultivate the habit of thinking of what we are doing," as many educators claim, but to extend "the number of operations which we can perform without thinking about them." A child who must think about the meaning of every word in a story reads with great difficulty; it's only once reading becomes automatic that he or she can move on to "higher thinking" skills, such as understanding the motivations of a character or the causes of the Civil War.
 "The home, by default, and not the school . . . supplies most of the intellectual capital which enables children to acquire more," Mr. Hirsch argues, and it's this "discrepancy between students who are lucky enough to have gained the needed background knowledge at home and those who have to depend mainly on what they get sporadically in school" that results in the failure of many public schools to improve the economic fortunes of poorer children. "The learning of luckier students snowballs upon their initial advantage, while that of the less fortunate ones, dependent on what the incoherent American school offers, never even begins to gather momentum."
 For this reason, Mr. Hirsch sees the creation of a national curriculum, assessed by improved, content-based standardized exams, as the necessary precondition of greater equity and excellence in American education. Furthermore, for a population as mobile as America's, a national curriculum is the only way to assure that students who move frequently can pick up in their new schools where they left off in their old ones. He cites staggering statistics: one-fifth of all Americans relocate every year. A sixth of all third graders attend at least three different schools between the first and third grades. "In a typical inner-city school, only about half the students who start in September are still there in May."
 In "Horace's Hope: What Works for the American High School," Theodore R. Sizer offers an unconvincing rebuttal to Mr. Hirsch's arguments. Mr. Sizer is the chairman of the Coalition of Essential Schools, a nationwide association of very successful public schools. (Interestingly, he proves their success by citing the scores on standardized tests that he elsewhere criticizes.) All share Mr. Sizer's pedagogical philosophy, which advocates a kind of "less is more" and "integrated" approach to teaching; students might explore a single subject for several months and then present an "exhibition" of their work to demonstrate what they've learned. Mr. Sizer insists that his schools provide an intellectually rigorous environment, and I'm sure that they do, but he's too doctrinaire that his way is the only way. Mr. Hirsch, by contrast, applauds project-based learning, as long as it covers the necessary material.
 The bigger problem with the progressive movement has less to do with its methods per se than with the great difficulty of replicating its successes on a larger scale. As Mr. Hirsch points out, several generations of teachers have gone to education schools, received the gospel according to John Dewey, taught for many years and even retired. Still, progressive educators continue to insist that their ideas have not been "properly tried." Perhaps they haven't been. As Mr. Sizer himself acknowledges, creating a truly progressive school is not easy. But there's something ultimately impractical about a reform program that is so difficult to implement.
 Even Horace, Mr. Sizer's fictional composite character, introduced in 1984 in "Horace's Compromise," his landmark study of the American high school, has been at his reforms for quite a while. Mr. Sizer created Horace to express the collective frustrations of many high school teachers in "factory model" schools, and at first Horace seemed like an inspired device. In "Horace's School" (1992), he reappeared to help establish a reform program at his fictional school, Franklin High, and Mr. Sizer's imagined battles with parents, fellow teachers and administrators were entertaining and instructive. But in "Horace's Hope," the last installment in the trilogy, Horace seems a little worn out. Isn't it time that he collected his pension and retired?
 There is a case to be made for Horace's brand of reform, but Mr. Sizer fails to make it in this book. Horace's observations mostly seem like afterthoughts to Mr. Sizer's arguments. His writing is uncharacteristically uneven. He is at his best when describing the structural obstacles to good teaching: enormous schools and classes that prevent individual contact between teachers and students, no matter what the manner of instruction. But to judge by his own criteria -- that is, as an "exhibition" of higher thinking -- "Horace's Hope" is disappointing. Mr. Hirsch's book is far more substantial.

GRAPHIC: Drawing.

INDUSTRY: NAICS611710 EDUCATIONAL SUPPORT SERVICES (84%); NAICS511130 BOOK PUBLISHERS (84%); SIC7372 PREPACKAGED SOFTWARE (84%); SIC2731 BOOKS: PUBLISHING, OR PUBLISHING & PRINTING (84%); 

COUNTRY: UNITED STATES (95%); 

STATE: WISCONSIN, USA (79%); 

CITY: MILWAUKEE, WI, USA (79%);

HEADLINE: The 'Black' for '02 Open: Bow to Public Courses

BYLINE: By DAVID WINZELBERG 

 BODY:
SINCE it opened 60 years ago, the Black course at Bethpage State Park has been the course that Long Island golfers love to hate. From its strangling rough to its myriad of sand traps, its layout has frustrated even the best of players.
"Golfers like the challenge to try and defeat the Black," said Larry Schwartz of Huntington, who estimated that he has played the course in Farmingdale 100 times. "It's a masochistic sport." 
 "There is a certain challenge, a certain mystique," said Patrick Rossetti of Massapequa, who has been battling the Black for nine years. "It's a really great public course. There aren't that many, and this is one of them."
Triple bogeys notwithstanding, golfers have always pointed to the Black with pride as the crown jewel of public golf. Now the course, one of the most challenging in the Northeast, will be the site of the U.S. Open in 2002, confirming what Long Island golfers already knew.
"We've always known the course is a gem," Jim Evans, director of golf for Long Island state parks and a former Bethpage supervisor, said. "A guy with $20 can say, 'This is my home course, and the U.S. Open is going to be played here.' "
The U.S.Golf Association will use 3,500 volunteers to help manage the crowds of 25,000 to 30,000 people a day expected at Bethpage for the U.S. Open. Three days of practice rounds will precede the four-day tournament, in which a field of 156 players will compete for more than $2.5 million in prize money, and the prestige that comes with winning one of the four most coveted titles in the golfing world.
On a bench near the starter's booth on a recent Sunday afternoon, Paul Martingale of Lynbrook said that it would be "pretty slick" to be the host course. "I'll be able to see the shots that they're taking," Martingale said. "I'll know every hole that they're playing."
"This is the first time a government-owned golf course will have ever hosted the U.S. Open, and it's because Bethpage Black is such a great public course," said Bernadette Castro, State Commissioner of Parks, Recreation and Historic Preservation.
Except for the Pebble Beach Golf Links in California, where greens fees and a cart can cost $295, the Open has never been played at a true public course.
"It has been categorized as a private sport for the rich," the executive director of the Metropolitan Golf Association, Jay Mottola, said. "But that's not the orientation of the game today. This reinforces that. It's good for golf."
"Most of the outstanding golf courses are private, but most golf played is public," said David Fay, executive director of the United States Golf Association, "Bethpage is the quintessential public facility."
According to state parks officials, Bethpage is the largest public golf complex in the country. Once dubbed the "people's country club," golfers played more than 300,000 rounds on the five courses there last last year, generating $4.8 million in revenue.
A sixth course may be added, said Edward Wankel, deputy commissioner of operations. That course would be built through a public-private partnership on 175 mostly unused acres in the northwestern corner of the park, behind the picnic area. The new course could open by 1999.
The original complex is built around the former Lenox Hills Country Club, which the state purchased in 1929, and became what is now the Green course. That was followed by the Blue, the Red and the Black in the 1930's. The Yellow course was added in the 50's.
The Black was designed by Albert W. Tillinghast, who also designed the Red and the Blue. Many major championships, including U.S. Opens, have been played on other Tillinghast courses, which include Baltusrol in Springfield, N.J., and Winged Foot in Mamaroneck in Westchester County.
"Golf courses have pedigrees," Mr. Fay said. "And the Black has a very prominent pedigree. Tillinghast was one of the finest golf course architects of all time."
"You are talking about Rembrandt in terms of golf," said Chuck Workman, the head pro for 14 years."The course has kept up with modern technology. The ball has improved. The clubs have improved. But the integrity of the golf course is still there."
Some of Mr. Tillinghast's innovations, like elevated greens, structured bunkers and false fronts, are used on the Black, but according to Mr. Workman, it was his use of the natural terrain, including the prevailing winds, that makes the course so good.
"Tillinghast was a genius," an assistant supervisor, Mickey Walsh, said. "You can play another golf course and maybe remember two or three holes. After you play the Black, you remember something about every hole."
Mr. Walsh began as a maintenance worker at Bethpage in 1964, when the greens were cut by hand. Today a crew of five maintains the Black, less than half the industry standard, according to Mr. Walsh.
"I wouldn't match it to a country club," the manager, Jim Larsen, said.
Mr. Larsen added that some private clubs employed up to 15 or 16 workers for each course.
The Black is also open seven days and is closed once every two weeks, making maintenance difficult. At the height of the season play begins at first light, as early as 5 A.M. Around 9 P.M. the last golfers may be just coming off the course.
Because of its heavy use, the Black has been tamed somewhat in recent years. "We've made some changes to speed up play and make it more enjoyable for the weekend golfer," Mr. Larsen said. "We've tried to eliminate some of the hazard."
But despite those efforts, the course continues to feature its four-inch rough and 76 sand traps. "There are traps out there bigger than your backyard," said Mr. Walsh.
The par 71 Black plays to 7,065 yards from the blue championship tees, but the layout has a rating of 75.4 from the Metropolitan Golf Association, one of the highest in the New York metropolitan region.
To prepare for the Open, the Black will be tougher, with new tee boxes, deeper rough and, yes, even more sand. "There are traps out there that haven't even been used," Mr. Larsen said. "There are grass bunkers now. But they will be filled in with sand."
According to Mr. Fay, the U.S.G.A. will spend $1.5 million to $2 million to improve the course, with the major work on expanding some greens and the 18th hole. "There's not much you need to do," he added. "Just a shower, shave and a change of clothes."
The makeover is to begin in July, and the course could be closed as long as nine months during the work.
"They will improve it even further," Mr. Rossetti said. "There's no downside to having the U.S. Open."
Officials estimate that the tournament will inject $25 million into the economy. "There is a tremendous amount of benefit in having the Open," Ms. Castro said. "It's good for golf. It's good for the economy. It's a real lift for Long Island."
In addition to fees and admission revenue, the Long Island economy can expect to benefit through restaurants, hotels, car rental agencies, television crews and print press and various suppliers and supporters.
Long Island is no stranger to championship golf. In the 1920's and 30's legends like Walter Hagen, Bobby Jones and Gene Sarazen won major championships on Long Island courses, playing at the Inwood Country Club in Far Rockaway and the Fresh Meadow Country Club in Great Neck.
Besides the Northville Classic, which brings the Senior Professional Golf Association Tour to the Meadow Brook Club in Jericho each year, the last major events on the Island were the U. S. Opens in 1986 and 1995, at Shinnecock Hills in Southampton.
The Black is no stranger to tournaments. It has been host to the Metropolitan Open and the New York State Open.
Although the U.S. Open is six years away, speculation has begun over how the world's top players will fare.
"They'll have some good rounds," an assistant pro at Bethpage, Hiram Ruiz, said. "But they'll have some big ones, too."
"The course won't be eaten up," said Mr. Larsen.
"I think it's going to be an exceptional challenge," said Mr. Fay, whose job will be to determine how tightly to set up the course.
In the meantime, the Black will be around to confound seekers of par, who will still be greeted by the sign that reads, "Warning -- the Black course is an extremely difficult course which we recommend only for highly skilled golfers."
Then again, there is always the Yellow.
 
Notable Holes on the Black Course
5th Hole, par 4, 446 yards.
The elevated tee affords a scenic view of one of the toughest chances for par on the Island. Only a tee shot of more than 225 yards will clear unforgiving rough and reach the large bunkered fairway. The approach narrows to an elevated green that slopes away and is nearly surrounded by sand.
12th Hole, par 4, 480 yards.
Another big drive, about 230 yards, over bunkers and rough to reach the fairway on this sharp dogleg left, still leaving well more than 200 yards against the prevailing winds to the pin. The two-tier green is protected by traps on the left and one on the right.
15th Hole, par 4, 438 yards.
The fairway of this long par 4 slopes to the right, toward a huge bunker and knee-high rough. Thick woods line the left side. A dead-on approach is needed to hit the small two-tiered green with sand in front and to the left. Miss right and wind up 25 feet below the hole. Too far and land in trees.
17th Hole, par 3, 200 yards.
A tricky and shallow two-tiered green is the reward for clearing all the sand -- nearly 4,000 cubic yards -- that surrounds it. Bring a beach chair if you miss. DAVID WINZELBERG

HEADLINE: Desperation or Simple Greed?

BYLINE: By BILL SLOCUM 

 BODY:
ACCORDING to the state's Uniform Crime Report, which charts all arrests made in Connecticut, there were 30 percent fewer murders last year and burglary complaints declined 13 percent. Even car thefts were down.
But one category is notable for the way it keeps on rising: As with every year since 1992, 1995 was a bully year for embezzlement. Arrests in that category have more than doubled over the last four years, according to the report, to 111 last year, from 47 in 1992. 
 Law enforcement officials say that figure may well continue to rise in 1996. "We are getting more referrals from a variety of state agencies," said the Supervisory Assistant State's Attorney, Julia D. Dewey. "We are seeing all kinds of embezzlement, and the workload for cases is rising dramatically."
Mrs. Dewey heads the statewide prosecution bureau, an arm of the Chief State's Attorney's Office in Rocky Hill, which oversees investigations of white-collar crimes. Even though embezzlement remains statistically a minuscule element of the Connecticut crime scene, Mrs. Dewey said her office has become so inundated with such cases lately that "we could be doing this full-time."
There are many reasons given for the rise in embezzlement arrests, but one gets frequent mention.
"You have to look at the economy," said a spokesman for the State Police, Sgt. Dale Hourigan. "It hasn't been that well. A lot of people are desperate."
Although Connecticut is over the recession of the early 1990's, the recovery has been tepid in comparison to the rest of the country, with only a third of jobs lost returning since 1993, according to the Connecticut Business and Industry Association. Could the continuing popularity of downsizing among many large-scale employers be adding to a morale crunch among Connecticut's workers, enticing more of them to turn to crime?
"I suspect there is some correlation between the economic opportunities available to people and the measures they will take for economic security," said Robert Noonan, a management counselor with the Business and Industry Association. "Some will take illegal measures."
Mr. Noonan noted other possible causes for the rise, including drug use. He said his office gets queries about how to deal with suspicious employees every two weeks or so. "Often it is just petty theft, but occasionally it is more serious," he said.
Mrs. Dewey has a simpler, one-word explanation for the rash of embezzlement cases: Greed.
"Indirectly, money is not as readily available as it was during the 1980's," she said. "Investments have gone up, the real estate market is no longer booming, and life styles are harder to maintain. Conspicuous consumption is still up, if our cases are any indication. That's why we're having the embezzlements, because they are still spending."
Embezzled funds may be used to pay for everything from a child's education to jewelry and cars, Mrs. Dewey said. One woman was arrested after siphoning funds from the charity she worked for to pay for a series of plastic surgery operations.
One reason for the increase in arrests may be that victims, whether businesses or charities, are now more likely to report cases to authorities, rather than settle them privately or in civil court. "Before, businesses didn't want the public to know how easy it was," Mrs. Dewey said.
These days, Mr. Noonan added, "Among our members, I see very little tolerance for an employee shown to be involved in embezzlement."
Chief Inspector Paul J. Healy, who heads Mrs. Dewey's investigative team, cited better enforcement as a likely reason for the arrests. Utilizing the skills of accountants and former police officers, the statewide prosecution bureau looks into suspected cases by establishing a "fact pattern" in which audits and computer charts all play key parts.
In Mr. Healy's area of operations culprits are seldom violent, and often, when plagued by guilt, welcome the opportunity to come clean. "Ninety-five percent of the arrests we make are by appointment," he explained.
Embezzlement can take many forms, from not registering a sale and then pocketing the proceeds to stealing from a company cash register or from a corporate account. Some of the more enterprising schemes take the form of check-kiting or "lapping," in which an employee responsible for recording payments to a company pockets a payment and covers the imbalance by applying part of a larger payment to the incriminating invoice. A well-done lapping scheme can go undetected for years.
Particularly vulnerable to embezzlement are trust funds and estates where a single individual has sole authority.
"A common thread we see is a lack of basic accounting procedures, and a trust where the owner or complainant does not intrude," said Robert A. Brousseau, a fraud examiner with the statewide prosecution bureau.
Indeed, recently a former Evangelical Lutheran Church treasurer from Cheshire was arrested on charges he embezzled $800,000 from the New England Synod of the church. The Chief State's Attorney, John Bailey, noted that the treasurer, a certified public accountant, was able to delay an independent audit by convincing the synod that an independent review was unnecessary and expensive.
One case currently being handled by the State Police in central Connecticut involves Department of Transportation employees who allegedly used computer software-design equipment leased by the state to assist them in performing privately contracted, off-duty jobs. Lieut. John Mannion, head of the State Police central district's major crimes unit, says thousands of dollars in rental fees were unknowingly being paid by the state for the freelance assignments.
The computer is both friend and foe in embezzlement investigations. Mr. Healy called it "a better ally for us than for the perpetrator," since it is harder to conceal incriminating records in a central computer system. But he added those cases in which the computer is used to conceal fraud are "the toughest to solve."
"Ironically, one would assume those with jobs that supposedly require a higher intelligence would perpetuate more complex crimes," he said. "Yet we've found cases where highly educated lawyers do incredibly stupid things, like writing checks to themselves, while less-educated employees funnel embezzled checks through a computer."
One of the most sophisticated instances of embezzlement Mr. Healy recalled took place in an auto-parts store in northeastern Connecticut. The estimated loss was thought to exceed $200,000, but the suspected perpetrator was charged with only a a small fraction of that amount. "This guy was the manager who did the physical inventory, and he was able to delete all the records from the computer," Mr. Healy said.
Mr. Healy said his office routinely and increasingly assists the State Police and local police departments. And the embezzlements are being taken seriously indeed.
"I would compare an embezzler to an armed robber," said Lieut. Timothy Barry, head of the State Police's western district investigative unit. "A robber uses a gun, while the embezzler uses a pencil, but the results are the same."
 
Five Steps for Employers
ROBERT D. NOONAN of the Connecticut Business and Industry Association says business owners who suspect they are being victimized by embezzlers should follow a five-step plan:
1. Consider signs of a potential problem, like an inability to reconcile cash receivable. "It can be worse when an individual has multi-function responsibilities and can fix their trail," he said.
2. If suspicion arises, verify the loss. "It's a fundamental step, but it can be overlooked."
3. If a loss is discovered, draw up an investigation plan. Do so quietly. "It's necessary to identify the source of the losses without bringing attention to the fact the losses have been discovered."
4. Begin a process to establish or refute the conclusion that one or more employees are involved in wrongdoing. "This may involve an internal group of managers working in conjunction with a corporate counsel, law enforcement and auditors."
5. Once you identify someone as being involved in an improper activity, the management team should guide the company's handling of the suspect or suspects. Mr. Noonan recommends caution, noting that charging an employee with embezzlement without adequate cause can invite a lawsuit.
Finally, the company must decide whether to lodge criminal charges or pursue. the matter through civil means. "You may have an employee exercising poor judgment who may not think he or she is breaking the law," Mr. Noonan explained. "In other cases, it's more clear they are involved in an illegal activity." BILL SLOCUM

HEADLINE: Hospital Care In the Face Of More Mergers

BYLINE: By ELSA BRENNER 

DATELINE: WHITE PLAINS

 BODY:
UNAWARE that changes at hospitals have left administrators wringing their hands, Simona Harrison hobbled out of the White Plains Hospital Center here on a recent afternoon blissfully unconcerned. At that moment, she was only happy that recent advances in medical care had allowed her to be going home after just a few short hours.
"It seemed like I was in and out of there in almost no time," said Mrs. Harrison, an Ossining resident who underwent orthoscopic surgery in the morning for a torn cartilage on her right knee. "All I know is that for me, everything was great." 
 Mrs. Harrison's speedy recovery from surgery can be attributed in part to state-of-the-art procedures and new drug therapies. In fact, about 60 percent of all surgery performed in recent years is ambulatory, allowing patients to arrive in the morning and return home by evening without having to stay in the hospital even overnight.
Technology, however, is only one reason that Mrs. Harrison's visit was so brief. Managed-care companies are dictating shorter hospital stays, and many doctors and hospital administrators are reporting that patients must be very sick indeed to be admitted -- which comes as good news for most people.
"Who would want to stay overnight when you can be home with your family?" said Richard H. Gabel, a White Plains psychiatrist who had surgery for a ruptured tendon in his ankle. "They treated me royally, told me to ice my foot and keep it elevated -- and sent me home."
For hospitals, though, the emphasis on ambulatory surgery and other forms of outpatient care means more unused beds and less revenue from traditional sources.
Institutions' incomes have been further limited by fee schedules set by managed-care insurers, which tightly control how much hospitals can charge for services.
"Increasingly, there's the pressure to cut costs in what has become a highly competitive market," said Arthur E. Weintraub, president of the Northern Metropolitan Hospital Association, which represents health care institutions in seven counties.
It is within the context of an industry shakeout that Northern Westchester Hospital Center in Mount Kisco and White Plains Hospital Center here announced an agreement on Sept. 17 to affiliate -- the latest in a hurried round of alliances, mergers and other agreements among many hospitals in Westchester.
"It's like everything is on fast forward: we're moving ahead at breakneck speed," Mr. Weintraub said, likening some of the affiliations to shotgun marriages. "The alternative of going solo is not very viable anymore." He said hospitals in New York City and the state hope that by forming larger and more powerful bargaining units, they will be able to meet the insurance companies head on and negotiate better deals.
Other recent affiliation agreements in Westchester have included the merger in June of St. Agnes Hospital in White Plains with Our Lady of Mercy Medical Center in the Bronx under the Our Lady of Mercy Healthcare System.
In Manhattan, two of the oldest and most prestigious hospitals -- New York Hospital-Cornell Medical Center and Columbia-Presbyterian Hospital Center -- announced in July that they would form one of the largest nonprofit health care systems in the country. That announcement followed news in mid-June that Mount Sinai and New York University Medical Centers would merge. Less than two weeks later, Beth Israel and Long Island Jewish Medical Centers made a similar announcement.
Analysts have said that the mergers signify that New York is finally catching up with the rest of the country. While managed-care providers have not been bound by state-set rates, private indemnity insurers have. That will change on Jan. 1, when New York's new Health Care Reform Act, which many observers are describing as landmark legislation, takes effect.
The new law will open the door to even tougher contract negotiations between all insurers and hospitals, and it is expected that some hospitals may not survive the new pressures. In the meantime, few are waiting for the law to take effect, and most have already formed alliances. These merger vows -- or as some are calling them, marriages of convenience -- have taken many forms, including corporate affiliations, joint ventures and outright mergers.
The arrangement between Northern Westchester Hospital Center and White Plains Hospital Center represents a first in the county between two virtually equal institutions. They have formed a parent corporation, Health Star Network, but the hospitals say there are no plans to consolidate medical services, although they expect to achieve some economies by sharing information systems.
The board of Health Star will have 10 members with equal representation. Walton E. Burdick, chairman of the new corporation, said that the only layoffs expected were through attrition and each hospital would continue its own mission.
Other arrangements have involved a stronger institution assisting a weaker one, as is the case with St. John's Riverside Hospital in Yonkers and Yonkers General Hospital, which is classified by the state as a financially distressed institution.
A similar arrangement has been struck between New Rochelle Hospital Medical Center and Mount Vernon Hospital, which is also classified as financially distressed.
"Especially a hospital like ours cannot afford to stand alone," said Richard L. Petrillo, the chief executive officer at the Mount Vernon institution.
The two hospitals formed a holding company to gain leverage in contract negotiations with insurers by representing more people and covering a broader area, said John R. Spicer, the chief executive officer at New Rochelle Hospital Medical Center.
The hospitals will share services like housekeeping, information management and human resources. In addition, Mount Vernon, which has sent very sick babies to New York City hospitals, will now send them to New Rochelle's high-risk nursery, and New Rochelle will refer its mental-health patients to the Mount Vernon institution, which specializes in such services.
At United Hospital Medical Center in Port Chester, Kevin W. Dahill, president and chief executive officer, said his hospital, like the others, had anticipated the changes several years ago and formed a relationship with New York Hospital-Cornell Medical Center, which became the parent company.
But the hospital also plans to slash its $72 million budget by $8.4 million over the next 18 months, and it has cut about 30 staff positions out of 930 and plans another 30 cuts in the coming months.
"For the first time, we in the hospital business are having to play by the same rules as most businesses in the private sector," he said.
"It's certainly a volatile environment now," he added, "but it's also a great opportunity for those of us who have some vision."
And while most administrators agreed with that sentiment, they also criticized the managed-care groups for what Jon B. Schandler, the president and chief executive office at White Plains Hospital, called "putting the squeeze on us."
At New Rochelle Hospital, Mr. Spicer also complained that the managed-care groups were "calling the shots."
Doctors, too, view the managed-care companies as the enemy. Mark L. Fox, a Scarsdale ear, nose and throat specialist and past president of the Westchester County Medical Society, a professional organization, said his colleagues were angry at the insurers for dictating how care is delivered.
"In the good old days, which was just a couple of years ago, a patient could see any physician in a traditional fee-for-service arrangement," he said.
"But now they come to a specialist only after a primary-care referral," he said, "and as a result, we end up seeing patients that are a lot sicker by they time they follow all the steps the insurers demand."
The managed-care companies, however, see things from a different perspective. "The changes are not unlike any other open-market system," said Leslie S. Moran, a spokeswoman for the Health Maintenance Organization Council of New York in Albany, which insures close to five million people.
She denied that managed-care groups set procedures and rates that jeopardize patient safety, adding: "Whenever an industry reaches a certain penetration in a market, you shift the paradigm and challenge the way things have been done. That makes people nervous."

HEADLINE: Where Machinists Can Get Training

 BODY:
Regarding the lead article ("The New World of (Skilled) Factory Jobs," Sept. 15), I would like your readership to know that Macdac Engineering is pro-actively addressing the shortage of skilled machinists by offering, free of charge, training in off-line Computer Numerical Control (CNC) programming.
This free training is available to unemployed machinists, manufacturers with apprenticeship programs and any shop and vocational school teacher throughout New England. 
 Classes are taught using Mastercam, a widely used manufacturing software system for creating machining programs for milling, lathe and wire EDM CNC machine tools.
As a reseller of Computer-Aided Design/Computer-Aided Manufacturing products and services, we readily recognized the machinist shortage problem several years ago.
Small and mid-sized manufacturers often complained of a lack of skilled workers for hiring.
We felt it was our corporate responsibility to offer training to help reseed the local economy with skilled machinists, educated in the latest technology and enabling New England manufacturers to stay competitive.
As continuing evidence of this shortage, we find that machinists who successfully complete our program are immediately snatched up for jobs by local employers with whom we have a relationship.
We recently expanded our offer to teachers -- who have limited access to CNC programming training and frequently pay for it themselves. By training the area's teachers for free in the industry's No. 1 machining software, we saw it as a way to bring real economic opportunity closer to students interested in the profession.
Macdac believes that apprenticeship programs are the key to revitalizing the region's manufacturing climate because they provide basic skills for a well-rounded machinist. Our training program is also hands-on, teaching apprentices how to program using the actual types of parts their company manufactures -- molds, or tool and die, for example.
To qualify for our training, people need some hands-on machining experience and the ability to read a blueprint. Familiarity with computers is also helpful.
Individuals can get more information or register for the training classes, by contacting us at Macdac, telephone 860-870-8182 or E-mail: info@macdac.com. We offer two convenient New England training sites. Classes are held every other Wednesday at our corporate offices in Ellington and every other Friday at our Northboro, Mass., offices.

DAVE STANLEY
 
The writer is president of Macdac Engineering Inc., in Ellington.

HEADLINE: What to Wear to School?

BYLINE: By ROBERT E. TOMASSON 

 BODY:
AS he climbed on the back of his mother's chair while waiting to get a new jacket and pants at the Rogers Magnet School in Stamford, 5-year-old Sam Kesler may not have been especially aware that he was about to adopt one of education's oldest and now newest trademarks: school uniforms.
As old as the nation's parochial and other private schools, school uniforms are being increasingly looked at by public school officials and by parents as a positive tool to instill school pride and discipline while reducing the social and economic competitiveness of children trying to outdress each other. 
 "We wanted to look at reducing the whole issue of 'What am I going to wear to school?' and 'Do I look stylish and in line with someone else?' " said the Rogers School principal, Mary Jennings. The point was to create "more of a sense of belonging to a larger organization that has a meaningful purpose, and that purpose is getting an education."
For Sam's mother, Maureen Kesler, the navy blue jackets and white shirts, as well as the tartan skirts and white blouses for girls, are a positive addition to her son's school.
She was well familiar with uniforms, having spent her childhood in parochial schools. But her son will have an option she did not have: One day a week, Sam will get to wear whatever he wants to school, said Mrs. Kesler, to satisfy whatever sartorial independence he might develop.
For two days this month, scores of parents lined up to make their selections from racks of jackets and skirts and crates of navy socks and clip-on ties along with T-shirts and sweatshirts emblazoned with the school name. Anticipating the voluntary dress code that takes effect Tuesday at Rogers, several children were already wearing the new outfits.
While the State Department of Education does not track the number of public schools that have school uniform programs, queries to uniform companies and local boards indicate that the number is in the range of 40.
In addition to that at Rogers, a voluntary dress code went into effect this month at the Toquam School, another of Stamford's four elementary magnet schools (the city has 12 elementary schools).
In addition to Stamford, the Neil Roberts School Uniform Store is responding to dress programs this fall in Waterbury and New Britain. Bridgeport and New Haven also have dress code programs.
By and large, the adoption of uniforms in public schools seems to be increasing along with the growth of magnet schools with their distinctive curriculums meant to attract students from outside immediate neighborhood school districts.
In its fourth year as a magnet school, Rogers seems to have accomplished much of the turnaround it sought, with the uniforms adding a finishing touch.
The school, situated off Route 1 in a working class area of Stamford, had been a tired, largely segregated institution. Today about half of the 720 students receive free lunches and 64 percent are minority students. About a quarter of the students are selected by lottery from a pool of those seeking to attend one of the four elementary magnet schools.
To a recent visitor who spent two days there, the school presented an exuberant atmosphere with a relaxed discipline. Every classroom in the school -- one of eight nationally that is a demonstration school of the Yale Bush Center for Child Development and Social Policy -- has four computers.
Maureen Tobin, a parent of two children at Rogers, grew up in England where school uniforms are standard. She said she's an enthusiastic supporter of the Rogers program and described the school's academics as "very comparable" to those of Spence, a Manhattan private school her older daughter attended.
But attitudes and implementation of dress codes are by no means standardized. The Toquam Magnet School also adopted a dress code this month that relied on what Evelyn Panos, the assistant principal, termed "attitude orientations."
Instead of inviting a uniform company to the school, the school has started a campaign aimed at establishing "a very positive dress code" that pointedly excludes T-shirts and droopy pants. Samples of appropriate clothing are displayed and parents are told of stores where they may be bought.
Elsewhere, two other schools are illustrative not only of different dress codes but also of the extremes of public education in Connecticut.
One of the first public schools in the state to adopt uniforms was the Helene Grant School in New Haven where the experience over past last eight years was described as fantastic by the principal, Jeffie Frazier. "It has given the children self-esteem, promoted creativity," said Mrs. Frazier, and created greater classroom participation by children previously inhibited because of what they felt was their inadequate appearance.
The dress program at Helene Grant is atypical -- mandatory, albeit tacitly. "Parents who send their children here know we have a dress code," Mrs. Frazier explained.
Eric G. Norfeldt: A Classical Education is the name of a new magnet school that opened this fall in West Hartford. The principal, Penny Jojin, described a regimen of heavy homework, French in kindergarten and Latin in the fifth grade. Mrs. Jojin had been in favor of establishing a dress code, perhaps embellished by a school emblem similar to those of private schools.
After extensive discussion with parents, three options were offered: school uniforms or a dress code to include rules such as tucked-in shirts and mandatory belts or keeping the status quo where students and their parents made individual choices on dress. The overwhelming response, the principal said, was for the status quo.
Mrs. Jojin said that the school is in a largely white, affluent area, and that while students from outside the district have boosted the minority enrollment to about 12 percent, many of the traditional arguments used in favor of school uniforms (the need to suppress gang colors, for example) did not apply.
There seems little chance that school uniforms will progress beyond elementary schools.
In New Britain, where two magnet schools have had successful uniform programs, parents and students were asked their opinions. The assistant superintendent of schools, Dr. James Rhinesmith, said that parents of high school students showed little interest in uniforms, and that while the parents of middle school students liked the idea, their children did not.
In the elementary schools, he said, parents and students alike supported the regimented program.

HEADLINE: At Universal It's Only a Movie

BYLINE: By AARON LATHAM; AARON LATHAM's contribution to Hollywood rides was the mechanical bull in "Urban Cowboy," for which he wrote the screenplay.

 BODY:
"HOW wet do you want to get?" asked the guide. "Very," said my 19-year-old daughter. "Then sit in the front row." The big wooden doors -- proclaiming JURASSIC PARK -- swung slowly open like jaws, and we floated inside.
Riding up front in an inflatable rubber raft, my daughter, Taylor, and I entered a spacious, peaceful lagoon. The theme music from the movie welcomed us to a tranquil, lost world. The big California sky poured down sunshine and illuminated the pale green foliage. This place was wonderful, but I wasn't fooled. I had been warned: The bottom was going to fall out. 
 Our 25-passenger raft drifted down a creek that got narrower and narrower. The plants grew thicker and closer, seeming to advance on us. The leaves got darker now, blocking out much of the light. Gently rippling waterfalls gave way to geysers. A 50-foot ultrasaurus ate a tree.
On the left bank, two small "compy" dinosaurs fought over a Jurassic Park popcorn box. The disembodied voice of the actor Richard Kiley, our host on the tour, explained that great pains had been taken to make sure that the dinosaurs ate a properly balanced diet. We all smiled, but I still kept wondering: When was the bottom going to fall out?
Fog rolled in. A stegosaurus mother, protecting her child, swung her plated tail at us. The water ran faster and darker. Something was wrong. The electrical fences were shorting out. A wrecked Jeep dangled from a guard rail directly over our heads. An empty raft drifted by carrying a turkey-sized "spitter" dinosaur: It reared up and spat at us!
But we were still all right. When was the bottom going to fall out?
Alarms clanged, pipes broke, a catwalk collapsed. Then we saw the gap in the fence that was supposed to protect us from the predators. Our raft veered into the bedlam of a pumping station coming apart at the seams.
Then the bottom fell out!
Our raft plunged straight down in total darkness.
Jurassic Park -- The Ride opened as part ofUniversal Studios Hollywood this summer and has been setting records ever since. The area's attendance is up an amazing 40 percent over last year. On July 5, it set a one-day record of 43,000 visitors. (At about $30 a ticket, that works out to almost $1.3 million for one day.) The ride is so big that it has even been credited with helping to fuel a new tourist boom in California, which had struggled after years of earthquakes, riots, and recession. Tourism is expected to be up about 6 percent statewide this year.
Actually a visit to Universal Studios isn't a tour in the way, say, a tour of Venice is a tour. A guide doesn't point out this building on the right and that other one on the left. There aren't any churches or museums or many other landmarks. Nor is there a behind-the-scenes look at Hollywood at work, which would really be quite boring since movie making is the slowest business on earth next to fossil manufacture.
What Universal Studios Hollywood really is is a trip into what it feels like to be in a movie. It's not about what it's like to be an actor in a film, but rather what it's like to be a character in a Hollywood adventure story. It isn't about what Laura Dern experiences, but rather what "Dr. Ellie Sattler" goes through.
"We try to create situations that allow you to be a participant, not a passive observer," says Phil Hettema, who was the lead designer on the Jurassic Park ride. "We want you to be the hero of the story."
This approach applies not only to Jurassic Park, but also to most of the other rides in the park.
When I came face to face with King Kong inside an innocent-looking sound stage, I wasn't worried about what he was going to do to Fay Wray: I was concerned about what he was about to do to me. I felt like a Nathan's hot dog looking into hungry eyes.
Later, as we rode a Bay Area subway, I felt the earth begin to shake. Water mains broke. Wires broke and crackled. A police car slammed into the side of our subway car and knocked us off the rails. I had an experience entirely different from anything felt by Charlton Heston or Ava Gardner. They did their quake scenes in hundreds of boring pieces, but the tour puts the pieces together and plays them at full speed. I grabbed my daughter and held on tight. She laughed at me. Then a 60,000-gallon flood slammed the car.
Having somehow survived the Big One, No. 7 on the Richter scale, we made our way Back to the Future -- or at any rate to a ride by that name. We climbed aboard Doc Brown's time machine, a converted DeLorean. I naturally hit my head on one of the silver winglike doors. We strapped ourselves in and took off through time chasing a villain who was a threat to the time-space continuum. The time machine rose, flew, dodged this way and that and then headed right at the clock tower in the old town square. Look out!
Now we were more in a movie than ever, for we weren't hurtling at a real tower but rather one projected on a screen that was eight stories high. But it certainly felt as if we were about to hit bricks and mortar. Ouch! We crashed right through the clock -- our DeLorean shuddered -- and we found ourselves flying over the crackly, smoky volcanic crust of the new and cooling Earth. Our DeLorean flew right down the throat of an erupting volcano. Then we were in the age of the dinosaurs. A big rex gave us a toothy smile and we flew down his throat, too.
Coming off the ride, I not only felt carsick but didn't know any more about filming special effects than I did when I went in. But the object wasn't to impart information but to provide a feeling. Back to the Future -- The Ride is an extension of the film maker's art rather than just another stop on a tour. It mixes actual movement, effects like real smoke, overwhelming sound and a wrap-around visual world. The Back to the Future ride suggests a possible road to the movies' future.
And now forward to the past: In 1915, a movie pioneer named Carl Laemmle bought the 230-acre Taylor chicken ranch on the north side of the Hollywood Hills. He set up a motion picture studio on the ranch, but he kept the chickens. Laemmle figured movies might go out of fashion, but eggs wouldn't.
To help defray the cost of movie making, he decided to sell tickets to people who wanted to see his movies being made. He charged them 25 cents, which included a box lunch with chicken and egg salad sandwiches. The spectators watched from bleachers where they were encouraged to cheer the heros and boo the villains. The directors told the crowds that the actors usually did a better job if they were given noisy encouragement. Of course, the movies being made were silent. As the spectators were leaving the studio, they were offered a good deal on eggs and many bought.
When sound came along in 1927, the Universal tour was shut down because the spectators made too much noise. The studio also had to get rid of its zoo -- 30 lions, 10 leopards, elephants and lots of monkeys -- because the animals were noisy too.
After a lapse of almost four decades, Universal came up with a concept for reviving the tour, and again it involved lunch. In 1964, when the studio noticed that its commissary wasn't doing much business, it decided to allow tour buses to stop for lunch at Universal.
This business was so successful that it soon expanded to include a visit to the commissary basement where visitors were treated to a makeup demonstration by a movie professional. They also got a close-up look at some of Edith Head's Oscar-winning costumes. And a couple of stunt men showed up to put on a phony fight.
So was born the Universal Studios Tour sequel. The staff consisted of a ticket seller ($2.50 for adults and $1.50 for children), two guides and two bus drivers. That first year the tour recorded 39,000 tickets sold -- considerably less than its new single-day record. Now over five million people visit each year.
Lots of the early visitors came hoping to see stars and many were disappointed, but not all. John Wayne used to drop in at tour headquarters to eat hot dogs at lunch. Telly (Kojak) Savalas often handed out lollipops to visitors. John Travolta once plowed his car into a tour bus and stayed to chat.
But one of the most fascinating stories involves not somebody running into the tour but someone running away from it. When he was just 18, Steven Spielberg took the Universal Studios Tour. He went AWOL, simply wandering away from the tour to explore on his own. He soon picked out a likely looking bungalow, sneaked inside, found an empty office, and set up shop. The next day, he entered the studio, carrying an empty briefcase (except for lunch), walked knowingly past the studio guard, and reported to "his" office. This time he brought along some plastic letters so he could add his name to the bungalow's directory. So Steven Spielberg went to "work" at Universal. His foot in the door, however insecurely, he met studio executives, impressed them . . . and shortly went on to make "Jaws," "Close Encounters of the Third Kind," "E.T.," and "Jurassic Park," all for Universal, his original Hollywood "home." That Universal tour keeps paying dividends of one kind and another.
Of course, now Mr. Spielberg helps design the tour that originally brought him to the studio. His involvement began in earnest with the transformation of his movie "E.T." into a ride. He wanted to put visitors in the bicycle seat and let them ride over the city that appears in perfect miniature below. Then he added a development not seen in the movie: We go "home" with E.T. to his planet. The E.T. Adventure opened in 1991.
Having enjoyed that ride, Spielberg then threw himself into the amusement park version of his "Jurassic Park." Universal spent $110 million on the ride, which is about what a high-tech adventure movie usually costs.
We hit the bottom of the black hole. I was shaken but thankful to be alive and to have the big one behind me. Our raft started to struggle up out of the dark abyss. My breathing was slowly returning to normal. I smiled benignly on my daughter. I liked this ride. I was feeling just what the ride designers wanted me to feel.
Then I saw the most frightening monster that ever shook the earth with its heavy steps. A Tyrannosaurus rex four stories tall stood astride our poor creek. He came at us out of the shadows of nightmare. I stared up at a toothy head that was as big and heartless as a cement truck.
Then the bottom really did drop out! While I and everybody else were staring up at the king of tyrants, the earth simply fell away. This ride had quite deliberately outmaneuvered us . . . convinced us the worst was over . . . waited for us to relax our guard . . . then fired its biggest gun. The fear of falling is one of mankind's oldest, most primal fears. Falling and dragons! We had both. Our raft pointed almost straight down. I told myself to hang on because it would be over soon. But it just wouldn't end. We fell and fell and fell . . . 84 feet, the longest man-made water drop in the world.
Then we hit the lagoon below and a tidal wave engulfed us. I have never been wetter, not even scuba diving. Every freckle was wet. Every hair was drenched. Every piece of paper in my wallet was soaking and turning back into wood pulp. Maybe that's what killed the dinosaurs: they all drowned.
While I tried to catch my breath, Taylor pointed out a shaving cream can lying on the bank of the lagoon. It looked like the red-white-and-blue can in which the greedy movie villain tried to steal some dinosaur embryos from Jurassic Park. But what were these embryos doing here? And what would happen if the can somehow floated out to sea? What disasters would occur if the dinosaurs escaped from the island? Our ride was ending, but there was clearly going to be more trouble ahead.
How long would it be before this ride, like the movie, had a sequel? We would be back.
Several years ago, my Hollywood agent told me that one good way to sell a movie idea was to come up with the ride first. I laughed. I thought she was kidding.
 
Getting to, and into, the action
Universal Studios Hollywood, 100 Universal City Plaza, Universal City, (818) 508-9600, is between Hollywood and the San Fernando Valley. Take the Hollywood Freeway (101) to exits for Lankershim Boulevard or Universal Center Drive.
Open daily except Thanksgiving and Christmas, 7 A.M. to 11 P.M. Memorial Day Day to Labor Day, otherwise 9 A.M. to 7 P.M. Admission is $34; $26 for those aged 3 to 11; $29 for 60 and older.
