HEADLINE: Commercial Property/Jamaica;
Plans Advance for Office Complex in Jamaica

BYLINE: By JOHN HOLUSHA 

 BODY:
MAYBE this time it will actually happen. Twice before, developers have been selected for a two-acre city-owned site in central Jamaica, Queens, and twice the project, which has its roots in the Lindsay administration, has failed to go forward.
Now the Mattone Group, itself based in Queens, has been conditionally selected as the developer of an $80 million office-retail-entertainment complex on a long-vacant site on top of the Parsons/Archer Avenue subway station in the old downtown shopping area in the southeast part of the borough. 
 Significantly, anchor tenants for the project have been identified, with Sony Theaters prepared to put in a Magic Johnson 11- or 12-screen first-run movie house in an area that has been without a movie house for many years.
In addition, the Catholic Medical Center, which operates five hospitals in the city, has agreed to take space in the office tower that would rise over two floors of retail stores and the third floor, where the film screens would be situated.
The project would be the first major, mostly private, investment in this part of Queens in over two decades. Joseph M. Mattone Sr., the president of the Mattone Group, said the recognition that there is an underserved middle-class market in this part of the city is what may make the current effort successful.
"This is an urban renewal project that goes back at least 25 years," he said. "The problem in the past was a lack of tenant response to developers' efforts."
But, he said, there are 350,000 people living in the area, who now drive to other areas of the city -- or, more commonly, to the suburbs -- to shop and for entertainment. "And it is a middle-class community," he said, "with family incomes of $45,000 to $60,000 a year."
The deal could still fall apart. Mr. Mattone said it would be essential to lure national-brand retailers, like the Gap, the Limited, and Old Navy to the project to recapture shoppers who now go to regional malls like Roosevelt Field and Green Acres in Nassau County.
The developer has to reach an agreement to lease or purchase the site from the city. And the amount of subsidized parking remains unresolved.
Nevertheless the Jamaica project appears to reflect the Giuliani Administration's interest in economic development and determination to push long-stalled projects to completion. "With the financial backing of Sony, we have a high level of confidence that this will get done," said Charles Millard, president of the city's Economic Development Corporation.
He said the city is putting pressure on developers and their financial backers to start moving ahead or risk losing their designations. "We are saying to developers that it is time to put up or shut up," he added. "We want to create incentives to get these things finished." He cited the case of the Renaissance Plaza office-hotel project in Brooklyn that is now being excavated after 13 years of delays.
Mr. Millard also noted that many investors had avoided areas with a heavy minority population, like southeast Queens, in the past despite data on rising income levels.
THE Queens project is described as "a huge victory" by F. Carlisle Towery, who helped designate downtown Jamaica as a focus for development as an architect-planner with the Regional Plan Association in the early 1960's and has been trying to make it happen as president of the Greater Jamaica Development Corporation since 1967.
Many of the intervening years have been difficult. The downtown shopping area once had three department stores, Macy's, Mays and the locally owned Gertz. "Macy's left in 1978 and Gertz closed in 1981," Mr. Towery said. "Mays held out for a few years longer and then it was gone too."
Banks moved their headquarters elsewhere and lawyers, real estate brokers and insurance agents, whose livelihood is based on commercial transactions, followed. The seven movie theaters that once operated in the area went dark as well and the local newspaper, The Long Island Press, folded.
"Jamaica took some huge hits over a 10-year period that would have killed some downtowns," Mr. Towery said. But it did not die. The old Gertz store was successfully converted into an office building and fast food franchises moved into empty retail spaces.
It is not that the area is a remote backwater. Within walking distance is a major terminal of the Long Island Rail Road. A new subway stop opened in 1989 directly below the site at Jamaica and Parsons Avenues, which was cleared for development in the early 1970's.
Nor has there been a lack of public investment. York College, a four-year school that is the newest member of the City University system, has grown on a 50-acre campus just to the south of the downtown area at a cost approaching $300 million. A million-square-foot building to house offices of the Social Security Administration was built on an adjacent block, and another for the Food and Drug Administration is scheduled to rise nearby.
"The essential strategy was for the public sector to be the pioneers in the area to create a setting for the private sector," Mr. Towery said.
But private investors proved highly resistant to such blandishments for a long time. In 1985 the Mack Company of Rochelle Park, N.J., was chosen as the developer amid high hopes. But there were delays in finishing the subway extension and other difficulties and the company was dropped in 1987.
The city tried again in 1994, but the proposed developer, Rosenshein Associates of Rochelle Park, lost its designation last year after failing to come up with a suitable plan.
One of the problems in developing the area, Mr. Towery said, is that there is no nightlife. Although there are plenty of people about during the day, when work is over they go home, leaving the streets all but deserted.
That is the reason for the focus on the Sony/Magic Johnson theater part of the project. "The activity will be at night and on weekends," said Paul A. Travis of Washington Square Partners and a consultant to Mr. Mattone. "And that is going to help the retail," he added. "It is very difficult to have successful retail operations without nights and weekends."
He said the development group hopes to complete its deal with the city, which would involve purchasing rather than leasing the site, by the end of the year. The plan is to start excavation by next summer with completion scheduled for the fall of 1998, in time for the Christmas shopping season.
One of the remaining issues is parking under the building. Mr. Travis said studies have found that residents of Queens tend to use automotive transportation for just about everything other than commuting to work. That means the more parking that can be provided, the more likely people are to see a movie and shop.
THE current plan calls for one level of parking with 225 spaces, but Mr. Towery and others say what is really needed is two levels, with 400 spaces. But charging market rates would discourage the overriding goal of filling the offices during the day and the movie theater and shops during nonbusiness hours.
"We probably won't be able to charge more than $5 a day or $75 a month, compared to $250 to $300 a month elsewhere," Mr. Mattone said. "And it will probably be only $1 for someone who goes to a movie."
So the developers are looking to public agencies for financial help in building the garage. "This is a commercial project that stands on its own from the ground up," said Mr. Mattone.
"Parking is always a difficult and expensive issue in New York," said Mr. Millard. "E.D.C. understands that the state plans to subsidize the cost of parking."

SECTION: Section 13NJ; Page 16; Column 4; New Jersey Weekly Desk 

LENGTH: 712 words

HEADLINE: ATLANTIC CITY;
The Talk of the Town

BYLINE: By BILL KENT 

 BODY:
CAN Seymour (Pinky) Kravitz save Atlantic City?
Never mind that, on paper at least, the city may not seem to need saving, with record levels of new investment, gambling, new construction and infrastructure improvements. Mr. Kravitz, an Atlantic City public school teacher and radio personality for most of his 69 years, thinks otherwise. 
 "What was once a solid, functioning community where you could make a home for yourself has basically shattered," he said in an interview before beginning his daily radio show, Mondays through Fridays from 4 to 6 P.M. on WOND-AM.
"There's been hundreds of millions of dollars in taxes spent, but the people who live here haven't benefited all that much because they're divided racially, economically, ethnically and politically into so many factions and interest groups that they're almost paralyzed with rage. Unless something's done right now to bring people together and get everything out in the open, we're going to lose whatever shot we have at taking control of our destiny."
Mr. Kravitz has been broadcasting for 39 years. Unlike others who have used talk radio as a bully pulpit, he is generally viewed as an impartial advocate for the improvement of Atlantic City as a whole.
"I think that Pinky is a very beautiful person," said Dorothy Dorrington, a former teacher and school board president. "I've known him ever since I came into the school system in the 1950's, and he pours his heart out to the community so that when things happen that need attention, they get attention. He truly loves Atlantic City."
The fact that he has been broadcasting from improvised studios in casino hotels since 1979 -- currently from a lounge at the Trump Plaza -- is not, he said, the reason he refuses to blame the casino industry for the city's growing pains.
"There were a few chilly years at the beginning, but since then, the casinos have bent over backwards for Atlantic City," he said. "They pay two-thirds of the local taxes. They support hundreds of charities. They donate hundreds of thousands of dollars in equipment and services to schools and local organizations."
Last month, on his radio show, he called for a town meeting in Convention Hall for residents to sound off about themselves, elected and appointed officials, and, of course, the casino industry. Within weeks, he came up with a commitment from Convention Hall, and a date, Oct. 10 at 7 P.M. He has arranged to have the meeting broadcast locally on radio and cable television.
"Everybody gets three minutes to say what they want, without interruption," he said. "And everybody will have to listen, without interruption. Any issue is up for grabs."
If anyone else had suggested this meeting, it would not have gone beyond the planning stages. Known for his schmoozy interviewing style, close connections in local politics and unabashed boosterism, Mr. Kravitz has managed to keep his 6-foot, 4-inch frame above the fray.
Having interviewed out-of-towners from Eleanor Roosevelt to Sammy Davis Jr., former locals like Dean Martin and Jerry Lewis, and almost every Atlantic City politician in nearly 40 years. Mr. Kravitz has become an expert at what he calls "playing fair with people."
"Tall guys have to learn early on to be especially fair with people, or they'll think you're picking on them, even if you're not," he said. "I always leave anything that's said open to debate and interpretation."
Only once did an interview go sour, Mr. Kravitz said. After appearing on his show, former Mayor Jay Bradway tried to have him dismissed from the schools. "He didn't like the questions I was asking," recalled Mr. Kravitz, who has since retired. "You win some, you lose some."
He is confident that his town meeting will change the way the city's residents think about their future.
"I keep reminding people that this isn't the first time Atlantic City has had a town meeting," he said. "We had one back in 1975, when the state voted against statewide casino development. Everybody was worried and angry and complaining and divided. I went to the town meeting, and I stood up and said: 'I don't think we should give up on the casino issue. I think we should try one more time to get it passed in Atlantic City.'
"And look what happened."

SECTION: Section 3; Page 6; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1251 words

HEADLINE: MUTUAL FUNDS;
Insurance for Funds: Safety for Whom?

BYLINE: By CAROLE GOULD 

 BODY:
INVESTORS might be attracted by Fidelity Investments' plan to insure money market funds because it would increase the safety of their investments. But money fund insurance represents a much bigger opportunity for the fund companies, analysts say: it promises to limit their liability.
From an investor's point of view, "I think that money market insurance is really unnecessary," said Peter G. Crane, editor of the Rated Money Fund Report, a trade publication of IBC/Financial Data in Ashland, Mass. 
 Burton J. Greenwald, a mutual fund consultant in Philadelphia, called the insurance plan "a marketing gimmick" with no "substantive value" to the investor.
Investors as a group have not shied away from uninsured funds. This year, they have poured more than $100 billion into money market funds through August, outpacing last year's big gain of $144 billion. Total assets in money funds, which many people use interchangeably with bank savings and checking accounts, reached $856.7 billion at the end of August, according to IBC, surpassing for the first time the $806.4 billion that the Investment Company Institute, the Washington trade group, counted in bond/income funds.
Money market funds are usually the most stable type of mutual funds, investing mainly in short-term notes issued by corporations or the Federal Government. Money market accounts opened at banks make similar investments, but these accounts carry Federal deposit insurance.
Like most mutual funds, money market funds are not insured. So investors could lose some or all of their investment, although that has rarely happened.
Fidelity is proposing to form an insurance company that would be owned by the mutual funds it insures. Those funds are owned by their shareholders, not Fidelity. The insurance would cover only $100 million of the $80 billion that Fidelity investors keep in its 23 money funds. Because the insurance premiums are deducted by the funds as an operating expense, Fidelity estimates that the premiums will cost shareholders 0.005 percent to 0.01 percent a year (or one-half to one basis point).
The insurance, awaiting approval from the Securities and Exchange Commission, would cover only losses that occur when the issuer of a portfolio security defaults on its obligation. It would not compensate for losses from interest-rate shifts, called market risk, or from any possible fraud by Fidelity or its employees, or similar operational risks.
Among other big fund companies, the Vanguard Group, Merrill Lynch and Franklin Templeton are looking into insuring their money funds, but Capital Research and Management, which offers the American Funds group, has no such plan.
A unit of the Marsh & McLennan Companies, the insurance broker in New York and owner of Putnam Investments, said it had received "a tremendous amount of interest" in the last four weeks in an insurance plan it was selling to fund companies. "We've been talking to about 20 complexes -- seven or eight quotes are out now -- and I expect those folks to be going to their trustees for approval," said Michael C. Nicolai, senior vice president of Marsh & McLennan Inc., who declined to name the companies.
Given the small amount of coverage Fidelity is offering, "for safety, a Treasury fund would be a lot safer than an insured money market" under the proposal, Mr. Crane said.
Moreover, the investor's option of owning money market funds that hold strictly United States Treasuries, or Government money funds that own Treasuries along with short-term issues of Government agencies, limits what Fidelity or any other fund company can pay for credit insurance and still be competitive.
After comparing yields the last two years, Mr. Crane found that taxable money funds, which include Government funds, had an average yield advantage of only 0.06 percent over just Government money funds. That, he said, appeared to leave little room for the cost of credit insurance.
United States Treasury money funds are the safest available. Their yields have been 0.3 percent lower, on average, than those of taxable money funds the last two years.
It makes sense, therefore, to consider that amount as the ceiling for the cost of insurance. Bear in mind that Treasury dividends are exempt from state taxes, which lowers the ceiling to 0.25 percent or less, depending on where the investor lives.
So "insurance," in the form of Government-guaranteed securities, already costs investors from 0.3 percent for pure Treasury funds to 0.06 percent for Government funds.
"There definitely will be talk of types of insurance going forward," Mr. Crane said. "But it's tough to see how it could be cost-effective, given that the spread between Government and regular taxable money funds is so narrow."
WHEN Vanguard offered an insured money market fund during the 1980's, it paid substantially more, roughly 0.25 percent, for the coverage it bought from St. Paul Insurance, said Ian A. MacKinnon, senior vice president and head of fixed-income funds for Vanguard. In 1989, Vanguard rolled the $154 million fund into its United States Treasury money market fund.
"It was a prohibitively expensive deal for shareholders, given the minute risk of loss," Mr. MacKinnon said. "The investing public did not beat a path to our door."
The Vanguard insurance applied to an entire portfolio, but Fidelity proposes to cover only a little more than 1 percent of money fund assets.
To put that $100 million in perspective, however, it is more than the losses incurred in recent years by several money funds that lost money by investing in the complex financial instruments called derivatives. Virtually all of those losses were covered anyway by the fund management companies, several of which made cash infusions to prevent "breaking a buck," or letting the fund's net asset value fall below the $1 share price that money market funds aim to maintain.
That has happened only once. In September 1994, Community Asset Management liquidated a money fund because it could not reimburse the fund for $2 million of losses.
Fidelity's policy does not cover derivatives losses, but they are less likely to occur nowadays because restrictions have been tightened on the types of investments that money funds can own. And, clearly, Fidelity has safeguards in place protecting customers against employee fraud.
Why does Fidelity want to offer coverage that is so limited? Over time, the premiums should accumulate because the risk of credit default is so small, a spokeswoman said. That would create a reserve against future loss, or perhaps lower premium payments, she said.
Analysts suggest other benefits. Before, investors understood implicitly that Fidelity would guarantee their money funds' $1-a-share price even though the company never said it would, said John Rekenthaler, publisher of Morningstar Mutual Funds in Chicago. That is because other fund companies have almost always made good on such losses.
An insurance program implies that reimbursements would be limited to the coverage, he said. And limiting liability is likely to spread like wildfire in the industry, he contended: "If someone sets a precedent by not supporting a dollar share price and gets away with it, the other fund companies will follow suit."
Moreover, the loss would be borne by fund shareholders instead of coming out of the company's profits. "Why not pass the cost on to the customers?" Mr. Rekenthaler said. "That's only good business practice."

GRAPHIC: Graph: "The Cost of Safety" shows lag in yield due to the safety of investing in Government, Rated, Insured, or Treasury bonds. (Source: IBC's Rated Money Fund Report)

HEADLINE: DESTINATIONS;
Music to Whose Ears? Arts Center and Neighbors Try to Work Things Out

BYLINE: By DAVID W. CHEN 

 BODY:
The Scottish Festival scheduled today ends the first season of private management at the PNC Bank Arts Center, formerly known as the Garden State Arts Center. But that's not what nearby residents are likely to remember.
Instead, what will stand out are memories of the alternative-rock ENIT Festival on Aug. 17. The music went on all night and more than 100 people, primarily from Holmdel, filed complaints with the police about the noise. Twenty-five people were arrested for drugs or under-age drinking. 
 Lewis B. Thurston 3d, executive director of the New Jersey Highway Authority, which owns the arts center, later assured residents that there would be no more all-night concerts. But that has not stopped the growing chorus of concern, manifested in meetings, local newspapers and letters to public officials, that ENIT symbolizes bigger troubles down the road.
What they fear is a $12 million expansion of the arts center to 17,500 seats, from 11,300, that is being worked out by the state and the private operator, GSAC Partners, as part of a 20-year lease agreement starting with the 1997 season.
Supporters say the expansion is crucial for economic survival. "The whole idea of expanding the arts center is that we'll be able to pull bigger acts, and get more people to come," said Deborah Darrell, a spokeswoman for GSAC Partners, which includes Sony Music Entertainment, Blockbuster Entertainment Corporation, Pace Entertainment Corporation and Delsener/Slater Enterprises.
Detractors say the expanded project would produce too much traffic and noise for a township of only 12,000. "The mindset of the operating people is that only money matters," said Lois Bastian, who has lived in Holmdel for 30 years. "I've heard more and more people say the arts center used to be a nice place, but no more."
For most of its history, the tax-exempt arts center, which opened in 1968 with a concert featuring Van Cliburn and the Philadelphia Orchestra conducted by Eugene Ormandy, has co-existed peacefully with its neighbors.
The arts center hasn't been losing money; over the last seven years, it has averaged a net profit of $1.7 million. But classical music is not where profits are made, and so, Mr. Thurston said, the center has had to cater to other crowds. In 1995, for example, there were 14 sold-out shows -- mostly rock performers such as Hootie and the Blowfish, the Cranberries and Live.
To stay competitive, the arts center must be big enough to attract acts like Neil Diamond, Billy Joel, Bon Jovi or Elton John, said Mitch Slater, president of Delsener/ Slater Enterprises, who has booked acts at the arts center for the last 13 years.
The hope is that private management will accomplish that. First, the center's name was sold to PNC Bank for $9.2 million over the next 10 years. Then, the management group signed an interim lease agreement for 1996. Now, negotiations are continuing for the additional 20-year lease that would guarantee the highway authority a minimum of $1.65 million annually and give the state $30 million in sales taxes over 20 years, Mr. Thurston said.
But some people who live near the arts center are leery of what private management means.
 For example, in 1996, for the first time, beer and wine were sold at the arts center, prompting concerns about the increased risk of drunken driving -- especially since the arts center is accessible only by car off the Garden State Parkway and there are no public buses, said Michael Santos, a Holmdel resident and critic of the center.
Then there all the complaints about the ENIT festival, which featured acts such as Porno for Pyros and Black Grape.
Such concerns have not gone unnoticed. "We're very sensitive to the concerns of Holmdel, and we want to insure that we have good programming that's not unduly disruptive to the community," Mr. Thurston said.
However, maintaining order at outdoor concerts has become increasingly difficult everywhere. From 1994 to 1995, litigation related to rock concert injuries skyrocketed more than 500 percent, and insurance rates for promoters 75 percent, said Paul Wertheimer, founder of Crowd Management Strategies in Chicago, a consulting firm specializing in rock concerts and safety issues.
Meanwhile, more than 70 citizens' groups have formed in recent years to fight amphitheater noise and 10 lawsuits have been filed, said Jim Lewis, executive director of the National Clearinghouse on Amphitheater Noise, in Palo Alto, Calif.
Neighbors of the PNC Bank Arts Center are not at that point yet.
"We're not looking to reinvent the wheel here," said Mayor Robert Giannone. "We're looking to maintain the peace and tranquility that we had."

HEADLINE: IN BRIEF;
A Partnership to Rebuild Downtown Englewood

BYLINE: By DAVID W. CHEN 

 BODY:
A generation ago, Armory Street was a solid, middle-class neighborhood just a few steps away from downtown Englewood. Over time, the street deteriorated until it was considered one of the worst streets not just in Englewood, but in all of Bergen County, because of its dreary auto repair shops and drug dens.
Two weeks ago, the city and Propitious Inc., a minority-owned development company, agreed to rehabilitate most of the one- and two-family houses on the block for eventual sale to owner-occupants. The plan is part of a broader redevelopment effort to bring businesses, and residents, back to downtown Englewood. 
 In April, the city initiated a $150,000 effort to make over the traffic circle at West Palisade Avenue and Tenafly Road, site of the historic Liberty Pole. Now, the Department of Community Affairs is providing grants to repair roofs, paint exteriors, replace heating and electrical systems, and complete other tasks to bring the buildings on Armory Street up to code. The Bergen County Community Development Program is providing money for new streets and sidewalks, new lighting and better drainage. And on Oct. 26, the Volunteer Center of Bergen County will spearhead an effort to plant a flower garden and beautify the street. DAVID W. CHEN

HEADLINE: MARKET WATCH;
Inflation Bonds May Prove Hard To Sell

BYLINE: By FLOYD NORRIS 

 BODY:
Inflation bonds are coming. Now the question is whether they will sell.
One viewpoint on that comes from a couple of fixed-income strategists at J. P. Morgan, Nanette Abuhoff and Hussein Malik, who forecast big demand: "Considering that the inflation rate is historically low, and the economy is running at or through full employment, it is reasonable to think that inflation risk is currently biased to the upside," they write in a well-researched analysis. "In such an environment, we expect demand for those bonds to increase." 
 It sounds logical, and the implicit recommendation for investors to buy is probably a good one. The 10-year bonds announced by the Treasury last week, and to be auctioned in January, will pay a fixed interest rate -- the real rate. But the principal will rise with inflation, so each interest payment is likely to be larger than the previous one. And when the bond matures, investors will get back dollars that can buy as much as the money they invested. The bonds will prove to be a good buy if inflation rises, but not if inflation continues to decline.
And that is why they are likely to be a difficult item to sell in 1997. Investors all too often keep their eyes firmly fixed on recent performance in deciding what to buy, and inflation has not been a threat recently.
In early 1982, the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index had a dividend yield of 6 percent and was trading at less than eight times earnings. Anyone who looked at historical norms would have concluded the market was cheap. But that was widely deemed irrelevant. What counted was that the stock market was lower then than it had been 15 years before. Stocks were proven poor investments, and few wanted them.
Now, almost 15 years later, stocks are expensive, and popular. After all, they have risen rapidly, and those who got in years ago have made lots of money. The dividend yield on the S.& P. 500 is 2.2 percent, while stocks go for about 15 times earnings.
The history of inflation bonds in Britain, which pioneered them in 1981 when inflation was rampant and people were scared, is that the biggest demand came at the beginning. So popular were they that investors accepted a real yield of just 2.5 percent, and that figure soon declined as people passed up double-digit yields on normal bonds to buy protection from inflation. Since then, as inflation scares have eased, higher real yields have been needed to attract buyers.
Inflation is not dead, and these bonds make a lot of sense for many investors, particularly individuals worried about retirement and pension funds whose liabilities will grow sharply if inflation does return. But those investors can, if they wish, buy inferior inflation protection now, in the form of investments like natural resources stocks. They can, if they want, even buy gold. None of those investments are doing well these days, however, because investors are just not concerned about inflation.
Attitudes could change by January, of course, and the Treasury may seek to hold down the amount of inflation bonds being sold, so that supply does not exceed demand. But it is likely that the bonds will have to be sold with a real rate higher than the optimistic figures of 3.25 percent or less that some expect.
If the real yield does come in at a relatively high figure, these bonds are likely to be good investments. But they still will be hard to sell. Investors all too often buy with their eyes firmly fixed on the rear-view mirror. And from that vantage point, inflation is no concern at all.

HEADLINE: SPENDING IT;
Dialing for Dollars, With Competing Flat-Rate Plans

BYLINE: By ROY FURCHGOTT 

 BODY:
AT&T introduced a flat-rate calling plan last week amid much fanfare. Under the program, customers can make a long-distance call anywhere in the country, at any time of the day, for 15 cents a minute.
Flat-rate plans are not new. MCI Communications, Sprint and a host of smaller long-distance providers have had similar plans for years. And MCI, whose flat rate is nearly identical to AT&T's, responded quickly, filing a request with the Federal Communications Commission on Wednesday to lower its rate to 14.5 cents a minute this week. 
 "We are not interested in starting a price war," an MCI spokesman, Michael Tierney, said. "We are trying to preserve our position as costing less than AT&T."
Flat-rate plans sound tempting, and they certainly offer a bit of freedom. You don't have to check the time before making a call under most of the plans or worry about the cost of calling across the country. But consumer groups say the plans are a good deal only for a certain type of person: someone who makes most of his calls during the day, when regular rates are often the highest.
"AT&T's flat rate targets small business and heavy daytime callers," said Sam Simon, general counsel of the Telecommunications Research and Action Center, a nonprofit group in Washington that evaluates phone plans.
He said there were better plans for people who make many short-distance, nighttime and weekend calls.
AT&T acknowledges that its new plan may not be the least-expensive available for residential consumers, who typically make most of their calls in the evenings and weekends. "If you are on a savings plan, on average, for all kinds of calling, the cost is 14.5 cents a minute," said Mark Siegel, an AT&T spokesman.
But the flat-rate plans are still popular. J. D. Power & Associates, the market research company, measured consumer satisfaction with long-distance services. Sprint scored first because of its flat-rate Sprint Sense plan, which charges 10 cents a minute during off-peak hours and a daytime rate of 25 cents a minute.
"There is a very strong perception that Sprint Sense is a good value because of flat pricing and because the bills are so easy to understand," said Zaiba Nanji, the director of telecommunications services at Power. Some consumers may even be willing to pay slightly more because the plans are so simple, Ms. Nanji said.
"For two years it has been our most successful product, bar none, because of the simplicity," said Robin Pence, a spokeswoman for Sprint.
If you want to save money, you'll have to do your homework.
Mr. Simon said that one way to get the lowest rate from your current long-distance company was to ask it to look at your bill and suggest its best plan for you. Or ask for comparisons from other carriers.
Getting the plans in writing helps you see surcharges or penalties that might not be in the sales pitch. If you don't want to do all this comparison shopping, the Telecommunications Research and Action Center sells a chart that compares most of the calling plans (800 344-8722, $5).
Even if you don't choose a flat-rate plan, under no circumstances should you use a basic program, said Bradley Stillman, the telecommunications policy director for the Consumer Federation of America. "Enroll in a plan," he warned, "or you will pay a rate that is way too high."

HEADLINE: INVESTING IT;
Inflation Is Coming! Inflation Isn't Coming! Bond Shoppers Can Decide

BYLINE: By LESLIE EATON 

 BODY:
ECONOMISTS are excited about them, tax accountants may make a fortune from them, and Government officials say they are good -- and good for you, Average American Investors. But are the new "inflation-protection bonds" Washington announced last week really a great new development, or the investment version of the Susan B. Anthony dollar?
No one will know for sure until the Treasury tries to sell some, which will not occur until January. But many investors may be intrigued. "The appeal of these securities, while it is not universal, is pretty broad," said Ian A. MacKinnon, senior vice president for fixed-income investments at the Vanguard Group. 
 Here are the answers to some basic questions about the bonds.
Q. What's the point of these new bonds, anyway?
 A. Savers and investors will get a Government guarantee that the purchasing power of their money will keep pace with inflation -- and they will make some money, to boot.
 Q. How much money?
 A. That depends on what rate investors demand when the Treasury tries to sell the bonds, which in turn will depend on the public's appetite for these securities and where interest rates are when the sale occurs. Bond-market seers suggest the rate will be 3 percent to 4 percent.
 Q. Would that be a good deal?
 A. Probably. Over the last 70 years, the real rate of return on bonds, meaning the return adjusted for inflation, has been 1.8 percent, according to Ibbotson Associates.
 Q. So I should sell all my stocks and use the money to buy these new bonds?
 A. Well, no. If you are a tad nervous about the current state of the stock market, these bonds may be attractive. But over the very long haul, stocks of large companies have returned 7.2 percent (on top of inflation), which suggests that people with long-term goals will probably still do better by keeping much of their money in stocks.
"The downside of these bonds is that they could be horribly misused by people who think they can have their cake and eat it too," said Harold Evensky, a financial adviser in Coral Gables, Fla. He suggests that people who already own bonds or bond funds might redirect as much as 20 percent of the total portfolio to the new securities.
 Q. What does the Government get in the deal?
 A. In return for inflation protection, the Treasury expects to pay investors a little less interest than it pays on regular bonds.
 Q. How exactly will the new bonds work?
 A. As with regular bonds, an investor lends the Government money, say $1,000, and the Government promises to pay interest on the loan of, say, 3.25 percent. The Government also promises to repay the borrowed money on a certain date; to begin, all the new bonds will mature in 10 years.
The unusual feature of the new securities, which you can buy directly from the Fed or from a broker, is that the Government will adjust the size of the loan (the bond) to keep pace with inflation. So if inflation continues at 3 percent a year, the $1,000 loan will, over 10 years, become a $1,347 loan. The interest rate will remain fixed, but because the principal amount of the loan or bond is growing, the interest payments will be bigger.
 Q. Basically, you get the same-sized wedge of pie, but make more money because the pie is getting bigger?
 A. Exactly.
 Q. Who measures the inflation?
 A. The Government calculates it based on the Consumer Price Index for urban areas.
 Q. Hasn't there been talk that the C.P.I. overstates inflation?
 A. Yes. That may be a benefit for investors, though obviously there is some temptation for the Government to monkey around with the index. Treasury promises only that changes in the index will not be retroactive.
 Q. Do these bonds have risks?
 A. If you have to sell them before they mature, the amount you will get will depend on how much someone else is willing to pay -- and that could be less than you paid for the bonds in the first place. It is unclear how liquid the bonds will be, especially in the relatively small amounts that amateur investors typically buy and sell.
 Q. Are there any other drawbacks?
 A. Taxes. Not only do you have to pay Federal taxes on the income you receive, you have to pay taxes on any increase in the principal amount of the bonds -- even though you cannot realize that increase until the bond matures or you sell it. In some cases when inflation is high, you could owe more in taxes in a given year than you receive in income.
 Q. So I should try to buy these bonds in an account, like an I.R.A. or 401(k), that I don't have to pay taxes on?
 A. Good idea, although it may be some time before your retirement plan includes these bonds as an option. Relatively few 401(k) plans let participants buy individual securities, and it may be several years before mutual funds are available.

GRAPHIC: Graph: "A Good Investing? It Depends" shows how an inflation-bond investor in the top tax bracket (39.6 percent) would fare under various inflation scenarios.

HEADLINE: EARNING IT;
Swallowing Bitter Pills for Pay: The Trials of Guinea Pigs

BYLINE: By DAVID J. MORROW 

 BODY:
LAST November, Adam Schumacher, a junior at the University of Connecticut at Storrs, stumbled into the campus infirmary with a sore throat and found himself with a job offer. If he would use a new gargle on his throat for just an hour, the company sponsoring the test would not only cover his medical bills, but also pay him $50 for his trouble.
"It was the easiest $50 I ever made," Mr. Schumacher said. "I'd definitely do this again, if I can find a test that I like." 
 Mr. Schumacher has plenty of company. Attribute it to the economy or to a dearth of attractive jobs for the unskilled. But this year, tens of thousands of people will take part in some 3,000 new clinical trials nationwide, testing everything from AIDS vaccines to remedies for cat allergies. And increasingly, these human guinea pigs are paid -- and sometimes paid well -- as researchers compete to recruit healthy, motivated subjects.
Walter Reed, the renowned Army bacteriologist, was one of the first to pay volunteers, offering people at the turn of the century $100 in gold to participate in yellow-fever experiments, and to give their estates $100 more if they died.
The Depression speeded the trend, with unemployed men paid $2 apiece to swallow tiny glass test tubes in experiments. Historically, subjects have also included prisoners and researchers themselves. But it has only been in the last 20 years that scientists have come to rely on paid subjects who were not desperate or coerced.
While payments for subjects remain a small expense in the $15.8 billion that the Pharmaceutical Research and Manufacturers of America say is spent each year on drug research, it adds up to several million a year, leading a small but growing number of people to earn meager livings as guinea pigs by staying enrolled in several trials at once.
And while the trials pay, they are far from free of risk, or of trouble. Even though the tests are monitored by institutional review boards and the Food and Drug Administration, volunteers sometimes become sick or die. (In many drug trials, the test ends only when the subject finally suffers a reaction.) Participants may also misunderstand the time involved. And many of the tests can be grueling, if not painful.
"Doing these studies isn't for the squeamish or weak," said John Sanchez, 30, an unemployed Brooklyn musician who hopes to pocket $5,000 from medical trials over the next several months. A familiar face at every research institution in the New York City area, he takes a breather from the trials only when his arms are too bruised from needle pricks.
In Mr. Sanchez's first trial, he was paid $1,200 to take a mood-altering drug and then submit to blood and urine tests for two weeks. Shortly thereafter, he signed up for a trial that paid $250 -- and required a spinal tap. A day after the procedure, his head hurt so much that he returned to the hospital and was treated without charge.
"It was easy money during the first trial, but that spinal tap really hurt," Mr. Sanchez said. "You feel like you're doing something important for science, but the fact is that this is a job. You want to find the ones that pay well but don't demand too much of you."
So if you need a few extra dollars this month, medical science has a deal for you. Forget about donating your blood or giving away one of your kidneys. These doctors want to rent your whole body.
Consider these ads published recently in Washington area newspapers:
 
* "Women, if you want to have fun learning, enjoy good food, get $$ paid$$, and have a chance to help important research that can help you stay safer from H.I.V.," one ad says, it is time to apply. "Confidentiality maintained."
* "Healthy overweight children and normal weight children with two overweight parents needed," says an appeal for a National Institutes of Health research study.
* "If you currently use heroin or cocaine," another says, "and are interested in participating in a paid inpatient drug research study at the Johns Hopkins Bayview Medical Center, please call the recruiting department."
INDEED, who knew that being allergic to bee stings, having genital herpes or being an addict could be so lucrative? Years ago, clinical trials like these would have paid almost nothing, but not any longer. To recruit their subjects, medical schools and independent laboratories are raising the stipends to compensate them for their time, inconvenience and risk. The median stipend at clinical trials nationwide is now about $950, 27 percent more than it was three years ago, according to Dataedge, a medical research firm in Fort Washington, Pa., but in some cases hourly rates may be only slightly above minimum wage because of the time involved.
"Some of the drugs that are being tested today simply require more time and effort than some of those that were developed in the past," said Steve Millikin, research director at Pharmaceutical Product Development, a clinical research company in Morrisville, N.C. "Since volunteers are spending more time at the clinic, of course, they should receive more money."
The rise in pay is gradually changing the recruiting strategies. Doctors once recruited volunteers for clinical trials by praising participation as a service to society. As subjects are paid more, researchers call them what they are, part-time workers, and recruit them with cash.
Researchers say the higher stipends enable them to hire more responsible subjects. While college students usually have no qualms about donating blood or urine samples, they sometimes balk at making daily diary entries about their reactions to a drug. Researchers say they think older subjects do not mind these menial chores, and may perhaps provide even more detail.
"A great many of the people who volunteer here are altruistic," said Karen Charron, coordinator of immunization research at the Johns Hopkins University School of Public Health in Baltimore. "They're blood-donor types. They're interested in the good of the community."
The change has also given hundreds of thousands of people a growing source of income, though one with distinct drawbacks. Of the 3,000 clinical trials that will begin this year, many will pay $100 to $200 for each day. A select few pay as much as $1,500 for a few weeks of work.
Researchers say they don't have a profile of the people who volunteer for these jobs. The subjects range from civic-minded citizens who donate blood regularly, to people who suffer from the disease being studied. Others may be relatives or friends of someone who died from the disease. There is also a concern, though, that the pay is attracting people for whom passive activity is more appealing than an entry-level job at McDonald's.
Medical researchers say trial stipends are not high enough to coerce poor people into risking their health for a fast buck, but the evidence is not entirely convincing. Discomfort is a part of the business, and many volunteers quickly learn to accept it as an occupational hazard.
At the Johns Hopkins University School of Public Health, subjects receive $1,500 for participating in vaccine studies in which their hands are placed over a container of malaria-carrying mosquitoes until they are bitten at least five times.
Even minor aches and pains can pay: Come down with the flu, and doctors at the Camino Medical Group in Sunnyvale, Calif., will pay $100 for an overnight observation.
Try such experiments on some bunnies and animal-rights groups might storm the place. Humans are on their own. Eleonora Florance, a nurse in Annandale, Va., earned $450 last year testing a drug for seasonal mood disorder. Not only did Mrs. Florance have to give a blood sample every half-hour, she spent the night with a heat-sensitive probe in her rectum.
"The probe was uncomfortable, but these procedures don't surprise me since I'm a nurse," she said. "My kids said, 'Ooh, Mom, how could you stand it?' But learning about research is good for my profession. And I'm sort of a volunteer junkie. I sign up for almost everything."
 While trials may be painful, they can also be embarrassing. Sharon Donnelly, 42, a social worker in Maryland who counsels delinquent teen-agers, signed up for a three-year AIDS vaccine trial last year at Johns Hopkins and was shocked when a male nurse delved into the intimate details of her sex life.
"I was a little taken aback that they wanted to know every sexual practice and who did what to whom," said Ms. Donnelly, who receives $20 for each monthly visit. "Not only are you violating your privacy, you're violating your partner's as well."
Still, so many people are agreeing to submit to these procedures that qualifying for a trial can be difficult. Researchers determined to recruit the best subjects often reject more people than they accept.
"There is definitely no shortage of healthy volunteers for trials," said Dr. Leslie Norins, publisher of Clinical Trials Advisor, a national trade newsletter. "Some may come in for the money, but the truth is these trials are hard work. They can take a lot of time. You really have to want to be there."
Rookies often associate pain with a big paycheck -- a big mistake. The drug companies that sponsor the studies say they pay subjects for their time and inconvenience, not for exposing themselves to hazards.
"Clinical trials aren't about buying bodies for research," said Gary Steinman, president of GLS & Associates, a pharmaceutical consulting firm in New Preston, Conn. "The drug companies don't want volunteers to take chances for the money. They pay for the time involved, along with such inconveniences as blood and urine samples that may also be required."
Rates differ by trial, but most companies, including giants like Merck, pay subjects $100 to $200 for each testing day. A trial that required someone to come to a laboratory once a week for a month, for example, would pay $400 to $800. Multiple blood samples or extensive paperwork would bring more.
Experienced guinea pigs learn how to play a "feed and bleed" study, medical lingo for a clinical trial. The best tests are those that do not require much blood but are spread out over several weeks to raise the stipend. Other boons are trials that can be completed in a few days.
Jim Bauer, a retail banking strategist at Chase Manhattan, pocketed $250 this spring by taking a drug intended to relieve jet lag. During a business trip to Britain and Ireland, Mr. Bauer needed only to take one pill a day for four days.
"The drug really worked well because I felt great," he said. "The money was nice, but it wasn't as easy as it sounds. I had to go to the hospital where the study was conducted three different times. I had some time wrapped up in the trial, but it definitely paid dividends."
Most guinea pigs work only occasionally. But some make it at least a temporary career, seeking trials that pay top dollar and always staying enrolled in one test or another. Some do as many as five a month.
As part of an internship, Kim Conkle, a junior at Ohio State University, is participating in a vitamin C study at the National Institutes of Health in Bethesda, Md. Ms. Conkle will live in a hospital room for six months, take daily vitamin C supplements and undergo a regimen of tests for a mere $15 a day.
"They pay for our room and board, so it's more than the money," Ms. Conkle said. "I plan to go into medicine, so this is just wonderful experience for that."
Trials are easy to find; every large medical school conducts research, and most actively recruit volunteers.
Many trials are also listed on the Internet. Two of the most popular sites are Centerwatch Clinical Trials Listing Service (http://www.center watch.com) and Clinical Trial Posting (http://www.clinicaltrials.com). Both sites list trials by diseases and by geographic research centers in the United States and abroad.
BUT volunteers may be surprised by the requirements -- not to mention what the researchers plan to do to them. For many studies, volunteers have to be healthy nonsmokers who shun drugs and alcohol and say they have had no more than four sexual partners in the last 12 months. But trials that examine sexually transmitted diseases may recruit subjects who have had many sexual partners, and studies of drug users seek out addicts.
On top of that, doctors want volunteers who are responsible and altruistic, not hustlers out for a fast buck. The most sought-after subjects are healthy people in their 30's and 40's who don't mind being stuck by a needle on occasion. In many cases, the ideal recruits are men, since many drug companies are concerned that they may test a woman who is pregnant but does not know it.
Taxes and insurance may also be nuisances. Some insurance policies won't cover illnesses or injuries contracted during clinical trials, leaving the guinea pig to foot the bill. Typically, subjects have to sign a consent form that entitles them to nothing more than free treatment for any conditions caused by the trial. And while trial stipends can be lucrative, clinics are required to report stipends of more than $599 to the Internal Revenue Service.
But that is far from a guinea pig's biggest risk. Despite precautions, the trials can be dangerous and even deadly. Three years ago, Marc Kusinitz, assistant director of science communicatons at the Johns Hopkins School of Medicine, signed up for a malaria vaccine trial to make $600 for his child-support payments. Mr. Kusinitz was told in advance that as part of a control group he would contract the disease. Days after laying his hand on a can of malaria-infected mosquitoes, he woke up tired and achy.
"I felt like hell," he said. But it was ready cash, and as a Ph.D. in biology he also had a professional interest in science. "One thing about being in the study," he said, "is that doctors are close by. And I was treated very quickly."
Malaria is curable, and Mr. Kusinitz recovered. Subjects are not exposed to incurable diseases like AIDS, because trial procedures are monitored by institutional review boards that are supposed to guarantee the safety of participants.
Some guinea pigs aren't so lucky. Hoi Yan (Nicole) Wan, a 19-year-old University of Rochester sophomore from New York City, died after suffering a heart attack in April after taking part in a research project in Rochester on smoking and air pollution, her family contends. As part of the test, which paid $150, Ms. Wan underwent a bronchoscopy, a procedure in which a flexible tube is inserted down the throat and lungs to retrieve cells. Before the study, Ms. Wan signed a consent firm acknowledging that the procedure could cause shortness of breath, wheezing and irregular heartbeat. The hospital also agreed to treat her for conditions sustained from the procedure if she should become ill.
MS. WAN was observed for about an hour after the procedure. Later that day, she collapsed at a friend's house and was treated at Strong Memorial Hospital in Rochester for cardiac arrest. In a lawsuit filed in State Supreme Court in Queens, Ms. Wan's family contended that the University of Rochester, the university's medical center, Strong Memorial and the two doctors who conducted the bronchoscopy had been negligent.
"What happened is that the doctors sprayed a lethal dose of Lidocaine down Hoi Yan's throat," said Salvatore Aspromonte, a lawyer with Dansker & Aspromonte Associates in New York City, which represents the Wan family. (Lidocaine is a local anesthetic.) "Clinical trials aren't trials when the doctors are reckless. Then, they're dangerous."
A spokesman for the University of Rochester Medical Center, Robert Loeb, said: "The lawsuit is in litigation. We plan to defend ourselves. The doctors who were involved in the study are still on staff and seeing patients." He said the doctors would not take phone calls about the suit.
On Thursday, the New York State Health Department reported after a four-month investigation that the medical center had violated its own trial guidelines by increasing the dose of the anesthetic on Ms. Wan. "A case like this points out the potential difficulties that can arise if the researchers don't pay enough attention to human subjects," Health Commissioner Barbara A. DeBuono said. "All these trials have review boards that oversee their procedures. The doctors can't change these procedures without checking with the review board first. That's not good practice or good medicine."
Dr. Jay H. Stein, vice provost for health affairs at the medical center, said in a statement that the Health Department report also concluded that the researchers involved "did follow standards that are consistent with established practices." The statement continued, "In light of this tragedy, we have established new guidelines on the use of Lidocaine during bronchoscopy."
It is unlikely that the hazards will keep human guinea pigs from drug testing. Some are hooked on the money, but others like the idea of helping science. Medical researchers are the first to say that without human guinea pigs, vaccines for polio, diphtheria and other diseases could not have been developed. The next goal is an AIDS vaccine.
"It was a tough decision for me to do this AIDS trial," said Ms. Donnelly, whose decision several years earlier not to have a baby was sealed when she enrolled in a trial that was capable of causing birth defects.
"Giving up hope of ever having a baby is hard," she said. "But it's good to know that I'm helping someone with this study, so in a way, I'm giving birth to something anyway. The money is just a bonus."

SECTION: Section 3; Page 1; Column 1; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 3087 words

HEADLINE: When a Magazine Is Too Brash for the Bottom Line

BYLINE: By ROBIN POGREBIN 

 BODY:
AS Kurt Andersen remembers it, he was having fruit and cereal last February in the private dining room of the 42d-floor office where Henry R. Kravis runs his leveraged-buyout firm. Mr. Andersen, who was then editor in chief of New York magazine, says Mr. Kravis's breakfast message was clear: Consider killing a story about the rivalry between Felix G. Rohatyn and Steven Rattner, two of Wall Street's most powerful investment bankers.
Mr. Kravis is a partner at Kohlberg Kravis Roberts & Company, which controls New York's parent, the K-III Communications Corporation, so his opinion mattered. Although Mr. Kravis declined to be interviewed for this article, Mr. Andersen described their conversation this way: "He didn't want the piece to run. He said K-III and K.K.R. need relations with the investment banking community to be untroubled and robust." 
 It wasn't a suggestion that Mr. Andersen could dismiss lightly. Nevertheless, he dismissed it. Later that day, he briefed Harry A. McQuillen, the executive vice president of K-III and president of the media group, and told Mr. McQuillen he planned to run the story anyway. "He said, 'Absolutely do what you have to do,' " Mr. Andersen said. (Mr. McQuillen said he did not recall the conversation.)
The story was published in the March 11 issue, and while Mr. Kravis never mentioned it to him again, Mr. Andersen believes the financier did not forget it. In the months that followed, Mr. Kravis made his displeasure known to Mr. Andersen about other articles in New York, which K-III bought from Rupert Murdoch in 1991. And in late August, the New York editor was dismissed. While the official word was dissatisfaction with circulation numbers, Mr. Andersen and many of his colleagues said that Mr. Kravis was the not-so-invisible hand wielding the hatchet.
"The firing of Kurt Andersen is the kind of thing that sends a chill throughout magazine journalism," said David Denby, New York magazine's longtime film critic.
New York City's publishing and journalism circles seem to be learning the hard way what Wall Street has known all along: for Mr. Kravis, master of the deal, New York magazine is no big deal. He may like having it as the brightest media star in the K-III constellation of mass-market consumer magazines, which includes Seventeen and Soap Opera Digest, but he is not going to bend his investment principles or abandon his friends just to indulge its celebrity editor.
No matter that New York magazine's national political columnist, Jacob Weisberg, and its news editor, Larry Doyle, have quit in protest or that the business columnist, James J. Cramer, says he will join the mini-mutiny when his contract is up. And let the media community engage in all the collective hand-wringing that it wants. With K-III's stock languishing at around $10 a share since it went public a year ago, Mr. Kravis will do whatever it takes to get the price up and get out with some juicy profits.
Indeed, he is enforcing the same dictum for New York and its sister publications that increasing numbers of once-independent newspapers, magazines, television news shows and publishing houses are hearing from the giant corporations that have swallowed them up: make your numbers or else.
MR. KRAVIS'S main objective is to make money, lots of it -- and he has a pretty good track record so far. That is why he put part of Kohlberg Kravis's treasure chest behind K-III (Mr. Kravis's firm owns 73 percent of the company), which seeks to buy solid properties on the cheap, improve their performance and then sell them at a profit.
In the two and a half years since Mr. Andersen joined New York, the magazine, for all its success in winning over younger readers with its edgy tone and provocative subject matter, apparently fell short of K-III's goals. The company had hoped to reach an average weekly circulation of 435,000 to 440,000, Mr. McQuillen said; instead, Mr. Andersen had brought it up to just 425,000 from 415,000.
"Basically, we felt that at this point, the book could use a change in editorial direction," said Mr. McQuillen, who oversees K-III's magazines. "Kurt had done some good things with the magazine, but when all is said and done we didn't get as big a lift as we would have liked."
For all the attention New York brings to K-III, it is, in the financial scheme of things, almost inconsequential, representing less than 2 percent of the company's operating profit and trailing in circulation the company's lower-profile publications that have broader national appeal, like New Woman.
"It's too small to affect what I do," said Phelps B. Hoyt, a fixed-income analyst with KDP Investment Advisors who follows K-III's public bonds. "This falls way below my radar screen."
K-III officials say the company makes slightly better profit margins on some of its less sexy trade publications -- like National Hog Farmer and Adhesives Age -- as well as on some of its holdings in other areas, like Channel One, the school satellite network, and Pacific Shipper, a directory of shipping schedules for freighters.
"Out here in Montana, I wouldn't know what's going on with New York magazine," said Chuck Hunter, an investment research manager with the Montana Board of Investments, the state pension fund, which is an investor in the Kohlberg Kravis funds that own K-III. "Our ignorance is almost complete."
GIVEN New York magazine's modest financial stature relative to K-III's entire portfolio, and its disproportionate potential for upsetting Mr. Kravis's friends, some people may wonder why K-III holds on to the property. Indeed, when asked if he ever thought about simply dumping New York, William F. Reilly, K-III's chairman and chief executive, replied, "I've been tempted from time to time."
In reality, Mr. Reilly understands the value of having a high-profile magazine like New York in K-III's stable: it gives the company added clout among advertisers, credibility on Wall Street and cachet in the media community. "It's a door opener," Mr. Reilly said.
But if the magazine is essentially window dressing for one of Kohlberg Kravis's most visible properties, Mr. Kravis is clearly the one who decides how the goods are displayed.
And while Mr. Kravis can be expected to care little about stories in K-III magazines like Modern Bride or Fleet Owner, what New York magazine publishes matters deeply to him. "These are his friends," said a former K-III employee who insisted on anonymity. "This is what he cares about, this is where he lives."
Aside from the piece on Mr. Rohatyn and Mr. Rattner, the articles in New York that Mr. Kravis is said to have found objectionable include one last January on money laundering by the Russian mob that raised questions about the role of Republic National Bank, which is controlled by Edmund J. Safra, the former American Express executive. Another was a feature in March about "walkers" who escort New York's wealthy women around town; one of the walkers was Mr. Kravis's friend Oscar de la Renta, who helped the financier's ex-wife Carolyne Roehm get her start as a fashion designer. Mr. Andersen said Mr. Kravis himself called to relay the displeasure of Mr. de la Renta's wife, Annette, in response to the piece.
Some suspect the final straw was last month's lead story on Bob Dole, which depicted the Presidential candidate with his eyes closed on the cover next to the headline, "Bob Dole, Wake Up!" and identified Mr. Kravis as one of Mr. Dole's wealthiest New York contributors.
THIS is not the first New York magazine regime to feel the heat from Mr. Kravis. According to writers at New York and one K-III executive, all of whom insisted on anonymity, Mr. Kravis once phoned Edward Kosner, Mr. Andersen's predecessor, and asked him to yank an article on Leon Black, the former Drexel Burnham Lambert executive.
Mr. Kosner, now editor in chief of Esquire magazine, said that he did not recall a call from Mr. Kravis but that the story had stalled because both Mr. Kravis and Mr. Black refused to discuss a deal they were negotiating.
The author of the article on Mr. Black, Craig Horowitz -- who is still a contributing editor at New York -- said the piece was killed because of pressure from Mr. Kravis, but declined to discuss the circumstances.
According to a high-ranking K-III employee, Mr. Reilly was disappointed by what he viewed as Mr. Kosner's cave-in to Mr. Kravis. Indeed, Mr. Reilly, by all accounts, does his best to play middleman and mollify both sides. While he shares Mr. Kravis's attention to the bottom line, he has also repeatedly sought to protect Mr. Andersen from Mr. Kravis's opinions.
While Mr. Reilly declined to discuss his role as a buffer, Mr. Andersen said that Mr. Reilly and Mr. McQuillen frequently ran interference between him and Mr. Kravis, though not always successfully. "Henry Kravis was always on them, always unhappy," Mr. Andersen said. "They filtered as much of that as they could to prevent him from calling me. Harry would say, 'You've got to do what's right journalistically. We'll take care of Henry.' "
BUT in the end, Mr. Reilly and Mr. McQuillen said, they did not dismiss Mr. Andersen at Mr. Kravis's direction. "He never asked me to fire Kurt and he never asked me to pull a story," Mr. Reilly said.
Rather, K-III executives maintain, Mr. Andersen simply failed to meet K-III's financial expectations -- an exacting standard of which other K-III editors are well aware. "You're not working for executives who are former editors or publishers," said Caroline Miller, the editor in chief of Seventeen magazine who -- along with New York's executive editor, Michael Hirschorn -- is a leading candidate to succeed Mr. Andersen. "They are not people who have done your job before or would like to be doing it or who want to tell you how to do it.
"That gives me a tremendous amount of freedom. But it's completely understood that if the magazine doesn't perform well, changes will be made."
(Richard Babcock, the editor of K-III's Chicago magazine, has been serving as acting editor in chief.)
Mr. Andersen said his magazine was performing well, that he had done exactly what the company originally asked him to do, and been given nothing but positive feedback from his bosses.
He said his mandate was to reduce the median age of readers and "give the magazine some edge," a talent Mr. Andersen had honed as an editor of Spy magazine.
"There was never any discussion with me or the publisher of, 'We've invested this money and we expect a return on it to this extent,' " Mr. Andersen said.
Indeed, staffers at New York magazine say K-III's party line seems wholly unconvincing. "None of the reasons that they've given make any sense," said Mr. Doyle, the former news editor. "They knew we were going to have to shake off older readers to get younger ones -- if they didn't know that, they are morons. The same people who watch 'Murder She Wrote' don't watch MTV."
By most industry standards, New York was doing well enough. For the six months that ended in June, its total average paid circulation was 433,832, up four-tenths of 1 percent from that period the year before.
In addition, the number of ad pages had increased slightly to 1,176 for the six months that ended in June, up from 1,152 for the period in 1995, and ad revenue increased 4 percent in that period, to $23.3 million, according to the Publishers Information Bureau.
The magazine also had a cash operating profit of about $6 million on revenues of about $30 million under Mr. Andersen, said someone close to the publication's operations. Moreover, Mr. Andersen points out that during his first year at the magazine, in 1994, newsstand sales were up about 20 percent over all, and that the circulation of subscription-driven magazines like New York tends to move less sharply than those driven by newsstand sales.
Given those numbers, Mr. Andersen said he could not help but feel blind-sided by K-III's decision to oust him before his $500,000 annual contract -- including a $110,000 bonus -- was up in February.
Even some advertisers are scratching their heads. "I guess what I don't understand is that things seemed to be going well," said Roberta Garfinkle, the director of print media at the advertising agency McCann-Erickson. "The circulation was up a little bit, ad pages were up a bit. In this economy, that would appear to be a good sign."
Not good enough for K-III. The company contends that it sounded the alarm bells loud and clear early on, but Mr. Andersen didn't seem to hear them. "Over the last six to nine months, all key barometers took a negative turn," Mr. McQuillen said. "Newsstand was off 8 percent; the insert cards in newsstand copies and subscription copies were off 10 percent; renewals were off by 9 percent."
Although New York had seen a jump in readership among 17- to 25-year-olds, the core readership among 40- to 50-year-olds had begun to defect, he noted. "Readers were leaving the book," Mr. McQuillen said. "The core readership was diminishing, even though we were getting a group of younger readers. Our hope was for the book to do both."
In addition, two K-III focus groups last April, of about a dozen people each, indicated that some readers were disillusioned with the magazine. They wanted more stories about New York, more service-oriented pieces and a less nasty tone in the magazine. And New York's spring direct-mail campaign -- a solicitation of new readers through junk mail -- was disappointing.
TO be sure, the K-III empire has not exactly been a runaway success. The company is highly leveraged, and its stock performance -- shares closed at $10.375 on Friday, up barely from its initial price of $10 on Nov. 1, 1995 -- makes some people wonder whether Mr. Kravis showed his usual savvy in putting about $600 million into K-III. It is hard to imagine Kohlberg Kravis making the same kind of return it reaped this month on the sale of Duracell International Inc. to the Gillette Company: more than $3 billion in profits from a $350 million investment.
"What the New York magazine thing says is that K-III has yet to be a particularly successful investment for KKR," said one investment banker who follows the company closely and spoke on the condition of anonymity. "KKR has not made a significant amount of money on this investment yet and it is a very large one for them. Therefore, there is a high state of nervousness and agitation around K-III."
Investors who participated in leveraged buyout funds put together by Kohlberg Kravis in 1987 and 1993 bought stakes in K-III before it went public and paid about $6 a share. With the stock at $10, they have a slim paper profit, far below what investors typically expect from such high-risk investments. What's more, if Mr. Kravis's investor group, as the majority owner of K-III, tried to take profits by reducing its stake, it would never realize that modest gain because the public investors would feel abandoned and bid down the stock.
But others think Mr. Kravis is on the mark. Media conglomerates are hot these days, precisely because so many investors see huge profit potential in the supposedly synergistic interplay among entertainment, information and cyberspace.
Despite the stock's recent sluggishness, K-III has been generating impressive cash flow since Mr. Reilly and two fellow executives left the Macmillan Inc. publishing house in 1989 to start K-III with Kohlberg Kravis's financial backing.
K-III's free cash flow -- a measure of profitability calculated by taking net earnings and adding back interest, taxes and noncash charges like depreciation and amortization -- has grown more than 30 percent a year since then, to $216.1 million last year. Sales surpassed $1 billion in 1995, up from $845 million in 1993; Mr. Reilly projects they will triple to nearly $3 billion by the end of the century.
"This is a good management team," said John A. Canning, Jr., the president of Madison Dearborn Partners, a private equity investment company in Chicago, another Kohlberg Kravis backer. "K.K.R. knows what they're doing on this one."
Even K-III's patchwork of properties seems to make Wall Street happy. In a seven-year acquisition binge, it has amassed more than 400 product lines across 15 different businesses, including 125 magazines. Recent purchases have included 14 Cahners consumer magazine titles, including American Baby and Sail, for an estimated $180 million, and Westcott Communications, the nation's largest producer of national training courses broadcast by satellite, for $422 million.
"Over all, I like the prospects because they have so many things that are unique niches," said Mr. Hoyt of KDP Investment Advisors. "The more diversification, the less chance of a surprise negative."
That sort of analysis does not hold much water with some investors and media types. They worry that K-III's hodgepodge of holdings reflects a lack of purpose. "They have not entirely figured out the thrust of their company -- whether they want to own a series of trophy properties or a bunch of lesser-known, cash-cow-type things," said someone at another publication who is familiar with K-III and insisted on anonymity.
Not surprisingly, New York magazine staff members cringe at the owners' bottom-line imperatives, calling them misguided and potentially counterproductive. "One of the most revealing comments that's ever come out of there -- I forget if McQuillen or Reilly said it -- was, 'There is no magic in this,' " said one New York magazine writer who insisted on anonymity. "There are those of us in this business who like to think there is some magic in it. It's not like producing airplane parts or cigarettes or some mass-market food. It is different."
OR is it? In discussing Wall Street's perception of K-III, Mr. Reilly likens one of his magazines to other consumer products. "Seventeen is as much a brand as Ivory soap," he said. "If you have a brand position, you're more stabilized. Nobody is knocking Campbell's soup off the counter -- or Seventeen."
This commodity-minded culture, Mr. Andersen said, does not bode well for his yet-to-be-named successor, or for the future editorial independence of New York magazine. "Anybody who has an ambition to do tough, important journalism should give serious thought to whether they're going to be able to do that happily at New York," Mr. Andersen said, "and not perpetually be looking over their shoulders to a degree that is paralyzing."
