SECTION: Section 3; Page 2; Column 3; Money and Business/Financial Desk 

LENGTH: 1269 words

HEADLINE: DIARY

BYLINE: By HUBERT B. HERRING 

 BODY:

INTEREST RATES
Who's Afraid of the Fed?
There's always a lot of nail-biting about what the Federal Reserve is going to do to interest rates. But does it really matter? Or is the economy such a mighty machine that it will find its own course, take it or leave it. For investors, of course, it's all the same; since some people believe that a Fed rate rise will hurt stocks, everyone has to act as if that is true. So in the weeks leading up to last week's Fed meeting, practically every rise or fall in the market was pinned on the latest twist in the great Fed guessing game. When the Fed finally met, it left rates alone; there were suggestions that Alan Greenspan didn't want to rattle things right before the election. Naturally, stocks, which logically should have risen, fell instead; maybe investors were simply exhausted by the waiting game, or maybe they could stand only so much good news.
 
THE ECONOMY
The Poverty Rate Falls
The good news, as reported by the Census Bureau last week: household income -- yes, adjusted for inflation -- is up for the first time since 1989, the poverty rate fell to 13.8 percent in 1995 from 14.5 percent the year before, poverty among the elderly is down, and the poverty rate for blacks dipped below 30 percent for the first time. So whether the glass is half full or not, it definitely has a bit more water in it. The bad news, of course, is that in a nation as ridiculously wealthy as this one, nearly 30 percent of any group should be living in poverty.
 
BOB DOLE
Wrong Station, Perhaps?
With so many economic numbers looking good -- unemployment low, inflation in check, stocks soaring -- President Clinton would just love to point at the charts and crow, "I did that!" That would clearly be an exaggeration, though -- probably a big one. But the fact remains: those numbers do look good. So one can only marvel when Bob Dole, defiantly sporting his "15 percent" button to remind any inattentive souls of his promised tax cut, argues that the economy is "not on the right track." Yet that's what he said last week in an address to the Economic Club of Detroit, lambasting Mr. Clinton as an "old-style, dyed-in-the-wool, big-spending liberal."
 
AT&T STOCK
Sorry, Widows and Orphans
Now this doesn't quite make sense. They say the economy's a pillar of strength, yet a traditional pillar of that economy -- the pillar that the proverbial widows and orphans lean against -- is wobbling. Last week AT&T said it wasn't doing too well this quarter, and its stock tumbled nearly 10 percent in a day. One analyst was puzzled by how AT&T could do so badly in "an industry that is growing very rapidly." But others hinted that AT&T could be the next tottering giant, following in the oversized footsteps of I.B.M. and General Motors. The rapid growth of long-distance service, with its hot competition, could be the problem. And speaking of that competition, AT&T also made its latest move last week, offering a 15-cent flat rate for domestic calls. But that couldn't breathe life into its shares.
 
FAREWELL, CASH?
(Pretty) Smart Cards
Some people can't leave anything alone. Now you'd think that cash was a perfectly good way of paying for many things, and that the ritual of passing bills and making change represented a modest social interaction in a society fixated on computerizing itself into immaculate anonymity. But no: "smart cards" are on their way, with computer-chip "cash" neatly imbedded in them. Last week, though, Bell Communications Research raised a red flag, saying it had found a possible flaw that might make it possible to counterfeit the cards. The sabotage it envisioned, though, was surprisingly low-tech: one variant had something to do with first putting the cards in the oven. That, it seems, sends the cards haywire, providing an opening for the computer thief to creep in and tinker. It all makes dollar bills look awfully sensible.
 
AIRLINES
Pan Am, Infrequently
It's sad, really sad, what befalls our defenseless language. You'd think that if someone said "Pan Am," the topic would be the legendary airline. No, it's just some people who bought the name and started a few flights last week. Or take "frequent flier." Those words are already so far removed from the idea of flying frequently -- "Brother, can you spare a few miles," the New Age panhandler will say -- that a whole generation is surely coming of age with no memory of the original meaning. And now that reborn Pan Am is muddying the water -- or muddying the air -- even further, offering one and a quarter frequent-flier miles for each mile flown. And you can get a free ticket with 20,000 miles, not the usual 25,000. Here's the question, though: How frequently can you fly on an airline that goes only from New York to Miami and Los Angeles?
It was clearly a week of new beginnings in the airline industry, as the Transportation Department also gave Valujet permission to start flying again.
 
COLLEGE COSTS
That Tuition Monster
It's a number guaranteed to give parents an instant anxiety attack: $20,000 a year for tuition (plus room and board, of course). Multiply by 4 (or 8, or 12) and you're not just talking serious money -- you're talking, for most people, impossible money. But when the College Board released its annual tally of college costs last week, it said that fewer than 5 percent of students actually pay that $20,000. More than half, in fact, pay less than $4,000. And while tuition again rose faster than inflation -- 6 percent at public schools, 5 percent at private -- "college is still accessible" for most Americans, the head of the College Board said. One big shift is that while there is plenty of aid available -- $50 billion last year -- loans are taking a bigger chunk than grants.
 
DEATHLY BATTLES
Funeral Giants at War
You'd never go to the equivalent of a Wal-Mart or a McDonald's to arrange a funeral -- you'd naturally go to that homey, familiar place in the oversized Victorian down the street. This business, dependent on civility and soothing tones, would never tolerate a voracious corporate giant -- or would it? Actually, it would, and two of them are in a battle royal, with the Loewen Group digging in its heels last week against a $3.1 billion takeover offer from Service Corporation International. The illuminating thing about all this bursting into the open, of course, is the staggering size of these giants -- and that, appearances aside, one of them just might own that mom-and-pop funeral parlor on the corner. Big Mac with a side order of gladioluses, anyone?
 
IMAGINED FORTUNES
Talk About Thin Air
They can't be serious. Even in this age of Wall Street hotshots stretching the truth or bending the rules a bit to amass some obscenely large fortune, it's just too outlandish. But when Federal prosecutors last week charged Christopher Bagdasarian with fraud, conspiracy and a raft of other misdeeds, this is the tale they told: that Mr. Bagdasarian got $24 million in bank loans solely on the basis of letters from an accomplice at Deloitte & Touche saying, falsely, that he was worth millions, and that he spent the money on a California mansion and private jet. Not satisfied with that, prosecutors say, he formed a company called Normandy America and tried to take it public -- even though any company assets were pure fiction. No investors were hurt, for Normandy's moment in the sun lasted all of one day -- Aug. 15, 1995 -- before trading was halted. No money changed hands. Prosecutors weren't joking around: the charges could mean up to 165 years in prison.

HEADLINE: Fears and Jitters;
One Man's October Surprise . . .

BYLINE: By JAMES BENNET 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
FROM a historical perspective, the biggest October surprise in this Presidential campaign could well be if there actually is one.
The bogyman of front-runners and the fantasy of laggards and antsy journalists, an October surprise -- a bolt of news upending the race -- could include anything this year from an economic jolt to a disaster in Bosnia to an indictment of a Clinton Administration official, or even of a Clinton. At least so speculate the bored journalists, the Clinton aides who are nervous or superstitious and, most fervent of all, the Dole campaign officials. 
 But Alan Brinkley, a professor of American history at Columbia University, had to cast back to the last century for an example of such a surprise. "Lincoln's re-election seemed in real doubt in the spring," he said, "and the changing course of Union fortunes in battle insured his victory."
Still, even that turnabout failed to qualify as a genuine October surprise, he said; those changed fortunes were cemented by the fall of Atlanta -- on Sept. 2, 1864.
Professor Brinkley sounded rather unsurprised that a journalist was inquiring about surprises. "I've never seen such a desultory campaign, and journalists themselves seem so desperate for something to write about," he said. "No offense."
One Clinton aide pointed out that a surprise could benefit the President, who has already, Dole officials grumble, had his share of political breaks, including more good economic news last week. Some surprises that have cropped up late in campaigns have merely confirmed the course of the election.
In October 1972, for example, Henry A. Kissinger declared that "peace is at hand" in Vietnam. But President Nixon was not exactly in dire need of a boost in the polls against George McGovern, who lost by a landslide.
The term "October surprise" dates from the 1980 election, when Ronald Reagan and his aides worried that Jimmy Carter would pull off a coup by springing the hostages in Iran before the election. He didn't, of course, and some analysts have tried to prove, so far inconclusively, that the Reagan campaign had a hand in thwarting him.
Indeed, while history offers few actual October surprises, it does present some tantalizing might-have-beens. In 1940, Wendell Willkie warned that Franklin D. Roosevelt had a secret deal with the British to enter the war. But he never presented any evidence, said Michael R. Beschloss, a historian of the Presidency.
"One could say that if the full extent of Roosevelt's dealings with the British, and particularly with Churchill, had been been revealed, it would have changed the course of the election, and Willkie would have won," Mr. Beschloss said.

HEADLINE: Promising Trade-Off for the Needy

BYLINE: By PETER T. KILBORN 

DATELINE: ST. LOUIS, Sept. 24

 BODY:
After 40 years of combating poverty in the obscurity of inner-city St. Louis, George Eberle Jr. now finds welfare experts from around the country knocking on his door.
They have come to look at the method Mr. Eberle devised that lets people who are short of cash get a baby sitter, simple car repairs or rides to the doctor's office. 
 People obtain these services through a type of barter system, using make-believe money called "time dollars." To earn the dollars, which can also be exchanged for donated goods like household staples, they do other services, like reading to the blind or cleaning a neighbor's home.
All these chores add up to real work. And with the enactment of the nation's new welfare law, with its emphasis on moving people from dependency to jobs, Mr. Eberle's system has caught the eye of foundations, research organizations and public officials. While they acknowledge it has limitations, they say it can be a tool both to create a sense of community through a novel form of commerce, and to meet the new law's work requirements.
"This is the place you would go look" for a model of a welfare-work system, said Michael E. Hartmann, director of research at the Wisconsin Policy Institute in Madison, which is urging communities in that state to develop work-bartering systems like this one. Richard P. Nathan, director of the Rockefeller Institute of Government in Albany, said, "We ought to be trying to replicate that and expand it."
Gary J. Stangler, director of the Missouri Department of Social Services, said the system should help the state meet some tough provisions of the new welfare law, like one requiring that 25 percent of a state's welfare recipients be working at least 20 hours a week in the coming fiscal year and 50 percent be working at least 30 hours a week by 2002.
Mr. Stangler said the system should help the state meet the terms of the law for another reason -- it helps provide child care. Child care is scarce in the inner city, yet vital for young mothers who are being required to work under the new law. The time-dollar system has allowed for widespread use of child care, along with close supervision and training of the providers.
Mr. Stangler credits Mr. Eberle's organization for insuring the quality of the program's supervision and organization.
Mr. Eberle, 68, is president of Grace Hill, a nonprofit, private agency in Murphy Blair, a neighborhood on the city's Near North Side. Established as a settlement house by the Episcopal Church in 1903, Grace Hill is now a social services conglomerate, running a job-training center, five health-care centers and other programs, in addition to the time-dollar system. It has an annual budget of $15.6 million and a staff of 400, drawn largely from the neighborhood.
The time-dollar system started slowly, but with the help of a grant from the Robert Wood Johnson Foundation nine years ago and then with computerization, transactions have soared. In 1992, people earned 9,552 time dollars. By last year, their earnings had quintupled, to 45,026 dollars, and through August this year they had reached 41,014. About 3,000 people participate, some occasionally, a few more than 20 hours a week.
"They're doing an extraordinary job," said Edgar S. Cahn, a founder of the Legal Services Corporation during the Johnson Administration and now president of the Time Dollar Institute, formed in Washington a year ago to promote barter systems.
Mr. Cahn, who, like Mr. Eberle, was an early apostle of cash-free commerce, helped organize a similar barter network in Miami that reaches 4,000 people. But, he said, that program has yet to achieve Grace Hill's level of technological sophistication. Much smaller networks have been started in many other communities, including Washington, Brooklyn, Chicago, Cleveland, Portland, Me., Grand Junction, Colo., and Norfolk, Va.
Mr. Eberle, who has a degree in social work from Washington University here, went to work at Grace Hill at age 26 and became president six years later, in 1960.
He developed the time-dollar system here as a way to breathe life and community spirit into places that stores, factories and most other private sources of employment and commerce had fled.
"How do you increase the capacity of a community to respond to its needs?" he asked. "How do you take a neighborhood and capture the sparks that are there?"
He said that most people have talents and needs, that most will respond to one another's needs and that in responding they can create social capital -- a community's wealth as measured in skills, services, interdependence and good will.
But to fuel the engine of social capital-building in a community where the rent can consume most of a welfare check, Mr. Eberle needed something other than money. So he developed the time-dollar system. Two sisters, Leopra Drummond, 59, and Mary Toles, 53, both unemployed residents of Murphy Blair with chronic health problems, explained how the system works.
"If we do something for a neighbor, we're paid for it in time dollars," Ms. Toles said. "We have to make out a slip."
"We go to someone's home," Ms. Drummond said. "Clean their house, wash their dishes, wash their clothes."
"Go to the store for them," Ms. Toles said. "Read to them if they're blind. I'm having trouble with my sight. Certain times, a neighbor will come and write for me, write recipes. I pay them with time dollars."
The transactions are recorded in a computer, with 30 terminals reaching nine neighborhoods, that also serves as a yellow pages of time-dollar services. The time dollars can also be exchanged for goods at two simple stores run by Grace Hill and stocked through donations.
"We can use them to go purchase household items," Ms. Drummond said. "Toilet paper, tissues, perms for the ladies' hair, the shampoo, the conditioner."
"Pampers and diaper wipes," Ms. Toles added. "Bug spray, rat poison. Just regular household goods."
In addition to the Johnson foundation, another leading philanthropy, the Rockefeller Family Fund, is sponsoring bartering projects. During the summer, officials of another, the Annie P. Casey Foundation, toured Grace Hill. For public-assistance officials and philanthropies like these, barter systems offer a means of helping the nation's poorest communities adjust to the welfare law.
Officials say they are impressed by how the system can generate work and work experience as it helps foster commerce and community cohesion.
The most widely used services include child care, house cleaning, tutoring, making phone calls for people who cannot, delivering meals to the homebound and giving people rides to stores and appointments.
Work paid for in chits rather than real dollars may be unconventional, but it appears to suit the law's definition of work.
The law says work can include time looking for a job, some vocational training, and community service for public and nonprofit employers for which workers continue receiving welfare checks in lieu of regular pay.
The system also opens a road to the world of real work. It helps groom communities' least-educated, least-employable residents by teaching them workplace disciplines like making appointments and showing up on time.
Quran Boykins, 27, a single mother who left school in the 11th grade and has children aged 10 and 7, is in the throes of that transition.
As a time-dollar worker, she said, "I go to my mother and wash for her for two hours, and that's two time dollars. I go to another person and do four hours of cleaning, and that's four time dollars."
Meanwhile, she was working toward a high school equivalency diploma at Grace Hill's job-training center. Then, a few days ago, she climbed another rung. She took a job in a hospital kitchen that pays $4.70 an hour.
The system has limits. Some of its services, like relatives doing chores for relatives, probably would not be considered work under the welfare law, Mr. Stangler said.
And while it supplements the resources of families that qualify for welfare checks, it is of little use to single, working-age adults who are largely ineligible and need real money for rent before they need help cleaning the house.
Still, inner-city residents seem to value the time-dollar system, and for more than the work it provides. "This is basically a way of having some self-esteem, of having good feelings," said Charles Bradford, 58, whose diabetes forced him to retire on a small pension from a good job as a machinist.
Mr. Bradford earns time dollars helping to manage the system and doing odd jobs for people like moving furniture.
"It takes away the pain of not having a real job," he said. "I feel good about myself at the end of the day."

GRAPHIC: Photo: A St. Louis program may be a model for plans to let people work for goods and services. George Eberle Jr., center, head of the group running the plan, helped residents use a computer to match skills and needs. (Bill Stover for The New York Times)

LENGTH: 775 words

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE DRUG ISSUE;
What Price Victory? Study Feeds Contention

BYLINE: By CHRISTOPHER S. WREN 

 BODY:
At first glance, a dispute about economic correlations of price and consumption might seem an arcane quibble. But in this election year, some Republicans on Capitol Hill have accused the White House of skulduggery by suppressing a study that maintains it is more cost effective to reduce the cocaine supply by interdicting it in Latin America than to reduce demand by treating addicts in the United States.
The study, "An Empirical Examination of Counterdrug Interdiction Program Effectiveness," was submitted to the White House's Office of National Drug Policy this year by the Institute for Defense Analyses, a research group that usually works on contract for the Pentagon, though never before on a study of the price of illegal drugs. 
 Its authors criticized a study by analysts from the Rand Corporation who determined that cutting American consumption of cocaine by 1 percent through interdiction at the source would cost $783 million a year but only $34 million by treating addicts. The study by the Institute for Defense Analyses concluded that reducing demand by 1 percent by interdiction at the source would cost $8 million a year.
Specialists in drug policy who have read the new study have dismissed it as flawed and naive.
"I think a huge yawn is the appropriate reaction," said Mark A. R. Kleiman, a professor at the University of California at Los Angeles. "It's pretty clear that they started out with an answer and fitted the question to the answer."
The report has been construed as supporting Bob Dole's contention that President Clinton failed to stop drugs from coming into the country by diverting resources to treatment.
The Subcommittee on National Security, International Affairs and Criminal Justice in the the Republican-controlled House plans to hold a hearing on Tuesday into why the study was not released by the new White House director of drug policy, Gen. Barry R. McCaffrey. His office circulated copies of the report to drug experts for comment, which has been overwhelmingly negative.
A member of the subcommittee, John L. Mica, Republican of Florida, said he believed that the report was suppressed because it was highly critical of the White House policy.
"If what I've heard is borne out by the facts, we're going to be calling for some resignations," Mr. Mica said in a telephone interview. "To have the report not only ignored, but also buried, is going to raise a furor."
Though he stopped short of asking General McCaffrey to step down as the White House's director of national drug policy, Mr. Mica added: "The first action of the new drug czar was to make it known that none of this was to get out. I'm completely dumbfounded by his action."
Before he took his new post, General McCaffrey commanded the United States Army Southern Command in Panama, where he ran the drug interdiction in Latin America that the new study cites so favorably. While he wished he could take the credit, the general said in a telephone interview, "it seems whimsical to suggest that our contributions drove up the price and drove down the use of cocaine."
The Administration is allocating two-thirds of its $15.3 billion drug-control budget to the supply side of the drug war and one-third to the demand side. "We are absolutely supportive of rigorous source-country strategy, and continued interdiction," General McCaffrey said. "But at the end of the day, the war will be won around the kitchen table, with parents talking to children."
The study includes a chart that shows that since 1985, increases in the price of cocaine in American cities have coincided with interdictions in Peru, Bolivia and Colombia.
But Peter Reuter, a drug-policy expert at the University of Maryland, said, "This implies, for example, that the hundreds of thousands of domestic arrests and imprisonments, the 70 or so tons that the D.E.A. seizes domestically and all other aspects of domestic enforcement have no impact on price, or at least that their impact is constant."
He and others also questioned the study's description of the illegal drug market as free and unrestricted. Drug syndicates sometimes maintain substantial inventories to minimize disruptions and hold back cocaine to manipulate the price.
Critics have said the study inaccurately calculated the rise in drug prices. To determine the cost of drugs on the street, the study used the prices for drug shipments as large as 22 pounds. But street sales, reckoned in fractions of an ounce, are inflated by markups by local dealers. The study cited prices of $50 to $85 a gram in 1992 and 1993. One expert said the prices were actually $120 to $168 a gram.

HEADLINE: In France, Leaders Brace For the Worst From Unions

BYLINE: By CRAIG R. WHITNEY 

DATELINE: PARIS, Sept. 28

 BODY:
President Jacques Chirac and his unpopular conservative Government hunkered down this week to face a second autumn of unrest over spending cuts that they insist are necessary even with unemployment at its highest level in nearly 50 years.
Some subway and bus workers in Paris went on strike Friday for a pay raise and an end to job cuts, but they did not bring service to a halt. Commuter traffic in the Mediterranean city of Marseilles, however, was severely disrupted. 
 Teachers have called a nationwide strike for Monday, and all the big railroad and civil service unions plan to stop work for 24 hours on Oct. 17. They are protesting the Government's plans to trim the public payroll as part of a spending freeze intended to cut the budget deficit.
Some union leaders have predicted a repetition of the two-week national transportation shutdown that brought most of the French economy to a standstill late last year, but Mr. Chirac and Prime Minister Alain Juppe insist that the painful austerity is needed to get France ready to adopt the common European currency planned for the end of the decade.
Mr. Juppe promised that the new European money system would bring "an economic big bang for Europe and for France," which needs one more than ever now that unemployment has risen to 12.6 percent of the labor force.
But a big bang in 1999 would be too late to save Mr. Juppe's Government from the possibility of defeat in national elections in 1998. With public opinion polls showing support for his policies now below 30 percent, there have been mutterings of revolt from the ranks of his party, the Rally for the Republic, and its partners in the Government coalition.
Mr. Chirac sharply reminded party conference participants in Le Havre this week that he and Mr. Juppe expected their loyalty, and the Prime Minister has asked the conservative-dominated Parliament for a vote of confidence on Wednesday.
His plans include a budget freeze to keep the Government deficit next year to $56.7 billion, the 3 percent-of-gross-national-product limit imposed by the monetary clauses of the Treaty on European Union in 1992. The Government has also called for an increase in alcohol and cigarette taxes to bring down the health insurance and retirement system deficit to $9.4 billion by 1997.
The United States Government budget deficit, by comparison, is less than 2 percent of the American gross national product, according to the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development.
"We have been living for decades with the idea that we could do whatever we wanted, and pass on the deficit to future generations," the Finance Minister, Jean Arthuis, said. "We have been paying Government functionaries' salaries partly by borrowing. We have to end such irresponsible forms of behavior."
But the budget freeze -- and plans to reorganize deficit-ridden national transportation systems -- will mean that tens of thousands more jobs will be lost to attrition, labor unions predict, and prominent Government supporters like former Prime Minister Edouard Balladur have pleaded recently for a letup in the austerity.
With one national poll by the French Public Opinion Institute showing 83 percent of the voters in favor of a change in economic policy, Marc Blondel, whose Workers' Force union led the national rail shutdown last year, tested the militancy of his supporters at a demonstration on the Place de la Bastille a week ago. He predicted that as many as 100,000 people might show up, but despite sunny skies, only about 10,000 did.
"I keep hearing that things are going to explode this autumn," said a rival union leader, Louis Viannet of the Communist-led General Confederation of Labor. "I really don't know, but we are in a situation which could very rapidly trigger a reflex of revolt."

HEADLINE: Even Allies Astonished At Spending On Museum

BYLINE: By The New York Times

DATELINE: HARRISBURG, Pa.

 BODY:
As controller of this modest-size city, Jim McCarthy is expected to review public expenditures with a sharp eye, which is why he became alarmed when aides told him last year that the Mayor had bought a $35,000 cannon.
"I said, 'A cannon?' " Mr. McCarthy recalled. " 'What are we doing with a cannon?' " 
 While the city council has agreed to spend $7.8 million in a controversial effort to create a first-rate Civil War museum to stoke the local economy, Mayor Stephen R. Reed has been stockpiling historical objects at a far greater rate than many had expected.
In a city with an operating budget of $81 million this year, Mr. Reed has been shifting the proceeds of bond issues to allocate about $13.7 million over the past two years just on items for the museum, which has yet to be built.
He bought Abraham Lincoln's hat box, minus the hat, for $40,000; Ulysses S. Grant's harness and tack box, for $65,000, and Stonewall Jackson's gauntlet, for $68,000.
To those treasures he added hundreds of more mundane items like a mess kit for $5,500; a tin shaving cup, $100, and a bale of cotton, also $100.
The artifacts are scattered, stored in the public safety building, the city government center and even a maintenance garage.
The breadth and cost of the collection have stunned critics. In an editorial titled "Reed's Folly?" The Patriot-News said the purchases ranked as "one of the more bizarre municipal expenditures in the state, if not the country."
The plan for the museum, which Mr. Reed envisions will draw tourists from the nearby Gettysburg National Military Park, is particularly questionable to those who see far greater needs in this city of 54,238.
"You look around and you see houses that are in desperate need of help," said Wendi Taylor, 48, who lives in the Uptown neighborhood. "You see roads that need to be improved. You see trees that need to be cut back and sidewalks that need to be repaired. It seems to me, for the good of our community, a Civil War museum shouldn't be on the top of the list."
But with the Mayor, the city council and most other city officials, including Mr. McCarthy, all Democrats, Mr. Reed has faced few obstacles, but some raised eyebrows. Councilwoman Peggy Grove, as well as Mr. McCarthy, support the project, but said they were astonished by the level of spending.
Mr. Reed, who is in his fourth four-year term, dismissed suggestions that he was ignoring higher priorities like street repair and housing to focus on the museum. "We've spent much larger sums than $13 million on virtually all of those things," he said.
Besides, he added, to be a success, the museum has to have an expansive and impressive collection. "It can't be a run-of-the-mill small museum that has the usual customary musket, cartridge box, belt buckle," Mr. Reed said. "It's got to be one of a kind."
The proposed name reflects such ambitions: the National Civil War Museum at Harrisburg.
Mr. Reed said the collection would cover from 1850 to 1895 and include exhibitions on slavery and the westward expansion. Officials expect construction of the museum to begin late next year, with the opening in 1998.
A history buff, Mr. Reed is counting on Harrisburg's proximity to Gettysburg, 38 miles south, to attract Civil War enthusiasts. He estimated that 10 percent of the 1.7 million visitors a year in Gettysburg would also visit Harrisburg, the state capital.
Though home to Camp Curtin, a large training ground for Union troops, Harrisburg saw no action in the war, and it has no battlefield.
"One of our intents in our exhibits is to try to pique people's interests," said Katie Lawhon, a public affairs specialist at Gettysburg. "We want to encourage them not only to leave the museum and get on the Gettysburg battlefield, but to visit other sites related to the Civil War. Harrisburg is definitely a part of that."
Mr. Reed helped revitalize the city when it was on the brink of financial collapse at the start of his administration in 1981.
When a downtown hotel went bankrupt, the city bought it. When the prized minor league baseball team, the Senators, threatened to move to Massachusetts, Mr. Reed had the city buy it, too.
"Yes, he's popular," the president of the Council, O. Frank DeGarcia, said. "Why? Because he gets things done."
Occasionally, Mr. Reed and his staff conduct economic development seminars for officials of other small cities, encouraging them to diversify.
"The museum suddenly didn't emerge as 'This is Harrisburg's answer to building the tax base,' " Mr. Reed said. "That would be absurd. It is part of a much bigger picture that includes many different types of endeavors."

HEADLINE: POLITICS: OVERCOMING DIFFERENCES;
Dole, in Choosing Kemp, Buried A Bitter Past Rooted in Doctrine

SERIES: A POLITICAL LIFE: Overcoming Differences

BYLINE: By ELIZABETH KOLBERT 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
Even in an age of politics by autobiography, Bob Dole is to an unusual extent running for President on the strength of his own history -- his Dust Bowl upbringing, his sacrifices in World War II, his memories, as he said in his acceptance speech, of "an America only the unknowing call myth."
But when Mr. Dole embraced Jack Kemp as his running mate last month, he put a long stretch of his personal history aside. 
 As the two men have traveled across the country together, beaming and bantering with each other, it has not seemed to matter that they had spent nearly 15 years arguing and exchanging insults. Nor did it seem to matter that Mr. Kemp had secretly helped engineer one of Mr. Dole's most costly and humiliating defeats as a legislative leader.
Nor did it even seem to matter that just five months before Mr. Dole picked him as a running mate, Mr. Kemp had dealt Mr. Dole the ultimate political affront, endorsing his opponent in the Republican primaries, Steve Forbes, well after it was clear that Mr. Forbes had no chance of winning the nomination.
Although private animosities are often put aside for national campaigns, the history of conflict between Mr. Dole and Mr. Kemp is, in fact, much more than that. A look at their shared past shows that the two men clashed not merely because they were rivals for political power -- though this was doubtless part of it -- but because they saw themselves as representing alternative futures for the Republican Party.
Until last month, Mr. Dole could have been called the essential Midwestern conservative, a deficit hawk who advocated that the Government not spend more than it took in.
As a young Representative, he was, for example, one of the few Congressmen to vote against the historic tax cut proposed by President John F. Kennedy.
Mr. Kemp, by contrast, made his mark in Republican politics as one of the early champions of supply-side economics. He has advocated sweeping tax cuts as a way to solve many, if not most, of the nation's problems, and has pointed to the Kennedy tax cuts as an inspiration. He has long maintained that deficits don't really matter and that the fears they generate are a mere "bugaboo."
No less significantly, Mr. Kemp has often chastised advocates of austerity for holding back the Republican Party. The debate between the two factions, in his terms, has a world historical cast. On his side of the divide are the "New Testament Republicans," on Mr. Dole's side the "Old Testament Republicans."
At the time of its announcement, many compared Mr. Dole's selection of his former antagonist to his earlier decision to step down from the Senate. Again, Mr. Dole had surprised the political establishment with an imaginative and risky move: in picking Mr. Kemp, he was forgoing the obvious advantage of a running mate from a battleground state.
But the deeper ties between the two decisions may lie in the way they both elide history. Like Mr. Dole's choice to give up the Senate and campaign as "just a man," his running with Mr. Kemp again demonstrates his willingness to ignore the past even as he campaigns on its basis.
 
The Early 80's
 
Opposing Views On Economic Policy
The antagonism between Mr. Dole and Mr. Kemp dates from the early years of the Reagan Administration, when both were at the center of a series of bruising political battles over supply-side economics.
More than anyone else, perhaps, Mr. Kemp moved the supply-side agenda from obscurity to the center of the national debate. In early 1980, he signed on an important supporter for the agenda: then-candidate Ronald Reagan. (David Stockman, the director of the Office of Management and Budget during Mr. Reagan's first term, has recalled that Mr. Kemp returned from a trip to California early that year and announced that Mr. Reagan had been successfully "converted.") Mr. Reagan subsequently made Mr. Kemp's proposal for a 30 percent across-the-board tax cut one of the central features of his Presidential campaign.
Once Mr. Reagan was elected, his Kemp-inspired plan became one of Mr. Dole's chief concerns. It fell to him, as the new chairman of the Senate Finance Committee, to push the tax plan out of committee and through the Senate.
In years since, Mr. Dole has claimed credit for the tax bill's passage, and many of his allies today argue that it could not have happened without him. At the same time, his position was ambiguous enough for Mr. Kemp to later claim, with some justice, that Mr. Dole was actually a foot-dragger who resisted the tax cut "every step of the way."
Certainly, Mr. Dole made no secret about his reservations about the plan. Much to the annoyance of the supply-siders, Mr. Dole mused out loud about the dangers of a large tax cut even as the tax bill was wending its way through committee, worrying in front of reporters that it could spur inflation.
But the real break with Mr. Kemp and his allies occurred after the Reagan tax plan was already approved.
Within months of the bill's passage in the summer of 1981, Mr. Dole began to push for a package of tax increases -- or "reforms," as he preferred to call them -- to bring the growing deficit down. This, in turn, Mr. Dole argued, would lower interest rates, which many at the time argued were hampering an economic recovery.
In response, Mr. Kemp went public with his opposition to any sort of tax increase. In an Op-Ed piece in The New York Times, Mr. Kemp criticized those like Mr. Dole who, he argued, were "being lured by the siren song of Wall Street." He blamed high interest rates not on the Government's fiscal policies but on its monetary policies and argued, as he often did, that the answer to the problem was a return to the gold standard. He called the ballooning deficit the "new bugaboo."
The Op-Ed piece was typical of Mr. Kemp. During his tenure in the House from 1971 to 1989, he did not serve on any tax-writing committees, nor did he devote himself, like Mr. Dole, to the nitty-gritty of legislative compromise. Instead, he often aired his views directly through the press. "Kemp had a lot to say on taxes," said an aide who worked for Mr. Dole in the early 1980's, "but basically he had little to do except make pronouncements."
Mr. Kemp's headline-grabbing strategy enraged many of his colleagues, including, it seems, Mr. Dole. "Bob was sometimes apoplectic about the attention Jack got by virtue of his clarion calls," said former Congressman Barber Conable, who at the time was the ranking Republican on the House Ways and Means Committee.
Mr. Dole's tax package, which included a host of revenue-raising measures from higher excise taxes on cigarettes to increased airport fees, was known as the Tax Equity and Fiscal Responsibility Act, or Tefra. Though Mr. Reagan reluctantly agreed to support Tefra, Mr. Kemp still tried to get the measure killed as it went to conference.
Working outside the Republican Congressional leadership, he sent telegrams to his allies summoning them to a series of private stop-Tefra meetings on Capitol Hill. In a letter he circulated among them, he warned ominously that "quietly, without debate, the Republican Party is in danger of making a U-turn back to its familiar role of tax collector for Democratic spending programs." Participants in these closed-door meetings said that about three dozen legislators and Administration officials attended, including Representative Newt Gingrich and Trent Lott, then a Representative.
Many in the Republican leadership were sharply critical of Mr. Kemp for holding the meetings, and Mr. Dole publicly chastised the participants. "I am somewhat distressed to see Republicans leading the charge to undermine the President," he said, and he went on to compare the opposition to the tax package organized by Mr. Kemp to the Democrats' opposition to President Johnson over the Vietnam War.
Eventually, Mr. Dole won the battle over Tefra. The measure passed the House in August 1982, though Mr. Kemp voted against it, arguing, in a formulation favored by supply-siders, that it was a similar sort of tax increase that had led to the Depression.
Afterward, Mr. Dole enjoyed a burst of positive press for his achievement. During one of the many interviews he gave at the time, Mr. Dole told The Washington Post, "I never really understood all that supply-side business."
 
The Mid-80's
 
The Differences Grow More Bitter
The next few times he clashed with Mr. Kemp, Mr. Dole would not be so lucky.
In the summer of 1984, Republicans gathered in Dallas to write the party's platform for the fall election. Along with allies like Mr. Gingrich and Mr. Lott, Mr. Kemp was advocating an unequivocal plank on tax increases that would, effectively, put President Reagan on record as ruling them out. Mr. Gingrich explicitly referred to such a plank as "Dole-proof."
Not surprisingly, Mr. Dole opposed such a plank.
"Let's not trap the President," he argued at platform committee hearings in August. When the plank backed by Mr. Kemp and his allies was adopted, Mr. Dole dubbed the group, often called the Young Turks, "the Young Hypocrites."
"They think they can peddle the idea that they've taken over the party," he said. "Well, they aren't the Republican Party, and they aren't going to be."
Many saw the fight over the platform as the opening round in the battle for the 1988 Presidential nomination, and to a certain extent it was. But Mr. Dole and Mr. Kemp faced off in a far more consequential battle just one year later.
In 1985, Mr. Dole, ever more concerned about the Government red ink -- and about holding onto the Senate's Republican majority -- decided to face the issue in classic deficit-hawk fashion. By now the Senate majority leader, he pressed his colleagues to adopt an austere budget that would have eliminated 13 domestic programs and, even more significantly, reduced cost-of-living adjustments for Social Security recipients.
In May, the Senate adopted Mr. Dole's proposed budget after a drama of rare suspense: Pete Wilson, then a Senator, recovering from an emergency appendectomy, cast the crucial "yea" vote from a hospital gurney, while Vice President George Bush cut short a Western swing and broke the resulting tie.
As with Tefra, Mr. Kemp once again went to work behind the scenes in opposition to Mr. Dole. The Senate budget plan, he argued, was a mistake, and he called Donald T. Regan, the new White House chief of staff, to press this argument. "I think we can get this budget back on track," Mr. Kemp later said he had told Mr. Regan.
Along with some other House Republicans, Mr. Kemp met with Mr. Regan in a series of negotiating sessions from which Mr. Dole was deliberately excluded. The meetings, at least from Mr. Kemp's perspective, had the desired effect: a few weeks after Senate Republicans had gone on record favoring Social Security cutbacks, President Reagan made a deal with the Democratic-controlled House and announced that there would be no cutbacks, after all. Aides to Mr. Dole complained that the Senator had been "blind-sided."
Associates say Mr. Kemp opposed the proposed Social Security cuts both on ideological grounds and on the more obvious grounds of practical politics. "Kemp had a strong commitment to Social Security as one of the last remaining Federal programs that's family-centered," said Jeffrey Bell, an economic consultant who has advised Mr. Kemp. At the same time, Mr. Bell said, he saw Mr. Dole's budget as "political dynamite."
In political terms, the Senate Republicans' budget vote had been enormously costly -- the Republicans lost the Senate the next year -- and the proposal's unraveling represents one of the most bitter defeats of Mr. Dole's legislative career. Mr. Dole made no secret of his outrage or of whom he blamed for his defeat. When he accused Democrats and "a few noisy Republicans" of "playing politics" with the budget, the allusion to Mr. Kemp was clear.
For the rest of that summer, Mr. Dole and Mr. Kemp continued to spar in private and in public, eventually producing what still ranks as the most memorable exchange of their long rivalry.
At a meeting of young Republicans in July, Mr. Dole remarked with barbed humor that "Kemp wants a business deduction for hair spray." Speaking to the same group, Mr. Kemp came back with the retort: "In a recent fire, Bob Dole's library burned down. Both books were lost. And he hadn't even finished coloring one of them."
 
The '88 Campaign
 
The Deficit Hawk Vs. Mr. Growth
A few months ago, a longtime adviser to Mr. Dole, trying to explain how the former Senator thinks, singled out Mr. Kemp as a politician he found particularly irksome.
"He can't figure out how you can get to be Jack Kemp," the adviser said. In Mr. Dole's view, he said, Mr. Kemp is "non-deserving."
Over the years, Mr. Dole has often suggested that Mr. Kemp's political success was a function of his ability to side-step difficult choices. While Mr. Kemp preached painless supply-side solutions, he once said, "some of us do all the dirty work." At another point, referring obliquely to Mr. Kemp and his allies, he said: "They never made a hard choice in their lives."
For his part, Mr. Kemp often was publicly disdainful of what he called Mr. Dole's "root canal" approach to economics. And he openly blamed Mr. Dole and his fellow deficit hawks for keeping Republicans in the minority.
"The Republican Party lost this country with an almost obsessive concern with a balanced budget," he said during the heat of the 1985 budget debate.
In 1988, when the two men both ran for the Republican Presidential nomination, their mutual antagonism became one of the running themes of the campaign.
Although Mr. Dole largely tried to ignore Mr. Kemp, who was running far behind both him and Vice President Bush, he continued to stress that the time had come to make tough choices, and that Mr. Kemp was trying to duck them.
At one candidates' debate, for example, Mr. Dole declared: "We've got to deal with the deficit. We're not going to grow out of it as Jack used to say, just cut taxes and don't worry about it." At another debate, when Mr. Kemp spoke about "morning in America," Mr. Dole responded tartly that it was actually "high noon."
"That's the time to get ready and do something," Mr. Dole later elaborated. "It takes a while to get up in the morning. We need to deal with the deficit now."
Mr. Kemp, meanwhile, explicitly tried to focus the contest on his and Mr. Dole's competing economic programs and their implications for the party. In broad, even sweeping terms, he portrayed the race as a fight over two views of the nation's future -- one (Mr. Dole's) dark and gloomy, the other (his) sunny and glad.
The race, he said at one point, would come down to "a pain, sacrifice and bitter medicine Bob Dole campaign versus a growth, hope, opportunity progressive, populist, conservative Jack Kemp campaign."
Mr. Kemp's campaign press secretary, John Buckley, now Mr. Dole's communications director, expanded on that message, at one point declaring the contest between the two men a "struggle for the heart and soul of the Republican Party."
"The fault line in the Republican Party now is between the high-tax, deficit-mania wing that Bob Dole is the living embodiment of and the high-growth, low-tax wing that Kemp personifies," Mr. Buckley said.
With Mr. Bush's 1988 victory, the antagonism between the Mr. Dole and Mr. Kemp became, for several years at least, largely irrelevant. Mr. Kemp left Congress, and Mr. Bush subsequently appointed him to head the Department of Housing and Urban Development. Mr. Dole, meanwhile, continued on as Senate minority leader. The two had relatively little to do with each other until the beginning of this year, when the Presidential campaign once again brought them into conflict.
 
The '96 Campaign
 
A Snub by Kemp, Then a Partnership
At the start of the 1996 election cycle, many in the so-called "growth" wing of the Republican Party were pinning their hopes on Mr. Kemp. After he announced he would not run, in January 1995, one of Mr. Kemp's friends and supporters, Steve Forbes, the publishing magnate, decided to join the race himself. Mr. Forbes ran almost entirely on the basis of his tax plan: the graduated income tax, he proposed, should be replaced with a flat tax that would represent an almost universal tax cut.
The economic prescriptions that Mr. Forbes was pushing were Mr. Kemp's prescriptions, as well; Mr. Kemp had, in fact, headed a Congressional advisory panel that, just as the primaries were about to start, had recommended a flat tax. This made Mr. Kemp's endorsement, always a prize, seem that much more valuable.
All winter, Mr. Dole's aides pressed Mr. Kemp to declare his support. "Scott Reed was calling on an hourly basis," David Smick, a former aide to Mr. Kemp, said, referring to Mr. Dole's campaign manager. At one point, the Dole campaign, according to Bob Woodward's campaign book, "The Choice," even went so far as to charter a plane to take Mr. Kemp from Washington to New Hampshire to make the endorsement. Mr. Kemp, however, repeatedly demurred.
"Last time we're dealing with the quarterback," Mr. Woodward quotes Mr. Dole as saying after Mr. Kemp passed up the chartered plane.
By the eve of the New York primary in early March, Mr. Dole's organizational strength had proved so overwhelming that Mr. Forbes's candidacy was effectively over. Nevertheless, it was at this moment that Mr. Kemp decided to endorse him.
For weeks, the Dole campaign had been hammering away at Mr. Forbes's flat-tax plan, arguing that it would increase the deficit and, by eliminating the mortgage interest deduction, actually raise many Americans' taxes. Announcing his endorsement, Mr. Kemp said that he could not tolerate the attacks on Mr. Forbes's "radical but profound" proposal.
The Dole campaign, and most of the Republican establishment, reacted to the inopportunely timed endorsement with a predictable mixture of fury and derision. Mr. Gingrich, Mr. Kemp's longtime ally, was reportedly so angry that he told Mr. Kemp he was finished politically.
A few days after the endorsement, Mr. Kemp told reporters, "I'm in my wilderness years."
Mr. Kemp's endorsement of Mr. Forbes was particularly astonishing because Mr. Dole's campaign manager, Mr. Reed, was one of his own former aides.
Mr. Reed had worked both on Mr. Kemp's 1988 Presidential race and as his chief of staff at H.U.D; at the time he went to work on the Dole campaign, he was executive director of the Republican National Committee. Mr. Reed later recruited Mr. Buckley, Mr. Kemp's 1988 press secretary, to run the Dole campaign's communications office, and also signed on another former Kemp aide, Kevin Stach, as a policy aide.
When the time came for Mr. Dole to pick a running mate, people familiar with the discussions say, Mr. Reed and Mr. Buckley both strongly advocated Mr. Kemp. Still, given the history of their relationship and Mr. Kemp's ill-timed endorsement, his selection seemed, even to many of his allies, almost ludicrously unlikely.
Mr. Dole alluded to the widespread surprise that greeted his selection in the address-cum-pep talk he delivered to Republican members of Congress a few weeks ago. "A lot of people said it would never happen," he said. "In fact, Jack said it would never happen."
Mr. Dole explained his choice of Mr. Kemp as complementing his decision to propose a 15 percent tax cut as the centerpiece of his campaign.
"It was a difficult choice," he said, "but it seemed to me with the economy being on the front burner and having an economic package, there is no better salesman in America than Jack Kemp. He's been out there doing it year after year after year."
Advisers to Mr. Dole maintain that his economic plan in no way represents a repudiation of his record on fighting the deficit. "Dole has not changed his spots," said Roderick DeArment, Mr. Dole's former chief of staff, who is now an adviser to his campaign. Mr. Dole, Mr. DeArment said, firmly believes that he can balance the budget while delivering a dramatic tax cut.
"He is convinced he will do it and can do it," Mr. DeArment said.
Many others, however, argue that it is difficult, if not impossible, to square Mr. Dole's history as a deficit hawk with his current proposal.
"I think he basically decided he had to do this to jump-start his campaign," Norman Ornstein, a Congressional scholar at the American Enterprise Institute, said of Mr. Dole's tax-cut plan. "Certainly it goes against the grain of much of his world view for a long time."
By this account, Mr. Dole's embrace of Mr. Kemp is less a change of heart than a change of tactics. Mr. Dole has not reconciled his deeply rooted differences with Mr. Kemp; he has simply set them aside. As Jack Pitney, a professor of government at Claremont McKenna College and an expert on recent Republican politics, put it: "Dole realizes he can't always be Dole. Sometimes he has to be Kemp."
 
A Political Life
 The first article in this series about Bob Dole's life appeared on May 19. It reported on how his early years in Kansas shaped his politics. The second article, on June 21, was about his relationship with Richard M. Nixon, who was both a mirror and a mentor. The third article, on July 8, focuses on abortion and how the issue first affected Mr. Dole's political campaigns in 1974 and continues to do so in 1996. The fourth article, on Aug. 9, was about his legislative years. The fifth article, on Sept. 15, examined Mr. Dole's management style. These articles are available to computer users on The New York Times on the Web, at http://www.nytimes.com/library/politics/dole.html.

HEADLINE: City Plans to Auction Off A Bronx Group's Dream

BYLINE: By LIZETTE ALVAREZ 

 BODY:
Decades ago, the Bronx Borough Courthouse stood gleaming and grandiose, gazing down over boisterous stickball games and lawyers' social dens as the Third Avenue El rumbled by. Then fire and decay devoured the neighborhood bit by bit, and the lavish courthouse tumbled along with it, losing first its purpose and later its windows, roof and doors. For nearly 20 years, it was forgotten.
But for the community advocates who are slowly rebuilding this beaten-down area of the South Bronx, the courthouse, awash in graffiti swirls and caked with soot, has a redeeming trait: potential. They are convinced that the 1914 Beaux-Arts building at East 161st Street and Third Avenue can serve as a catalyst for neighborhood renewal, and in the last four years they have raised nearly $1 million to transform the building into a town center for the poorest Congressional district in America. All along, they thought city officials wanted the same thing. 
 Now the city, eager to divest itself of an empty courthouse that would cost $43 million to repair, is putting the building up for auction on Wednesday. Opening bid: $10,000.
"It's happening once again, I thought," said Yolanda Garcia, a founder of Nos Quedamos (We Stay), the group behind Melrose Commons, the $500 million urban renewal plan under way in the area. "I wasn't surprised. There was just anger, because they've known all of the time and effort that had gone into this building."
Petr Stand, an architect who works with Nos Quedamos, echoed a common belief among Bronx residents that the borough is considered the "armpit of New York." "If this were Manhattan," he said, "it would be very different."
While Nos Quedamos sees nothing but promise in the building, the city sees a financial drain. In the last 10 years, it has spent $400,000 to replace the roof, seal the windows and put up a fence, said Paula Young, a spokeswoman for the city's Department of General Services.
"It happens to be a beautiful, extraordinary building in our portfolio that we have no use for," she said. "The building has been allowed to fall into massive disrepair. The city doesn't have the money to commit to it."
The community advocates hope to turn the building into a headquarters for their redevelopment plan, a place where residents could stop in for information about housing and social services. They also envision it as a spot for social gatherings. They see it as a critical component of the renewal plan for Melrose, a community once so hard hit by arson, crime and indifference that more than half its population fled.
The plan, which involves building 1,700 housing units in the 35-square-block area east of Yankee Stadium, was approved by the City Council two years ago.
The courthouse, Ms. Garcia said, would "create a downtown in a place that never had one."
Ron Shiffman, who sat on the City Planning Commission for six years and reviewed the project's paperwork, said the courthouse seemed to offer an important remedy for the ailing neighborhood.
"I thought the planners and the community that worked on it really were putting their hands on a very significant aspect that has been lost in a lot of the revitalization efforts," Mr. Shiffman said. "We need not only new housing, but also to build that abstract, harder-to-feel thing called community."
In a sense, controversy befits the once-majestic courthouse, which was built in anticipation of the Bronx becoming a separate county. Even before the first block of granite was laid in 1905, the building had sparked a feud and a lawsuit between two architects, both of whom claimed credit for the design. The architect Michael Garvin won; his name adorns a small plaque on the facade.
Standing on a five-sided lot, the 86,000-square-foot building was already out of fashion the day it was completed in 1914. While skyscrapers taunted the clouds above Manhattan, the Bronx structure stubbornly clung to classical notions. Inside, sweeping marble staircases, terrazzo floors and leaded glass windows reflected an era of prosperity in the borough.
"The attempt was not only at esthetic beauty, but also an idea of solidity, of an imposing nature that said this is the majesty of government," Mr. Ultan said. Never mind that tucked high in a niche over the courthouse entrance, positioned to catch the eyes of Third Avenue El riders, Lady Justice sits comfortably without her usual blindfold.
As the Bronx's population boomed, a larger courthouse was built a few blocks away on the Grand Concourse. The older building, with only a single vast courtroom, was relegated to relic status. In 1978, the city stopped using the building.
Community advocates stepped in 15 years later, after time and indifference had battered the courthouse, to propose a plan. Initial meetings with the city over the courthouse's future began with the Urban Assembly, a nonprofit urban redevelopment organization, in 1993.
Richard Kahan, the group's president, pitched the concept: the groups would raise enough money to renovate and lease 3,000 square feet of the courthouse, pay many of the utility costs, seal off the rest of the interior and, little by little, fix it up. "Guerrilla landmarking," they called it.
The city seemed amenable, so the groups got busy. They raised $900,000 through public and private grants, corralled architects and stone cleaners into working for nothing and began discussions with possible tenants. The groups stressed to the city that they could not afford to rent or buy the entire space all at once and still pay for heating, renovation and insurance costs.
At one point, a draft copy of a lease circulated and appeared near approval by the city, Ms. Garcia said.
Last year, the city decided the proposal wouldn't work: $1 million was $42 million too little to cover expenses for a building deemed unsafe by the Buildings Department, Ms. Young said.
"We examined many different ideas and none were realistic in the long term," she said. "It will cost the city too much money to improve the building. And to lease a building on the short term, I don't think it's responsible and it's a piecemeal approach."
If Nos Quedamos wants to preserve the courthouse as a town center, it should attend the auction, Ms. Young said. "Nos Quedamos has informed everyone they have $1 million," she said. "They should bid on the building and use the one million as seed money."
Even if the group does not buy it, she added, "whoever buys that building will have to improve it. Nos Quedamos can negotiate with them. It will still be an anchor. Any improvement is an improvement, and everyone will benefit."
Because the building is landmarked, a buyer could not simply tear it down to build something else on the site.
Sitting in her office in a neighborhood once threatened by bulldozers, Ms. Garcia disagrees with the city's approach.
"The city built this public building with public funds for the public, and now they want the public to buy its own building back so it can be kept public," Ms. Garcia said. "That's not democracy as we know it."

GRAPHIC: Photo: The city wants to auction off the former Bronx Borough Courthouse. (G. Paul Burnett/The New York Times)
 
Map of the Bronz showing the location of Melrose Commons.

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE STRATEGY -- STATE BY STATE: Florida;
Florida Victory No Sure Thing For the G.O.P.

BYLINE: By R. W. APPLE Jr. 

DATELINE: MIAMI, Sept. 27

 BODY:
It was one of the more spirited strategic arguments within Bob Dole's campaign: Should he spend serious time and money fighting for Florida, where Republican nominees have prevailed in every Presidential election since 1980 but where he has been running behind, or should he husband his resources for the big Midwestern swing states and hope for the best here?
"You go all-out in Florida, and so maybe you win it," said one of those who argued against a major effort in this state, the nation's fourth-largest. "But you still lose if you can't compete in Illinois, Ohio and Michigan." 
 In the end, Mr. Dole decided that he could not afford to neglect Florida, which is a measure of the bind he finds himself in: states that ought to constitute his base, like this one, have still not been nailed down with less than six weeks to go until Election Day. In Florida, according to several polls, the Republican nominee trails President Clinton by 5 to 11 percentage points.
The most reliably Republican of the megastates, with 25 electoral votes, finds itself up for grabs.
Now the question is how large a commitment Mr. Clinton will make here. In 1988, the Democratic nominee, Michael S. Dukakis, closed his Florida headquarters in early September, writing the state off. In 1992, Mr. Clinton did something similar, spending less than $50,000 on television advertising and still losing to George Bush by only 100,000 votes.
"Carrying it this time is a matter of will for the President," said Geoffrey Garin, a Washington consultant with close ties to Florida Democrats. "If he wants to put the resources into the state -- which, of course, means diverting them from other places -- then he will win Florida."
So far, Mr. Clinton seems determined to do so. He has visited the state five times in this year alone, and his campaign recently opened 14 joint regional offices with local candidates. The Democratic National Committee has spent $3.2 million, at last count, on Florida TV commercials. But the test will come in the final couple of weeks, when the competition for dollars gets fierce.
Several local issues appear to be helping Mr. Clinton here, even though the Democrats' once-huge registration lead has dropped steadily with the state's explosive growth, at a rate of 5,000 people a week. Mangrove swamps have become retirement communities, orange groves around Orlando have given way to one of the world's largest resort communities, and the Democrats, who 20 years ago had a 4-to-1 registration edge, now outnumber Republicans only 46 percent to 41.
But many newcomers are retirees -- the state's median age is 38.3, the highest in the nation -- and many are ill-disposed toward Mr. Dole.
"The elderly are afraid," said Richard Sher, a political scientist at the University of Florida. "Their worries about what a Republican administration might do to Social Security and Medicare are real, however bogus the Democrats' charges may be. Clinton has pushed Dole around on that issue, and there has been absolutely no escaping the 'Republicans Slash Medicare' TV ads."
Senator Bob Graham, a Democrat, sees changes in the state's big Hispanic community, concentrated in the south, that could help Mr. Clinton.
"The Hispanics are no longer all from Cuba," he said, "and the ones from Cuba are no longer all Republicans. There has been a significant influx of people from Nicaragua, Colombia, Venezuela and elsewhere in the last few years, many of whom vote Democratic. And the President, by standing up to Fidel Castro lately, has helped blunt the charge that Democrats are soft on Communism."
Republican proposals to tighten immigration, declare English the official language of the United States and withhold certain benefits from legal immigrants have not won Mr. Dole any points among Hispanics, either. Representative Lincoln Diaz-Balart, a Republican from the Miami area, not long ago denounced these points on his party's agenda.
A third boost for the President has come from the national Republicans' somewhat ambiguous stand on the environment. A healthy environment, of course, is money in the bank for Florida; it is one of the things that brings tourists and settlers to the state. Local Republicans have taken the lead in defending the Everglades, the Keys and other areas from overdevelopment and pollution.
Nathaniel Reed of Hobe Sound, one of Florida's leading conservationists, epitomizes Republican disarray on the environment. Assistant Secretary of the Interior in the Nixon Administration, he broke with his party in the 1994 race for governor and again this year, and others in his party may follow.
But Mr. Dole's big problems here are his big problems everywhere.
One is the economy. Four years ago, times were tough along the I-4 corridor, named for the Interstate highway linking Tampa to Orlando and Daytona Beach. Defense cutbacks were starting to bite in the high-tech plants spawned by the Kennedy Space Center, retirees in St. Petersburg fretted because their money market funds were paying less interest, and real estate prices were flat.
But as Mr. Dole returned to Florida on Thursday, the state's seemingly inexhaustible boom had resumed in the politically pivotal I-4 corridor and most other places. He complained that the recovery was much too slow, but his words were all but drowned out by word from the Census Bureau that for the first time in six years, family incomes nationwide had risen last year.
A second, even more intractable problem is female voters, which is why Mr. Dole concentrated on meeting them this week in West Palm Beach and Miami.
Robert Joffee of Mason/Dixon Research said his polls showed an enormous gender gap that accounts for Mr. Clinton's entire lead. The President, he said, is trailing Mr. Dole among men, among white non-Hispanic men and even among white non-Hispanic men who describe themselves as independents. But Mr. Dole runs 20 points weaker among women than among men, 31 points weaker among white women than among white men and 47 points weaker among independent white women than among independent white men, Mr. Joffee said.
"For years in this state, we've had white Democratic men -- good ol' boys -- crossing over to vote for Republicans," the pollster added. "That's still happening. But this time, you have just as many if not more Republican women crossing over to the Democrats, joining a lot of independent women."
Four years ago, exit polls showed that the Republican nominee for Governor, Jeb Bush, a son of the former President, lost to Lawton Chiles, the Democratic incumbent, because he could not hold the votes of Republican women. Like Mr. Bush Mr. Dole opposes abortion. And there are strong indications that Republican women here, particularly in suburbs and prosperous retirement communities, continue to resent their party's stand on that issue.
In a swing through the state for a series of articles published last week, Tom Fiedler, the Miami Herald's political editor, found women particularly resistant to Mr. Dole's stand in the Republican-dominated communities along the Gulf Coast from Sarasota to Naples.
Two years ago, many Republican women in that region skipped the governor's race, Mr. Fiedler said.
Tom Slade, the Republican state chairman, said he thought Mr. Dole's message was finally beginning to take hold in the state, and he predicted that as the nominee's advertising campaign got going in earnest, "traction will improve."
But he conceded that abortion was a bugbear, not least because women preoccupied with that issue paid the Republicans no heed on others.
"We need to get them past that," Mr. Slade said. "The reality is that the Supreme Court has settled abortion, and no one can do anything about it as long as we're a nation of laws. We're not going to pass a constitutional amendment banning abortion in 900 years. Pretending otherwise just hurts us."

In His Own Words

 BODY:

BOB DOLE
Speaking yesterday to the nation by radio:
 "He won't tell you that wages for men and women have been stagnant, and that has forced more spouses into the work force just to make ends meet. And he won't tell you that bankruptcies last year were higher than ever before, and so was consumer debt as people struggle to pay their bills.

Or the fact that he appointed a Surgeon General who suggested legalizing drugs. Or the fact that teen-age drug use has increased by 104 percent during his Administration."

HEADLINE: Rich Nations May Forgive Debt of Poor Nations

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 28

 BODY:
The world's major industrial powers agreed today to forgive up to 80 percent of the debt owed to them by the poorest nations, primarily in Africa, if those countries agree to extraordinary economic reforms.
Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin said the agreement, reached by the finance ministers of seven large industrial nations, opened the door to the formal endorsement of the plan by the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund, which have issued most of the loans. 
 A senior Administration official said the first beneficiaries would likely be Uganda and Mozambique. But 10 to 15 other countries may ultimately benefit, the official said.
The reforms that will be required include lower trade barriers, privatization of state industries and more openness toward foreign investment.
Italy, Germany and Japan had initially opposed the program, complaining that the cost -- roughly $6 billion -- was too high at a time of tight budgets. But they relented under pressure from the United States and several European nations.
For decades, the world's largest countries have insisted that poor nations repay almost all of their loans. A forgiveness program, they feared, would discourage poor countries from making difficult decisions to reform their economies.
But that policy has made it virtually impossible to convince private companies to invest in poor countries because virtually all the foreign exchange entering those countries was used to pay debts. Clinton Administration officials say they hope the new program will bring at least some private investment, particularly in Africa and Southeast Asia.
Oxfam International, a leading private advocate of debt relief, has calculated that the new plan could save Uganda up to $80 million a year over the next three years, enough to pay for health care for 2 million people, teaching materials for 2 million primary school children and immunization for a million children.
At a news conference this evening, Mr. Rubin said the ministers felt that the world economic outlook had improved since the spring and that "conditions appear in place for a strengthening and broadening of the expansion of the world economy into 1997."
He also said the ministers had welcomed the strengthening of the dollar. Last year the Administration was criticized for the dollar's slump.

On Tuesday, the Federal Reserve decided that the economy was purring along so well -- not too hot, not too cold -- that it would pass up its last chance to raise interest rates before the election.

 Mr. Dole noted the dark cloud in the report: People aren't making more, they are just working more jobs. DAVID E. SANGER

HEADLINE: Whose Money? And Whose Problems?

 BODY:
To the Editor:
At a time when the average American family pays more in taxes than it spends on food, shelter and clothing, I find it difficult to believe that anyone would suggest that Americans aren't warranted a real effort at tax reform ("A Reckless Tax Cut," editorial, Sept. 25). Rejecting Bob Dole's plan, you offer no solution to the excessive taxes borne by American families, but conclude with the insipid contention that President Clinton's plan "would do no harm." 
 Moreover, I don't accept your claim that "the economy is now operating at full bore." According to the Commerce Department, the yearly rate of economic growth since Mr. Clinton took office is only 2.5 percent, compared with 3.2 percent the decade before and 4.4 percent in the past five expansions.
Just days before your editorial appeared, the Federal Reserve reported that manufacturing is dropping, along with productivity. The Fed's decision not to raise interest rates is proof that it doesn't see the economy "operating at full bore."
Tax cuts create economic growth. Economic growth creates jobs. Mr. Dole's plan encourages savings, investment and job creation, and it will strengthen America's ability to remain strong in the international economy.
 
WILLIAM V. ROTH JR.
Chmn., Senate Finance Committee
Washington, Sept. 25, 1996
