HEADLINE: MINDING YOUR BUSINESS;
59 1/2 Questions About I.R.A. Withdrawals

BYLINE: By BRUCE FELTON 

 BODY:

Q. I'd like to begin withdrawing money from my individual retirement account, but I'm worried about making a mistake and incurring a stiff penalty. Any suggestions?
 
A. Rated for clarity and readability, the rules for I.R.A. distributions rank somewhere between "Finnegans Wake" and a computer repair manual written in Apache. "Even financial advisers who deal with distribution rules day in and day out sometimes have trouble making sense out of them," said Raymond Rogers, head of Distribution Strategies, an I.R.A. consulting firm in Riverside, Conn. 
 But mistakes can be costly. Take out your money even a day too early or too late, or miscalculate the allowable withdrawals, and you could face stinging financial penalties along with unnecessarily high tax bills. In 1993, the most recent year for which figures are available, owners of I.R.A.'s and other tax-privileged retirement plans paid nearly $1.4 billion in such penalties, according to the Internal Revenue Service.
One source of information is I.R.S. Publication 590, which offers a detailed explanation of I.R.A. regulations, including those pertaining to distributions. Free copies are available by calling the agency at (800) 829-3676.
Under I.R.A. tax rules, which vary somewhat from those for other retirement plans, the earliest you can begin tapping your I.R.A. is at age 59 1/2. But that doesn't mean you must or even should withdraw money. "A lot of people wrongly assume they've got to start drawing down their I.R.A. as soon as they become eligible," said Martin Levanthal, head of a New York accounting firm bearing his name. If you're still earning an income at 59 1/2, the best tactic may be to leave your I.R.A. alone and let it continue growing. "Otherwise," he said, "you wind up paying higher taxes on your distributions."
Withdrawing I.R.A. money too early is another problem. Doing so before age 59 1/2 means you will generally have to pay a Federal penalty of 10 percent of the amount withdrawn, on top of income taxes for any money not taxed already. (Some states add a penalty of their own. In California, for example, it is 2.5 percent.) The I.R.S. penalty is waived, however, if the money is needed because of death or disability. Beginning on Jan. 1, it is also waived if you apply the money toward the portion of medical expenses that exceeds 7.5 percent of your adjusted gross income, or if you have been collecting unemployment compensation for more than 12 weeks and use the funds to buy medical insurance.
But resist dipping into your I.R.A. for any other reason. "When workers are downsized out of a job, they sometimes feel they have to invade their I.R.A.'s just to meet living expenses," said Joel Isaacson, the head of an accounting and financial planning firm bearing his name in New York. "But there are almost always better alternatives, such as selling off bonds or money market funds, or even borrowing from relatives."
After age 59 1/2, you enter into a sort of I.R.A. utopia, where you are free to withdraw as little or as much as you'd like without penalty, although the payouts, of course, are subject to income taxes. However, once you turn 70 1/2, you have until April 1 of the next year to take your first distribution. If you miss the deadline, or withdraw less than the minimum, you'll face a penalty of 50 percent of the amount you should have withdrawn but didn't.
Nor is delaying that first distribution until the last possible nanosecond necessarily a good idea. Suppose you become 70 1/2 in May and wait till the next year to take your first distribution. Because you must also take a second distribution sometime that same year, you may pay more income tax than if you had made the first withdrawal earlier. You may also squander a key tax benefit with such doubling-up: Most states allow I.R.A. owners a substantial yearly deduction on distributions; in New York, the amount is $20,000. But such deductions are on a per-year -- not per-withdrawal -- basis.
Investors do not have to worry about which of their I.R.A.'s to use. They can take the money from any account, or any combination of them. Thanks to Congress, for the next three years they do not have to worry about the so-called excess distributions rule, either. This summer, Congress temporarily shelved that provision, which imposes a 15 percent tax on withdrawals of more than $155,000 a year.
But I.R.A. owners must still choose between the two ways of computing the minimum annual distribution. Both methods involve dividing the I.R.A. assets by life expectancy, but in one the expectancy is fixed from the outset and in the other it is revised every year.
For example, according to I.R.S. actuarial tables, a person aged 70 1/2 can expect to live another 16 years. Under the "fixed" method, the person would divide the I.R.A. balance by 16 the first year, 15 the second year and so on to figure the minimum withdrawal. With the "recalculation" approach, the same person would have a 16-year life expectancy the first year but a 15.3-year life expectancy the second year.
Because the expected lifespan increases with age, the recalculation method means investors can take out smaller required payouts and stretch them out over a longer period than they can under the fixed approach. If you are concerned about outliving your income, can get by with less money or just want more of your I.R.A. assets to keep growing tax-deferred, recalculation may be right for you.
The fixed method, however, has a tax advantage. If the person using it dies before the I.R.A. is depleted, the beneficiary can continue to receive the payouts according to the original life expectancy. But under the recalculation approach, the whole I.R.A. balance must be paid to the beneficiary in the year after the person's death. That means high taxes.
I.R.A. withdrawals have plenty of other wrinkles; in figuring life expectancy, for instance, you can use just your own remaining years or the joint expectancy of yourself and your beneficiary, with restrictions. But, despite all the twists, negotiate them carefully: Some choices, like the life-expectancy calculation, cannot be changed, even if you pick a new beneficiary -- or a new spouse.

CORRECTION-DATE: October 13, 1996, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
Because of an editing error, the Minding Your Business column on Sept. 29, about withdrawals from individual retirement accounts, misstated the rules for using the recalculation method of figuring I.R.A. payouts. After an account holder's death, the balance of the account may have to be paid to the beneficiary by the end of the next calendar year, but only under certain conditions: The account holder's age must have been the sole factor in figuring the payout schedule, and the beneficiary must not be the spouse of the account holder.

HEADLINE: EARNING IT;
The Nanny Track: A Once-Simple World Grown Complicated

BYLINE: By KIRK JOHNSON 

 BODY:
THE word "nanny" sounds simple -- two little syllables, pronounceable, more or less, by the average 2-year-old. But it comes loaded with complicated associations of class, history, money and love. It conjures up Mary Poppins and the English upper crust and sunny sylvan childhoods. To guilt-burdened modern parents, "day care" sounds institutional, "baby sitter" sounds young and "care giver" dry and emotionless. Nanny sounds warm and comforting, like a big hug.
And semantics is just the beginning. In the 1990's, the nanny business is on a collision course with complication: As the number of working parents has grown, millions of Americans who grew up in middle-class homes without nannies have become employers for the first time, often with no clue to what that means. And as their ranks grow, nannies have begun asserting themselves, demanding to be treated as, well, employees. Office politics can seem like an afternoon of Lego blocks by comparison. 
 What can happen?
* One New York couple was told by their nanny just before a family vacation that she wanted her salary doubled or she would quit. They refused, but then realized that a vacation plane ticket was in the nanny's name and could not be transferred. Off they all went, happily or not.
* One mother who jogged early in the morning instructed her live-in nanny to listen for sounds from the nursery beginning at 6, even though the nanny's formal work day did not begin until 7. The nanny complained to her agency, which agreed that mommy's jog time indeed lengthened the work day. The schedule was adjusted.
* Prime Minister Benjamin Netanyahu of Israel and his wife, Sara, learned the power of the modern nanny -- not to mention the power of the press -- when their children's sitter, dismissed for burning the soup, painted an unflattering picture of Mrs. Netanyahu. That event has brought waves of anxiety about what is private and what is not in a home with an employee.
The problem of this emerging workplace, nannies, employers and agency owners say, is that there is little agreement on what kind of place it is. In this world, the workers become managers of the house and children after the parents' daily departure and often work under few rules. Parents, reluctant to assert authority as employers but wanting to protect their children, often do not want to admit the change that an employee in the house can bring.
"Millions of professional couples have become employers and 99 percent of them have no idea how to do it," said Lewis Maltby, director of the American Civil Liberties Union's workplace rights office and the employer of a nanny for his two children, ages 4 and 8, at home in Princeton, N.J.
Mary Clurman, co-owner of Apple Pie U.S.A., a nanny agency in Princeton, says both parties in the work relationship keep raising the stakes. Parents insist on older nannies with credentials, then resent the formalization they helped create. Nannies want respect, but mourn the loss of warmth.
"What makes it such an awful business is keeping up with the escalation of expectation -- I feel the follicles of my hair fry several times a day," Ms. Clurman said.
Even without nostalgia, the American nanny business was simpler not so long ago. Compared with that of Britain, where nannies were invented and where formal training has been available for decades, it was smaller, less structured and limited to the most affluent families. Nannies were more likely to be young women looking for a new experience far from home. It was barely a business and hardly a career.
TWO forces have pushed it toward a new place over the last decade. The first, concentrated mainly in the late 1980's, was a growth in demand as birth rates soared for baby boomer couples. Ten years ago, there were about 25 nanny placement agencies in the country, according to Wendy Sachs, president of the International Nanny Association, a group formed in 1986. Now there are more than 500. Estimates of full-time nannies in the United States range from one million to three million.
The second force -- the nanny story of the 1990's -- is the crystallization of that growth into the beginning of an industry. Nannies formed groups. Nanny agencies, paid by employers to find proper nannies, insisted on contracts to protect all parties. Questions arose: When does a nanny's day, and pay, start? Is it possible to protect a family's privacy if a nanny talks to the neighbors or a reporter about embarrassing doings? Can you make a career of nannying?
The new forces have piqued the interest of lawyers and entrepreneurs:
* Auxiliary businesses have sprung up, like the Nanny News, a magazine created four years ago by a New Jersey woman, Vanessa Sandom, who found her own nanny search to be so confused and frustrating that she decided to share what she learned.
* A school in Colorado, the Starkey International Institute for Household Management, is offering a course this fall that may best be called a nanny upgrade. It promises to turn a $300-a-week nanny into a $600-a-week nanny by teaching poise and etiquette. "Some nannies are asking $800 to $1,000 a week, and some are able to get it," said Rita Kirschner, president of the Fox Agency in New York. "Some of our families are willing to pay whatever it takes -- they want a Mary Poppins."
* New legal issues have emerged. The highly publicized nanny troubles of people like Zoe Baird, whose nomination to be Attorney General was withdrawn for failure to pay home employment taxes, have spurred more families to seek advice about legal liabilities and protections. More employers are asking nannies to sign agreements barring them from discussing the doings of the house -- even though, according to Mr. Maltby at the A.C.L.U., such binders are almost certainly unenforceable.
* New technologies have appeared. Companies like Babywatch and In-Home Nanny Surveillance Inc. rent tiny cameras concealed in everything from VCR's to teddy bears. At the Quark Spy Center in New York, 30 to 40 percent of the business comes from renting miniature video cameras to parents who check on life with the baby when they are gone, said Melinda Meals, the vice president of operations and marketing.
Emotions are the undercurrent of all these forces. Nannies who are drawn to the work by the love of children say the anxiety and mistrust they feel from many employers -- especially over the video camera issue -- are creating new walls between them. Parents, easy targets for guilt, say that managing to hire and retain the perfect nanny can, in the end, feel like a defeat.
Carol Chory found such a nanny in Karin Brown. Miss Brown, born and raised in Oklahoma, has a degree in early-childhood development and until recently taught swimming at the local pool in her spare time. She is 33 and has been a stabilizing influence on the sometimes chaotic two-income Chory household in central New Jersey for nearly eight years.
Perfection has its price, though. Mrs. Chory's moment to pay up came two years ago when, arriving home from work, she was approached by her 10-year-old daughter, Cristy, who was eager to read to her a school-assigned essay titled "My Hero." Many of Cristy's classmates were writing about their parents, or big-name athletes. Cristy's hero was Miss Brown. The essays were to be read aloud in school, with the heros and/or parents invited to bask in the glow. Mommy could be there for the proud moment, couldn't she?
"I had to wrestle with it for a while," Mrs. Chory said. But after the initial "moment of shock," she said the essay made her realize how lucky she and the children were. "I wound up feeling very proud," she said.
Donna Palermo of Massapequa, L.I., rented a video camera to check on her nanny earlier this year. What she saw was the nanny ignoring her then 8-month-old daughter, Alexandra, and a friend's child, for almost the whole day without even speaking to them. Ms. Palermo dismissed the nanny and bought the camera for $5,000. She now rents it out herself.
But making sure the nanny cares and does her job well is only the beginning of a sound and secure relationship. Legal experts say that while the tax issues in hiring a domestic employee have drawn the most attention, the rule of thumb is that any nanny employment relationship, whether a contract is signed or not, makes the employer subject to all the rules and entanglements of a small business. If the nanny falls and breaks a leg on the job, she is covered by workers' compensation laws. All medical bills, and salary through recuperation, are the responsibility of the employer.
Though most nannies still work without them, written agreements are probably not a bad idea. Even if they're simple and not drafted by a lawyer, they set boundaries and responsibilities that can prevent conflicts, nannies and parents say.
Federal minimum wage and overtime laws do not apply, but in most states, a wage statute requires a certain minimal pay. Sexual-harassment protection can apply, too. California, for instance, requires a workplace of only one employee for full protection. New York State covers only places of business with at least five employees, though domestic workers in New York City are protected by city statutes, giving them the right to sue for sexual harassment. The Internal Revenue Service recently changed rules for reporting child-care payments, making it easier to catch people in the cash-only part of the economy.
Videotaping without the knowledge of the nanny is generally legal, depending on where the camera is placed and what kind of camera it is. Anywhere in a bathroom or in the nanny's private quarters could invite a sexual-harassment suit. Any camera with a microphone could invite the attention of the F.B.I. because audio recording without consent is a violation of Federal wiretapping laws.
BUT the changes go beyond liability, to definitions of living spaces. If a home becomes a small business when you pay someone to be in it, the lines get blurred. "Now that you have staff walking around your home, is it a private home any more?" said Judith Abranovich, a nanny and co-president of the National Association of Nannies. "It's our office, and I'd like to be treated like any other worker in America."
Some nanny defenders say they believe their children are getting a far better childhood than they would with just a mom around. Ms. Sandon, who founded the Nanny News (recently taken over by Ms. Clurman, the nanny agency owner), said her children were spared her bad moods.
"They get me in a good mood at night, and they get the nanny in a good mood during the day," she said. "If they had to live with me on a full-time basis, they'd die."

CORRECTION-DATE: October 13, 1996, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
An article on Sept. 29 about the growth of the nanny business misstated the application of minimum-wage laws to nannies' pay. Under the Federal Fair Labor Standards Act, live-in nannies are entitled to the minimum wage for their time spent working; they are not covered by the law's overtime provisions, although some states do require overtime pay. Most nannies who do not live in their employers' households are covered by the Federal minimum wage and by the overtime provisions.
The article also misstated the minimum number of employees that a place of business must have before it is covered by New York State and New York City statutes on sexual harassment. It is four, not five.

HEADLINE: Rebel With a Cause

BYLINE: By Walter Reich; Walter Reich, a psychiatrist, is the director of the United States Holocaust Memorial Museum in Washington.

 BODY:

A Desperate Passion
An Autobiography.
By Helen Broinowski Caldicott.
Illustrated. 366 pp. New York:
W. W. Norton & Company. $27.50.
 In her most prominent years of anti-nuclear activism, during the 1970's and 80's, Helen Caldicott seemed to some nothing less than a force of nature, a ferocious warrior engaged in a desperate battle to save an endangered planet from the poison of nuclear energy and the world-ending cataclysm of nuclear war. 
 In those same years, Helen Caldicott seemed to others hopelessly naive about geopolitical realities, driven as much by her need for attention and adulation as by her concern for humanity, an unguided missile of misbegotten assumptions and mistaken facts that set back the cause of peace far more than it advanced it. Readers' reactions to her autobiography, "A Desperate Passion," will be shaped, inevitably, by their prior attitudes toward her goals.
 Those who saw Dr. Caldicott, the first president of Physicians for Social Responsibility, as a leader for the good will read her book as the memoirs of a fighter for life and peace who blessed the world through her desperate efforts to save the children she loved as a pediatrician from the ravages of nuclear catastrophe. Those who saw her as a whirling dervish of simplistic anti-nuclear rhetoric unwilling to accept any arguments other than her own will read her book as a confirmation of their worst judgments of her tireless crusade.
 For her own part, Dr. Caldicott, though she may have doubts about the choices she made in life -- choices that she acknowledges resulted in the shortchanging of her own children and her marriage -- expresses few doubts about the virtue of her cause and the consequences of her struggle. She concludes that in the end, "a miracle occurred. The work of the international peace movement helped to pave the way for a Russian, Mikhail Gorbachev, to perform the final act. He sanctioned the collapse of the Berlin wall, and the ever-present tension between the nuclear superpowers dissolved with the disintegration of the Soviet Union. This, I believe, was the single most important event of the 20th century. When the cold war ended, so did my desperation."
 On this point, and on many others, her critics would interpret history quite differently. The enemies she battled for so long -- especially officials in the Reagan Administration who focused American military policy on building what they saw as a necessary shield against a Soviet nuclear threat -- would insist now that it was precisely their strategy, including the Star Wars program against which Dr. Caldicott railed with particular energy, that brought the Soviet Union to economic collapse. They would insist that it was this policy that made Mr. Gorbachev understand that the ideological system used by the Soviet Union to control its population and threaten the world couldn't cope with the challenges imposed on it by a vibrant democracy and economic powerhouse -- one that, despite the efforts of the nuclear freeze movement, succeeded in mounting a military buildup that brought the Soviets to their knees, forcing them, through Mr. Gorbachev, to sue for peace and dismantle their state.
 Though "A Desperate Passion" may inspire Dr. Caldicott's supporters, it won't convert her detractors. More interesting than the defense of her political views, and the description of her efforts on behalf of the anti-nuclear movement, is the account of her journey to that movement's public pinnacle and the struggles, both private and organizational, that she experienced along the way. It was a journey that brought her from Australia to America, from medical work to political activism, from a life as mother and wife to a life as an actor on a very visible international stage, and, more than once, from personal despair to intense exhilaration. Having trained in pediatrics, and having traveled to Harvard Medical School, she was increasingly drawn to the challenge of speaking out against nuclear dangers. When she was 19, she had experienced a presentiment of her anti-nuclear calling in her reaction to "On the Beach," by the Australian novelist Nevil Shute, in which she read of a world doomed by the radiation of a nuclear war. She ultimately helped found Physicians for Social Responsibility and Women's Action for Nuclear Disarmament.
 Dr. Caldicott's life in the anti-nuclear movement wasn't free of disputes. Repeatedly she refers to betrayals by her rivals in Physicians for Social Responsibility and International Physicians for the Prevention of Nuclear War (the latter group eventually won a Nobel Peace Prize). Her distress at having been deposed from the presidency of the first group by those rivals, and her sense of them as dishonest and underhanded in their striving for power and recognition, punctuate the book with bitter regularity.
 After the Nobel ceremony, Dr. Caldicott writes, "I felt as if I had been kicked in the guts." Had she given the acceptance speech in 1985 rather than a rival, she writes, she would have used the occasion more effectively. "Looking back through the retrospectoscope," she writes, "I can see that I learned many lessons from these traumatic events. . . . I learned that, to a degree, I had become arrogant, that I needed to encompass humility within the equation of my life -- a difficult lesson to learn at any time, particularly for a physician. I learned that living life as a damaged victim is self-destructive." After subsequent crises and depressions, she felt freed and strengthened.
 One of the criticisms leveled against Dr. Caldicott was that she displayed an excess of passion in her public presentations, which made her message seem, to some, to be based less on facts and reason than on emotion. In her book, she attempts to explain the origins of the passion, and especially the despair, that she frequently felt. But her explanations often seem too simple, too pat, too elusive. Those supporters who have found her to be a force of nature no doubt have a point. But it's a force the full explanation for which a biographer other than herself will some day have to provide.
 Though the danger of nuclear war has not disappeared since Dr. Caldicott worked to diminish it, its nature is quite different. It is now small states, rogue states and terrorist organizations that are most likely to cause nuclear catastrophes, and it will take means other than the organizing passions of Helen Caldicott to protect the planet from their growing threat.

CORRECTION-DATE: October 27, 1996, Sunday

 CORRECTION:
A review on Sept. 29, about Dr. Helen Caldicott's autobiography, "A Desperate Passion," included an erroneous reference, based on the first page of the book, to the nationality of the novelist Nevil Shute, the author of "On the Beach." Shute (whose full name was Nevil Shute Norway) was an Englishman, not an Australian.

SECTION: Section A; Page 17; Column 5; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 709 words

HEADLINE: In America;
Men and Jobs

BYLINE: By BOB HERBERT 

 BODY:
The two big issues in poor black communities are jobs and men. Not enough jobs to go around and not enough men taking enough responsibility for the well-being of their children. The combined effect on the children is hideous. They are sent into the competitive cauldron of the real world grotesquely unprepared. Many do not survive.
I spoke about both issues last week with Geoffrey Canada, president of the Rheedlen Centers for Children and Families, which runs several successful social development programs in New York City. One is the Countee Cullen Community Center in Harlem, which offers year-round counseling, education and recreation as an alternative to the streets for poor youngsters and their parents. 
 "The jobs issue is paramount for communities like central Harlem," said Mr. Canada. "But so many of the jobs, the kind you can get without an awful lot of skills, are gone. So we have young people who start off really excited about work. They're 14, 15, 16 years old and they're out with their working papers knocking on doors, and they find that there are no jobs. They become 17, 18, 19 -- the same thing. No jobs. And after a while the resilient kind of behavior that you need to continue looking can be lost."
Mr. Canada's programs stress the need to improve the academic and other skills of the youngsters, preparing them for college and jobs beyond the entry level. The Harvard sociologist William Julius Wilson, in his new book, "When Work Disappears," writes: "In the new global economy, highly educated and thoroughly trained men and women are in demand. This may be seen most dramatically in the sharp differences in employment experiences among men. Unlike men with lower education, college-educated men are working more, not less."
Said Mr. Canada: "Too many of our children don't have the skills to compete for the better jobs available. They look around for alternatives but there aren't many. People think they end up selling drugs but most do not. Often they just give up. They think, 'There's no way I'm going to make it. I'll go out and try to sell a few tapes on the corner.' They become very marginal in terms of even thinking they can compete in the job market."
The consequences have been dire, but the drastic shortage of employment in the ghettos neither explains nor excuses the mass abandonment of black children by their fathers.
The reluctance in many places to discuss this is understandable. The shame attached to this phenomenon is profound.
"This is increasingly a problem across the country," Mr. Canada said, "but it is an absolutely critical problem in our community."
He quickly went through some of the reasons. "We have an awful lot of young men in jail and lot of them have families. They are locked up at a time when they ought to be bonding with their children. We have a lot of men involved in the underground economy -- sometimes legally, sometimes not -- and they are not spending regular hours in households with their young people. And we have a lot of men, quite honestly, who have never been taught how to be fathers. Many of them never had a father themselves, and no one ever sat down with them and explained to them that these are the things that fathers do -- that you are supposed to be the one who holds your kid's hand and takes your child to school and says I love you and reads the kid a story. Many of our young men never had that and weren't taught that."
Mr. Canada and others like him are trying to undo the damage of many lost years. They are trying to change attitudes and draw as many men as possible back into the family fold.
"Agencies like Rheedlen cannot save children by themselves," he said. "The primary educator of the child, the primary influence on the child, really has to be the parent."
One of the striking things at Countee Cullen is the variety of ways in which young black men are involved in the care of the children in the program. Perhaps it shouldn't be striking, but it is.
"We are trying to bring more men into our children's lives," said Mr. Canada. "We want the kids to have that experience, to know that they can count on men -- not only to be strong, not only for protection, but also to give them a hug, to hold their hand."

HEADLINE: Taking In the Sites;
Finding What Was Said Back Then and What Now

BYLINE: By MIKE ALLEN 

 BODY:
Bill Clinton foresaw the pocketbook election of 1996 during the second Presidential debate of 1992. "You have to increase investment, grow the economy and reduce the deficit," he said. "You'll have a shot at me in four years, and you can vote me right out if you think I've done a lousy job, and I would welcome you to do that."
In the next debate, he set an even higher bar: "I'd like to create more millionaires than were created under Mr. Bush and Mr. Reagan." 
 Those snippets turned up during a simple search for "Clinton and taxes" in a cache of debate transcripts posted on the World Wide Web this month in preparation for the Presidential debates that begin Sunday in Hartford.
The search tool can pluck one answer at a time from 20 Presidential and Vice Presidential debates, going back to John F. Kennedy and Richard M. Nixon in 1960. The Commission on Presidential Debates, the nonpartisan group sponsoring this fall's series, hired Net.capitol Inc., a six-month-old company in Alexandria, Va., to post the 400 to 500 pages on the commission's Web site.
"The debates since 1960 are not as rhetorically rich or powerful or well-crafted," said Mark Kuhn, the commission's Internet communications coordinator. "You also get a sense of the misstatements and repetition when candidates speak without a prompter."
The archives include a clipped, halting Bob Dole, the running mate of President Gerald R. Ford in 1976, sparring with Walter F. Mondale. "So I say, take a look and you'll vote for Ford," Mr. Dole said. His answers are littered with "uh" and "(cough)" notations.
But Mr. Dole's answers also indicate that this fall's debate will not be devoid of humor even if, as seems possible, Ross Perot is absent. Mr. Dole said, of his 50 percent rating from that debate's hosts, the League of Women Voters, "Either I was wrong half the time or they were wrong half of the time."
Mr. Perot, the Reform Party nominee, stole the show during the 1992 debates, but, barring a court's intervention, won't be there this year. Those who miss him can find consolation by tapping into the Perot '96 site and reading unabridged versions of Mr. Perot's protests, including a 14-page lawsuit filed in the Federal District Court for the District of Columbia, and a 10-page complaint to the Federal Communications Commission.
The site operated by the Commission on Presidential Debates includes a seven-point guide, "How to get the most out of a debate," which encourages viewers to consider the candidates' strategies while watching and warns about reaction shots that distract from the candidates' words.
The inquisitive also can find the commission's guidelines for selecting debate locations. Each participating city was required to pay $500,000 to the commission. The halls were to have a minimum ceiling height of 35 feet, air-conditioning maintained at 70 degrees, and "comfortable, fully padded seats with unobstructed views of the stage."
A companion site is Debate Watch '96, based at the University of Kansas, the debate commission's citizen-involvement program. After each debate, the program will sponsor discussions in living rooms, schools and libraries and the results are to be posted on the Web. The site includes instructions for on-line discussions.
Sites in each of the three host cities have opened debate sections. The Current, the electronic edition of The Hartford Courant, plans to broadcast sound from Sunday's debate over the Web as well as commentary from three "cyberpundits."

HEADLINE: Costa Rica Chafes at New Austerity

BYLINE: By LARRY ROHTER 

DATELINE: SAN JOSE, Costa Rica

 BODY:
For nearly half a century, Costa Rica has had a reputation as "the Switzerland of Central America." With no army and a stable, democratic political system, the 3.5 million people of this mountainous country have grown accustomed to a standard of living that has made them the envy of Latin America, with benefits that include free education and health care.
But now that European-style welfare state is being dismantled by the son of the man who created it. Faced with huge deficits and a creaky Government bureaucracy that at its peak accounted for one of every six jobs, President Jose Maria Figueres is slashing away at the traditional system of cradle-to-grave protections and warning his people of continued austerity ahead. 
 "The people of Costa Rica must understand that, in the words of the only Milton Friedman phrase I ever use, there is no free lunch," said Leonardo Garnier, the Minister of Planning and Economic Policy. "If I don't pay now, I have to pay later."
Since taking office two years ago, Mr. Figueres, a 41-year-old graduate of West Point, has trimmed the Government payroll, undone the state's banking monopoly and shut down a Government railroad. He has agreed to sell some state enterprises and to allow private investment in others. At the same time, his Government has raised taxes, lowered tariffs and reformed a pension system once renowned for its generosity.
"It's not the same country anymore," said Victor Perez Mora, 54. A printer who worked for the National Lottery for 22 years until accepting a buyout offer in 1995, he now says he has to scrimp because the Government has fallen behind on payment of his retirement benefits. "Costa Rica always had a certain mystique, but now that is gone," he said.
It began in 1948, when Jose Figueres, known as Don Pepe, led a brief rebellion that resulted in the abolition of the military and installation of democratic rule. Once in power, Mr. Figueres and the National Liberation Party established state monopolies in areas like banking and utilities and set out to win the loyalty of voters by providing a network of social services with almost no fees.
The origins of the current crisis can be traced back to the 1970's, when Costa Rica borrowed heavily abroad to support the social safety net, building up a foreign debt that has soared in this decade to more than $4 billion. The day of reckoning was postponed throughout the 1980's by more than $1 billion in aid from the United States, which was alarmed by the emergence of left-wing, anti-American Governments in neighboring Nicaragua and Panama.
With the cold war's end, though, Costa Rica was largely left to fend for itself, and turned so heavily to borrowing on local markets that interest payments now swallow 30 percent of the budget. Two months ago, Washington closed its aid mission here, saying Costa Rica has "achieved a wholly sustainable economy" and should no longer be considered a developing country.
Compared with the rest of Latin America, living standards here are still at an enviable level, with an average life expectancy of 76 years, a literacy rate of 94 percent and a per capita income double that of the regional average. The United Nations' most recent Human Development Index puts Costa Rica ahead of Asian "tigers" like Singapore and even above some Western European countries, like Portugal.
But the current retrenchment has made Mr. Figueres Costa Rica's most unpopular President of modern times and led to accusations that he is betraying the principles of his father and their party, which is a member of the Socialist International.
According to a recent poll, only 12 percent of those surveyed think he is doing a "good or very good" job. Some 54 percent considered his performance to be "poor or very poor."
"I voted for him because I thought that being the son of Don Pepe, he would do good things," said Grace Castillo, who is trying to make a living running a small bakery after being laid off from her secretarial job. "But I am completely disgusted. He never tells the truth and cares nothing about us down below."
In late August, violence erupted in the Caribbean port of Limon after the Government announced a plan, likely to lead to layoffs and lost benefits, to allow private investment in the country's ports, airports and railroads. Three people were reported killed in the disturbances, unusual here, 78 people hurt and stores, warehouses and restaurants looted.
Government workers, who have had to bear the brunt of the belt-tightening, have been especially vociferous in their complaints about Mr. Figueres. But it is the perceived weakening of the education and health systems that seems to alarm Costa Ricans most.
"When I was a child, there was abundance," Ms. Castillo, 37, said. "At school, they gave us free milk, vaccinations, vitamins, even dental service. But now the idea of a truly free education has passed into history, and we parents pay not just for books, but even for the chalk."
At hospitals, the situation is much the same, with patients increasingly being told they must wait months to see a specialist. Many people tell stories of having to go on their own to pharmacies to buy medicines and supplies that the hospitals treating them no longer have.
The Government acknowledges that the social safety net is deteriorating. But officials say the decline is not a result of their policies but of years of lavish spending and they are acting now to prevent further erosion and to protect what is left.
"This Government is not so skilled as to be able to destroy all this so rapidly," Francisco de Paula Gutierrez, an American-trained economist who became Minister of Finance in July said, mocking an argument that is heard frequently here. "This is not something that happened overnight, but a process of accumulation over 15 or 20 years."
The international aid agencies and foreign economic analysts agree and say the retrenchment was long overdue. "They were living beyond their means," said one such expert. "Figueres should be given credit for making a lot of long-term decisions that are going to have good effects."
There are indications that the situation will get worse before it gets better. The 1997 budget, which is now being considered by the Legislative Assembly, calls for cuts of up to 28 percent in spending by ministries like those that deal with housing, health, culture and the environment.
"We don't think this plague is going to end any time soon," said Rigoberto Ulloa of the National Association of Public Employees, a union of Government workers. "The Government calls it the modernization of public services, but we consider it a way to fire workers and weaken public institutions."
Increasingly, those with money are turning to doctors in private practice, sending their children to private schools, and hiring private security guards for protection.
"The mechanisms of social integration are deteriorating, and the possibilities of social segregation are growing," said Carlos Sojo, a researcher at the Latin American Faculty of Social Sciences. "More and more, we have one system for those who can afford to pay, and another for those who cannot."

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE GRASS ROOTS;
In Ohio, the Campaign Is Just Now Gearing Up

SERIES: AN AMERICAN PLACE: Up Close and Political

BYLINE: By MICHAEL WINERIP 

DATELINE: CANTON, Ohio, Sept. 29

 BODY:
To read the major newspapers and watch television, the race for President is over, with Bill Clinton easily being re-elected in the polls. Even the Doonesbury comic strip's Presidential character, the White House waffle, has started looking ahead to what it is going to be like as a second-term lame duck.
Yet here in middle America, where the citizenry still goes through the formality of voting in November, the real political push has not even begun and will not for a couple of weeks. 
 "The heavy lifting is the last three weeks," said Don Singer, head of the local Job Training Partnership agency, who is running the Clinton campaign in Stark County. "This is a place with a lot of independent voters who make up their minds right at the end." Mr. Singer does not expect to receive his computer printout of 150 swing precincts from state Democratic headquarters in Columbus until mid-October.
The Stark Republican chairman, Charles Brown, still has not made up his mind where to put the campaign's get-out-the-vote telephone bank. "Somewhere where we'll be able to call all over the county with the fewest long-distance calls," he said.
As for the Perot organization, the other day a reporter called Al Coppock, a supporter, to find out who was directing the Perot effort in this county of 370,000 residents. "It's really just a group of us who call ourselves volunteers," he said. "I'll get back to you."
He called back three hours later to say, "The group has decided that, I guess, I should be the spokesperson." The Perot campaign will not have a headquarters here ("just an answering machine in someone's house," Mr. Coppock said). Nor did it in 1992, holding meetings at the public library. Nor was that a serious handicap.
In 1992, Mr. Perot got 25 percent of the vote in Stark County. He collected 21 percent of the state vote without making a single campaign appearance in Ohio.
To watch a Presidential election take shape in a place like Stark, the seventh most populous county among 88 counties in this crucial swing state, is to be reminded that the vast amount of information and politicking descends upon the citizenry from faraway places.
The Democrats' and Republicans' Presidential commercials and the Perot infomercials are all nationally generated and are watched by Cantonians on cable television tuned to Cleveland stations. (There is no commercial television in Canton). National political television news comes from the networks, CNN, C-Span. (The local television news from Cleveland is basically crime, the weather, The TRIBE! and the winning Pick 4 state lottery number).
Canton's newspaper, The Repository, relies primarily on The Associated Press for national political coverage. Commentary on WHBC, the local radio station, comes from conservative syndicated programs, including three hours of Rush Limbaugh daily and reports by Paul Harvey, who recently used a large portion of his newscast to say that even with Alzheimer's, Ronald Reagan would be a better President than Bill Clinton.

Still a Brawl In the County
The Clinton campaign leaders here like Mr. Singer know the national and state poll numbers and are confident they are ahead. The latest statewide poll, released today by the University of Akron, has the President ahead by 13 points. But the Stark Democrats also feel the pressure because they are the ones who have to deliver, because they realize the problem for Democrats is always getting their voters out, and they are aware how few voters can make a difference in a county like Stark and a state like Ohio.
Mr. Clinton carried Stark County by 40-to-35 percent over George Bush in 1992, almost exactly his national spread. But in Stark, with Mr. Perot getting so many votes, that five-point spread was only an 8,021-vote difference, less than half the people who attend the annual McKinley versus Massillon high school football game here.
Stark Republicans realize they are behind, but they believe they have a chance, and Alfred Tuchfarber for one, who oversees the Ohio Poll for the University of Cincinnati, thinks the party could still do it. Last spring, Mr. Clinton led in Stark and statewide polls by high double digits; the Ohio Poll, taken in mid-September, shows him leading by nine points.
Four years ago at this time, Mr. Clinton was ahead of Mr. Bush in the Ohio Poll by eight points, and in the end, Mr. Clinton barely carried Ohio, winning by less than two percentage points. Ohio is both a strong bellwether -- missing only twice for President in 100 years -- and Ohio is hard on Democrats. The last Democrat before Mr. Clinton to carry Ohio for President was the last previous Democratic President, Jimmy Carter, who won by just 11,000 out of 4.1 million votes cast in 1976.
As the Republicans have pulled back resources in states they feel Mr. Dole cannot win, like California, they have upped the ante in places like Ohio that he must win. Mr. Dole has made eight campaign stops in the state, three in the last six weeks, and he is to return to Ohio on Tuesday. Last week the Dole campaign spent $350,000 on television advertising in Ohio, well above what Democrats had expected.
Those commercials have mainly been attacks on Mr. Clinton's drug-enforcement policy, hitting the President where he is most vulnerable, on character issues. They seem to resonate here a lot more than the earlier Republican commercials, which focused on Mr. Dole's pledge to cut Federal taxes by 15 percent.
Even many Republicans here question the wisdom of Mr. Dole's tax proposal, when the latest unemployment rate for Stark is 4.4 percent, half of what it was four years ago, and when the biggest employer, the Timken Company, is posting record profits, opening new operations in China, increasing its research budget by 15 percent and announcing it will spend $55 million to renovate its Harrison steel plant here.
On the other hand, one of the big local news stories the last few months has been the growing presence of gangs and drug use in county schools. Two weeks ago, more than 100 Stark community leaders met in the courthouse downtown for a Gang Summit to discuss the problem. And while there were people from the cities and suburbs, blacks and whites, it was primarily white Republican suburban leaders running the show, including Common Pleas Judge Lee Sinclair of Louisville, Ohio, and the president of the county commission, John P. Dougherty of Jackson, Ohio, men from the very places where Mr. Dole must win big if he is to carry Stark.
"In Ohio," Mr. Tuchfarber said. "I see anything from a 3-to-4 point Dole win to an 8-point Clinton win."

From Big Business, Big G.O.P. Money
Throughout the year, the Stark Democrats and Republicans have held old-fashioned picnics, Swiss steak dinners, chili cook-offs, hamburger helpers, golf outings, candidates' nights -- in short, modest-sized affairs that cost $10 to $25 a ticket and give the local candidates and the parties some exposure. Mr. Brown, the Republican chairman, likens the local political activity to a parade that is off in the distance for most people but is just noisy enough so the citizenry has a vague sense that when the time comes, the appropriate entourage will march by.
The time comes in mid-October, with phone banks to pinpoint support, literature drops to signal the arriving moment and squads of drivers to deliver the vote.
It is striking, too, that with all that has been written nationally about the changing face of party politics, in Canton, the financial and volunteer backbone of the two major parties continues to look like a Herblock political cartoon: Republicans in their top hats versus Democrats in their hard hats, with "Business" and "Labor" stenciled across their chests.
Executives of the Timken steel and roller-bearing company and Timken family members continue to be the largest local contributors to Republicans, as they have for most of this century. In the last year, 43 Canton residents who identified themselves as Timken employees on campaign forms made $189,325 in political contributions -- every penny of it to Republicans, according to a computer search of Federal election records by the Center for Responsive Politics, a nonpartisan research organization.
Setting the tone for Timken executives is W. R. (Tim) Timken Jr., 57, the chairman of the Timken Company, and a Dole supporter who says he has never knowingly voted for a Democrat. Mr. Timken and his immediate family gave $100,000 to the Republican National Committee this year, as has been their custom in recent Presidential elections.
Mr. Brown, the Stark Republican chairman, complains that the state Democratic Party pays for the Clinton headquarters here, while the county Republicans have to raise their money locally. That is less of a problem than it sounds, thanks to the Timken Company. In the last year, 30 Timken executives (none of them Timken family members) contributed a total of $50,750 to the Stark Republican Party.
Longtime Timken executives say that in the same sense that it is part of the Timken corporate culture to be involved in community volunteer efforts like the United Way and Football Hall of Fame Week, there is an expectation that top corporate managers will take an active interest in the Republican Party.
Mr. Timken's grandfather, H. H. Timken, was a $5,000 contributor to the Republican National Committee in 1918 and the fourth generation Timken to run the company is not one who believes in ticket splitting. "You have to look at the two parties and ask, 'Which am I?' " Mr. Timken said in an interview earlier this year. "If you agree with 80 percent of what the Republicans say, then you vote Republican. I don't agree with the people who say, 'I vote for the person.' A Democrat here, a Republican here, they cancel themselves out."
People here refer to Timken, which employs 6,000 people in Canton and 17,000 worldwide, as a Republican company. There are Timken executives as well as executives working for companies doing business with Timken who say privately they are voting for Mr. Clinton this time, but would not let their names be used. Last month, a Timken manager approached a reporter at the county fair and was eager to be interviewed until he realized some of the questions being asked were political. "If you used my name and said I was for Clinton, it could affect my future," he said.
Mr. Timken says that would not happen. "You don't have to be a Republican to work here," he said. "We have Democrats who work here." But he also said: "You have to look at which of the parties has the philosophy to benefit the economy and enterprise. It's no wonder that so many people, not only at the Timken Company, but business in general, believe in the philosophy of the Republican Party."

Labor's Donations Go to Democrats
On the Democrat side, local union contributions are harder to pinpoint because the money is distributed by labor leadership on the state level, but the link is unmistakable. In 1994, labor unions contributed a total of $2.8 million to candidates in Ohio's statewide and legislative races, with $2.6 million of it going to Democrats, according to a study by the Citizens Policy Center and the Kiplinger Reporting Program at Ohio State. In Ohio, the four biggest organizational contributors to state races in 1994 were all unions, representing teachers, automobile workers, government workers, as well as the A.F.L.-C.I.O.
The Clinton re-election headquarters in Stark, which opened Sept. 14, shares a building with two other major tenants, the steelworkers' union and teachers' union. The person who will put together much of the plan to deploy volunteers for the Democrats in Stark's 150 swing precincts is John Mroczkowski, regional director of the United Steelworkers' union here.
The Democrats say they will need 170 volunteers out in the precincts doing mail drops. "We count on the unions heavily for volunteers," said John Ferrero, the Democratic Party county chairman.
Mr. Dole has repeatedly ridiculed the teachers' unions as enemies of education reform, and the local chapter presidents are out in force reminding members that the Ohio Education Association, their union, has endorsed Mr. Clinton.
Mark Mathieu, a union president in a suburban district known as Canton Local, is holding weekly after-school meetings with teachers at each of the district's five buildings. "I tell them, 'Your future is at stake on a lot of these issues,' " said Mr. Mathieu, a Republican who is voting for Mr. Clinton this time.
The labor rank-and-file vote is not the monolithic Democratic vote it once was and Democrats acknowledge a surprisingly large number of union members here voted for Mr. Perot in 1992. In a poll taken in Stark in May by The New York Times with Ohio State University and the Northwestern University's Survey Laboratory, 52 percent of the union members interviewed indicated they voted for Mr. Clinton in 1992, 20 percent said they voted for Mr. Bush and 26 percent said they voted for Mr. Perot.
With Perot's appeal diminished this year, the Democrats are pushing hard to recapture that union vote. Jim Repace, president of the 2,000-member electrical workers' local at the Hoover Company, voted for Mr. Perot in 1992, but is behind Mr. Clinton this time, he says. His support for the Democratic President has grown as his local union's relationship with Hoover management has gone from amicable to miserable in recent months. Last summer, the company decided to move its new line of upright vacuums to El Paso, Tex., where nonunion workers will earn $7.50 an hour, half the hourly wage here.
Mr. Repace has been inviting political speakers to the monthly union meetings with hopes that the battle with Hoover management would motivate the membership to action, but he is not so sure. "I'm telling you, I'm not getting the response I'd like to have," he said. "We're getting the normal amount to the meetings."
At the opening of Clinton headquarters here, Harold Roach, the former Stark County Treasurer, whose son Mark is the current County Treasurer, delivered in 15 seconds that age-old message the Democrats will be pushing to win over voters in all the nation's Cantons these next few weeks.
"I saw in the paper Timken roller bearing is spending $55 million on remodeling," he said, "We know how strong Timmy Timken is a Republican. We got to remind people he made that money on a Democratic economy."
 
An American Place
Living midway between Democratic Cleveland and Republican Columbus, the citizens of Canton, Ohio, and surrounding Stark County have a history of voting for the winning Presidential candidate. This is the 27th article in a yearlong series that explores the forces shaping the political debate at the grass roots. (Previous articles are available to computer users on The New York Times on the Web, at http://www.nytimes.com/ specials/canton.)

HEADLINE: Metro Matters;
On Workfare, Fig Leaves And Silence

BYLINE: By JOYCE PURNICK 

 BODY:
"Nothing in this subsection shall preempt or supersede any provision of state or local law that provides greater protection for employees from displacement."
Section 407(f)(3) of the new Federal welfare legislation, which deals with workfare. 
 DOES anybody read these days?
Because anyone who actually has read the new welfare law might make a surprising discovery: the same section that sanctions the replacement of unionized workers with unsalaried welfare recipients also gives local governments the means to limit that same practice.
But Stanley Hill, leader of New York City's largest municipal union, has not called on New York to pass a protective law; he wants the Federal law amended. And anyway, "politicians can mess around with laws," he said over the weekend. Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani has not proposed that the city or state pass its own law either, though he says he objects to hiring replacement workers and is furious with the Metropolitan Transportation Authority because it plans to replace 500 cleaners who leave through attrition with welfare recipients. The City Council Speaker, Peter F. Vallone, said yesterday that he asked his staff to look into the matter of a local law just last week and nobody in Albany has said a public word about it.
It is always possible that, the law being the size of the Manhattan telephone book, few have gotten as far as Section 407. More likely, the silence is deliberate because there is little support for a stricter local law. It would constrain unions and government, and they need all the flexibility they can get.
THE reality is that barring a resurgent economy, there simply are not enough low-skilled jobs around for both union workers and welfare recipients. So labor leaders are protecting their members -- and therefore their standing in their unions -- by extracting no-layoff pledges from City Hall, as Mr. Hill did for his union, District Council 37 of the American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees. And by agreeing not to lay off any union workers, Mr. Giuliani and other politicians keep the cooperation of labor leaders -- who control many a vote -- even if workfare spawns a cheap work force.
Some contend it already has and that under the welfare law, that phenomenon will grow. City officials predict that in a few years, 100,000 welfare recipients will have to go into the city's workfare program, which now numbers 35,000. How many will get real jobs? A hint came last week, after Mr. Hill staged his quicksilver rebellion and demanded a moratorium on workfare; the Mayor promised to find permanent city jobs for those in workfare -- but only about 1,000. The city has 204,000 employees.
The point is, with the new law mandating ever more workers from the welfare ranks, workers with little skill and experience will be scrambling for the same jobs. What looms is an unpleasant, no-win competition between the poor on welfare and the poor not on welfare.
The Mayor says the city's workfare workers are doing only "supplementary" work -- in former union jobs abandoned because of budget cuts. But definitions are loose. For instance, how long does a task have to be neglected before it becomes "supplementary?" A year? Two years? Five?
From the beginning, the debate about workfare in New York City has centered on its value to recipients and to the city, not its effect on unions. Because from the beginning there was an unspoken understanding that welfare workers could become unofficial replacement workers, despite curbs on workfare in the state social services law.
NOW the new Federal welfare act expressly states that governments "may fill a vacant employment position" with a welfare worker as long as there were no layoffs. City Hall started its program long before the President signed the bill, getting around some constraints on workfare by labeling the work supplementary, and now that is no longer necessary.
The Metropolitan Transportation Authority's tentative agreement with its union -- subject to approval by rank-and-file members and Mayor Giuliani -- removes the fig leaf, as one critic put it. The agency would drop 500 jobs through attrition (no layoffs), welfare recipients working for their benefits would fill those jobs, and then an unspecified number might be hired as permanent, salaried employees.
"The reality is, the fig leaf was going to disappear anyway," said Marc V. Shaw, executive director of the authority. "This is the next step, the new and improved workfare program because people doing it will have a shot at jobs. That issue is out there, and this is the answer."
The city or the state could make his deal impossible by enacting a new law. But if prevented from using welfare workers, government might again have to expand its labor force, something it contends it cannot afford.
So Section 407(f)(3) gets the silent treatment, which in one way is a shame: invoking it would at least generate a debate, force union leaders and politicians to level with the public about laws, fig leaves -- and reality.

HEADLINE: Patents;
A dispute over prepaid college tuition programs turns into a full-blown constitutional debate.

BYLINE: By Teresa Riordan 

 BODY:
IT'S rare that a patent dispute raises constitutional issues. But an infringement dispute over prepaid tuition programs has just erupted into a full-blown battle over how heavily a state can tread on the commercial rights of an individual.
Since the late 1980's, many parents have been funneling their savings into prepaid tuition programs, which index their returns to college inflation rates. Peter A. Roberts, who is the chairman of College Savings Bank of Princeton, N.J., and was granted a pioneering patent covering a computerized version of the concept in 1988, contends that some states, chief among them Florida, have stolen his idea and are infringing on his patent as they run similar businesses. 
 Earlier this month the Justice Department intervened, filing briefs on behalf of the College Savings Bank in its lawsuit in Federal District Court in Trenton against the Florida Prepaid Postsecondary Education Expense Board. Oral arguments on a motion to dismiss the suit are scheduled for Nov. 4.
"The question here is whether states, as they move more and more into competition with private business, ought to be treated like other people or whether they are to be completely unaccountable," said Herman Schwartz, a professor of constitutional law at American University in Washington.
A prepaid tuition program does not offer astounding returns. "The key feature is that it guarantees to meet the future cost of college," Mr. Roberts said. "Because it is backed by the F.D.I.C., it eliminates the risk that money market accounts or bonds would have."
College Savings Bank has about 12,000 accounts and $95 million in deposits, while the Florida program, the largest in the nation, has more than $1 billion.
Kevin J. Culligan, Mr. Roberts's lawyer and a partner at the New York firm of Fish & Neave, said Florida relied on Mr. Roberts's input while putting together its own program. "First they picked his brains, then they picked his pockets," Mr. Culligan said.
Because of the 11th Amendment to the Constitution, which largely indemnifies states from being sued by individuals in Federal court, Mr. Roberts traditionally would have had no recourse against Florida or any other state that he believed had infringed his patent. But in 1992, Congress revised the patent and trademark laws to allow individuals to sue states for trespassing on their intellectual property.
For its part, Florida denies that it is infringing Mr. Roberts's patent. In a brief filed in April, Florida contended that "Congress lacks the power to abrogate the state's 11th Amendment immunity to such claims." And then in a brief filed last week Florida argued that a recent Supreme Court ruling, Seminole Tribe v. Florida, negated the Congressional act of 1992. Robert G. Shepherd, one of the lawyers Florida has hired in New Jersey and a partner with Mathews, Woodbridge & Collins in Princeton, said he could not comment on the case.
Professor Schwartz, who is not involved in the College Savings litigation, disagreed with Florida's interpretation of the Seminole decision, calling the decision "dreadful" and saying it was "unclear" on the question of the 11th Amendment.
Meanwhile, Mr. Roberts has other worries. In August Congress passed a new law, originally introduced by Senator Bob Graham, Democrat of Florida, that exempts state-sponsored tuition plans from tax and also defers taxation of earnings for investors.
"I expect an explosion in the growth of these programs run by states," Mr. Roberts said. "That tax break is a tremendously unfair advantage for them."
The patent under dispute is 4,722,055.

A New Method Of Gene Sequencing
Mutations in the gene known as p53 have been linked with all manner of cancers, including those of the colon, lung and breast. Since such mutations have been associated with particularly aggressive forms of these cancers, a patient's course of treatment can depend on whether such an aberration is found.
Testing can be expensive, though, costing $500 to $1,000. Because the gene might be mutated in hundreds of different ways, inexpensive genetic probes, which search and find bits of DNA identical to themselves, are ineffective. Instead, the gene must be sequenced -- transcribed, essentially, into DNA building-block letters -- so that it can be proofread for errors.
Visible Genetics Inc. of Toronto this month patented what it says can be a speedier and significantly less expensive method for sequencing p53.
Instead of sequencing the entire gene or a big chunk of it, Visible Genetics first screens it using an existing crude test like fragment analysis.
"By using the crude test we narrow the mutation down to a specific part of the gene," said John K. Stevens, chairman of Visible Genetics. "Then we can sequence just that area."
But because the preliminary test is so imprecise, a complete sequencing must be done when it fails to find a possible area of mutation.
"We're not necessarily more accurate than anyone else," Mr. Stevens said. "But because we have to do less sequencing, we end up with a much less expensive test."
Mr. Roberts said that because the process combined existing techniques, "most people when they read this patent shake their head and say, 'No way you could get a patent on this'; but nobody had ever thought to put these two things together for a diagnostic test."
Visible Genetics received patent 5,545,527.
 
Have Business Card, Will Tee Off
Robert J. Veylupek and George Wallace Jr. of Las Vegas, Nev., have patented a "combination business card golf tee."
The golf tee is formed when a perforated tab is locked into an opening at one end of the card. "The tee comprises the card in an arched form, with the ends of the card acting as legs for the tee, and the absence of the body portion of the tab forming a saddle elevated above the ground into which the ball may be placed," the patent states.
Mr. Veylupek and Mr. Wallace received patent 5,503,396.
Patents are available by number for $3 from the Patent and Trademark Office, Washington, D.C. 20231.

HEADLINE: I.M.F. and World Bank Clear Debt Relief

BYLINE: By PAUL LEWIS 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 29

 BODY:
With the world economy apparently in position to grow steadily and low inflation predicted for the next four years, finance ministers from rich and poor countries today formally endorsed a new plan to lighten the debt burden of the poorest nations.
Under this plan, which the Group of Seven industrial powers agreed to on Saturday and formally approved today, creditor nations stand ready to forgive these countries about 25 percent of their debts, once they have started to develop free-market economies favorable to private enterprise and foreign investment. 
 The calculations assume that up to 80 percent of eligible debts will be forgiven. For creditor governments and banks, only the debt incurred before the mid-1980's will be eligible for this relief in most cases.
But all debt owed to the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank -- agencies that started lending to poor countries 15 years ago, after a wide debt crisis cut off the flow of government and private loans -- will be eligible for relief under the plan.
The agreement represents a breakthrough in dealing with deeply indebted nations, most of them in Africa, because it raises the level of forgiveness that creditors will offer to well above the 67 percent ceiling now, and for the first time wipes out debts owed to international aid agencies. It comes at a time the economic outlook for industrial nations is relatively hopeful, giving them both the incentive and the opportunity to turn their attention to the poorer countries.
The I.M.F. predicts that worldwide growth will pick up to an average annual rate of 4.5 percent between 1998 and 2001 -- significantly above the 3.5 percent to 4 percent growth averaged over the last 25 years.
At the same time, the currency markets have been tamer, leading the American Secretary of the Treasury, Robert E. Rubin, to remark that this year's Group of Seven foreign-exchange review "was not of the kind we had a year ago," when the Clinton Administration was under fire over a sharp slump in the dollar's value.
Heavy debt burdens, and their associated costs, can stifle growth in the poorest developing countries, leaving them unattractive to foreign investors, I.M.F. officials stress. The aim of the relief plan is to help these countries achieve a sustainable debt burden -- 200 percent to 250 percent of their annual export earnings.
Oxfam, an international-development charity that advocates debt relief, calculates that this could save Uganda -- likely to be among the first countries to receive help -- as much as $80 million annually over the next three years. The East African nation's export earnings last year were about $600 million; its total debt is $3.4 billion.
That saving would provide enough money for basic health services for as many as two million people, immunize a million children and buy teaching material for two million primary-school pupils.
Some 20 countries are considered to have unsustainable or possibly unsustainable debt burdens -- including Zaire, Zambia, Nicaragua, Bolivia, Madagascar and Tanzania.
These countries' total debts, both those eligible and ineligible under the new relief plan, are estimated at $97 billion. Of this, 58 percent is owed to creditor governments, 22 percent to international institutions and 14 percent to foreign banks.
Governments would simply write off their loss. The World Bank's president, James D. Wolfensohn, said he would allocate $2 billion from future profit for debt relief, and the I.M.F. is asking its members for contributions, but is ready to sell some of its gold supply if these steps prove insufficient.
While the debt-relief plan represents the only tangible achievement of this year's World Bank and I.M.F. meetings, Secretary Rubin has presented it as part of a broader attempt to preserve growth and world financial stability by guarding against new Mexican-style crises.
He described the debt plan as one of a series of measures decided on in principle by the Group of Seven at their heads of government meeting in Halifax, Nova Scotia. These included instructing the I.M.F. to tighten surveillance of member nations' economies to spot crises in the making; encouraging nations to provide more information about their financial health; strengthening regulation of international markets, and new plans to increase the amount of money the I.M.F. can offer countries in distress.
Other potentially troublesome aspects of the world economy were discussed in the weekend conference. Of these, Mr. Rubin has said he now expects Japan to maintain the recovery in domestic demand that seems to be under way, providing an expanding market for foreign goods.
Another worry is whether Europe's plan to start its single-currency plan in 1999, and the attendant race to cut national budget deficits, means that growth forecasts for the Continent may be too optimistic.
