HEADLINE: TIES THAT BIND -- A special report.;
Behind the Scenes, D'Amato Wields Vast Power in Albany

BYLINE: By JAMES DAO and JANE FRITSCH 

 BODY:
When New York City police officers wanted legislation in Albany to help increase their pay, they turned to Washington, where Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato was eager to be of service.
When New York hospitals needed some political muscle to head off state cuts in money to train doctors, they enlisted Senator D'Amato to lobby state officials on their behalf. 
 And when Republicans in the State Senate wanted to oust their majority leader, Ralph J. Marino, they had Senator D'Amato orchestrate the coup.
From matters of policy to politics to personnel, Mr. D'Amato has emerged as one of the most powerful forces in Albany. So effective is his ability to call in the favors he has meticulously conferred during three decades of political life that he is often able to overwhelm the bosses of the State Legislature and to twist the arm of the Governor himself.
It is an unusual role for any United States Senator, and it is a particularly incongruous position for Mr. D'Amato, who is accustomed to occupying center stage. But he must work the levers of state government from behind the scenes to avoid publicly humiliating his protege, Gov. George E. Pataki.
"Al D'Amato is the single most important Republican, one of national stature, in New York State," said State Senator Nicholas A. Spano, a Republican of Westchester. "He's a skilled technician. He's got his influence in every portion of the state. I'm not surprised when people reach out to him."
In a recent interview, Mr. D'Amato sought to play down his influence on Mr. Pataki. "He's the captain of the ship and I'm the cheerleader," Mr. D'Amato said. But after a pause, he went on. "They call me continually," he said, referring to state officials who consult him about legislation and policy. "But I was constantly called by other administrations, too," he added.
Mr. D'Amato said that he and Mr. Pataki were "a team working for the state of New York" and that New York was "fighting for survival."
"I particularly want him to be successful and I will do everything and anything I can," Mr. D'Amato said.
"I think that in this state, Senator D'Amato plays a role in most things that happen," said Lawrence Elovitch, a well-known lawyer who is a friend of Mr. D'Amato and who lobbies on trial lawyer issues in Albany. "He certainly is titular head of the party. He certainly has a close relationship with all members of the administration. He has his hand on most things that happen in this state."
No other United States Senator in recent New York history has wielded as much power over state government as Mr. D'Amato. Even such popular and respected Senators as Jacob K. Javits and Daniel Patrick Moynihan have never dominated Albany the way Mr. D'Amato does.
"New York Senators tend to be more, how should I say, Olympian in style, big-picture in nature," said Gerald Benjamin, a political scholar and assistant dean at the State University of New York at New Paltz, who is considered an expert on modern New York State political history. "Javits and Moynihan were famous for focusing on foreign policy, welfare. D'Amato is much more interested in state politics."

Hospitals
Big Cuts Avoided In Doctor Training
When the Greater New York Hospital Association looked for someone on whom to bestow its annual award last spring, the choice was obvious: Senator D'Amato. He was honored at a dinner at the Waldorf-Astoria, a reward for his strenuous efforts to save New York's hospitals from ruinous Federal -- and especially state -- budget cuts.
Mr. D'Amato, a highly public advocate of hospital interests in Washington, had been working behind the scenes to fend off deep cuts proposed by Mr. Pataki in money to train new doctors.
As part of a broader plan to cut hospital costs, Mr. Pataki had proposed to reduce by more than half the $1.9 billion spent by the state each year for graduate medical education. The cuts would help trim a health care system bloated with extra doctors, Pataki aides said.
But such cuts would go to the heart of New York City's public hospitals, which rely heavily on trainee doctors to serve the poor, and would also hit hard at the private teaching hospitals in the city. So hospital administrators immediately asked Mr. D'Amato for help. As chairman of the Senate Finance Committee, Mr. D'Amato was deeply involved with hospital-financing issues and had been fighting proposed Federal cuts in graduate medical education.
"I personally talked to Senator D'Amato about the proposal," said Kenneth E. Raske, the president of the hospital association. "He's extremely knowledgeable about it."
Mr. D'Amato was concerned that the efforts he was making in Washington for New York hospitals were being undermined at home, Mr. Raske said, so the Senator began contacting officials in the Pataki administration.
"He talked with members of the executive branch" Mr. Raske said. "The Senator is a man of great passion and commitment, and he can be extremely influential on any issue. He's an awfully impressive ally."
Mr. D'Amato said he intervened because hospital financing was an issue of great importance to New York. "I just called it to the attention of the budget people and others that the reductions that were called for initially would not save the state real money," Mr. D'Amato said.
In the end, Mr. Pataki backed down and restored most of the money.

Governor Pataki
Marching in Step With Benefactor
Mr. D'Amato solidified his place as the state's pre-eminent political leader two years ago when he engineered and arranged the financing for the election of Mr. Pataki, the first-term State Senator whom he had plucked from obscurity.
More than any other recent political figure, Mr. D'Amato has transformed New York politics, resurrecting a debilitated Republican Party and at the same time methodically installing a network of politicians loyal to him in counties throughout the state. Like Mr. Pataki, those politicians are beholden to Mr. D'Amato for using his formidable fund-raising powers to shore up their campaigns.
Thus it is hardly surprising that Mr. Pataki so often seems to march closely behind Mr. D'Amato.
Last year, for instance, the state Republican Party, and Mr. D'Amato in particular, were sharply criticized for using New York's Byzantine election laws to keep challengers to Bob Dole off the primary ballot. At the time, both Mr. D'Amato and Mr. Pataki defended the system.
But just after the primary, Mr. D'Amato reversed himself and said he thought the laws should be changed to make it easier for challengers to get on the ballot. Mr. Pataki quickly agreed and successfully proposed a law to do just that.
During the primary season, Mr. D'Amato, worried about the national party's drift to the right, complained that the House Speaker, Newt Gingrich, was "scaring Americans," and called Patrick J. Buchanan, the conservative commentator, a "philosophical ayatollah." Mr. Pataki followed suit, although in muted terms, accusing both men of preaching intolerance.
And when Mr. D'Amato criticized Mr. Dole's campaign earlier this year, Mr. Pataki soon did the same.
To be sure, Mr. Pataki does not bend to all of Mr. D'Amato's demands. But the sheer breadth of Mr. D'Amato's contacts within the Pataki administration, his undisputed closeness to the Governor and his iron control over the state party raise questions about Mr. Pataki's independence.
Mr. Pataki said he considered Mr. D'Amato "a friend and very important asset in Washington" who does not dictate how state government should be run. "He is the Federal representative," Mr. Pataki said. "I run the state."

Police Bill
P.B.A. Turns Tide Through D'Amato
In many ways, Mr. D'Amato's role in Albany is that of a super-lobbyist, someone who has so much influence with so many officials that it has become only natural for people to reach out to him when they want something from state government.
That fact was spotlighted earlier this year when New York City's powerful police union, the Patrolmen's Benevolent Association, began a serious push for passage of legislation that would have changed the way the city arbitrated labor disputes with police officers. The change would have made it easier for the union to win such fights, leading, city officials believed, to a steady increase in police salaries.
The Legislature passed the measure with hardly a dissenting vote. But Mr. Pataki vetoed it, agreeing with Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani's vociferous objections that it would add $200 million to the city's labor costs.
For most governors in this century, the veto would have ended the issue. But the P.B.A. turned to Mr. D'Amato for help. Many P.B.A. members live on Long Island, and the union has had a long and close relationship with the Senator. James J. Lysaght, a lawyer for the P.B.A., gave $10,000 to Mr. D'Amato's legal defense fund in 1993, when the Senate Ethics Committee was investigating allegations that his brother, Armand, was improperly using Mr. D'Amato's office to lobby for a client, the Unisys Corporation, a military contractor.
The Senator "said he would speak to Republican legislators," said Lou Matarazzo, the P.B.A. president, adding, "It must have been helpful."
In the end, Republicans said Mr. D'Amato's support for the overriding of the veto made it easier for them to cast a historic vote against the Governor.
Indeed, both the Democratic-controlled Assembly and Republican-controlled State Senate easily overturned Mr. Pataki's veto. And Mr. Pataki did not work strenuously to fend off the vote to override. Perhaps, legislators said, Mr. Pataki was deferring to Mr. D'Amato's strong support for the bill. At the time, Mr. Pataki said that was not true.
Senator D'Amato said recently that he had long supported efforts to get better pay for police officers. "New York City is losing some of their brightest, finest, youngest, most talented police officers because of pay inequity," he said. "I made my view known."

Personnel
7 Commissioners Linked to D'Amato
Mr. D'Amato's ability to influence state government rests on his strong personal ties to many of the state's top policy makers. Seven of the 16 commissioners appointed by Mr. Pataki to run departments in Albany have close personal relationships with Mr. D'Amato or owe their jobs to his sponsorship.
Several are old friends of the Senator. Some are expected to play important roles in his 1998 re-election campaign. And all have the power to decide issues of great interest not only to Mr. D'Amato but also to many of his major contributors.
"He doesn't need to call the Governor," said one Republican state senator, speaking on the condition of anonymity. "He has so many surrogates who will do things for him. They know what he wants, what he dislikes. They are people who have been close to him. They will do things the way they sense he wants it done."
One is Neil D. Levin, the State Superintendent of Banks. Mr. Levin went to work for Mr. D'Amato on the Senate Banking Committee shortly after graduating from law school. They remained friends after Mr. Levin left to work for Goldman, Sachs & Company, the investment firm. Mr. D'Amato and his former girlfriend, Claudia Cohen, even introduced Mr. Levin to his future wife. When Mr. Levin applied for the state banking job, he sent his resume first to Mr. D'Amato with a note that read, "As always, I really appreciate your help."
Mr. D'Amato has close relationships with many other Pataki administration officials. Charles A. Gargano, the Commissioner for Economic Development, has long been Mr. D'Amato's chief fund-raiser, and at Mr. D'Amato's request, he played that role for Mr. Pataki. One of Mr. Gargano's top deputies, Edward Martin, was Mr. D'Amato's press secretary in the 1980's.
Michael H. Urbach, Commissioner of Taxation and Finance, has been a regular in the Senator's Albany poker game. John F. O'Mara, chairman of the State Public Service Commission, which regulates public utilities, is one of Mr. D'Amato's most trusted advisers.
And Zenia Mucha, the Governor's spokeswoman, ran Mr. D'Amato's press operation for nearly a decade. She remains the Senator's close and loyal friend, speaking to him several times a week by phone.
The Senator's ties to Pataki appointees have given him the kinds of access and influence in state agencies that are normally available only to the Governor or his most senior aides. Earlier this year, for example, Nassau County residents complained to Mr. D'Amato that insurance companies refused to write homeowners' policies on Long Island's South Shore. Mr. D'Amato swiftly called the State Insurance Superintendent, Edward J. Muhl.
Before he joined the Pataki administration, Mr. Muhl had worked for Reliance Insurance Group, whose chief executive, Saul P. Steinberg, has been a major contributor to Mr. D'Amato's campaigns. Within days, Mr. Muhl had promised to investigate possible redlining. And he pledged to open a Long Island field office and amend state policies to make it easier for brokers to issue coverage for coastal homeowners.
Even Democrats who had complained about coastal insurance problems for months expressed grudging respect for Mr. D'Amato's ability to get things done.
"D'Amato is to George Pataki and Ed Muhl what Jim Henson was to Kermit and Grover," said Assemblyman Alexander P. Grannis, a Democrat of Manhattan. "Moynihan doesn't get involved in these local issues; D'Amato will. And that is the bread and butter of a successful politician."
Mr. D'Amato's influence extends to Mr. Pataki's political operations. Kieran Mahoney, who helped manage Mr. Pataki's 1994 campaign and remains one of his top political advisers, had worked on Mr. D'Amato's staff and ran his 1992 campaign. Arthur J. Finkelstein, who is Mr. Pataki's chief pollster, has been the Senator's main political strategist since 1980. And William D. Powers, the chairman of the state Republican Party, is a former D'Amato aide who was picked by the Senator to run the party machinery.
Mr. D'Amato's involvement with the day-to-day workings of Albany runs so deep that he sometimes intervenes in internal personnel matters. In the spring of 1995, for example, a dispute erupted between the Budget Director, Patricia A. Woodworth, and some of Mr. Pataki's closest advisers over how to handle the budget fight.
Ms. Woodworth pushed for deep cuts while Michael C. Finnegan, the Governor's counsel, advised moderation.
With Ms. Woodworth on the verge of quitting, Mr. D'Amato demanded that the administration find a way to assuage her. Her exit would have been a huge embarrassment to the first-year Governor. Ms. Woodworth was persuaded to stay, and later Mr. D'Amato appeared at a news conference in Albany with Ms. Woodworth at his side, in a clear public show of support.
It was a paternal role that Mr. D'Amato has become accustomed to playing. "It's like managing the kids," said John E. Sweeney, the State Labor Commissioner, who is former executive director of the state Republican Party.
He said he frequently consulted Mr. D'Amato on matters large and small. "He calls you right back," Mr. Sweeney said. "I think he appreciates the respect. And he's earned it. He's more experienced; he's the dean. His perspective is probably more valuable than anyone else's could be. He's been through the wars."

Majority Leader
Marino Is Out And Bruno In
Mr. D'Amato holds significant sway over the Republican-controlled State Senate, not only because he is the party's undisputed leader but also because he played a pivotal role in installing its most powerful member, the majority leader, Joseph L. Bruno, Republican of Rensselaer County.
Shortly after he was elected, Mr. Pataki demanded the ouster of Ralph J. Marino as majority leader because of a longstanding feud. But the Governor-elect's allies turned to Mr. D'Amato to get the job done. And he did, orchestrating the Thanksgiving Day coup in 1994 that unseated Mr. Marino and transformed Mr. Bruno overnight into one of Albany's most powerful figures.
For nearly a week, Mr. D'Amato had conspired secretly with a handful of senators to build support for Mr. Bruno, dispatching trusted aides to lobby senators and making calls to pressure the fence-sitters into dumping Mr. Marino. In the end, the Marino forces were routed; it was one of the few times that a sitting majority leader had been deposed.
"D'Amato knew how to count," said one Republican official who was involved in the revolt. "He knew what people to call and how to build the coalition. He knew how to do it in a way so that people knew the train was leaving the station. That's not George Pataki's style or one of his strong points. Pataki sent the signal and then went to Florida. D'Amato did the rest."
With that masterly political stroke, Mr. D'Amato had both installed the majority leader and made Mr. Bruno a political debtor whose note would inevitably be called in.
And Mr. D'Amato called in the favor earlier this year during the long fight over the state budget. Mr. Bruno, usually Mr. Pataki's strongest ally in the Legislature, had been increasingly aligning himself with the Democratic Assembly Speaker, Sheldon Silver. Mr. Bruno and Mr. Silver even considered drawing up their own budget to replace Mr. Pataki's.
Mr. D'Amato viewed such a move as a bad precedent that would isolate Mr. Pataki. So he called Mr. Bruno and chided him for disloyalty. Mr. Bruno retreated.
Mr. D'Amato also pulled the reins on Mr. Bruno last spring over a bill to increase the minimum insurance required of automobile owners. The bill was being pushed by trial lawyers, some of Mr. D'Amato's most important allies and financial backers. Mr. Bruno, siding with the insurance industry, opposed it. Buttonholing Mr. Bruno at a fund-raising event in Albany, Mr. D'Amato pressed him to support the legislation. Mr. Bruno grudgingly agreed, according to lobbyists who spoke on the condition of anonymity.
The bill passed easily and was signed into law.
"Al was very active," said State Senator Dale M. Volker, a Republican from western New York. "His influence was very, very strong."

The Relationship
A Pragmatist And a Hard-Liner
Friends of both men say Mr. Pataki and Mr. D'Amato genuinely like and respect each other, though they are not particularly close and do not frequently socialize together. But it is not a relationship of equals, the friends say. When the two men appear at public events together, Mr. D'Amato is generally the host or master of ceremonies. And it is Mr. Pataki who routinely consults with Mr. D'Amato, and defers to him, on political questions, not vice versa.
As close as they are, Mr. Pataki and Mr. D'Amato differ in significant ways. Mr. D'Amato is ever the pragmatist, often abandoning ideology in search of a deal. Mr. Pataki is more the ardent fiscal conservative willing to anger special interest groups, including some, like police unions and trial lawyers, that are close to Mr. D'Amato.
Earlier this year, Mr. Pataki won a telling political battle with Mr. D'Amato over legislation to prevent certain workers' compensation lawsuits. Mr. Pataki, siding with business interests, wanted to outlaw the lawsuits.
New York trial lawyers, who opposed the measure and are big financial backers of Mr. D'Amato, asked the Senator to intercede. Having lost in nearly every legislative battle this year, Mr. Pataki refused to budge on the measure, making it a major issue that delayed passage of the state budget.
At least twice, people involved in the negotiations said, Mr. D'Amato intervened with Mr. Pataki to try to broker a compromise, but Mr. Pataki would not budge.
Then in early July, the trial lawyers devised a new compromise and again turned to Mr. D'Amato, who was on a fishing trip in Colorado. He spoke repeatedly by cellular phone to the trial lawyers, who faxed him a copy of their proposal. Mr. D'Amato then relayed the proposal to Mr. Pataki, recommending that he accept it. Mr. Pataki stood his ground. The Governor's version of the bill passed.
Mr. D'Amato said in a recent interview that Mr. Pataki had been right about the issue. "I told people he was serious and he was," Mr. D'Amato said. "I think it was a great victory for him, without trying to be mean-spirited to the litigators."
Mr. Pataki said workers' compensation was just one of several cases where the two men disagreed. "I have a pretty clear sense of what I think the state ought to be doing," he said. "And he has a pretty clear sense of what he ought to be doing, in Washington."

An article on Monday about Senator Alfonse M. D'Amato's influence in Albany misidentified the Senate committee he heads. He is chairman of the Banking Committee, not the Finance Committee.

PUB-DATE: September 30, 1996
Correction Appended

SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 1; National Desk 

LENGTH: 1249 words

HEADLINE: POLITICS: THE CONGRESS;
Congress's Leaders Debate Issues With a Seldomly Seen Decorum

BYLINE: By ADAM CLYMER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Sept. 29

 BODY:
Congress's four top leaders argued over Medicare, tax cuts, education and poison gas tonight, giving the uncertain Congressional elections unique national visibility and displaying a calm civility rarely seen on the floor of either House or Senate as this session ends.
"We have delivered for the American people," said Senator Trent Lott of Mississippi, the majority leader, answering the opening question -- why Republicans should be returned to power -- with a litany of recent legislative accomplishments, including welfare, immigration and health insurance. 
 But Representative Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, the House minority leader, contended that Republicans had sought to cut school lunches, Head Start and student loans and had forfeited their right to power. "We have got to deal with people's practical, everyday problems," he said.
The first-of-its-kind national debate was televised on hundreds of public-television stations from the colonial-era House of Burgesses in Williamsburg, Va., and was followed or preceded by dozens of comparable exchanges between Senate and House candidates around the country. Tonight's debate, five weeks before the election, preceded by one week the first of three debates between Presidential and Vice-Presidential candidates.
If none of the exchanges compared even faintly to the pre-Revolutionary oratory heard in this same hall from Patrick Henry and others, the impression of thoughtfulness conveyed was far different from the shouting, insults and booing that typify much of the House debate seen on C-Span these days.
Apparently sensing the potential of the gentler approach, Senator Tom Daschle of South Dakota, the minority leader, used the debate's last moments not for partisan claims but to say, "The people want the decibel level to come down." He said, "What we need is working together, reaching across the aisle."
But the civility did not overwhelm their mission of scoring on the issues each side thought would play best with voters watching on the relatively staid PBS. Speaker Newt Gingrich, opening the discussion on foreign policy, said the first priority was to "defeat drugs," an issue he returned to several times.
And the first time Mr. Gephardt got the microphone he issued a familiar attack, saying, "The Republicans tried to cut Medicare to pay for tax cuts for the wealthiest Americans."
But Mr. Gingrich, who often uses the word "lie" to dispute that contention, reacted only by repeatedly explaining that spending for Medicare would have increased under the Republican budget plan, though not as quickly as without it. "It's a cut if you can't buy the same amount of health care," snapped Mr. Daschle. "It's a cut if your premiums go up."
The argument over tax cuts recurred and was practically the only occasion when the issues of the Presidential campaign directly intruded into the Congressional debate. The two Republicans praised and the two Democrats attacked the plan by Bob Dole, the Republican Presidential candidate, for a 15-percent tax cut.
Mr. Gingrich said it would provide $105 more per month for a family of four earning $30,000. Mr. Lott said it was part of the Republican concept of quality of life: "the opportunity to keep more of your own money."
But Mr. Daschle said sweeping tax cuts of that sort would increase the deficit, as they had in the 1980's, and he called the plan "deja-voodoo economics." Mr. Gephardt said the Republicans' tax cuts would mainly help the rich, saying, "We believe tax cuts should be focused on middle-income Americans, and people trying to get into the middle class."
Mr. Gephardt complained about cuts in spending for education, in which he included the Public Broadcasting System's own "Sesame Street." Mr. Lott replied that the $443 billion catchall spending bill the Senate is expected to approve on Monday actually provides increases in education money. "That's an election-year conversion," Mr. Daschle said.
Mr. Gingrich argued that money for education was not as much of a problem as how it was spent. "We spend far too much money on bureaucracy and not enough on teachers," he said. In his approach he repeatedly praised teachers, perhaps seeking to distance himself from attacks on them at the Republican National Convention.
Ridiculing Washington's own school system, he said it spent $9,000 a year per pupil but had too many bureaucrats to teach them well or even to attend to maintenance like repairing boilers and roofs. Congress sought to fix that this weekend with an extra $12 million, but specified that it could not be managed by regular school administration officials but instead by the financial control board that Congress set up last year to try to straighten out the city's messy finances.
Where Mr. Gingrich had cited drugs as a first priority in foreign policy, Mr. Daschle said Congress's duty was to consent to ratification of the Chemical Weapons Convention, which would prohibit manufacture, sale and use of poison gas. Mr. Lott complained that compliance with the pact could not be verified, and Mr. Gingrich said, "In foreign policy, words aren't enough, you've got to have deeds."
The two sides squabbled over campaign finance legislation. Mr. Gephardt challenged the Republicans to support limits on money raised and spent, playing on Mr. Gingrich's argument last year that the system needed more money, not less. Mr. Gingrich hit back by denouncing money spent by the A.F.L.-C.I.O. to work against Republican freshmen, saying the Supreme Court would prohibit limits on that sort of campaign spending.
"This money is dues money that is coerced," Mr. Gingrich said.
"That's not true," said Mr. Gephardt.
Mr. Gingrich was correct. The bulk of the A.F.L.-C.I.O.'s spending this year comes from a special assessment added to regular union dues, and approved at a special convention of the labor federation last winter.
Aside from taxes, the only direct reference to the Presidential campaign came when Jim Lehrer of PBS, the moderator, asked what the relationship between the Presidential and Congressional races was. Mr. Lott pointed to the measures President Clinton had vetoed, and said "We need a President who will work with us to get this job done." But he never mentioned Mr. Dole's name in that context.
Mr. Daschle breezed by the Clinton connection, saying "This President is going to be re-elected." Then he made his appeal for civility.
After the debate, Mr. Gingrich told reporters that these productions by PBS were significant because "they bring a lot more attention to Congressional races."
"It is, after all," he said, "the first branch of Government mentioned in the Constitution. We got the politics away from the 30-second attack commercial into at least a relatively reasonable dialogue."
Republicans had argued to have the participants seated, and Mr. Lott told reporters that that may have contributed to the tenor of the session. "When you put people behind podiums, and you put them in spear-throwing posture, they're more inclined to do that," he said. "It calmed the format down and brought it more into a dialogue, rather than sound bites."
But the Democrats also said they thought they had gained in their effort to nationalize this year's elections, as Republicans successfully did in 1994. "It's not just 435 local elections," he said. "There's a national set of issues that should be discussed. I'm optimistic that a counter-wave is going to happen in 1996."

CORRECTION-DATE: October 9, 1996, Wednesday

 CORRECTION:
An article on September 30 about a televised debate between Congressional leaders incorrectly described the source of money the AFL-CIO is spending on advertisements. It comes from increased assessments by the Federation on member unions, not from increased per-person dues.
